Preface 

This book is part of a journey, of which the National Seminar held 
at Bodh Gaya in 2004 was an important juncture. Since 2001, this 
journey has had an ideological background which is closely linked 
with the innovative and consistent programme on documentation, 
research and dissemination of “Culture, Development and Identity: 
The Musahars of the Middle-Gangetic Plains’ under the auspices of the 
Dalit Resource Centre, Deshkal Society (Gaya, Bihar). In the process 
of interaction with that community and the gradual development 
of experience and understanding, we have realised the indifferent or 
contemptuous attitude towards the Dalits in public discourse. For 
centuries, the caste system in the Indian subcontinent has controlled, 
regulated and hierarchised knowledge. Brahmanism, as it evolved 
over a period of time, sought to legitimise the servitude of the Dalit 
castes through its hegemony over the social universe of knowledge. 

The proposition of the Bodh Gaya seminar was formulated with 
this critical understanding. Its prime objective was to develop Dalit 
Studies as a discipline to take it beyond the token importance given 
to it in mainstream educational institutions. We had to grapple with 
a series of challenging questions: Who is a Dalit? How are Dalits 
perceived? How are they represented in history, in literature and in 
the common understanding of our society? What is the relationship 
of the Dalit with the mainstream? Can a Muslim also be a Dalit? Can 
the Dalits be a unitary category and a unified social group? What 
is the nature of gender politics among the Dalit community? Can 
we talk of a Dalit culture apart from and beyond local community 
cultures? Is the construction of Dalit Studies dependent on the 
initiative of Dalit intellectuals? Or can their folk and popular culture 
be made to constitute the theme of Dalit Studies? 

viii Preface 

Prathama Banerjee took the responsibility of conceptualising 
this seminar and she contributed to the theoretical shaping of the 
emerging discipline of Dalit Studies. Arun Prabha Mukherji, Uma 
Chakravarti and Bela Bhatia enriched this process with their inputs. 
At every stage, Anand Kumar of JNU inspired us, and Savyasaachi 
of Jamia Millia Islamia added new dimensions to the project with 
his insightful comments. The journey could not have been possible 
without the support of Ashok Aounshuman and Manoj Jha. 

Our endeavour was fortunate to receive financial backing from 
the Ford Foundation. The Foundation has shown confidence in the 
project and facilitated us to forge a path through an area where there 
are few signposts. We hope they will find this book worthy of their 
support. The key role of inspiring and shaping the process was that of 
Sumathi Ramaswamy, Programme Officer with the Foundation. 

The media meaningfully propagated these programmes and 
provided visibility in the mainstream public space through continual 
coverage. The name of P. K. Chaudhary, a journalist with the Times 
of India, Patna, is worth mentioning here. 

Thanks are due to Rajeshwar Singh, for his efforts in coordinating 
the Seminar at every stage in Gaya. 

We succeeded in giving a formal shape to the proceedings of the 
conference through a book brought out by Deshkal Publication in 
2007 which received appreciation from scholars, researchers, the 
Dalit intelligentsia and policy makers interested in the broadening of 
the agenda of Dalit Studies. 

In 2009, we got an offer from Hemlata K. Shankar, Publisher 
of Orient Blackswan, for bringing out a revised and updated 
version of the book in association with Deshkal Society to extend 
its reach to a wider readership. We are grateful to Ms Shankar for 
making a significant contribution in taking this project forward. We 
acknowledge the suggestions of the reviewer of Orient Blackswan 
which were useful for revising this volume and giving it its current 
shape. Suranjan Roy, an editor with Orient Blackswan, took forward 
the publication process with commitment and sincerity. 

Dr Muhammad Mukhtar Alam, Dharmendra Kumar, Dineshwar 
Tiwary, Rafiul Ahmed, Dhananjay Kumar and Gulshan Ara Ahmed, 
all from Deshkal Society, deserve special thanks as, without their 
commitment, involvement and interest, this book might not have 

seen the light of day. 

Introduction 

SHASHI BHUSHAN UpapHYAYy 

I 

The papers in this volume are on diverse themes broadly related to 
the Dalits and they have several stories to tell. However, one common 
theme which is explicit or implicit in most of the essays is Dalit 
assertion. This volume is primarily about the agency and assertion 
of the Dalits in society, history and literature. One of the most 
important phenomena in post-Independence India is the assertion 
by the socially oppressed groups broadly known as Dalits. Whether 
it is the Dalit woman or man, the old days of apathy are gone and 
an active, sometimes militant, mood is largely witnessed. The former 
untouchables have become conscious of their oppression at various 
levels and they are now voicing their grievances in various forms and 
in different arenas where vocal resistance is pitted against new types 
of oppression and renewed attempts at dominance. Many social and 
political tensions grow from the assertion by the Dalits which the 
dominant groups construe as challenge to their dominance. 

Dalit assertion is to be seen in the context of oppression, both real 
and perceived. This oppression by the dominant castes was endured 
and resisted continuously. However, when the resistance becomes 
vocal and the agency more pronounced, everyone, including the 
oppressed, takes notice of it. The fact that the oppressed do not take 
their oppression as a matter of course but at least talk about it is 
in itself a pointer towards the fact of assertion. Over the years, the 
assertion had become more vocal and agitational. The Dalits today 
are increasingly eager to relate the tales of oppression as well as to 

2 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

launch movements against this. What Moffatt has called ‘replication’ 
might have been an older form of assertion. To adopt surnames and 
dress like the upper castes, to perform marriage and other ceremonies 
ostentatiously, to dig wells and to construct temples in order to show 
that they can do as well as, if not better than, the upper castes are 
forms of assertion. The fact that these activities are taken as assertion 
is evident in the violent upper-caste reaction to such supposedly 
imitative activities of the Dalits. 

Historically, the conscious assertion in a modern form can be 
traced to the last decade of the nineteenth century. It demanded 
the right of the Dalits to live with honour and dignity. The term 
used to denote this was ‘manuski’ (literally, of man). This emphasis 
on human beings was derived from the “Western (or modern) view 
of democracy and the equality of man’.” It is true that the nature 
of assertion was so varied and multi-layered that there could not 
be just a single source of inspiration. It is, however, equally true 
that modernity in its various forms provided the stimulus for the 
formulation of a new structure of assertion. 

It was during the colonial period that various factors brought 
the identity question into sharp focus. Urban development created 
new avenues of employment for the Dalit people. They could now 
find work in various capacities. Although lower-end jobs like that of 
sweepers, scavengers, leather workers, mill workers and construction 
workers provided the main source of employment, certain prestigious 
jobs as in the British Indian Army were also available to these 
castes. These jobs took them away from the restricting practices of 
untouchability they had experienced in rural areas. They now became 
conscious of the existence of new kinds of relationships. Among 
other important factors which provided a powerful impetus to the 
search for identity and equality among various groups, the colonial 
census was significant. Its attempts to locate Dalits with relation 
to Hinduism proved a potent factor in stimulating such thoughts 
among them. Certain other affirmative measures by the colonial 
government also helped in stimulating a separate identity for the 
disadvantaged castes. 

These developments elicited three types of responses from the 
Dalit groups: 

(i) The majority tried to make various adjustments within 
Hinduism by avoiding several practices considered polluting by the 

Introduction 3 

high castes, such as beef-cating, carrion-carrying, etc, by tracing their 
antecedents to the upper-caste Hindus who, for various reasons, had 
fallen in social hierarchy, and by adopting ritual practices of high 
Hinduism. However, this should not be viewed as simple cases of 
Sanskritisation; these also, in many cases, involved social assertion 
by these groups. Ac times this involved a radical redefining of socio- 
religious space within Hinduism and many of these movements for 
upward mobility based themselves on the Bhakti ideas. 

(ii) A significant number, particularly in south and west India, 
embraced Christianity in trying to shed their ‘untouchable’ pasts. 

(iii) The third trend, which has now developed into a major 
movement, stressed on a radically ditterent identity tor the oppressed 
castes. It defined the Dalit identity almost in racial terms by claiming 
that the Dalits were the original inhabitants of India who were defeated 
and subjugated by the invading Aryans. This is an identity which 
seeks the most ancient lineage and is non-religious in nature, despite 
occasional attempts to establish Buddhism as the original religion of 
the Dalits. The idea of being the original inhabitants of the land forms 
the core of this identity. At an ideological level, it has the greatest 
potential to serve as the defining identity for the Dalits today. 

The result of all these developments was evident in the emergence 
of thinkers and leaders from among the under-privileged groups 
from the late nineteenth century onwards who not only questioned 
the supremacy of the Brahmans but also tried to forge a separate 
identity for the socially discriminated castes. As early as 1890, Gopal 
Baba Walangkar, a retired army person and a Mahai leader, exhorted 
Hindu leaders to treat the Mahars as equals and work towards 
ending discrimination against them. He challenged these leaders, 
including the Shankaracharyas, to prove that the untouchables 
were lower in any way than anybody else.’ In 1894, he petitioned 
the colonial government for re-admitting in the army the Mahars 
who had been disqualified from recruitment in 1892. A different 
theme was introduced in this petition. Sent in the name of Anarya 
Doshpariharakham (Non-Aryan Group for the Removal of Wrongs) 
at Dapoli, it claimed that the untouchables were actua'ly Kshatriyas 
who were downgraded by the Peshwas.” 

Such assertion spread in various parts of the country and, by the 
end of the second decade of the twentieth century, it could be fouid in 
the form of the various adi (original)-oriented movements across the 

4 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

country. The notion that the Dalits were the original inhabitants who 
were defeated and subjugated by the invading Aryans had obtained 
a wide currency among Dalit thinkers and ideologues by the turn of 
the century. This imbued the Dalits with the pride associated with 
autochthony (i.e., being aboriginal or native to a particular area), and 
the identification of the putative enemy became easy. This is necessary 
in the process of identity-formation as it provides a defined locus for 
the assertion of the oppressed groups. Dalit intellectuals acquired this 
theme from the non-Brahman movements in western and southern 
India which, in turn, adopted it from British Orientalists. This idea 
was aggressively expressed by Jotirao Phule who effected several 
modifications in the Orientalists’ findings and believed that except 
for the Brahmans and the Banias, who were foreigners, all other 
groups in Indian society were indigenous. In fact, at times it appears 
in his writings that only the Brahmans were Aryans and outsiders, 
who subjugated the indigenous Kshatriyas and others through 
conspiracy and fraud and established their rule in India. This theme 
was carried on into the non-Brahman movement of south India 
where the Justice Party and Periyar conflated the Aryans, Brahmans 
and the northerners in opposition to the Dravidians, non-Brahmans 
and southerners. Dalit intellectuals took up the fundamentals of this 
theme but injected certain modifications to claim the prime original 
status for themselves. In its first manifestation, Walangkar claimed 
that the Mahars were the true inhabitants of Maharashtra while all 
other groups were outsiders. Thus, the upper-caste people in south 
India were ‘Australian-semitic, non-Aryan and African Negroes’, 
the Chitpavan Brahmans were ‘Barbary Jews’ and the Marathas 
were ‘“Turks’.” Kisan Faguji Bansode, another Dalit leader from 
Maharashtra, declared in 1909: 

The Aryans—your ancestors—conquered us and gave us unbearable 
harassment. At that time we were your conquest, you treated us even 
worse than slaves and subjected us to any torture you wanted. But now 
we are no longer your subjects, we have no service relationship with you, 
we are not your slaves or serfs... We have had enough of the harassment 
and torture of the Hindus... If you don't give us the rights of humanity 
and independence, then we will have to take our own rights on the basis 
of our own strength and courage, and that we will do. 

‘Adi Karnataka’ (original Kannadigas), ‘Adi Andhra’ (the original 
inhabitants of Andhra Pradesh), ‘Ad Dharm’ (the original religion) 

Introduction 5 

of Punjab, and ‘Adi Hindu’ (the original Hindu) in United Provinces 
were some of the important movements of the Dalit castes in colonial 

India. In a statement issued by the Ad Dharm movement in Punjab, 
ic was declared: 

We are the original people of this country, and our religion is Ad Dharm. 
The Hindu gawm came trom outside and enslaved us .... There was a time 
when we ruled India, brothers, and the land used to be ours. The Hindus 
came trom Iran and destroyed our gaum. \hey became the owners... 
They turned us into nomads.’ 

Similarly, the principal concern of Achchootanand, the leader of 
the Adi-Hindu movement, was to convince the people thar the 
untouchables were the original inhabicants of the land: 

Sing the song of Adi Hindus. 

Awaken the community from deep slumber. 
Aryans, Sakas, Huns, Muslims and Christians 
All came here from outside. 

They are all foreigners 

Who have captured our land. 

Tell these facts to everyone. 

Dalit assertion acquired ideological and political maturity with the 
emergence of B. R. Ambedkar as the foremost Dalit thinker and 
ideologue. He was not much enamoured of the adi ideology and 
considered the emergence of the caste system as a complex process. 
He proclaimed that without the abolition of the caste system 
untouchability would not be removed. Moreover, he asserted that 
since Hindu shastras supported the caste system, they should be 
abandoned if an egalitarian and just social order were to be created. 
Such radical notions not only invited the ire of caste Hindu leaders 
and ideologues but created conflicts with several Dalit leaders as well 
who believed that a less radical approach would benefit their cause. 
The crucial element in Ambedkar’s leadership was that from then on 
Dalit thinking became decisively grounded in the modern notions of 
rationality, democracy and equality. It sought to abandon everything 
associated with pre-modern, particularly Hindu, tradition. Buddhism 
was projected as the original religion of the Dalits, conceived 
modernistically as secular, socially oriented and non-sectarian. 

In the post-Independence period there have been conscious efforts 
by Dalits to assert their presence in the field of literature also. The 

6 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

emerging Dalit intelligentsia was not satished with the representation 
of their group in Indian literature. They believed that, due to their 
caste and class bias, the savarna writers had misrepresented the 
Dalits. It was, therefore, urgently required that the latter take the 
reins in their own hands and write about themselves. This literary 
protest has given rise to Dalit literature—a significant trend. It has 
brought the Dalits to the forefront as writers and protagonists. It 
has given voice to their protest. Some of these writers established 
the Dalit Panthers in 1972 which expressed dissatisfaction with 
the earlier Dalit parties which were accused of being opportunist. 
They instead sought to combine the caste and class identities of the 
oppressed for the future movements. Although the Dalit Panthers 
failed as an organisation, their spirit remains alive. The Dalits 
have found their voice, even though it is not expressed in a single, 
uniform pattern. An excellent literary expression of this assertion 
can be found in a poem by Namdev Dhasal, one of the founders of 
the Dalit Panthers: 

Turning their backs to the sun, they journeyed through centuries. 
Now, now, we must refuse to be pilgrims of darkness. 

That one, our father, carrying, carrying the darkness is now bent; 
Now, now we must lift that burden from his back. 

Our blood was spilled for this glorious city 

And what we got was the right to eat stones. 

Now, now we must explode that building which kisses the sky! 

After a thousand years we were blessed with a sunflower giving fakir; 

, ) 
Now, now we must, like sunflowers, turn our faces to the sun. - 

Il 

This volume is divided into four sections—Dalits and society, Dalits 
and literature, Dalits in history, and castes among Indian minorities. 
The first essay in the volume is by Jyotsna Macwan and Suguna 
Ramanathan and is based on their book, Journeys to Freedom: Dalit 
Narratives, which narrates various strategies adopted by the Dalits 
to break the chains of social and economic servitude; it is a saga 
of individual and collective assertion by the Dalits to overcome the 
disabilities imposed upon them through centuries of oppression 
and discrimination. The present article, however, deals with the 
differences between various Dalit castes in Gujarat which make a 
common identity very difficult. Such problems arise because, as the 

Introduction 7 

authors state, there exists ‘a structured caste system among Dalit paztzs 
themselves’. The caste consciousness and resultant ditferences among 
the Dalits are explored primarily with reference to three important 
Dalit castes in the region—Vankar, Rodnt and Vialmike. Among these, 
the Vankars, who were traditionally weavers, took advantage of the 
development of textile mills and moved into the cities and were 
modernised. The process also led to the conversion of a significant 
number to Christianiry and now, more recently, co Ambedkarite 
Buddhism. The Rohits, also called Chamars, are less advanced, 
and the Valmikis are the least developed. Besides caste distinctions, 
different levels of development have created further divisions. ‘These 
divisions are exacerbated owing to religious conversions, particularly 
among the Vankars. 

It appears ironical that in a volume centred around Dalit assertion 
the opening essay deals with sharp divisions among the Dalits which 
makes the torging of a Dalit identity a challenging task. However, the 
paper also implicitly tells the story of assertion which has developed 
into a situation where we can talk about Dalit identity and, along 
with the authors, make assumptions about a ‘Dalit world’. Thus, 
alongside the narrative of differences and rivalries one also finds the 
narratives of various levels of assertion. In fact, the differences have 
become sharper due to increased assertion which, however, is as yet 
devoid of a broader unifying vision and requisite ettort. 

Padma Velaskar’s paper explores the extreme nature of oppression 
of Dalit women who are at the receiving end of antagonism from 
three structures—caste, class and patriarchy. Each in itself has the 
potential to demoralise a person. It can be imagined what would 
happen if the three combine. Velaskar finds this situation more 
complex than simple arithmetic would dictate. As the member of 
a Dalit caste, the woman shares affinities with the male; but, on 
account of her gender, she finds similarity with non-dalit women; 
she is also a part of the working class. According to Velaskar, ‘the 
interaction of patriarchy with caste and class does not make for 
“greater” or “lesser” oppression, but for a qualitatively different type 
of oppression’. She thinks that we still do not have a critical theory 
which can take account of these intersecting levels of oppression. 
The struggle of Dalit women against these oppressive structures has, 
however, brought to the fore the reality that their oppression needs 
to be viewed in a more comprehensive manner. 

8 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

Ramaiah’s article surveys several fields, presents a disturbing 
reality, raises important questions, but proposes a debatable solution. 
He rightly criticises the assumption right since the colonial period 
that Indian villages are the ideal place to live, where each caste and 
group shares justly in the common village resources, and where 
there is happiness and freedom. He emphasises that in each village 
there are separate settlements for the Dalits. Moreover, Indian 
villages, contrary to the nationalist assumption, have been ‘domains 
of inequalities and exploitation’. His important contribution is a 
comparative study of two Tamil villages—one in which the Dalits 
are in a minority and the other in which they are in the majority. 
He compares the achievements and success rates of the Dalits in the 
two villages and shows that in the Dalit-dominated village the Dalits 
were in every respect better off than in the other village. On the basis 
of this observation, he reaches the conclusion that there is a need for 
separate villages for the Dalits away from the upper-caste villages: 
‘Ambedkar’s demand that a socially distinct community should be 
allowed to settle in separate villages is worth considering’. This is a 
disturbing conclusion to arrive at after a meaningful study. To begin 
with, the separate settlements in the villages follow various patterns. It 
is not that there are separate settlements only for the Dalits; it is quite 
possible that each caste may sometimes have a demarcated settlement 
of its own. Secondly, the practice of untouchabiliry may exist even 
among Dalit castes, as Ramaiah describes in relation to the Pallars, 
Parayars and Chakkliyars. A similar situation exists in various parts 
of the country between Mahars and Chambhars, between Chamars 
and Valmikis, and so on. If we pursue the above argument, it would 
imply that there is no possibility of a common Dalit settlement. Such 
a separatist solution will lead to fragmentation rather than unity and 
will not serve the purpose of social democracy which the author 
advocates. A better solution would be a firm, united and determined 
democratic struggle to end social and economic discrimination at 
various levels, as Ramaiah himself points out. 

The essays by Ritambhara Hebbar and Ashok Singh are focussed 
on a single village in Maharashtra and Bihar, respectively. In both 
cases, the protagonists are heroic in their own ways. Pattuguruji, in 
Hebbar’s account, is a Mahar who is constantly against the ba/utedari 
system; like the jajmani system of north India, service providers here 
are linked to a jajman who pays them, mostly in kind and annually, 

Introduction 9 

they may work for many jajmans at the same time, and the Mahars 
who work as village servants get ‘baluta’ in return, which consists of a 
small plot of land as well as some daily payment in kind tor survival. 
Pattuguruji is concerned about the attainment of social justice tor the 
Dalits in his village and beyond. He fights against the gaonkr (village 
servant) system because, in his opinion, it promotes idling habits 
among the persons concerned: ‘When | asked my people to leave 
gaonki, it was not because they had land to fall back on. The point was 
that | didn’t want them to be spongery. | was deeply convinced of this. 
I wanted them to develop ‘toiling’ bodies. Somewhere they had to 
learn how to earn tor themselves. In this earning, birth is justifed.’ 

Through his narrative, Hebbar explores the complex dynamics 
of caste relations within a village, and that of the Dalit struggle for 
equality and justice. This struggle is much broader and goes beyond 
the village and even caste. As the author says: “Dalit struggles are 
not just about caste; [they are] about a way to be. The end of caste 
discrimination was just the first step; the ultimate aim was to bear 
torm to a vision on how to live a lite of dignity.’ 

In Ashok Singh's story, a relatively silent heroism is at work—a 
struggle to break the shackles of total subjugation imposed on the 
Dalit castes of the village. In a Dalit family of north Bihar, the 
grandfather and father of two young boys were determined to educate 
them against all odds. And they succeed in not only educating them 
but also enabling them to get good government jobs. The Dalit 
struggle in the village, however, goes beyond this. The Dalits vote 
for a party (Congress) against the landlords’ wish. Moreover, one 
Dalit files a suit against his landlord for non-payment of wages, a 
completely new development in the context of the village. 

Sanjay Kumar's paper deals with the struggles of Dalits for getting 
rights over their homestead lands. This interesting ethnographic 
account highlights important issues related to the requirements of 
the law, intricacies of the administrative set-up and arbitrariness of 
the village landlords and other notables. He suggests that the law, its 
enforcers and the opinion-makers have to be sensitive towards the 
basic needs of the marginalised sections of our population. 

The next section contains three essays concerning the relationship 
between Dalits and literature. Dalit writers have often complained, 
and rightly so, that almost the entire canon of Indian literature has 
neglected Dalits as subjects. Even when the reality of caste oppression 

10 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

is portrayed, it is informed with pity and sympathy. Such depiction 
ignores the Dalits as conscious, living entities. This theme is taken up 
in Raj Kumar’s paper which traces the emergence and growth of the 
genre now widely known as Dalit literature. He points out that ‘the 
literature of this country, until very recently, has never focused on 
the problems of untouchables’. He believes that ‘the untouchables of 
India have continued to remain neglected and ostracised in literature 
as in Hindu society’. The Dalit literature which has emerged as a 
protest against such upper-caste literature ‘aims at creating a counter- 
culture and a separate identity for the Dalits in the society’. Such 
literary assertion is ‘aimed at bringing about social change through 
a revolution. This revolution will serve two purposes—to destroy 
‘the decaying components of existing social and cultural order’ and 
to reconstruct ‘the social reality in the direction of total freedom, 
equality and human dignity’. 

Smita Patil explores the internal dynamics of Dalit literature by 
analysing the representation of women therein. Dalit women, as 
Padma Velaskar has pointed out earlier in the book, stand at the 
intersection of caste, class and gender. This gives them a unique 
position in the Dalit thinking and movement. However, Dalit 
literature, with its emphasis on caste-based oppression, has paid 
relatively low importance to gender discrimination, particularly 
among the Dalits themselves. Patil has attempted to address these 
issues with relation to the writings by Dalit women and men. 

S. B. Upadhyay’s paper deals with two related themes—the 
representation of Dalits in Premchand’s literature, and the location 
of these writings with relation to what has come to be known as 
Dalit literature. The severe criticism of Premchand’s writings by some 
Dalit writers derives from a world view which considers individual 
experiences and self-representation as the basic mode of literary 
expression, even though it speaks in the name of the Dalit community 
in general. Premchand was the most important Hindi-Urdu writer 
in the colonial period whose works represent the Dalits as conscious 
and vocal subjects. Upadhyay thinks that, though Premchand was 
not a Dalit, the criticism of his writings as anti-Dalit is unfair. 

In recent years, there has been great interest among the Dalits 
to locate their position in the anti-imperialist struggle. They assert 
that the dominant historiography has completely neglected the role 
of Dalits in India’s struggle for Independence. They feel that the 

Introduction 11 

time has come to put the record straight. In this regard, there has 
been a proliferation of popular historical writings and establishment 
of memorials based on the lives and struggles of local Dalit heroes 
and heroines. The essay by A. K. Biswas stresses che role of a Dalit 
community in Bihar in the great revolt of 1857. Biswas regrets the 
fact that conventional historiography has ‘elbowed out’ trom its 
accounts the ‘common, non-descript’ rebels. This has resulted in a 
situation when ‘the names of valiant and selHess heroes of the first 
war of independence sound strange to our ears’, Biswas, on his part, 
has tried to bring the subaltern into the mainstream of history by 
writing about the heroic participation of the Rajwars in the revolt 
of 1857. 

Prathama Banerjee submits a stimulating theory for a general 
perspective on the reasons for the neglect of caste as a social and 
analytical category in the discipline of history. In the early cexts 
of history by Indians, caste was praised as a benign category and a 
specific mark of Indian-ness, being construed as a ‘rational division 
of labour and occupations’. At another level, it was considered as a 
sectarian and divisive category prejudicial to the development of a 
unihed nation. In either case, it remained as a relic of the past which 
was not relevant for a rationalist writing of history. Another reason 
for this neglect was the preoccupation with religious and communal 
antagonism which overshadowed caste in the historical discourse. 
Thus, the ‘long-term dominance of the question of religion/ 
secularism resulted in a subsumption of caste ... under the question 
of nationalism/communalism.’ Even when caste is taken into 
consideration in history writing, its transference into the category of 
race or class does not enable us ‘to truly grasp the materiality of caste 
as category. Banerjee argues that ‘the materiality of caste is above 
all ... a politics of the body’, an idea which is ignored by all political 
parties, including Dalits. This view of caste is highlighted by feminist 
writers who assert that ‘caste as a structure is centrally reproduced 
through a patriarchal enforcement of endogamy, where marriage and 
the control of women’s sexuality are paramount. 

The final section deals with the occurrence of caste among 
Indian minorities. In religious terms, it is only Hinduism which 
incorporated untouchability in its religious corpus while other 
religions, like Sikhism, Christianity, Islam and Buddhism, had no 
place for it. Thus it is only among Hindus that such groups might 

12 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

be said to have existed. However, the Indian social reality defied this 
religious logic. According to one scholar working in this field, this is 
a communal view of the situation. The colonial census had initiated 
this view. Despite evidences to the contrary, the British enunciated 
this view because they considered the religious groups as distinct 
from each other. The reality is otherwise. The lower-caste converts to 
Christianity were not severed from their castes on conversion. They 
continued to live and marry within castes. They also continued to 
suffer similar social discrimination as earlier along with their Hindu 
caste fellows.'” In effect, the low-caste converts to Christianity 
face dual discrimination. They are not entitled to reservations in 
jobs and educational institutions, while they continue to suffer 
untouchability both from the upper-caste Christians and Hindus. 
Even in the Churches, they are allotted separate seats so as not to 
pollute the caste Christians. One such convert, Wilson, wrote in 
anguish: 

Asa child ofa village Christian family, as. a son of a suffering rural pastor, as 

a student of a Theological Seminary, as a preacher, a pastor and a professor 

of theology, I have been a victim of this twice alienated predicament 

myself. As an Indian who experienced the oppressive and feudal nature 

of traditional Hindu sucial system and a Christian who experienced 

the demoralising and enslaving nature of traditional Christian spiritual 

vision, I found no other alternative left open to me but to take a leap 

to a humanistic cultural past which transcends dehumanising religious 
13 
systems. That is where I stand today. 

In fact, all major religions of India practise this kind of social 
discrimination against certain groups within and outside in varying 
degrees. They also possess a caste system akin to Hinduism. It, 
therefore, becomes illogical to define a Dalit only in religious terms 
as belonging to the Hindu social order. 

The three articles in the section explore the varying dynamics of 
this situation among Indian Muslims and Sikhs. The articles by Imtiaz 
Ahmad and Yoginder Sikand discuss the existence of discriminatory 
caste norms among Muslims, despite the doctrinal emphasis on 
equality among all believers. Islam was an important influence on 
Indian society in the pre-colonial period. It came to India with Arab 
merchants and rulers in the eighth and ninth centuries. But then it 
remained a marginal presence. From the twelfth century onwards, 
with the rulers owing allegiance to Islam, it became one of the major 

Introduction 13 

factors in Indian society and culture. Its influence spread both through 
the kings who conquered various regions and established Islamic 
institutions, and the Sufis who started a process of assimilation with 
the Indian tradition. 

Islam is generally perceived as an egalitarian religion. In fact it 
preaches equality among believers and males. In Islam, as practised 
in the Arabian peninsula, kafirs and women were not treated at par 
with male believers. Moreover, it established a hierarchy berween 
master and slave and between the rich and the poor. It prescribed 
appropriate forms of behaviour tor them and promised that all would 
be rewarded by Allah on the day of judgement. When Islam spread 
outside Arabia to a more complex world, Muslim society acquired a 
hierarchical character. Various justifications were given tor this. The 
notions of social hierarchy, which were not part of the Quran and the 
early Muslim community, were now gradually incorporated in the 
emerging schools of Islamic law (mazahib) and jurisprudence (figh). 
The notion of equality of status in the matter of marriage (kafaa) 
was now stressed by the scholars of Islamic jurisprudence which 
elaborated on the social equals within whom marriage could take 
place. This was in contrast to the Quranic tradition which emphasied 
piety as the only basis for selecting the marital partner. * 

In India, the situation became complicated when Islam encountered 
a society segregated on the basis of hereditary castes. The conversion 
to Islam from various strata of Indian society added to the complexity 
because the converts brought their old beliefs and practices to the 
new fold. Scholars have pointed out that the Indian Muslim society 
can be broadly divided into three social strata—ashraf (noble born) 
which includes the victorious nobility and the high-caste powerful 
Hindu converts to Islam; the aj/af (mean and lowly) which includes 
commoners either migrant or converts; and arzal (excluded) which 
comprises people engaged in the lowest occupations in Indian society. 
The people from the last category are almost treated as untouchables 
and excluded from the society both socially and physically as is the 
case with Hindu ‘untouchables’.'’ The Muslim society in India is 
further divided into endogamous castes, and intra-group marriages 
are not only justified but enforced by Islamic jurisprudence in India. 
Ahmad and Sikand both convincingly argue that in India Muslim 
society is stratified on the basis of castes which follow the rules on 
endogamy and commensality, though less strictly than the Hindus. 

14 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

Ronki Rams article deals with a similar phenomenon within the 
Sikh community, although the situation there seems to have taken 
a more explosive turn. It explores an important dimension of social 
relations in Punjab, particularly within the Sikh religious tradition. 
The religious tenets of Sikhism emphasise equality. However, the 
social dynamics among its followers ensures the overall domination by 
Jat Sikhs, who form the majority, and consequent marginalisation of 
the Dalits, an important segment in Punjabi society. The resentment 
felt by the Dalits has led to the ‘near-exodus of Dalits from Sikhism 
towards the alternative socio-spiritual space provided by the deras’. 
The paper traces the socio-economic roots of this conflict which also 
appears in a more open form in ‘Sikh-dera’ confrontations. Although 
the Sikh community does not have the same type of caste system as 
exists among the Hindus, the presence of endogamous castes and 
prevalence of social exclusion belies the sense of equality which the 

Sikh Gurus had preached.

SEGHE@IN A 

Dalits and Society 

l 

Resolving Dalit Identity: 
Vankars, Chamars, Valmikis 

Jyotsna Macwan anp SuGUNA RAMANATHAN 

This paper is based on our recently published book Journeys 10 
Freedom, a study that records the paths taken by the Dalits of Gujarat 
to end the oppressive systems of discrimination. Our research was 
based on interviews with fifty-six persons selected to achieve (within 
the constraint of our grassroots contacts) as wide a spread as possible 
with respect to jati, region, gender, social position, education and 
religion. 

After a brief introduction in Section I to indicate that this paper, 
while based on our research, has a different thrust, Section II sketches 
an outline of Dalit social history in Gujarat. Section III discusses the 
challenges facing the constitution of a unified Dalit identity. Section 
IV offers some concluding remarks. 

While the study tracked the paths to freedom taken by our 
respondents, this paper highlights certain identity problems that we 
observed in the course of our interviews. The unjust nature of the 
caste structure exploiting Dalits from a place outside the Dalit world 
is one thing; the problems confronting Dalits within their world is 
another, and it is the latter that forms the subject of this paper. Within, 
there are differentiated groups all competing for portions of a not 
very large cake. Identity in these circumstances adds to, or subtracts 
from, those fighting for a slice of that cake. Identity comprises both 

20 Jyotsna Macwan and Suguna Ramanathan 

inherited and acquired features; one’s sense of self is both a given core 
(culturally determined) and a continuously modified and porous 
border (changed by economic and political forces). Both core and 
borders are affected by the economic and political power equations 
obtaining at any given historical moment. In using the word ‘identity’ 
in this paper, we imbue it with a connotation of dignity and human 
potential, a right that every human being is entitled to. The fact that 
there are cases where identity has been stigmatised demands a reversal 
that restores dignity to the persons thus deprived. How can they 
undertake restoration of dignity? The answer to this question calls for 
an understanding of the complexity of the identity-making process 
at work among Dalits in Gujarat today. Identity in the Dalit world is 
immensely complicated by perceived differences among the different 
Dalit castes and a reluctance to let jati afhliation be absorbed into a 
larger Dalit identity. There is a structured caste system among Dalit 
jatis themselves and if, as we believe, Dalit unity is a crucial issue, 
we need to take account of such differences. Our research in Gujarat 
leads us to believe that the dream of unity is beset by a number of 
problems in this area, some of which we describe. We begin with a 
brief account of the different Dalit castes in Gujarat though we deal 
with only three, Vankars, Chamars, and Valmikis, in the course of 

the paper. 

Il 

Gujarat, unlike some other states, has not experienced any social 
movements that have challenged its traditional social order. Gandhi's 
decision to stay in Gujarat made it a ‘home’ for the nationalist 
movement, but that only served to preserve the traditional society. 
‘Harijans’, as Gandhi named them, were to remain in their places and 
the caste system kept intact in his vision of a village-based economy. 
Under Maratha rule in Gujarat, the Rajputs (also called Darbars) 
replaced the old zamindars, and they now survive as relics of a feudal 
past in the villages.’ The landowning Patels (regarded in some quarters 
as originally Sudras and today a powerful class) benefited greatly 
from the land settlements under British rule, and became landowners 
rather than agriculturists, moving upwards in the caste ladder. The 
wealthiest of these are called Patidars and command immense power. 
It was, and has been, in the interests of both Patels and Darbars 

Resolving Dalit Identity 21 

to scotch any caste resistance from the former ‘untouchable’ castes, 
for they are thereby ensured cheap (sometimes free) labour under 
the jajamani system. |. P. Desai, making a survey of untouchability 
in Gujarat in 1976, found it to be widespread in the rural areas in 
‘public’ situations, for example, buses, school seating arrangements, 
and seating at panchayat meetings. Privately practised discrimination 
is even more widespread, and transtormations have ‘left a great edifice 
of discrimination intact (Mendelsohn and Vicziany 1998, 41). 

The 1991 census lists thirty distinct Scheduled Castes and one 
group of unclassified persons, whereas the 1971 list had only forty- 
one Scheduled Castes. Some discrete castes have been clubbed 
together; some, like the Mochis (cobblers, leather workers) have 
been altogether excluded in Gujarat, unlike in neighbouring states. 
The Vankars, also called Mayavanshis, are easily the most numerous: 
they are followed by Rohits, also called Chamars or Bhambhis; the 
third largest group comprises the Bhangis, also called Valmikis. 
Included in the list are Senvas, Turis, Nadias, Garos, Pasis, Turi- 
Barots, Tidgars and others. The traditional occupation tollowed by 
each of the castes is now more or less a thing of the past; migration 
and industrialisation have changed that considerably. Except tor the 
Valmikis, those who remain in rural areas rarely engage in the old 
occupations. Many of them are agricultural labourers; too poor to 
own much land themselves, they are sharecroppers (bhagiyo) or daily 
wage labourers (mayoor). Hierarchical ranking amongst themselves 
is observed, with the Vankars at the top of the ladder. Endogamy 
keeps the caste identity as intact as with the upper castes. Garodas 
are Vankar priests who equip themselves for rituals and are the 
equivalent of Brahmins in the Dalit social scheme, needed because 
Brahmins ‘proper’ (Gaur Brahmins) are prohibited from performing 
rituals for Dalits in the Kali Yuga. Garodas refuse to accept Senvas 
as clients because the latter, who are traditionally basket makers, are 
responsible for castrating bulls and therefore regarded as murderers 
of cows. Tidgars (makers of bows and arrows) are lower down the 
caste ladder than Vankars and Rohits because they are seen as killers 
of innocent birds and animals (nirdos jiv).” 

A major problem, therefore, is that a parallel caste hierarchy is 
observed among the Dalit communities. Shalini Randeria’s research 
among the Sabarkantha Dalits explains that the Dalit social world 
replicates the internal system of caste hierarchy of the savarna model 

y-9) Jyotsna Macwan and Suguna Ramanathan 

(1992). In this system, the Garodas perform the role of priests, the 
Vankars and Rohits play the role of Kshatriyas (protecting the service 
castes dependent on them), and also of Vaishyas (Vankars engaged 
in the trade of woven cloth, the Rohits in the trade of hides, with 
both responsible for dead cattle). The Senvas, according to Randeria, 
perform menial tasks, like the Sudras in the Brahminical system; 
and the Vankars and Rohits do not accept food and drink from the 
Senvas. The Turis, Tidgars and Senvas contest among themselves for 
superiority. The Bhangis, who are untouchables, are excluded from 
the services of the others, and marginal in their sub-system. The 
dominant castes are the Vankars and Rohits, and the relationship 
between them is tense. 

I 

The challenges facing Dalit unity have their roots in both traditional 
sources and contemporary changes. The Vankars form the largest 
group. Their pre-eminence is hotly contested by the Rohits. The 
Vankars do not intermarry with the Rohits, but they may eat together 
(‘betis, no; rotis, yes), which indicates closeness in the caste ladder. 
But proximity has not meant peaceful relations; in fact, conflict 
between the Rohits and Vankars is a byword among the Scheduled 
Castes, proverbs and sayings testifying to this tension. The Vankars 
maintain that the Rohits, by continuing to deal in leather, bring 
down the opprobrium of upper castes on both communities. This 
is compounded by the fact that in rural areas there was traditionally 
some competition between the two castes as to the ownership of 
dead cattle that members of either caste can drag away from the 
village chora or square. The Vankars are traditionally weavers (not 
‘untouchable’ in other parts of India), and it may be the custom of 
disposing of dead cattle adopted by them in Gujarat that made them 
ritually ‘polluted’. 

There is no doubt that the Dalit caste that has made the most 
progress in Gujarat is the Vankar caste. Money and position build 
confidence and change the perception of outsiders, and those who 
have done well through education or enterprise experience the old 
caste identity differently from those who have not. It is clear that the 
Vankars want to distinguish themselves as distinct and different from 
the Rohits, whereas the Rohits want recognition from the Vankars 

Resolving Dalit Identity 23 

that they are not lower down. The rivalry between the Vankars and 
the Rohits manifests itself in a deliberate distancing by the Vankars 
from the Rohits and ditterent food habits. i 

The Vankar dominance, at the top of the Dalit pyramid, is to be 
attributed to several factors. To begin with, employment of Dalits 
in the textile mills of Gujarat was largely contined to the Vankars, 
many of whom moved to the big cities, drawn by the associated 
prestige and atractive salaries. The Vankars, as a caste of weavers. 
regarded themselves as naturally fitted to work in the textile industry 
and certainly it served to expose them to the lure of urban lite and 
changed their way of looking at the world. While the textile industry 
in Gujarat declined drastically as manufacture turned to power looms, 
the Vankars who worked in those mills were inevitably marked. They 
endured discrimination in the mills, crue, but exposure made them 
resistant and introspective and modernised them. The closure of the 
textile mills has led to wretchedness and misery (as recorded in our 
book), but the point to be made here is that modernity came early 
to the Vankars. 

Second, Christian missionaries directed their efforts towards the 
Vankars and gave them access to schooling. The result is that notable 
achievements like those of Joseph Macwan in the literary world or 
of Martin Macwan in the NGO sector, and many others, seem to 
be the monopoly of this caste group. It is clear that a snowballing 
effect gives the Vankars an edge over other Scheduled Castes in 
Gujarat. 

The Vankars, having reaped the fruits of developmental efforts, 
have undergone an identity change but government policy has 
introduced a complexity into the picture. Christian Vankars, the best 
educated among them, cannot take advantage of the reservation quota 
as they are now classified as falling under the Baxi Panch (the name 
for OBCs in Gujarat).’ Though conversion to Christianity has not 
erased caste distinctions or effected striking social change, it has on 
the whole brought to Vankars a sense of superiority over their Hindu 
brethren. The point to be emphasised is that a number of factors have 
entered to stratify the Vankar jati itself. The Vankars in Junagadh 
and Bharuch regions have turned to Ambedkarite Buddhism as a 
political weapon with which to fight caste discrimination, but this 
introduces yet another layer of difference and the chance of Vankar- 
Rohit unity correspondingly diminishes . 

24 Jyotsna Macwan and Suguna Ramanathan 

Among the Rohits, conversion has played a less important role. 
The example is often cited of a Chamar group that converted to 
Catholicism and reconverted to its old Hindu identity when the 
Vankars refused to intermarry with them. While the Vankars have 
done better through education, our sample shows that the Rohits of 
Sabarkantha district have taken to business and enterprise readily, 
showing little interest in religious conversion as a means of identity 
change or empowerment. The Shramjivi Samaj was a movement that 
was active in Sabarkantha in the 1980s to mobilise Dalits dispossessed 
of their land by the construction of adam on the Hatmati. The Rohits 
and Adivasis of the district banded together to fight for land. The 
Shramjivi Samaj has receded from Sabarkantha but the Rohits have 
continued their demand for schools and land, and effected change 
through a determination to pull themselves out of poverty. But many 
of our Rohit respondents showed awareness that the old divisions 
remain. Chaganbhai Parmar remarks: “The most tragic thing is the 
bitter fight between the Vankars and Chamars. Suppose there are 
some Vankar officials and I am a Chamar official and there is an 
interview, the Vankars will favour only a Vankar, or if I am on the 
panel J will favour only a Chamar’.” Political parties have made use 
of the rivalry. The Vankars are generally with the Congress party, 
and the Rohits, in recent years, increasingly with the Bahujan Samaj 
Party. 

The caste that has lost out the most in the Dalit caste hierarchy and 
been least affected by modern identity-changing forces is the Valmiki 
caste, untouchable not only to caste Hindus but to other Dalit castes 
as well. It is interesting to note that the Vankars in Gujarat refer 
to Valmikis as Harijans, denoting thereby that Gandhiji’s term is 
applicable to this former Bhangi caste alone. Though some of their 
traditional marks of humiliation have ceased to be imposed, their 
disposal of dirt and refuse, including dead animals and human excreta, 
has remained an important occupation. They are still sweepers for all 
sections of the village, and a niche for them has been created in the 
towns as ofhcial safaikamdars. The migration of Valmikis from the 
villages to the cities in great numbers only to obtain employment in 
the same type of cleaning jobs offers a striking contrast with migrant 
Vankar mill workers.’ The Valmikis are indispensable in all public 
places, institutions, factories, hospitals, cinema houses, business and 
NGO offices—anywhere where there are latrines to be cleaned. They 

Resolving Dalit Identity 25 

live in urban slums, ghettoes constructed by the municipalities in 
order to ensure that labour force is available to continue cleaning the 
excreta and the dirt generated by all of us. It is at this fundamental 
level that one observes no change in the traditional occupation of 
the Valmikis. 

The situation is riddled with an inherent contradiction. The 
humiliating nature of the work might result, one would expect, in 
a declining number of Valmikis ready to continue doing it; and yet, 
they stay with the same job that seems to be reserved for them. It is 
not that the Valmikis, especially the Valmiki women, do not want 
other jobs, bur society assures them of a permanent job only if they 
are ready to clean our backyards and latrines. Sanitation work may 
be the worst of all kinds of work; our senses bear witness daily to the 
repugnance we feel when we have to walk through a littered street, or 
in front of unclean urinals. What we seem to torget is that the same 
revulsion is felt by the Valmikis when they are asked to do certain 
types of jobs. Manubhai, one of our respondents, is quite explicit: 
unless you drink and numb your senses you cannot do this job ~* 
What is more tragic in his case, as the following narrative explains, 
is that he has attempted a number of times to find a job somewhere 
else, but has failed. All he gets is an order to sweep the rooms while 
he waits for the interview. 

I will tell you a case in the hospital where I work. There was a dead body. 
burnt to death. The body had remained there for four to five days. it gave 
off a foul smell, and it had to be taken away. It was kept in a closed house 
and there were insects on it. We were three of us. They said ‘Take the 
body away’. The cold room in the hospital was full. There is a separate 
P.M. room where it was to be kept on ice. The smell was so terrible and 
powerful that even after dousing it with dettol, phenyl, and turpentine, 
the smell did not stop. Then we told that person to bring a mask so 
that we could cover our faces with them. We decided to get on with the 
work of putting the body on ice. If we don’t do the work they fire us 
and suspend us. So we have to do it. We thought that we would bring a 
mask, tie it on our faces, put the body on ice and then go away from the 
place. We were two of us: Dinesh Vaghela and me from Mogari village. 
We took away the body and dropped it in the room. Due to the germs 
a saliva-like substance formed in our mouths. It was twelve at night. We 
went to a pan shop and told the shopkeeper to give us pan masala, and 
ate it. We drank water after two hours. I hope my elder son will become 
an engineer. I will not let him work in the occupation I follow, and I do 
not want him to be in my position. 

26 Jyotsna Macwan and Suguna Ramanathan 

The narrative is self-explanatory. Manubhai does not want his son 
to follow in the same footsteps but his expression sounds more like 
wishful thinking than a willed determination to put an end to this 
type of work. The State emerges as the main culprit for this sordid 
situation. Every municipal corporation has created a reserved niche for 
them, and Valmikis are the only community engaged in the cleaning 
department; indeed one might say that the market for sanitary services 
appears, from an economic perspective, monopolistic. The Valmikis’ 
bargaining power vis-a-vis the municipality is strong, though they do 
not use it; if they refuse to do the work, no one else will. 

Our respondents record that when it comes to assigning types of 
work, caste differences and discrimination operate in the department 
in charge of cleaning the city. Non-Valmikis do apply for the job, but 
they have other means to further their careers. Vasantbhai, describing 
some of the cases he has dealt with, says that if a non-Valmiki gets 
the job, he bribes or threatens someone else to enter the manholes; 
and adds that nothing can be done because most of the union leaders 
are savarnas. 

What kind of society is this that reserves the task of waste disposal 
to a particular group? To imagine oneself entering sewage manholes is 
all that is needed to understand the horrendous nature of the task. The 
dry latrines of the villages may have been, on the whole, replaced (or 
such is the claim), but inadequate water resources make the problem 
of removing human excreta a very real one in our overcrowded cities 
and towns. It is convenient for civic authorities to reserve these 
tasks for certain caste groups in the name of ‘an expertise’, ‘being 
accustomed’, ‘a readiness’. Who, given a choice, would do this work? 
The only answer to that question is ‘no one’; and in the light of 
that answer the State must take responsibility for perpetuating an 
appalling village system in a modern urban form. Why the occupation 
is not spread out among different castes (as occurs in other societies 
where too waste disposal has to be dealt with), why there are no 
disincentives for Valmikis taking these jobs, why Valmikis alone 
are given these jobs too readily—these are all issues that the State 
ignores. The civic authorities want the jobs done, and the easiest 
way of managing is to get a caste already ‘trained’ in it to undertake 
it. Occupation is tied to caste here in the most insidious modern 
setting. The war to be fought is against two different mindsets—one 
of the government that too readily gives employment according to 

Resolving Dalit Identity 27 

traditional caste occupations; and the other of a group that accepts a 
traditional occupation in a disguised form perhaps because it is easier 
to give up than to fight, because jobs are so desperately needed, or 
because practices have been too long internalised. 

It is clear that the Valmiki identity may well be the most ditheult 
to modify upwards and that the very schemes in place to afford them 
job security only serve to keep them tied to age-old occupations. 
In such a case, assimilation with other Dalit castes becomes doubly 
dificult. The drop-out rate in schools is higher among the Valmikis 
because of poverty, and a vicious circle results. The Valmikis lose out 
on the reserved quotas because the Vankars and Rohits fare better 
in the competition for the same jobs because, as said earlier, they 
have had more exposure and more education. Thus, the government 
jobs that no one wants—sataikamdars—are left for the Valmikis 
to fill. Extreme poverty turns this ironically into a safe ‘haven’ of 
employment. The resentment with which the Valmikis regard 
Vankars, as they see them moving ahead, suggests that a process of 
‘caste-isation’ of the class struggle is at work, where an entire caste is 
seen to be not only on the move but as snatching benefits that would 
otherwise go to those below. 

Traditional rivalry and modern differentiation among Dalits could 
be addressed by organisations working for them. This has not worked 
in the case of BAMCEF (Backward and Minority Communities 
Employees’ Federation), which has been active in spreading 
Ambedkarite ideology in Junagadh and Bharuch. There it has been 
split by succession issues. Political parties take over the breakaway 
organisations (e.g. a Dalit Sangh was founded by a Congressman to 
counter a Patel Sangh in the region), and the Ambedkarite ideal is 
forgotten. Uniting Dalit castes with OBCs remains a distant dream 
even though one of our respondents (J. D. Makadiya) suggests, “We 
may not build family relations but we must agree to come together 
on the political platform’, but another of our respondents (Ramesh 
Kaba) notes that the Dalit Yuva Sangathan which has members from 
all castes has fewer from the Baxi Panch group than from the Dalit 
castes. Yet another of our respondents (Manubhai Makwana) is 
convinced that political machinery ‘does not want us to be united’. 
The fact that in Sabarkantha, Dalit organisations are dominated 
by the Rohits, traditional rivals of the Vankars, makes the task of 
uniting Dalit castes complicated. The Dalit Sena is an organisation 

28 Jyotsna Macwan and Suguna Ramanathan 

that works for all Dalits, but Devabhai Makwana who was elected 
president in 1994 says that it is an illusion to think that it can work 
wonders. 

Finally, different sects also tend to disrupt Dalit unity by creating 
religious differences. Christian Vankars in central Gujarat testified 
to the superiority that conversion to Christianity had conferred on 
them by providing them access to schooling. Buddhism bestows 
yet another identity, and Hindu religious sects like Swadhyaha and 
Swaminarayan strive to keep Dalits within the Hindu fold. Thus, 
forces competing to shape Dalit identity introduce differences that 
are not easily overcome. 

IV 

Given these multiple factors shaping Dalit identity in Gujarat today, 
disunity is not very surprising. The graded inequality implicit in the 
system makes the forging of Dalit unity doubly difficult. What we 
are desperately in need of is a symbol or a set of symbols that can 
bring Dalits under one umbrella, spurring them on to record and 
reject a history of injustice. Many have negotiated the mainstream 
and disappeared in its flow, though most of our respondents testified 
to a subterranean, wordless exclusion that has not gone. 

Resolving the problems of Dalit identity is an immensely difficult 
task. It appears that only through the forging of alliances with 
oppressed communities elsewhere in the world on the basis of human 
rights can some change be brought about. Fighting for human rights 
against a syncretising Hindu model that privileges duty over rights 
is the only way out. Divisiveness and caste awareness among the 
poorest sections of society lead to perpetuation of poverty and low 
levels of development. It is worth noting that as economic levels rise, 
the middle castes have come together to form a class; distinctions 
matter less now than formerly. Even the highest castes, whatever 
their private arrangements in such matters as marriage, cannot lord 
it over the middle castes, now a middle class, as they once could. It 
is in the poorer sections, where caste divisions hurt most, that they 
flourish most; hence the need to band together in the struggle to 
break out of a structured system. 

Despite the challenges, the forging of a Dalit identity is of great 
importance. The fact remains that Dalits still endure discrimination 

Resolving Dalit Identity 29 

of different kinds; they are still poorer, have less access to education 
and less hope of a bright future chan the privileged castes. And given 
the liberalising of the economy, and the fact that the State is moving 
out of welfare sectors even though water, sanitation, health, and 
literacy have not been secured for all, it is of utmost importance 
to let the Dalit voice be heard loud and clear, demanding its rights 
to these basic necessities, refusing tasks that have been imposed on 
them in the name of duty and authority. For this, unity among Dalits 
is crucial. Obviously they cannot be unified on the basis of religion 
that either marginalises them or cuts them off from Dalits of other 
persuasions. Neither can class be a unifying factor. As indicated in 
this paper, class distinctions, as they have operated hitherto among 
Dalits, divide one Dalit jati from another, and an entire jati moves 
upwards into another class. This is where Martin Macwan’s struggle 
to unify Dalits on the basis of long-denied human rights makes 
sense. The discourse of human rights places the Dalit predicament 
in a world setting. The approach wins international support from 
cultures that would otherwise have a dim idea of the institution of 
caste; it appeals to other oppressed communities worldwide; and it 
has a rational as well as moral appeal stripped of religious trappings. 
With this the issue of Dalit identity moves on to the world stage and 
will be harder to ignore.
