At the Intersection of Caste, 
Class and Patriarchy: 
Exploring Dalit Women’s Oppression 

PADMA VELASKAR 

It is now well accepted that any adequate theoretical account of 
gender power relations and women’s oppression must be firmly 
located in societal power contexts. A contextualised, relational 
understanding of women would mean very fundamentally, in the 
Indian context, situating women in the caste (and also separately 
and more contemporaneously in class and other contexts) to which 
they belong by birth and socialisation and in which they acquire 
a position and identity rooted in structural and cultural realities. 
Situated at intersections of caste, class and patriarchy, which express 
power relations in the politico-economic, social and cultural domains 
and their legitimising ideologies, Dalit women’s gender oppression 
cannot be decontextualised from these realities. Simultaneously, 
however, while looking at the nature of women’s oppression vis-a-vis 
patriarchal male domination systems, fundamental inequalities and 
hierarchies between women can scarcely be glossed over. 

This paper raises and addresses some basic theoretical issues which 
are crucial to furthering our understanding of the Dalit women 
question. Secondly, it explores some aspects of the interface berween 
caste and patriarchy in order to understand the specificity of Dalit 
women’s oppression. Finally, it examines the need and scope for Dalit 
feminist critical studies. 

‘Dalit’ refers in this paper to former untouchable caste 

communities. 

32 Padma Velaskar 

Theorising Dalit Women’s Oppression 

It may be useful to state at the outset that we subscribe to the historical 
materialist view that starts with oppression and leads up to a theory 
of history in which history is seen in terms of changing relations of 
domination between groups (Delphy 1981). In relation to women, 
it emphasises that sexual oppression, economic exploitation and 
socio-cultural subjugation are the underlying sources of unequal 
gender relations and of gender categories that govern these relations. 
Having said this, | must qualify this statement by saying that I 
simultaneously subscribe to the basic insight, hugely supported by 
feminist anthropological study, that the pan-cultural fact of women's 
subordination has to be set against the diversity of social structures 
and cultural formations (Ortner 1974). By virtue of their location in 
these diverse societies, women, including those in obviously male- 
dominated societies, are subordinated but not equally so or in the 
same way, and are not completely powerless and perennial victims of 
the system. The attack on universalising, totalising and homogenising 
the category ‘woman’ has come from several diverse, and dissimilar 
quarters—materialist, post-structuralist, post-modernist and black 
feminists—emerging in the context of the new politics of cultural 
difference that has swept the world. 

In my view, there should be no difficulty in accepting that the 
social category ‘woman’ has differed and varied across time and 
space. It is a necessary realisation, which serves as a corrective to 
prevailing tendencies of feminist politics and theory. Yet, I do not 
think it wise to abandon the generic category altogether; rather, 
let us rework the category of ‘woman’. This is best done through 
a theoretical-historical exploration rooted in structural and cultural 
analysis that provides us with more acute understanding and not 
generalised notions of subordination and oppression. The need is to 
analytically demonstrate the emergence of women as socio-economic 
and political groupings within particular historical and cultural 
locations (Mohanty 1991). 

Structures and cultural values have real effects in material, 
representational and experiential terms and one cannot but be critical 
of recent trends that move away from structural analyses. Analyses of 
stratifications and social institutions, their bases of power and how 
they shape caste, class and gender are required. Not ‘differences’ 
per se, but power relations create and construct inequalities and 

Exploring Dalit Women's Oppression _ 33 

oppression. By not focusing on structural processes, we obscure the 
central dynamics of power relations in micro and macro aspects. 
Furthermore, antagonistic, and not merely benignly ‘different’, levels 
of the hierarchical spectrum uncover gender-specific mechanisms of 
oppression. Yet, at the same time, the attempt should be to break 
down the fixed polarisation between upper caste/lower caste, and 
men/women. In the context of ongoing social and political change, 
one should guard against over-determinancy of caste vis-a-vis social 
relations and in general against monolithic and homogeneous 
conceptions of any kind. 

In the Indian context, caste, class and patriarchy are, among 
others, three hierarchical axes of social structure which are crucial 
to the understanding of gender relations and the oppression of caste 
Hindu, Shudra and Dalit women. 

In an effort to understand and theorise Dalit women’s oppression, 
one has drawn upon relevant works of historical analysis and 
materialist, cultural and critical theory. Indigenous critiques. in 
particular Ambedkar’s theoretical analyses of caste, untouchability 
and patriarchy, have been indispensable to understanding their finer 
nuances and to provide a bottom-up view. Feminist theories inform 
the analysis centrally. 

Literary and non-literary works of Dalit women speaking from 
direct experience—Baby Kamble, Mukta Sarvagod, Shantabai 
Kamble, Kumud Pawade, Hira Bansode, Urmila Pawar, to name a few 
from Maharashtra—gave me the first sense of the deep complexity 
of the problem at hand. It was their insights of lived experiences 
which inspired me to begin this search. My field research among 
Buddhist and Mang women of Maharashtra enabled deeper study. 
Theoretical explorations by Dalit women writers have also been 
extremely valuable. 

The following section presents the tentative framework that 
we have developed to understand and explain the Dalit women's 
structural and cultural location. It is based on a historical exploration 
of the traditional social order of caste-untouchability-patriarchy 
and has drawn upon the above-mentioned sources. I express my 
indebtedness to those numerous Dalit women and men who shared 
their life experiences with a stranger and ‘outsider’ like me. My 
insights are wholly and completely dependent on these voices and | 
hope I have done justice to them. 

34 Padma Velaskar 

Caste Patriarchy and Dalit Woman’s 
Oppression—Exploring the Specificity 
of Her Historical Subordination 

The ideal typical framework explores the linkages and articulation 
between the three interlocking systems of caste, untouchability and 
patriarchy and the implications of caste-patriarchy for Dalit women 
in traditional society. The attempt is to examine the relationship 
between the structure of caste and the structure of patriarchy 
with a view to understanding the specificity of the ideological and 
sociological position of the former untouchable caste women. The 
view of caste taken here incorporates notions of caste as a material 
system and cultural ideological system of status hierarchy. 

Caste is a specific type of feudal organisation, and castes are 
economic and political groupings with the distinguishing essence 
of being status groups founded on distinctions of purity (Velaskar 
1998). In the main, four critical areas have been identified for 
interactive analysis and deeper, separate analysis. 

1. Untouchable Women’s Structural Location in the 
Social Relations of Caste-Untouchability 

Dalit women share equally the impure natural substance and occupy 
a shared structural location and cultural construction with Dalit 
men in the status order of caste hierarchy. To speak of a common 
subordination of all women being Dalit is to mystify the crucial fact 
of a basic sub-human social equivalence of men and women of the 
untouchable castes and a basic human social un-equivalence vis- 
a-vis women of upper castes who are accorded social and cultural 
power and position by caste. Sex is not a variable of consequence in 
relation to a fundamental and critical social definition and status. As 
untouchables, both men and women constitute impurity incarnate, 
and are both polluted and polluting. Thus, there is a fundamental 
commonality in relation to personhood and human dignity and 
social status, between untouchable men and women. 

2. Caste Feudalism and Control of Untouchable 
Women’s Labour 

Within the caste, the feudal mode of production, which brings about 
a shared social location and cultural construction of untouchable 

Exploring Dalit Women’s Oppression 35 

men and women, is an integral part of this coercive system. These 
people are engaged in various forms of servile, culturally derogated 
occupations and menial service. The labour system is also structured 
by the sexual division of labour. However, caste shapes very direetly 
an integral and crucial part of the gender status and identity of Dalit 
women. This is that of a derogated female labourer/slave, doing 
specific forms of menial service work which are socially necessary 
but constructed as polluting and interior. 

In stark contrast to high-caste women who are withdrawn from 
the public production system, Dalit women are its compulsory part 
and at its lowest end. Upper-caste women are excluded from the 
public sphere and restricted to the household where the primary tasks 
of domestic service and family care, which are their lot, are socially 
exclusionary and restrictive, yet culturally glorified. Moreover, 
they belong to economic classes/households whose burdensome 
productive work is allocated to the low castes—both men as well as 
women. For the untouchables, the low cultural constructions and 
valuations of work and labour are equally significant culrural markers 
of their place in the production system. 

3. Purity—Pollution Ideology and Construction 
of Low Caste Sexuality 

The third area is the cultural construction of sexuality. The structural 
cultural order of purity and pollution is extended to domains of 
sexuality and marriage and is crucial to the specific sexual subjugation 
of low-caste women. The dualistic construction of Indian women in 
gender ideology legitimises the sexual subordination of all women. 
But the specific cultural sexual denigration of the untouchable 
woman takes place in the dual context of the general denigration of 
sexual morality and marriage practice of Shudra castes and of two 
specific caste-related contexts: (a) the denouncement and prohibition 
of inter-varna and inter-caste marriage supported by a theory of 
miscegenation (varnasamkara) which denigrates sexuality of low- 
caste men and women; and (b) women’s low-level participation in 
the social production system which comes to be associated with lax 
standards of sexual morality. 

Therefore, in contrast to the idealised upper-caste wife/mother, 
the low-caste woman's sexuality is constructed as easily transgressable 
and the woman herself is constructed as sexually more promiscuous. 

36 Padma Velaskar 

Moreover, as public labourer, she is perceived as relatively free from 
male control. Thus, the tight control of the upper-caste womans 
sexuality and uncontrollable upper-caste male sexuality (being 
unable to cross the caste barriers imposed on upper-caste women), 
which is sanctioned by patriarchy, necessitates the polar opposite of 
the upper-caste ‘devi’ and women with loose morality who can be 
sexually exploited. Inevitably, and almost as a rule, it is the low-caste 
woman who fills this slot, this social ‘need’. She is also sexually abused 
in order to violate the masculinity and honour of the low-caste man 
and his community. 

4. Gender Relations and Patriarchal Ideology 
in the Dalit Household in the Context of 
Caste-Family Interface 

The force of Brahmanical patriarchal ideology is fully expressed in 
the relations of reproduction in the context of marriage and family. 
Herein lies the commonality with the high-caste woman. The Dalit 
woman's (especially in the role of wife/daughter-in-law) sexuality, 
fertility, and labour are controlled within the private domain. The 
specificity lies in that the control over domestic/reproductive labour 
is exerted not only by family/kin but also by caste and constitutes 
socially reproductive labour also. Woman's primary definition as 
public labourer provides no escape from family labour. Outside 
labour affords a relative measure of autonomy, economic and cultural, 
from family patriarchy. Family relations, in particular motherhood 
and kinship, are also a bulwark against the public patriarchy of caste. 
Simultaneously, and despite the freedom of divorce and remarriage, 
the woman-in-marriage may be subject to tight sexual surveillance, 
sexual abuse and violence. 

The Dalit Women’s Oppression 

The ‘ideal’ typical framework, though sketchily presented above, 
helps illuminate the varied sources and facets of Dalit women’s 
oppression (for a detailed analysis, see Velaskar 1998). Caste 
and gender oppression and class exploitation all exist, but in an 
interlinked way. Their many social, economic and cultural relations 
are inextricably and inseparably linked in private and public life. 
Caste uses gender to construct caste status, power relations, and 

Exploring Dalit Women’s Oppression 37 

culturally derogative differences, and to sexually oppress low-caste 
women. The caste-untouchability economic system directly disables 
and exploits, forging existential commonality berween men and 
women. The form and manifestations of caste-patriarchy oppression 
are distinctly ditterent from those experienced by upper-caste women, 
Thus, three interlocking systems of caste, class and patriarchy create a 
multidimensionality, simultaneity and intensity of oppression, which 
is destructive to the experience of Dalit women. 

Need to Develop Dalit Feminist Critical Theory 

The above analysis suggests that interactive/intersectional analysis 
would be a fruittul line of enquiry to understand caste, class and 
patriarchy and must be seen, not separately or in isolation, but in an 
interlinked manner. Caste, class or gender alone cannot adequately 
explain Dalit women’s experiences. They are intersecting and 
interlinking and not discrete systems of power. That is why, in the 
above formulation on this theme, I have chosen to use the conjoined 
term ‘caste-patriarchy’ 

As with all mainstream social science theory and discourse, the Dalit 
discourse has been largely confined to analysing and illuminating the 
lives of Dalit men, ignoring or negating the gender dimension. In 
fact, one is well aware of the polarised manner in which theoretical 
studies have proceeded in the fields of caste and Dalit studies and 
feminist studies. Leaving aside a few notable exceptions (Dietrich 
1992; Chakravarti 1993; Ram 1992; and Bardhan 1993, to name a 
few), feminist analyses in an attempt to recover women from their 
being subsumed under caste and class have ignored these social 
realities and underestimated their significance in structuring social 
relations, gender relations and subjectivities. Feminist literature 
continues to deploy the ambiguous and conjoined term ‘caste-class’ 
to register cognisance of social differentiation between women. But 
such a wide and ambivalent categorisation scarcely does justice to 
the densely complex natures of caste and class and their complex 
implications for gender. 

It may also be emphasised that the vast and complex literature 
on caste has similarly remained silent on women and gender issues. 
Despite the serious engagement with the question of Indian women's 
position by two great anti-caste revolutionaries, viz., Phule and 

38 Padma Velaskar 

Ambedkar, theories of caste, firmly rooted as they are in the long- 
standing tradition of ‘male’-stream social science theories, paid scant 
attention to women’s specific role and position in analysing the system. 
Dalit politics and scholarship has also remained largely gender-blind. 
Indeed, the Phule-Ambedkarian critique on caste-patriarchy did not 
see the light of day till Dalit women themselves recovered and re- 
appropriated it in the context of their daily experiences of oppression 
and of a newly emergent gendered political consciousness. 

It is evident that the interaction of patriarchy with caste and class 
does not make for ‘greater’ or ‘lesser’ oppression, but for a qualitatively 
different type of oppression. Understanding this difference should 
be the central goal of the Dalit feminist theoretical project. One 
would have to draw upon several theoretical perspectives to reconcile 
dichotomies and establish interconnectedness. Indeed to build an 
Indian theory of patriarchy we need to accommodate these differences 
between women so as also to take cognisance of commonalities 
between men and women that bear upon gender power relations 
within different caste/community groups and see the impact of these 
on women’s subjectivities. 

In the context of contemporary economic and social change one 
needs to examine Dalit women’s historical experiences from the 
perspective of caste, economic class, politics and patriarchal angles, 
focusing on the transformation in work, wages and family life. As 
in the past, not caste nor class or patriarchy alone can adequately 
explain Dalit women’s experiences in the present. Complex caste- 
class-political articulations are reflected in the atrocities against Dalits 
and in the continued sexual assaults and abuse of poor Dalit women. 
Political economy perspectives point out how women’s labour 
continues to be a source of capitalist accumulation. Its linkages with 
caste determine that impoverishment does not affect all women, or 
all women to the same degree. It affects the lowliest castes the most. 
As the material feminists point out, poverty is not mainly a function 
of gender or caste but a feature of capitalism which affects children 
and men too. Caste and gender divide the work force and underscore 
the profound exploitation of certain groups. 

It is precisely because capitalism has become more perverse, 
insidious and brutal that a rigorous and revitalised feminist analysis 
of class dynamics is politically necessary now. Women’s cheap labour 
guaranteed through caste-based and patriarchal gender systems is 

Exploring Dalit Women's Oppression __ 39 

fundamental to the accumulation of surplus value tor capitalist profit 
and expansion. Class also divides Dalit women. bringing about borh 
their embourgeoisement and sanskritisation. Dalit feminism must 
respond to the emerging salience of class along with the qualitative 
difference brought about by caste. They must attempt to emphasise 
unity of caste, class and gender and dismiss efforts to bifurcate identity. 
At the same time, they should challenge the over-determinancy of 
caste and give attention to gender, class and sexuality. 

In posing fundamental challenges to existing linkages between 
knowledge and power, and between text-book and hierarchical power 
relations and cultural/ideological hegemony, Dalit critical theory 
must continue to build upon social structural analyses of caste and 
patriarchy chat are prefigured in the writings of Phule and Ambedkar 
and further develop Dalit feminist theory. Feminist rejections of 
totalising, universalising and homogenising frameworks of patriarchy 
have already paved the way for deconstruction. There is a need to 
develop Dalit feminist theory which will uncover ideological and 
material specificities that constitute Dalit women, at both subjective 
and objective levels, and recover women's oppositional histories/ 
positions. We also need to account for the complex differentiation 
and relations that shape contemporary patriarchal forms and 
changing social and political lives of Dalit women. Finally, even as 
it struggles for space on a hitherto high-caste terrain, critical Dalit 
theory must engage with the issue of inter-subjectivity across other 
oppressed sections of society if it is to pursue its agenda of social 
justice. As of now, the situation points towards wide caste, class, 
communal and gender-based gulfs and in-commensurabilities. The 
present bleak scenario—of a virtual disappearance of radical social 
movements, a retreat of social progressivism and the taking the edge 
off Dalit protest—demands serious effort at an enlargement of both 
liberationary theory as well as social movements.
