Social Democracy in Indian Villages: 
The Experience of Dalits in 
Southern Tamil Nadu 

A. RAMAIAH 

Introduction 

India as a sovereign, socialist, secular and democratic republic is 
committed to secure to all its citizens justice—social, economic and 
political; /iberty of thought, expression, faith, belief and worship; 
equality of status and opportunity; and fraternity, assuring the dignity 
of the individual and the unity and integrity of the nation. The 
purpose of this paper is to understand how far India has been able to 
secure for the Dalits, since Independence, justice, liberty and equality, 
which are logically the prerequisites for ensuring fraternity among 
the Dalits and other citizens, particularly in the villages. However, 
the focus here pertains only to social democracy experienced by 
Dalits in Tamil villages. Social democracy refers mainly to the social 
status that the Dalits gained as citizens of free India and the extent 
to which they have been able to articulate and assert such freedom or 
democracy in their day-to-day social interaction with fellow villagers 
belonging to different castes. This paper is an attempt to answer 
whether or not Indian villages are conducive enough for the Dalits 
to experience social democracy. 

Indian Villages 

According to the 1991 census, India has over six lakh villages. 
There have been attempts by eminent sociologists to classify Indian 

42 A. Ramaiah 

villages by adopting different criteria. Though the aggregates of 
permanent villages have been classified on the basis of people's social 
differentiation, stratification, mobility and land ownership, it has 
been done mainly in terms of their class or economic status. Despite 
the fact that most Indian villages are caste-ridden and are, therefore, 
known for caste-based social relations and power politics, caste has 
not been given due importance in classifying villages, even in major 
works like Rural Sociology in India by Prof. A. R. Desai. He has 
clubbed ‘caste’ under ‘Other aspects of composition of rural people’, 
as if it were a non-issue (Desai 1996: 15, 21). Im fact, there can be 
hardly any village without one or a few families belonging to ‘lower 
castes’ or Scheduled Castes or Dalits, though there are many villages 
without non-dalit families. There is a popular saying particularly in 
western India: ‘Jethe gao, tethe maharwada’ meaning, ‘wherever there 
is a village, there is a maharwada [a settlement of Mahars, a Dalit 
caste in Maharashtra]’ (Zelliot 1966: 17; see Burra 1996: 152; Karve 
1996: 191). It is indeed not an exaggeration to state that India lives 
not in six-lakh-odd villages, but in more than twelve lakh villages 
since each village has an annexure village comprising only Dalits, 
often away from it. 

There were a number of studies, particularly during the 1950s and 
1960s, focusing on the unique nature of Indian villages. Interestingly, 
the findings of these studies differed very much from the popular 
image of Indian villages portrayed by the nationalists and literary 
writers. However, what is often underplayed in such studies is the 
phenomenon of social capital that each caste group enjoys, which has 
direct implication on the issue of social democracy in villages. Social 
capital here refers to the social and economic status of each caste 
group and the strength it generates by way of building mutual trust, 
reciprocity, and network within each caste or between like-minded 
caste groups for mutual benefit. By virtue of being placed at the helm 
of the caste ladder, the ‘upper caste’ is assured of higher social capital. 
And as per the norms of caste, those placed at the bottom of the 
caste ladder can hardly have access to social capital, as they constitute 
mostly the ‘untouchables’ who, according to caste dharma, ought to 
be deprived of such capital and therefore social democracy as well. 

The Indian village structure is such that it does not give any scope 
for Dalits even to aspire for social democracy. It is on this point that 
social scientists have differed in their analysis of Indian villages. This 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu | 43 

paper seeks to answer one fundamental question: whether or not 
Indian villages are conducive tor caste groups to experience social 
democracy. The paper comprises three sections. While section A deals 
with ‘perspectives’ pertaining to the fundamental question, Section 
B focuses on ‘proposed alternatives’. Section C brings to light the 
experiences of villagers in social democracy with a view to verifying 
the validity of existing perspectives and the alternatives proposed. 

A. Contesting Perspectives 

To explain whether or not Indian villages are conducive to social 
democracy, one may think of two broad but contesting perspectives: 
(a) ‘village as domain of social democracy’, and (b) ‘village as domain 
of inequalities and exploitations’. The attempt in this section is to 
explain Indian villages in the light of the two perspectives. 

(a) Village as Domain of Social Democracy 

For a non-Indian or even for the urban Indian elite, who has never 
lived in an Indian village even for a day, village lite may look simple 
and often romantic. It is often glorified as a place of harmonious 
simplicity and as virtually self-sufficient communities with few ties 
with the outside world. It is said with pride that Indian villages 
have remained unchanged despite complexities of modern urban 
civilisation. Influenced by such notions, the British Governor in 
India, Sir Charles Metcalfe, had described Indian villages in the 
following words: 

The Indian village communities are little republics having nearly 
everything they want within themselves and almost independent of 
foreign solutions. They seem to last where nothing else lasts. This union 
of the village communities, each one forming a separate little state in 
itself.... is in a high degree conducive to their happiness and to the 
enjoyment of a great portion of freedom and independence (quoted by 

Gandhi 1962: xi). 

Most of the literary giants and nationalists like Tagore and Gandhi 
voiced the same opinion about Indian villages. Emphasising the 
importance of Indian villages, Gandhi said: ‘India lives in its villages. 
If India is not to perish, we have to begin with the lower rung of 
the ladder. If that was rotten, all work done at the top or at the 
intermediate rungs is bound to ultimately fail’ (Gandhi 1935: 68). 

44 A. Ramaiah 

As noted above, most Indian social scientists, national leaders and 
politicians have claimed time and again that “India lives in villages’, 
and Indian villages are nothing but ‘little republics’ where everyone's 
dignity is upheld, and where there is scope for meeting everyone's 
needs and aspirations. The image of Indian villages as portrayed by 
the popular academia and national leaders has been aptly expressed 

in the following paragraphs: 

Indian villagers share use of common village facilities—the village 
pond (known in India as a tank), grazing grounds, temples and shrines, 
cremation grounds, schools, sitting spaces under large shade trees, 
wells, and wastelands. Perhaps equally important, fellow villagers share 
knowledge of their common origin in a locale and of each other's secrets, 
often going back generations. Interdependence in rural life provides a 
sense of unity among residents of a village. 

A great many observances emphasise village unity. Typically, each 
village recognizes a deity deemed the village protector or protectress, 
and villagers unite in regular worship of this deity, considered essential 
to village prosperity. They may cooperate in constructing temples and 
shrines important to the village as a whole. Hindu festivals such as Holi, 
Dipavali (Diwali), and Durga Puja bring villagers together. In the north, 
even Muslims may join in the friendly splashing of coloured water on 
fellow villagers in Spring Holi revelries, which involve village-wide 
singing, dancing, and joking. People of all castes within a village address 
each other by kinship terms, reflecting the fictive kinship relationships 
recognized within each settlement. In the north, where village exogamy 
is important, the concept of a village as a significant unit is clear... A 
woman born in a village in India is known as a daughter of the village 
while an in-married bride is considered a daughter-in-law of the village. 

But how far are these observations valid in the real rural social life? 
Defending the need for caste divisions in villages, Mukherjee (1987: 
182) argued: 

For the village community system it was a social need that the village- 
units should not burst asunder by the tension generated within them by 
contradictory aspirations of the people in social and material life; and 
this need was fulfilled by the jat:—division of society, whereby everybody 
(however humbly or loftily placed in the societal hierarchy) had a definite 
socio-spiritual position and specific work to do. 

Reiterating his point that caste has a positive role to play, M. N. Srinivas 
(1992: 9) opined: ‘In recent years, caste has come to be seen as [a]divisive 
institution but in traditional pre-British India, the castes of a village 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu 45 

or a small region formed an inter-dependent economic and social 
whole, having certain common beliefs, ideas and values. 

These views clearly convey that many eminent sociologists, social 
scientists and national leaders find nothing wrong with the caste 
system though some of them did feel that caste had begun to play a 
divisive role, particularly after Independence. 

(b) Village as Domain of Inequalities and Exploitation 

Against the benign views of the Indian village as discussed above, 
Ambedkar, the principal architect of the Indian Constitution, gave 
an altogether different image of Indian villages. To him, Indian 
villages were nothing but a domain of despair, injustice. exploitation, 
discrimination and dehumanisation. Indian villages were not a 
single social unit as often claimed, bur constituted mainly of castes. 
Ambedkar once told the Constituent Assembly, ‘| hold that these 
village republics have been the ruination of India... What is the village 
but a sink of localism, a den of ignorance, narrow-mindedness and 
communalism’? He questioned as to how those who were so strongly 
opposed to communalism, casteism and factionalism in the nation 
could be so blind to their presence in the village (see Beteille 2002). 
More specifically Ambedkar argued as follows: 

The population in the village is divided into two sections—(i) Touchables 
and (ii) Untouchables. The Touchables form the major community and 
the Untouchables a minor community. The Touchables live inside the 
village and the Untouchables live outside the village in separate quarters. 
Economically, the Touchables form a strong and powerful community. 
while the Untouchables are a poor and a dependent community. 
Socially, the Touchables occupy the position of a ruling race, while 
the Untouchables occupy the position of a subject race of hereditary 

bondsmen (Ambedkar 1979: 426). 

He further stated that in every village the ‘touchables’ have a code, 
which the untouchables are required to follow. These include the 
following: 

1. The untouchables must live in separate quarters away from 
the habitation of the Hindus. 

2. Their quarters must be located towards the south, since the 
south is the most inauspicious of the four directions. 

3. They must observe the rule of distance, pollution or shadow 
of pollution as the case may be. 

46 A. Ramaiah 

4. They should not acquire wealth such as land or cattle and 
also should not build a house with tiled roof. 

5. They have no right to put on a clean dress, wear shoes, put on 
a watch or gold ornaments. 

6. Even giving high-sounding names to their children is 
prohibited. Their names should rather indicate contempt. 

7. Sitting on a chair in the presence of a Hindu, riding on a 
horse or a palanquin through the village, taking a procession 
of untouchables through the village, not saluting a Hindu, 
speaking a cultured language and coming into the village 
on a sacred day which the Hindus treat as the day of fast 
and at or about the time of the breaking of fast: all these are 
considered as offences under these codes. 

8. Besides, going about speaking and thereby fouling the air 
and the food of the Hindus, and wearing the outward marks 
of a touchable and passing himself off as a touchable are also 
offences punishable under these codes. 

The code also consists of duties that the untouchables have to 
perform for the ‘touchables’. These include carrying the message of 
any event to the house of a Hindu and doing all manual work on 
important occasions without any remuneration. Such duties also 
include resubmission of their women to members of the village 
community to be made the subject of indecent fun. 

To ensure that these codes of conduct are strictly observed and 
adhered to, most villages have the village panchayat, known as 
Chavadi Court in Tamil Nadu. If any of the untouchables go against 
the order of the chief of the village panchayat who is often the head 
of the caste Hindus in the village, then the lynch laws are applied 
and accordingly they are subjected to merciless beating and torture. 
The caste Hindus do so with a view to striking terror in the hearts of 
untouchables and to demonstrate the absolute power of the village 
panchayat chief (Ambedkar 1989: 120-121). 

In contrast to the popular claim that the village is a domain of 
peace, Ambedkar viewed the Indian village as a place of perpetual 
contest between caste Hindus and the untouchables (Ambedkar 
1979: 426). 

In such a contest, the Untouchables are always at bay especially for two 
reasons. Firstly, they and the caste Hindus are unequally matched as far 
as their numerical strength is concerned; they are scattered into a few 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu ) 47 

families in each village all over India. Secondly, they are a disunited body 
infested with the caste system in which they believe ay much as do the 
caste Hindus. This has given [rise co] mucual ov alry and jealousy and 
made common action impossible (Ambedkar 1989: 265-66). 

This unequal sharing of power and status in villages between the 
two is further clear from the following observation: “The hide-bound 
caste system became rigid only within stagnant villages whose chief 
intellectual product, the Brahmin, was stamped with incurable 
rusticity elevated to religious dogma’ (Kosambi 1956; 243). 

Social scientists even in the post-Independence period have 
reiterated that democratic notions were never prevalent in our 
village communities (Desai 1987: 170; Karve 1987: 191). Studies 
prove that the image of the [ndian village as perceived by the social 
scientists is very different from thar of the nationalist or literary 
imagination. Changes are no doubt taking place in che villages, but 
those changes have not led to the elimination of either division or 

inequality (Beteille 2002). 

B. Alternatives 

The two nationalists—Gandhi, who considered Indian villages as 
little republics or a domain of social democracy, and Ambedkar, who 
considered them as having no scope for social democracy—have also 
proposed alternatives to secure every social group social justice in 
every village. Gandhi proposed a change ot heart on the part of caste 
Hindus, which he considered necessary as a penance for the injustice 
done to the Dalits. He was for decentralisation of power, particularly 
at the level of the village panchayat. 

Realising that the caste Hindus would never give up the status 
and power that they had been enjoying all along, Ambedkar viewed 
the entire problem differently. Though Gandhi favoured the village 
panchayat as a viable means to ensure justice to the Dalits, Ambedkar 
considered it a mechanism for the upper castes to perpetuate 
oppression and exploitation. Commenting on the power and 
attitude of the village Periambalagar, who is in a way the president 
of the village panchayat, Ambedkar argued that the Dalits, given 
their socio-economic and numerical vulnerability, would not be ina 
position to impress upon the village panchayat to protect them and 

ensure justice (Ambedkar 1989: 120). 

48 A. Ramaiah 

In conclusion, he observed: 

So long as the present (village) arrangement continues, it is impossible for 
the untouchables either to free themselves from the yoke of the Hindus 
or to get rid of their untouchability. It is the close-knit association of the 
untouchables with the Hindus living in the same village which marks 
them out as untouchables and which enables the Hindus to identify 
them as being untouchables (Ambedkar 1979: 425). 

He, therefore, suggested: 

At present, the Hindus live in the village and untouchables live in the 
Ghettoes. The object is to free the untouchables from the thraldom of 
the Hindus. 

It is the system of village plus the Ghetto which perpetuates 
untouchability and the untouchables therefore demand that the nexus 
should be broken and the untouchables who are as a matter of fact socially 
separate should be settled into separate villages exclusively of touchables 
in which the distinction of the high and the low and of touchable and 
untouchable will find no place (Ambedkar 1979: 425). 

Ambedkar demanded separate settlements also for the betterment of 
the Dalits’ economic condition. In this regard, he observed: 
They are a body of landless labourers who are entirely dependent upon 
such employment as the Hindus may find it profitable to pay. In the 
villages in which they live they cannot engage in any trade or occupation, 
for owing to untouchability no Hindu will deal with them. It is therefore 
obvious that there is no way of earning a living which is open to 
untouchables so long as they live in a Ghetto as a dependent part of the 

Hindu village (Ambedkar 1979: 425-426). 

It is now almost six decades since Ambedkar gave his warning in 
the Constituent Assembly against idealising the Indian village 
community. To make democracy more effective, particularly in the 
villages, many institutional strategies and innovative measures have 
been attempted since then. What has been our achievement so far on 
this front? To answer this vital question, we may look at a few Indian 
villages and record people’s views with regard to their experience in 
social democracy in their village life. 

C. Social Democracy in Tamil Villages 
Area of Study and Data Base 

® e e e » | e e . 
The qualitative and quantitative data’ used in this section of the 
paper are collected from two villages of southern Tamil Nadu, viz., 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu 49 

Akramesi and Keelaparthibanur, between November 1989 and April 
1998. Relevant data were also collected between April 2002 and 
April 2003 to update the conclusions drawn earlier. ‘The randomly 
chosen respondents include fifty members belonging to the Pallar 
caste and a few purposively selected leaders of Pallar and high castes 
of the two villages. The cwo villages are located in Paramakudi taluk, 
Ramanathapuram district, which is known for its protest against 
the caste system and the supremacy of Brahmins. If the taluk town 
Paramakudi is considered the central reference point, Akramesi 
village falls in the north-east at a distance of twenty-one km and 
Keelaparthibanur in the north-west at a distance of 16.9 km. 

Akramesi village is predominandy inhabited by che middle or 
dominant castes (caste Hindus), and the Scheduled Castes are not 
only numerically in a minority but also dependent on the former 
both for their livelihood and physical security. This village is 
surrounded by many villages with caste Hindus’ dominance. In 
contrast, the Keelaparthibanur village is predominantly inhabited 
by the Scheduled Castes, particularly the Pallar Caste—the high 
caste among the Scheduled Castes—and they are also economically 
independent. The caste Hindus here are relatively less in number. 
This village is surrounded by villages with similar characteristics. The 
choice of such villages is to understand whether or not the higher 
numerical strength and better economic status of Scheduled Castes 
really helps them experience social democracy, in other words, 
protect their self-respect and dignity and also protect themselves 
from atrocities and exploitations, particularly in their surrounding 
villages. Before understanding social democracy from the experiences 
of the Dalits of the two villages, it is necessary to discuss the problem 
of caste in the Tamil society in general and in Ramanathapuram in 
particular. 

Caste System in Tamil Society 

Unlike other parts of India where caste can be comfortably classified 
under the Varna divisions, Tamil society is characterised primarily 
by the three-fold division of Brahmins, Non-Brahmins and Adi- 
Dravidas or ‘Untouchables’. While the Brahmins are considered the 
highest in the caste hierarchy, the non-Brahmin castes are considered 
the middle-level castes. The more visible middle-level castes in Tamil 
Nadu in general and in Ramanathapuram district in particular 

50 A. Ramaiah 

include the land-owning castes such as Vellalar, Ahamudayar (Servai), 
Maravar (Thevar), Kallar, Konar (Yadavar) and the Telugu-speaking 
Naidus; trading castes such as Chettiyar, artisan castes like Kusavar 
or Kuyavan (potter), Kotthan (mason), Thachan (carpenter), Kollan 
(blacksmith), Thattans or Nahai Aasari (goldsmith); and the servicing 
castes such as Ambattan (barbers) and Vannan (washermen). The 
more visible castes among the Scheduled Castes in Ramanathapuram 
district are the Pallars, Parayars and Chakkiliyars. 

Social Relations between Dalits and Others 

The relationship between the higher castes and lower ones has 
always been very hostile in Tamil Nadu, particularly between the 
middle-level castes and the Dalits. And in such a relationship, the 
losers are often those at the bottom of the caste ladder and the 
gainers are those above due to unjust and unequal distribution of 
power and status. A few middle-level castes such as Ahamudayar, 
Maravar and Kallar, together known as Mukkulathor (three castes), 
are relatively more visible, particularly in Ramanathapuram district 
as they are not only owners of cultivable land, large in number and 
more assertive, but also known for committing atrocities on the 
Scheduled Castes. Any attempt on the part of the lower castes to 
alter the given power positions is met with dire consequences. These 
include the murder of the Scheduled Caste leader Thiyahi Imanual 
at Paramakkudi town and forty-two Scheduled Caste persons at 
Mudukulathur in 1957, of forty-four belonging to the Scheduled 
Castes at Keelavenmani in Tanjore district in 1968, five at Unjanai 
in Pasumpon Muthuramalingam district in 1979, and sixteen at 
Vilupuram in Chengalpat district in 1983. Besides, there were a 
number of murders of Scheduled Caste people at Cuddalore and 
Vilupuram in Chengalpat district in 1987 and at Podi in Madurai 
district in 1988. In 1992, two persons were killed at Paramakkudi in 
Ramanathapuram district (Ramaiah 1998: 92). 

The three-fold division of castes stated earlier is visible in the 
settlement pattern as well. While the Brahmins live usually in brick 
and tile houses on a separate street known as agraharam, the Adi- 
Dravidas live in thatched huts located slightly away from the main 
village, commonly known as cheri. And the non-Brahmins live 
in a mixed type of houses located often between the two types of 
settlements. 

’ 
Poe i en Ca ’ 
Ve on 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu | 51 

Caste Relations in Villages among 
Pallars and those above 

In Ramanathapuram district of Tamil Nadu, the Pallar caste. is 
considered to be the highest among the lower or the Scheduled Castes, 
and lower caste among the caste Hindus. ‘They are considered to be 
great cultivators, especially of wet land of the Tamil country. The 
Pallars of today were known as Mallar belonging to the Dravidian 
race about 2,300 years back, and were the rulers of the Tamil country 
during the fourteenth and fifteenth centuries. It is also asserted that 
they are the descendants of Pallavas who ruled the Andhra and Tamil 
countries once. They are known by other nomenclatures too such 
as Velalar, Kudumban and Devendra Kula Velalar. There are over 
eighty-four branches among the Pallars. The Mallar were called 
Pallar only after the fifteenth century by more powerful tribes from 
other parts of south India with a view to degrading their social status 
(Guruswamy 1998: 70—73). 

The Pallars constitute the largest population among the seventy- 
six Scheduled Castes of Tamil Nadu. According to the 1981 census, 
out of the total Schedule Caste population excluding the Adi 
Dravida—a category consisting of a number of Schedule Castes— 
the Pallars constituted the maximum with 27.60 per cent followed 
by the Parayar with 22.96 per cent, the Chakkiliyar with 14.29 per 
cent and the Arunthathiyar with 11.81 per cent. A majority of 
Pallars (33.4 per cent) reside in Tanjore district, followed by Madurai 
(21.2 per cent) and Ramanathapuram (about 3 per cent) districts. 
Puthira Vannan caste is considered to be the most ‘polluting’ caste 
among the Scheduled Castes as for generations they have been 
washing clothes of other Scheduled Castes. 

Though the Brahmins and a few upper-level middle castes such 
as the Vellalars and Chettiyars treat the Pallars as untouchables, the 
latter do not consider them as their opponents or direct enemies. 
For them the real opponents are a few middle-level dominant castes 
such as the Ahamudayar, Maravar and Kallar who indulge in open 
violence against them. This is evident from the fact that throughout 
Tamil Nadu most of the incidents of violence against Pallars have 
been perpetrated by those castes. Perhaps due to their glorious past 
and their origin as rulers, the Pallars have been militant in opposing 

discrimination of every kind. ~ LIBRARY 
NARAYAN FAQ MELGIRI 

tational Law Scneo! 

52 A. Ramaiah 

Talking to a Pallar man of Nedumbuli village near Paramakkudi 
town, it was found that the caste Hindus like the Maravar did not 
allow the Scheduled Caste women, including Pallar women, to 
wear blouses. By this covert means the caste Hindus compelled the 
Scheduled Caste women to expose their femininity. As this practice 
was in use for a long time, the Scheduled Caste women did not 
develop the habit of wearing blouses. This continued till the early 
1950s, particularly in villages like Nilayambudi near Paramakudi. 
Even at the time of this study we found many elderly women not 
wearing blouses and covering themselves only with the sari. 

Untouchability in Villages 

We shall now focus on the magnitude of caste discrimination and 
untouchability as experienced by the Scheduled Castes in general 
and the Pallar caste people in particular residing in the two villages: 
Akramesi and Keelaparthibanur. In Akramesi, the domination 
of middle-level castes (caste Hindus) over the Scheduled Castes 
in general and Pallar in particular was very much prevalent even 
during this study (November 1989 to April 1998). Out of the 
696 households in this village, the Scheduled Castes consisting of 
the Pallars, Parayars and Chakkiliyars accounted for only twenty- 
five households and the rest belonged to the caste Hindus, of whom 
the Maravar caste alone accounted for as many as 500 households. 
There is not a single village in a fifteen km-radius around Akramesi 
with a high concentration of Pallars or other Scheduled Castes. 

It is surprising to note that many of the observations made as 
early as 1952 regarding the nature and magnitude of untouchability 
practised in villages (Gough 1969: 90) were found to be true even 
at the time of the present study. Both economic and political powers 
were in the hands of Maravars and Ahamudayars—the two middle- 
level dominant castes. None of the Scheduled Castes including 
Pallars had land of their own, and they depended on the former 
both for their livelihood and physical security. Education for the 
Scheduled Caste children was generally discouraged. At the time of 
this study, only one Pallar had studied up to standard XI. Despite 
having a driving licence, he had to remain jobless. Whenever 
he applied for a job or for a loan from the government, the caste 
Hindus with their easy access to all the officials right from the village 
panchayat to panchayat union, tahsildar’s office and post office, did 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu 53 

every thing possible to disqualify him for the job and retained him 
in the village itself. None of the Scheduled Castes were allowed even 
to walk through the residential area or through the village's main 
street running through the residential areas of the dominant castes. 
They had to walk a long way along the periphery of the village to 
reach their huts. They were not allowed to enter any of the village 
temples visited by the caste Hindus and had no right to perform any 
rituals even outside the premises of such temples. The Pallars had 
a separate temple (but open to all castes) called Maravar Mahan, 
meaning son of Maravar (the dominant caste). The name of this 
temple itself indicates that the caste Hindus were equal to a god 
and the Scheduled Castes had to respect and worship them. The 
Pallars had to address the caste Hindus only as sami (God), whereas 
even a caste Hindu child addressed a Pallar man by his name or 
derogatorily by his caste. This is also true in the case of using the 
community well, meant for all castes. The Pallars were prohibited 
from fetching water from this well on the pretext that their vessels 
and buckets would pollute the water. The pond used by the caste 
Hindus for bathing was not even to be approached by the Scheduled 
Castes. Each Scheduled Caste had its own burial ground located far 
away from that of the caste Hindus and they were not allowed to 
take funeral processions through the main street of the village. At tea 
stalls owned by the caste Hindus, the Pallars were provided with tea 
or water in separate glasses locally known as vattai. Any Scheduled 
Caste person intending to have tea at such stalls was expected to 
pick up the vattai kept separately for them at one corner of the stall 
and show it to the person preparing tea, who would then pour it 
into the vattai from a distance. They were also expected to wash the 
vattai and leave it where it was picked up from. While the Pallars 
were to sit on the ground—many a time outside the stall—the caste 
Hindus were served tea on benches inside the tea stall. The Pallars 
were also prohibited from riding bicycles. They were expected to 
place their towel in their armpit while addressing the caste Hindus, 
not on their shoulder as the usual practice. The dhoti they wore was 
supposed to cover their legs only up to the knee and not their legs 
as it would be an insult to the high-caste Hindus. These restrictions 
were applicable not only to the Pallars and other Scheduled Castes 
of this village but to all the Scheduled Castes visiting this village. 
Besides, the Pallars were expected to do all the manual work outside 

54 A. Ramaiah 

the premises of the caste Hindus’ houses during both auspicious and 
inauspicious occasions. In return, they used to get either a meagre 
wage or a meal. They were generally expected to carry the food to 
their home or eat it in the backyard when the entire function was over. 
Sometimes they were given nothing for their services. Those trying 
to question the caste Hindus or disobey their demands met with dire 
consequences. The common punishment for such disobedience was 
nothing less than tying the person to a lamp post or a tree situated 
within the village and beating him in public till he collapsed. One 
of the respondents (a 14-year-old) said that a few years ago his elder 
sister was raped in broad daylight in front of many fellow Scheduled 
Castes for informing the Collector of Ramanathapuram district 
about the practice of caste discrimination in the village. This youth 
was an active communist party member and had sought the help of 
a local communist party leader, but he was refused since the leader 
was a caste Hindu and his loyalty was more towards his fellow caste 
men than towards the proletariat, contrary to what is emphasised 
in the party ideology. The police station situated in a small town 
about five km away from this village was of no use for the Scheduled 
Castes as none in the police station paid any heed to the heinous 
crimes committed against them. The Pallars from nearby villages, 
being numerically in the minority and living in a similar situation, 
never dared to come to their rescue. 

When the researcher interviewed Akramesi’s Kanakku Pillai 
(administrative officer or the person in charge of maintaining village 
records) and enquired about the practice of untouchability in the 
village, he said that the Scheduled Castes had no such problem, 
and there had been a very cordial relationship between them and 
the caste Hindus. He was also told by the Village Administrative 
Officer not to go to the village personally, as he could provide every 
bit of information about the village and the condition of Scheduled 
Castes. When the researcher insisted that there were problems 
between the Scheduled Castes and caste Hindus and hence he would 
like to visit the village personally to take stock of the situation, the 
Village Administrative Officer sarcastically said, “You go there, you 
will get “everything” from the caste Hindus.’ What he meant was 
that the researcher would be beaten up by the caste Hindus if he 
insisted on knowing the practice of untouchability. On the whole, 
the Scheduled Castes, including the Pallars in this village, had to lead 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu | 55 

an inhuman life. There seemed no commitment or genuine effort 
on the part of government ofhcials to ensure the physical security 
and enhance the economic status of the Scheduled Castes therein. 
The condition of the Parayars and Chakkiliyars was still worse. The 
Pallars treated the Parayars as untouchables and the Parayars regarded 
the Chakkiliyars so. Social interaction among these castes was very 
limited. Inter-dining and inter-caste marriages between them were 
also prohibited. 

What is important at this juncture is to find out why the magni- 
tude of caste discrimination faced by the Pallars has been very high in 
this village. Based on certain observations and information collected 
from the respondents and village leaders, the major reasons for the 
same are: (a) not only within Akramesi village are the non-Brahmin 
dominant middle castes numerically dominant but the village is also 
surrounded by these caste people; the Scheduled Castes are very 
few in number; (b) the Pallars of Akramesi village are economically 
dependent on the dominant castes in and around the village; (c) the 
police station located about two km away from this village is of no 
use to the Pallars and other Scheduled Castes as it is dominated by the 
non-Scheduled Castes who often work against their interest, and they 
get no support from their fellow caste people from nearby villages; 
and (d) none has completed even school education in this village and 
most of them have remained ignorant of their rights and privileges. 

In contrast, in Keelaparthibanur village, the Scheduled Caste 
population, particularly of Pallar caste, is large and they stand ahead 
in every respect. The caste Hindus here could not discriminate against 
the Pallars in any form. Unlike Akramesi village, Keelaparthibanur 
is divided into two hamlets. The Pallars reside on the southern side 
in a hamlet locally known as Keelavadakur and the caste Hindus live 
in Melavadakur located a furlong away on the northern side. At the 
time of this study, the Pallars were more in number with 130 houses 
against ninety-two houses of the caste Hindus. Most of the Pallars 
were landowners and some worked as sharecroppers on the land of 
Vellalars in the same village and also in Parthibanur town located 
two km away. Some of them worked merely as agricultural labourers. 
Though there were no Brahmins in this village, the Pallars often went 
near the Brahmins when they visited other villages and the nearby 
town. It was found that prior to Independence the Pallars were never 
allowed to enter the residential areas of the caste Hindus, particularly 

56 A. Ramaiah 

of the Brahmins. Whenever a Brahmin came out of his house, no 
Scheduled Caste person was expected to come in his vicinity as it 
would pollute the former's sanctity and, if it happened by mistake, 
he would go back home cursing the latter; he would come out once 
again only after a bath and making sure that the incident would not 
be repeated. However, as a mark of protest, a few Pallars of this village 
deliberately appeared before the Brahmins again and again. From 
1960 onwards, most of the Brahmins left the village selling their land 
and other properties and settling in nearby towns. They did so not 
only because their services in the villages were no more considered 
essential but also because of the opportunities that stemmed from 
their education and employment prospects in towns. 

Though the Pallars interacted with and had access to the residential 
areas of the Vellalars, who are next to Brahmins in the caste hierarchy, 
they were denied entry into the houses of the Vellalars; they had 
to wait at the thinnai (corridor). The Vellalars did not accept even 
water from the Pallars. But the Pallars did not protest much against 
these discriminatory practices as the Vellalars extended financial help 
to them whenever needed. However, the younger generation did 
protest against the Vellalars by requesting their parents not to have 
any relations with them and not to cultivate their lands any longer. 
Though there was no Chettiyar caste (goldsmith) in this village, even 
back in the 1950s the Pallars interacted closely with those living in 
the nearby towns like Parthibanur, Paramakkudi and Manamadurai 
by way of buying and mortgaging gold ornaments. The Chettiyars 
did not overtly prevent the Pallars from entering their shops as they 
were looking for customers to enrich their business and, hence, 
entertained both the caste Hindus and the Scheduled Castes. Since 
their shops were located only in the towns, there was no opportunity 
and also no need for the Pallars to visit their residential areas. 
The Thachchans (carpenters) from other villages had no problem 
extending their services to the Pallars of this village to earn their 
livelihood. But they would not accept water from their houses. They 
preferred water fetched directly from the well in a vaali (a metal 
bucket). Often they brought their food with them but sometimes 
they cooked food at their workplace, accepting uncooked rice and 
fresh vegetables from the Pallars. 

About twenty-five years ago, the Pallars had faced yet another 
problem. Till the late 1960s, the services of the Ambattan (barber) 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu G7 

and Vannan (washermen)—considered to be above the Pallars in the 
caste hierarchy—were not available to them both within the village 
and in the nearby towns; the two castes extended their services only 
to the caste Hindus. Pallars had to depend on the Chakkiliyars for 
hair-cutting and the Puthiravannans or Puthiravannars for washing 
their clothes. The Ambattans and Vannans refused to entertain the 
Pallars and other Scheduled Castes in their work places (service 
centres/shops) owing to the fear of losing customers from the caste 
Hindus. However, the situation started changing in the early 1960s 
when the Pallars started asserting their rights and protesting against 
such practices. The Ambattans and Vannans could not resist the 
pressure trom the Pallar youth who had been to colleges and worked 
as government officials, a few of them even as engineers and doctors. 
The caste Hindus also could not do much in this regard. These 
services were then extended to all castes. At present, the Pallars do 
not consider the Ambattans and Vannans as their superior, and for 
all practical purposes they do not interact much with one another 
except in the hair-cutting and laundry shops. 

The Pallars of Keelaparthibanur village consider the Maravars 
and Ahamudayars their real opponents though they do not 
regard themselves as inferior. The Pallars in this village are more 
advanced in terms of educational and economic status. They also 
do not depend on other castes for their livelihood. They resist and 
retaliate whenever the caste Hindus demonstrate their superiority 
in any manner. About fifteen years ago, the Maravars discriminated 
against the Pallars in every possible way and the latter had to accept 
all unjust practices. However, over a period of time they began to 
protest. Most such incidents culminated in major violence between 
the two castes although none could claim total victory over the other. 
Further, prior to 1975 the Pallars did not have access to the common 
bathing pond located at Melavadakur. But they fought against the 
caste Hindus and took up the matter with the police. Despite stiff 
protest by the caste Hindus, the Pallars succeeded in getting access 
to the pond. Moreover, in the late 1970s, K. Ukkirapandyan—one 
of the Pallars from this village—got elected to the state legislature 
from the Paramakkudi reserved constituency. In the late 1980s, 
the president for the Keelaparthibanur panchayat union was 
S. Malaichamy, a Pallar from this village. Besides, there was also 
A. K. Karupaiah, to whom most of the Pallars of this and nearby 

58 A. Ramaiah 

villages turned for help whenever there were caste conflicts or threats 
from the caste Hindus. Karupaiah could take up caste issues boldly 
since he enjoyed the support of leaders of the Congress (I) Party. The 
caste Hindus then did not dare to discriminate against them overtly. 
Whenever they attempted to do so, they were not spared. However, 
the caste groups soon came to a reconciliation and began to interact 
cordially. Although within the village the Pallars supported various 
political parties, they got united whenever the status and power of 
their caste was threatened by the caste Hindus. A few caste Hindu 
leaders did eat with the Pallars during the weddings of the latter to 
demonstrate their ‘unbiased’ attitude towards them and to ensure 
their votes but, in general, inter-dining and inter-caste marriages 
between them are not tolerated. 

It may be noted that though the Pallars were primarily engaged 
in agriculture, in a few villages like Kalaiyur, located on the southern 
side of Paramakudi town at a distance of 9.4 km, one or two old 
and poor Pallars had been doing, for decades, the job of digging 
the burial ground. When asked for the reason, they said, ‘We do 
this work for the caste Hindus not out of fear or caste inferiority 
but as we do not want to displease them. After all, they have helped 
us financially at times of crisis’. Moreover, doing such a job ensures 
them a handsome amount of Rs 20 to Rs 35. Elsewhere, the Pallars 
do not consider such work demeaning and perform it without any 
inhibition (Deliege 1997: 77). 

The above narration of the nature and magnitude of caste 
discrimination experienced by Pallars in both villages has brought 
to light a few important points. Though in most of the villages the 
Scheduled Castes are spread in small numbers, there are villages 
exclusively made up of Pallars. Most of the Pallars own land but their 
socio-economic status is not the same in all villages. While there are 
villages like Akramesi where most of the Pallars were illiterates, do 
not own even half an acre of land and depend totally on the caste 
Hindus for everything, there are villages like Keelaparthibanur where 
most of the Pallars own land (a few of them own more than ten acres 
of wet land), comparatively more of them are literate and have also 
achieved political power; caste Hindus can never look down upon 
them. It may, therefore, be hypothesised that in villages where Pallars 
are in the majority, most of them are also educationally advanced, 
economically independent, politically conscious, well mobilised and 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu 59 

powerful enough to fight against caste Hindus perpetrating atrocities 
on them. The situation is just the opposite in villages where they are 
in the minority. 

Untouchability among Scheduled Castes 

Another aspect of this paper is to bring to light whether or not there 
is hierarchy among the Scheduled Castes and if so the nature of caste 
discrimination and untouchability suttered by those at the lower 
rungs of the order. As stated earlier, in Ramanathapuram district, 
the more visible among the Scheduled Castes are Pallars, Parayars 
and Chakkiliyars. Evidence discerned from thirteenth-century Tamil 
inscriptions indicates that the Parayas or Parayars were close to the 
bottom in the caste hierarchy and were engaged in diverse fields of 
activities. The term parayan is derived from the Tamil word parai 
(drum), as certain Parayars act as drummers at funerals and village 
festivals (Government of Tamil Nadu 1972: 154). They are also 
engaged in cultivation, grass cutting and weaving. Fifteenth-century 
literature shows that the Parayars were also engaged in tanning and 
skinning, which according to classical or Brahmanical Hinduism is 
defiling and polluting (Karashima 1997: 23-4). During the eighteenth 
century, the Parayars also worked as tank-diggers, construction 
workers, servants, transport workers and a few as soldiers in the army. 
This period has been described as the ‘golden age for Paraiahs’ (see 
Washbrook 1993: 78). The Parayars worship the common Grama 
Devta (village deity) such as Ellamma, Mungilamma, Padaiyattal or 
Pidariyamma (Government of Tamil Nadu, ibid.) 

Although in the southern part of Tamil Nadu the Parayars are 
considered below the Pallars in the caste hierarchy, in the northern 
part particularly in Tanjore district, they do not acknowledge the 
latter's supremacy. Whatever be the history of the Parayars, in 
Ramanathapuram district most of them are, at present, landless 
labourers. Compared to the Pallars, they are fewer in number in most 
villages of this district. For earning their livelihood, they continue to 
play drums on auspicious occasions and others for caste Hindus as 
well as Pallars. They also make and sell household items made of palm 
leaf. Though in the past they seemed to have engaged in diverse fields 
of activities which had no social stigma, their association with leather 
works and drum beating and their habit of eating beef seem to have 
had an impact in pushing them down the caste ladder. The Pallars 

60 A. Ramaiah 

in most villages of this district consider themselves to be superior to 
the Parayars in social status and treat them as untouchables. They 
allege that the Parayars side with the caste Hindus during clashes 
and, hence, even label them as traitors. They use the Parayars as a 
symbol of abuse, although the only major difference berween the two 
is that the former refrain from eating beef while the latter relish it. 

Next to the Parayars in the caste hierarchy are the Chakkiliyars. 
They form an appreciable number in the district. Though no literature 
seems to be available to indicate their historical origin, they might 
be immigrants from the Telugu or Kanarese districts. Their gods 
include Madurai Veeran, Mariamma, Muneeswara, Draupathi and 
Gangamma. Taking into account the caste status ascribed to them 
and the lifestyle they have been leading for the last several decades, 
the Chakkiliyars are below the Parayars in the caste hierarchy. It 
is believed that they originally held a high position but were later 
degraded (ibid., 155). Their traditional occupations are sweeping, 
scavenging and removing dead animals, tanning and making 
footwear. They also play their drums and a wind instrument similar 
to the shehnai. In some villages, the Pallars prefer the Chakkiliyars 
to Parayars to play drums on the occasion of their Kula Deivam (clan 
God) festival although they do not allow the latter inside their temple. 
However, the Parayars’ interaction with the Pallars is very limited. 
The Chakkiliyars sweep the streets of the Pallars and, in turn, get 
food from them. They take away the dead cattle, remove their skin 
for making drums and consume their meat. All these activities are 
considered by the upper castes and the Pallars to be dirty, defiling 
and polluting. Hence, the Chakkiliyars are looked down upon. 
They have so far not challenged the caste supremacy of the Pallars. 
In general, inter-dining and inter-caste marriages between them and 
the Pallars are prohibited although the intensity with which these 
restrictions are observed varies from place to place. 

The Kuravan or Kuravar caste is found to be on par with or 
slightly above the Chakkiliyars in the hierarchy. They form the 
sixth largest Scheduled Caste population both in Tamil Nadu and 
in Ramanathapuram district. They earn their living by making 
household items out of bamboo and palm leaf. They hunt birds and 
rear country pigs (black pigs) both for their own consumption and 
for sale. They also hunt cats from the villages for consumption. Some 
of them work as fortune-tellers. Since they use trained kili (parrot) 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu 61 

to predict the fortunes of people, they are also known as kilt jostyars. 
However, they do not go to any other caste asking tor food. ‘They are 
also not required to perform any inauspicious tasks and rituals for 
other Scheduled Castes. The Pallars consider them as a lower caste 
although they have no means to demonstrate their own supremacy. 

The people of Puthiravannan caste are traditionally the washermen 
of the Pallars. For this service, they are entitled to collect food from 
the Pallar families. At the end of the year they are also given four to 
six marakkaal (a cylinder-shaped metal container used for measuring 
food grains) of paddy (each marakkaal would contain about four 
and a half kilograms of paddy). Besides, whenever a Pallar girl attains 
puberty, the women folk of the Puchiravannan caste take her used 
clothes. Similarly, the man who pertormis rituals on the occasion of 
death of a Pallar is entitled to some money (usually ranging from 
Rs 2 to Rs 15) and a new dhoti tied soon after bathing the body. If 
the deceased person happens to be a woman, bathing and changing 
of the dress is done by the Puthiravannan women. However, the 
rituals are usually performed by their men. 

How Pallars Remain Superior 

to Other Scheduled Castes 

Though like the Parayars, Chakkiliyars and other Scheduled Castes, the 
Pallars have also been segregated from the main village habitats, they 
enjoy a superior status among the Scheduled Castes. It is, therefore, 
important to understand the socio-cultural and economic reasons 
which keep them superior among the Scheduled Castes, particularly 
in Ramanathapuram district. Studies (Gough 1969) have indicated 
the prevalence of caste discrimination among the Scheduled Castes 
themselves. However, these studies have not looked in detail at the 
underlying reasons. 

The Pallars have a few traditions which make them different from 
and also superior to other Scheduled Castes. In Ramanathapuram 
district, the Pallars are more aggressive and militant compared to 
Parayars, are socially and politically conscious, and better organised. 
This difference is also mythologically expressed since the Parayars 
claim a Brahmin ancestry, while the Pallars are closer to fierce and 
warlike middle-level castes like the Kallar and associate themselves 
with a more martial tradition (Deliege 1997: 77; Moses 1985: 356). 
In recent years, the militant Pallars have claimed their descent from 

62 A. Ramaiah 

the god Indra. Secondly, while the Parayars prefer to call themselves 
Harijans, the Pallars tend to refer to themselves by their caste name 
so that they are not amalgamated with other lower castes. Besides, 
the very name ‘Pallar’ is not as shameful as other untouchable 
caste names and it is not associated with any defiling occupations. 
Though the Parayars are involved in diverse fields of activities, they 
continue to be associated with occupations like drum beating. 
Similarly, the traditional occupations of the Kuravars are considered 
to be degrading, defiling, and polluting, except agriculture and 
allied activities, in which primarily the Pallars are engaged (Deliege 
ibid.). In fact, the Pallars have been defined as a class of agricultural 
labourers (Thurston 1909: 472). Accordingly, they are held high in 
the Scheduled Caste hierarchy but all other Scheduled Castes are 
looked down upon by the Pallars. That they were once the rulers of 
the Tamil country has already been alluded to. 

In villages, attitudes towards meat eating determine one’s social 
status. Vegetarian Brahmins look down upon the non-vegetarian 
non-Brahmins. Among the latter, the beef eaters are considered to 
be inferior to mutton eaters and pork eaters. This in a way indicates 
that there is caste hierarchy even among the animals whose meat 
the villagers relish. Adhering to vegetarianism is one of the ways by 
which one asserts one’s superior position in the caste order. Even 
today these middle-level castes maintain, though not fully in urban 
areas, a distance from the Scheduled Castes. 

The Pallars maintain their caste superiority also by not eating beef 
which the other Scheduled Castes do. They refrain for two reasons: 
first, like the caste Hindus they consider beef eating as taboo and 
sin, and feel that eating beef is below their social status; secondly, 
since the main occupation of most Pallars is agriculture and allied 
activities, they depend on the cows and bullocks for ploughing their 
lands and for transporting their goods. These animals are indeed part 
and parcel of their social and economic life and, therefore, they have 
a sentimental attachment and sympathetic attitude towards them and 
thus do not kill them for their meat. However, some of the educated 
Pallars who are used to a metropolitan lifestyle, have adopted the 
habit of beef eating and have also developed better relations with 
other Scheduled Castes but they are discouraged from eating beef 
when they get back to their village. In general, the Pallars, particularly 
in Paramakkudi taluk, are not fond of eating pork which the Parayars 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu 63 

and other Scheduled Castes relish. A few elderly members who eat 
pork are ridiculed in public by the Pallars themselves, calling them 
Kattak kaalu, meaning short leg or pig. A few Pallars who reared pigs 
were indeed laughed at by others and as a result they had to give up 
pig rearing. 

The Pallars are considered to be superior to the Chakkiliyars and 
Puthiravannars also because of certain give-and-take relationships 
prevailing among them. For instance, the Chakkiliyars and 
Puthiravannars staying in the village collect food trom the Pallars 
on almost all days they work. On ceremonial occasions, they come 
with bigger containers to collect the leftover food. Moreover, the 
Puthiravannans are entitled to collect about six marakkal of paddy 
every year trom every Pallar family. They also serve as messengers for 
the Pallars, particularly for passing on to other villages inauspicious 
information such as death. On such visits, they are fed by the Pallars 
receiving the message and in some cases they also get a small quantity 
ot food grains like paddy and raggi. The Pallars give them water and 
food in padi (a small-size marakkal) and that too in the thinnai. They 
in principle discourage the Parayars, Chakkiliyars and Puthiravannars 
from wearing sandals within their villages. Unlike other Scheduled 
Castes, the Pallars depend neither on the caste Hindus nor on other 
Scheduled Castes for their living. After all, ritual status alone cannot 
keep a particular caste in a particular position in the caste hierarchy; 
a strong economic base is equally important (Beteille 1969: 6). 
Such discriminatory practices among the Schedules Castes are not 
common in all the villages. They are more prevalent in villages like 
Akramesi and less in villages like Keelaparthibannur. 

The Changing Scenario 

Though the above-mentioned observations are applicable to the 
Pallars in general and the villages under study in particular, there 
has been considerable decline in the last two decades in the rigidity 
with which these restrictions are observed, particularly in villages 
with high concentration of their population. For instance, in 
Keelaparthibanur village, the Pallars have become relatively liberal 
in their interaction and social relations with the other Scheduled 
Castes. Now, the Parayars and Chakkiliyars—considered to be lower 
than the Pallar caste—wear sandals while they are in the residential 

64 A. Ramaiah 

areas of the Pallars. With the initiatives of some of the liberal-minded 
educated Pallar youth, the other Scheduled Castes even participate 
in auspicious occasions of the Pallars such as weddings. Here, it is 
important to note that in Parthibanur town, no caste Hindu visited 
the saloon run by a Chakkiliyar as it was opened for the Pallars who 
had no access to saloons meant only for the caste Hindus. With their 
newly acquired education and government employment, most of the 
Pallars preferred entry to the other saloons causing closure of the 
Chakkiliyar’s saloon. Usually, the Puthiravannars and Chakkiliyars 
are not allowed to eat along with the Pallars. They eat only when all 
the guests have had their meals. This has been relaxed in recent years, 
especially in villages like Keelaparthibanur. With the initiative of 
young educated Pallars, the other Scheduled Castes are now allowed 
to dine with the Pallars. In a few Pallar houses they are also served 
food in the plates used by the members of the family. Moreover, 
the young educated Pallars from villages with high concentration 
of their caste invite their upper-caste friends home on important 
occasions and generally cunceal the caste background particularly 
of their lower-caste friends. They do so fearing that their parents 
would insult the latter in some way. Young educated Pallars from 
such villages do not expect the other Scheduled Castes to adhere 
to the old customs and practices or remain subservient to them for 
ever. This however is still rare and very often the elderly ones in the 
family express their unhappiness over it. But such interaction can 
never happen in villages with less concentration of Pallars. In fact, 
in such villages when the Pallars’ expected demands are not met and 
social restrictions are violated by the other Scheduled Castes, the 
former punish them with the support of the caste Hindus. 

Separate Villages as an Alternative 

In the name of village unity and village interest, every effort of Dalits 
to escape from the clutches of the caste Hindus has met with dire 
consequences. The experiences of Pallar caste people in the two 
villages—one having high and another having a lower concentration 
of Scheduled Caste population—clearly endorse Ambedkar’s demand 
for separate settlements for Dalits (1979: 426). This important 
suggestion still remains unattended. A recent study which focused 
on the efhcacy of the implementation of the Scheduled Castes and 

Dalits in Southern Tamil Nadu 65 

Scheduled Tribes (Prevention of Atrocities) Act, 1989 made a similar 
suggestion, which is quoted below. 

If there are separate settlements for Dalits far away from the upper and 
dominant castes, with their own means of livelihood particularly land, 
there will be no upper caste to discriminate and humiliate them, ‘There 
will be no upper caste person to forcefully occupy their lands and other 
means of livelihood and make them bonded labourers for ever; there 
will [be] no caste Hindus to rape their women and parade them naked. 
In such settlements, there will be no one to stop their children going co 
school. There will be none to tell their children that they are untouchables 
and un-seeable idiots. There will be no one to intuse a sense of inferiority 
complex in the Dalit children’s mind and kill their creativicy and the very 
personality in their early childhood itself (Ramaiah 2002). 

Conclusion 

Though India is legally bound to secure to all its citizens, including 
Dalits, justice, liberty, equality and fraternity, the foregoing 
discussion clearly brings to light that this it is not possible in the 
present pattern of rural settlement. It is so viewed in the light of 
the continuing practice of untouchability and increasing incidents of 
inhuman crimes against Dalits in villages and the fact that they still 
remain a grossly exploited section of Indian society despite a number 
of protective and development measures to safeguard their interests. 
Therefore, there is urgent need for reconstructing Indian villages. 
Such reconstructed villages will be ideal places where no member 
of any caste is forced to take up any occupation, or where every 
member of every caste will have the scope to take up the occupation 
of their choice, and where no child will be labelled as ‘untouchable’ 
at birth. It is only in such a village that fraternity in the true sense 
can become a reality. Therefore, Ambedkar’s demand that a socially 
distinct community should be allowed to settle in separate villages is 
worth reconsidering.
