The Category ‘Rural’ Revisited: 
A Dalit Perspective from a 
Village in Maharashtra’ 

RITAMBHARA HEBBAR 

a) aoe . » a | 
Caste is like the air; you cant see it. 

Introduction 

A question that has always intrigued me is how people, who have 
rebelled against a system that defined their very existence, seek 
an alternative sense of self. The rebellion has entailed renouncing 
their work and way of life, which till very recently circumscribed 
their world. How do they reconcile themselves to the changes and 
conflicts thereof which affect ‘others’ as much as they affect them? 
If the setting is rural, it shakes up not only the very foundation of 
village life but also the organisation of social life across villages, as 
no one can now take each other for granted. These questions are not 
restricted to one village or a community; they are for every village 
and caste to address in the face of change. Thus, the question ‘who is 
a Dalit’ is a complex one; it is general to the movement as much as it 
is specific to village life. It is collective in as much as it is individual. 
It concerns not just the Dalits, who in any case form a fluid category, 
but the non-dalits as well.” Given this scenario, what are the political, 
cultural and social resources available to Dalits in resistance and in 
recovering a sense of self? 

The referent of Dalit struggles is conceptual and multilayered. 
It cuts across geographical boundaries. It is contained within the 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 69 

discourse on equality and social justice, and untolds alongside the 
larger processes of rural reform, development and change. Despite 
the aversion of many sociologists towards the establishment of rural 
sociology as a sub-discipline, the category ‘rural’ became popular as a 
shorthand for village India and as an object of reform (Pocock 1960). 
Deified in the retorm agenda of the government and movements, 
both agrarian and Dalit, the category rural has provided visions tor 
change, that is, how ‘rural people should be’ and what ‘rural society 
should look like’.” The addressees of these visions, unfortunately, 
have been looked upon as mere passive recipients, rather than as 
sentient ones. They have, in due course, not only accepted these 
visions in part, but have also questioned them, adapted them to local 
conditions and thereby reformulated them. 

As a commentary on lite in the villages, the category ‘rural’ has 
not been able to incorporate or record what the villages offer in terms 
of an alternative understanding to rural reform, development and 
change. The specificities of a village as a locale (place) cannot be seen 
separately from the larger ideational space of egalitarian discourses 
that inform social relations and actors within a village. I seek to 
address this dynamics in the context of a village in Maharashtra and 
through the life story of a Dalit who struggled to put an end to the 
traditional practice of gaonki (the work of village servants) among the 
Mahars. His life story is interestingly not just about being a Dalit; 
it is, as he says, the struggle for what is right. It was his personal 
struggle for a life of dignity that became a struggle of the Mahars 
in the village. His philosophy appealed to many across castes in the 
village, though after a long solitary struggle. He gained support even 
from those who apparently had more to lose than gain by his stance. 
Herein, we find gaps in a typical analysis of Dalit struggles. These 
are not just about caste; they are about a way to be. The end of caste 
discrimination was just the first step; the ultimate aim was to give 
form to a vision on how to live a life of dignity—and so the revisit to 
the category, ‘rural’. 

The Village: Past and Present 

The village in question, Pandhari, is situated in the Osmanabad 
district of Maharashtra. With an area of 1,208 hectares, it has a 
population of 1,182. It falls within Tuljapur taluka. Tuljapur town 

70 Ritambhara Hebbar 

is famous for the Tulja Bhawani temple, which attracts pilgrims not 
only from within Maharashtra, but also from the neighbouring state 
of Karnataka. 

New to the village and to Maharashtra, I was intrigued by 
Pandhari’s caste configuration. Though it is a multi-caste village, as 
most other villages in India, its political history is specific to the area. 
The village was under the Nizam of Hyderabad, with the patil, or 
the headman, as its representative, and it was given as a jagir (estate) 
to the Nilgaon jagirdar. The present patil family in the village traces 
its lineage to him. In most other villages in Maharashtra, the patil is 
usually a Maratha but in Pandhari, a Brahmin family held the post. 
The patil owned over 200 acres of land, of which the family has been 
able to hold on to about eighty acres. Their residence, an old fort-like 
structure, despite its rundown appearance, does not fail to conjure up 
visions of its former grandeur. It literally looks down on the village 
from a height. The other two castes that are numerically dominant 
and almost equal in number in the village are the Marathas and the 
Mahars (who now prefer to be identified as Dalits). The Marathas 
lived within the village, while the Mahars lived at the fringes; their 
hamlet was known as the Maharwada. The Mahars have land (two to 
four acres on an average), unlike many of their counterparts in other 
parts of the country, and it was traditionally known as hadola. The 
term derives its name from the purpose the land was used for, which 
is to throw dead animals. The land was watani, that is, belonging to 
the watan or the political order. The other castes include the Sutar, 
Mang, Lohar, Teli, Tamboli (Muslims), Mulani (Muslims), Gosavi, 
Koli and the Nhavi. 

Pandhari was characterised by the balutedari system. It was a 
hierarchy of castes in the service of the watan, or the village, as well as 
a system that defined the rights of each caste in the village according to 
its economic and social place in the system. At the apex was the patil, 
responsible for collecting revenue and maintaining law and order 
in the village. The Marathas were traditionally agriculturists. Then 
there were the service castes such as the Sutar (who made wooden 
agricultural equipment), Teli (oil-presser), Lohar (ironsmith), Sonar 
(goldsmith), Kumbhar (potter), Chambhar (leather worker), Mang 
(who made brooms, ropes of various kinds for the village; also 
removed skin of dead cattle and bartered or sold it to the Dhor, the 
tanners) and Pareet (washerman or Dhobi). The other castes included 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 71 

the Gurou (priest) and Gram Joshi (astrologer). These priests were in 
addition to the ones who worked exclusively for the patil. A Kulkarni 
Brahmin was the patwari, or the village accountant. The Mahars 
tormed a large category of workers known as the ramoshi. ‘They were 
expected to perform any and all tasks for the villagers; they were the 
hereditary village servants. They guarded the village, kept track of 
newcomers and escorted government treasure and officials; they were 
expected to cut firewood, carry letters, keep the village clean, which 
included disposing of dead animals, and were in charge of cremation 
grounds. Their work was known as gaonki, work of the village. 

Village lite centred round this traditional order. The Mangs, 
Chambhars and Mahars were the ‘polluting’ castes, engaged in 
‘polluting’ tasks. In fact, the Mahars were expected to tie a broom 
across their waist so as to sweep their footsteps as they walked through 
the street. They were not allowed into temples nor could they take 
water trom the village well, and strict rules of segregation were 
followed. The Mahars, unlike the Mangs and the Chambhars, did 
not have specific skills. They did not work for selected families, but 
catered to the needs of the entire village. Boundary disputes, disputes 
within the village and even outside, and records of deaths and births 
were the domain of the Mahar. Their views on matters of dispute 
carried weight. They were also the most susceptible to exploitation. 
Unlike the Mangs and the Chambhars, their concentration was not 
on a work skill but on people. They worked for the village directly. 
Their labour was not combined with a craft, but was based on sheer 
physical toil. Their exploitation was direct and that much more 
obvious. The Mahars were then literally and figuratively on the 
margins of the village, village life and the Hindu social order. And yet 
they were the important link of wider social, political and economic 
networks. Given their nature of work, they were the informed and the 
informers of the goings-on beyond the confines of the village. This 
possibly explains the broad reach and appeal of the Dalit movement 
among them. 

Pandhari has undergone changes over the years. Many of the 
villagers have moved out for work, while many have left the village 
with their families to settle in towns and cities. Brahmins mostly left 
the village right after Independence. They moved to Osmanabad and 
Tuljapur. The only Brahmin family that has stayed back (besides that 
of the Patil’s) is the Kulkarni. The Sonar left the village before the 

Va Ritambhara Hebbar 

1970s, while the Koli moved into the village around the same time. 
Initially, he used to deliver water to individual houses in the village. 
Later, he bought land in the village. He now has a teashop just at the 
border of the village, near the bus stand. The year 1972 is etched in 
the memory of the villagers; it was the year of the drought and it led 
to significant shifts in the demographic profile of the village. The Teli 
migrated to Tuljapur. They sold off their houses and have opened shop 
in Tuljapur. The Lohars are in Sholapur, while the Sonars have settled 
in Pandharpur. With the introduction of manufactured goods in the 
villages and with the improvement in transport and communication, 
they have lost their traditional clientele, both within the village and 
from the neighbouring villages. 

But the most significant change is that the Mahars no longer 
perform the gaonki. They gave up their traditional occupation in 
the late 1960s. Many Mahar families migrated out of the village, 
especially after 1972, moving to Mumbai, Pune, Bhiwandi, and 
Kolhapur. They took up jobs in factories, government offices, the 
armed services, and so on. The Matang family now performs the 
gaonki, much to the disappointment of the Mahars. In the late 1960s, 
the Matang took over the task of the Mahar. But in 1985, another 
Matang from Bhiwandi came and settled in Pandhari. Soon enough, 
he took over the task of gaonki. He replaced the earlier ramoshi’s son, 
who did not find favour with the villagers. 

The post of ramoshi is no longer what it used to be. After the 
Mahars gave up gaonki, there was only one ramoshi who got 
remuneration from the whole village. The new ramoshi made enough 
money to give loans to the villagers. But he did not manage to stay 
in the village for long; he was suspected of having illicit relationships 
with Maratha women. The villagers forced him to leave. Now 
the position has reverted to the son of the earlier incumbent. He 
and his family earn well from the job. The younger brother of the 
ramoshi still goes round the village every evening asking for alms. 
Recently, they had invited a priest from Tuljapur to perform a ritual, 
for which the priest got a sum of Rs 150 along with conveyance 
charges, a generous sum paid by a villager for a regular ritual. The 
Mangs refer to the Mahars as Dalit, while they continue to address 
themselves by their traditional caste name. There was no official or 
ceremonial conversion of Mahars to Buddhism though they call 
themselves Buddhists and each Mahar house has a photograph of the 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 73 

Buddha and of Ambedkar displayed on the wall. ‘Their allegiance to 
Buddhism stems from their identification with the Dalit cause and 
not so much from their attraction to the religion per se. As of now, 
the Mahars, who have chosen to stay back in the village, and many 
of the Marathas, cultivate their land as well as work as wage labourers 
in various government projects and, of late, in the Employment 
Generation Scheme (EGS). There has been a marked change in the 
educational status of the Mahars. Almost all of them above the age of 
fitty are illiterate but there has been a steady increase in the number 
of literates among them. At present, all Mahar children go to school. 
There is a primary school in the village, attended by students from all 
castes. There is also a higher secondary school nearby. The Marathas 
are relatively better off than the Mahars. They have by and large 
stayed within the village and their population has grown over the 
years (they also have a greater genealogical depth than the rest of the 
castes in the village), leading to fragmentation of land. From among 
the persons who migrated, many have returned or are in constant 
touch with the village. A number of them maintain two families, one 
in the village and one in their place of work. 

Various processes triggered off the changes in the village. With 
the establishment of the modern Indian state and the collapse of the 
old princely order, the balutedari system was weakened substantially. 
Although the ideas of equality, freedom and social justice were 
less pronounced in the princely states in comparison with British 
India, Ambedkar had taken on an iconic status among the Mahars 
in the village as ‘their highly educated, English-speaking boy who 
was fighting their cause’. The breakdown of the traditional village 
economy was hastened by the irrelevance of traditional skills 
within the modern economy. It was not just the Mahars who were 
disgruntled; the service castes too were left wondering about their 
future in an economy that had no place for their specialised skills. 
The migration of service castes and the Mahars from the village was in 
search of alternatives, both economic and social. Among the Mahars, 
it was also political. They could not see the old order and the village 
accommodating them as equals, while urban areas not only offered 
them relative anonymity but also greater economic opportunities. 

Village life and the power equation within the village have 
changed, though caste is still the fundamental point of altercation 
and negotiation. The subservient castes are relatively more assertive, 

74 Ritambhara Hebbar 

while the dominant castes have toned down their demeanour. Caste 
differences are played out differently and caste assertions continue 
to mark the landscape of the village. Even though the Mahars and 
the Marathas work together on construction sites or in government 
programmes, they eat separately. A Maratha might eat meat cooked 
by a Mahar, bur will not take food at the Ambedkar Jayanti. That is 
a Mahar event, while Shivaji Mela is a Maratha festival. Similarly, 
Ganesh Festival is a Maratha event and has become an occasion for 
extravagant spending. There are two youth clubs in the village, one 
among the Mahars and the other among the Marathas. The members 
of the youth clubs come together only to organise their respective 
festivals. The self-help groups too are formed along caste lines. 

It is in the context of the present situation of the village that 
Pattuguruji’s life story seems more than a narrative of a Dalit. It 
presents an important rendering of village history and life. But most 
importantly, it addresses the query I began with, concerning the 
political, cultural and social resources available to Dalits in resistance 
and in recovering a sense of self, which I argue also critiques the 
existing framework of rural reform and development agenda. 

Profile of a Dalit, in His Own Words 

I present excerpts of Pattuguruji’s life story, not with the intention 
of eulogising his accomplishments, which in themselves are no mean 
achievements, but to bring out the dynamics of caste, kinship, village 
life and the rendition of the issue of social justice. The life history of 
Pattuguruji demonstrates, first of all, his struggle within the village 
and against the balutedari system and, secondly, the influence of and 
his engagement with persons and the ideas of social justice beyond 
the village. This dual nature of his struggle presents a critique of 
traditional practices based on caste, as it questions the sensibilities 
of the younger generation within the village on work and physical 
labour. The narrative, therefore, provides a distinctive understanding 
of self and society, tradition and modernity; in short, it unravels a 
way to be. Given below is the text of Pattuguruji’s narrative: 

We didn't have a primary school here. There was an informal, local 
school. I was twelve or thirteen years old at that time. I used to wander, 
grazing animals. We don't have a school in our village. Marathas study 
in the local school. There is a Brahmin master. Who is going to take you 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 

into the school? So what my mother did was to appoint a master for 
Rs 300 per year. That is, 30 students paid Rs 10 per year. My mother put 
me in this school. In 1942, | went to school: | was fourteen-ffteen years 
old at that time. I had finished class 4 when the school was closed down. 
The master went away; he had completed three years and was getting 
Rs 900 for his three years’ service in the school. After he left. | was put 
into a school in Thirth, Thirthbudruk. A primary school was there, but 
only up to the fourth standard. | had to study there for two years because 
I studied in a ‘gowti’ [rustic] school. They did not recognise my earlier 
school because it was a private school with no government recognition; 
teaching was informal. So they put me into the third standard saying, 
‘Your earlier education has no value here’. After my fourth standard, | 
gave my board exam in Tuljapur and passed. Then | got admission in the 
Zilla Parishad high school in Tuljapur. After | was given admission, my 
position became difhcult. My mother died that same year—1947. That 
year I got married and my wife was very young. There was no students’ 
canteen or boarding facilities at that time. We were not permitted into 
hotels. In school also, we faced problems. Gopalrao was the master in 
Thirth; he was a Brahmin. He would keep the Brahmin children in one 
group (on one side) and all the backward-class children in another group. 
While he was teaching, he would just ask, ‘Hey, did you understand?’ 
He would not come near us to hit or for any other reason. We never 
used to get water there. There was no drinking water facility at that time. 
After passing, I went to the higher secondary school at Tuljapur; Dinkar 
Kadam of our community had a vacant house there. | lived there and 
made my own food. People may have told the others that there was no 
point in studying or what use is it to your community since you have to 
do the gaonki... but who would dare to ask me? From the beginning, I 
was very clear and I would never let anyone talk to me like that. They 
might have told people from other communities who may have told my 
father and mother. Well, I did not have my mother, but my father was 
there. I completed my schooling up to the seventh there. When I finished 
school, a man named Naazar came to the village. He asked if there were 
any backward-caste youth in the village. He was told, “There is one of 
Banda Maske’s sons who we think has completed seventh standard, but 
we are not sure’. So he came looking for me. At that time, Ambedkar's 
spirit was getting into my body. Naazar asked me, ‘How much have you 
studied?’ I told him that I have studied till the seventh standard and | 
am yet to sit for the exam. He asked me, ‘Will you do a job (naukri)!’ 
I asked, ‘What kind of job?’ He said, ‘School master’ and I said ‘I'll do 
it’. He straightaway appointed me as a teacher in Kalra, beyond Kelari. | 
hadn't even sat for my board exam. I opened the primary school in that 
village, in the Saukarwada (house). I found that unlike in my village. 
there was no gaonki here. Two months into my job, my old man (father) 

75 

76 

Ritambhara Hebbar 

walked all the way to visit me, and pulled me out of the school. He said, 
‘All this naukri-pikri is beyond me. | cannot deal with all this’. At that 
time (1952), my total pay was Rs 65. I came back to the village. ‘There 
was nothing to eat in the house. My father used to cultivate land but my 
wife's father sold the bullocks and all the farming equipment. He turned 
us into beggars. 

In our house nobody ever did the gaonki. My father had always been 
a farmer. I did not take up farming, as there was nothing to farm with. 
I started going to the field for weeding for four annas. My wife couldn't 
do anything; she was too young, just five or six years old. ] survived by 
going with the women for weeding and being cursed for drawing water 
from the well. I had to tolerate that. There were days when | had to 
stay without water the whole day. I was not allowed to take water from 
anyone's well. 

I went from village to village buying eggs, chickens and leather, to 
carry them in a basket to Sholapur to sell them there. And with the five- 
ten rupees that I got from there, J used to manage for a week. So then 
I left the weeding and earth work. I had worked on the nadla bunding 
project, as a gadi [common labourer] to collect the earth. After it started, 
the villagers were after my life—with that politicking, that one giving this 
woman there, this happening to that Mahar woman, someone beating up 
the youth. So I started an agitation. I faced opposition when I took up 
issues. They came at me with sticks and knife. At this time, the gaonki 
was still being practised. There was no way of putting an end to this 
practice. 

What happened was that two of our Scheduled Caste boys drank 
water from a well. A complaint was lodged in the village. I said, ‘If they 
have drunk water from this well, you don’t go and drink water from 
there’. I started giving examples like, ‘They drank water, but don’t you 
have other wells? Should you hang them or what? What harm did it do 
to you?’ When I said this, the police, the patil and other village officials 
(Jeevanrao’s father Apparao was there at that time; Jeevanrao became the 
patil later) said, “This Banda’s [person's] son is really spoilt (maazlela)’. 
But I never took anything lying down. Never, ever. 

Once a Maratha youth entered a backward caste’s house. I used my 
torch to see who had come. He asked, ‘Who is that? Who switched the 
torch on?’ ‘I am Pattu’. (My name is Masa. My mother used to call me 
Chandrakanth, but my sister called me Pattu, because I was outspoken.) 
He asked, “What right do you have to shine the torch on me?’ | replied, 
‘What right do you have to come here?’ A fistfight started and several 
people came and caught hold of him. Then he fell at our feet and said, ‘] 
won't come here again, baba’. 

He came for a woman, a Dalit woman. Soon the whole village got to 
know. Everybody looked at me disapprovingly. ‘He's a rowdy. Look at 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 

the way he behaved when the water incident happened. He challenged 
us to file a case against the boys, if a case could be made out of it legally. 
He told us, “Don't you dare touch the boys”. Look at the arrogant way in 
which he speaks’. The whole village was against me trom that ime. My 
community was equally bitter about me and was willing to continue with 
the gaonki. They were upset with me. They were under the Marathas’ 
thumb and were dependent on them for toed and work. ‘This happened 
around 1952-53. 

Though my livelihood was at stake, | continued my agitation. Around 
that time, my brother-in-law, who was in the timber business, educated 
me about it and | cook ic up. My father was sitting idle and again the 
question of who's going to cook came up. My wife was too young. | got 
my father married again. 

A second time, the ‘spirit’ got into me. | gave up all my work and 
became a full-time activist. | wrote poems and did plays. I trained the 
youth for plays... Jat Bodh Natak (My Way). The first play the boys 
did was an epic play called Raja cha Raja. We had no one to play the 
harmonium, no percussionists, no support of any kind from anyone. 
Nobody to train us also. | prepared all these myself and put up a play 
against the village. A gowti (a Mahar) trom Devsingha said he would 
give loudspeakers. There was only one in Tuljapur belonging to Gautham 
Kasara. It worked on electricity. That speaker was brought and the play 
was a success. The play only had male actors; there were no women actors 
at that time. Phulchand and Ruidas Sonwane did women’s roles. 

When | was doing this play, people from the village tried to obstruct 
it. Sarpanch Balbir Lokhande’s brother sat on the dais and said that he 
would not allow it to be staged. Earlier, the person playing the main role 
of the king dropped out. But that role had to be played. Who was to do it 
and who was to train the person for the part? Who was going to play the 
harmonium? Who would play the tabla? All the responsibility was on me. 
I got a Sonar boy to play the harmonium. I played the tabla. I was also the 
make-up man. Training and teaching the boys was also my responsibility. 
Most of them were illiterate. So, I had to learn the entire script by heart 
to be able to teach them. Training them, putting the music together... | 
taught Phulchand’s brother Kashinath to play the harmonium. 

After the play, Balbir, whose brother had obstructed the play, was on 
my side. He said, ‘Pattu Master, all this fght is completely undignified. 
You manage your play, I'll handle my brother’. The play was a big success. 
We even got about Rs 300 as baksheesh. People said that the boys did a 
good job, without support of any kind. They had no resources. The gowti 
in the village brought a teacher from Barul. The teacher was unsure as to 
how he would teach these uneducated people. I took up the task myself. 
And then I did /ai Bodh Natak. By then, we did not need a harmonium 

player from outside; we had our own. The cast consisted of only Dalits;

78 

Ritambhara Hebbar 

none of the Marathas joined us. They were not even ready to sit or come 
near us. In fact, they went against us. What they used to do was stage 
their play the morning after ours was staged. Even then I continued to 
stage plays. And I undertook two or three programmes like this. I took 
one such programme to Andur. | would not have told you this but you 
had asked me for details from way back in my past. So we took the 
programme to Andur and did it the same way. The Mangrul people 
called us at the time of Jayanti. They said, ‘Pattu master has prepared 
plays; there are three plays in one programme. They are really good 
(mast) and we must have them staged in our village’. We did the plays 
there. Everybody liked it but our village was against us. ‘He's doing plays 
against us, also making up his own poems. The style is also Ambedkar's, 
shouting in a loud voice, with forty-fifty people around him doing the 
same thing. This needs to be stopped’. In order to break it up, they threw 
stones and other things like mud and soil at us and ran off. But | used to 
get encouragement from outside. 

Even after this, the gaonki continued. It wouldn't end till everybody 
stopped doing it. Limbaji and Madhav said, “The two of us will do it, 
malik, even if he makes people abandon the gaonki. However smart he 
might act, it is a question of our survival, we will do it’. They would beg 
for bhakri everyday, clean the village, and fill tobacco in the pipe. All this 
was around the year 1960. 

At that time, I bought a vehicle to cast the timber. I had reached 
Ingle’s land when the axle broke. It was not available anywhere near; 
you had to go all the way to Sholapur to get it as there was no garage 
in Tuljapur. Kinnar went there and I cut a chicken for the driver. Ingle 
procured daru (alcohol), our local brew (shindt), from Deosingha. And 
he got Phulchand and Ruidas, two young men, to drink the shindi. 
Phulchand and Ruidas got high. What were they to know? They were just 
young boys. They were out of control and falling all over the place. Nagu 
Lokhande joined in on his way back from work. Dhige Ingle wanted to 
instigate a fight. He told Phulchand to beat up Lokhande as he used foul 
words against his father. Phulchand was an idiot; he picked Lokhande 
up and threw him in the bushes. Lokhande was covered with thorns. | 
pulled him out of the bushes and told him, ‘Go home, baba. These boys 
are drunk, don't take them seriously’. By then, Kinnar came back with 
the axle. We started fixing it when I heard that everyone in the village 
had got together and gone to the Maharwadi and were beating them 
up. I rushed to the village. There was a crowd outside the boys’ house, 
calling them names and wreaking havoc. The two boys did not come 
out of the house. I told them to come out, and said, ‘Let’s see which of 
you has the guts to touch them’. This dispersed the crowd. The sarpanch 
Abba Nannavre took up the issue and registered a case against the boys 
in Tuljapur. In the meantime, I loaded my vehicle and sold the truckload 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 

in Sholapur. By the time I came back, the boys had been taken on foot to 
Tuljapur. | dropped my bag and lett for Tuljapur. | thought | would tell 
Pandurang Shinde (Nandu Shinde’s father) and Shahji Kadam trom our 
community, who was a leader at that time, and get the boys out. 

But what do you think happened? Devappa Nannavre got a jamaadar 
(head constable) drunk and let him loose on me. The two boys names were 
set aside and when | was at Pawar’s hotel drinking tea, | was summoned 
to the police station. | reached the police station and found Devappa 
Nannavre drunk. The constable, without any preamble, ordered me to 
go inside the room saying, “Chal Saale Tya Kholeet. He told me to take 
off my clothes. | obeved, but added, ‘Remember this, | have committed 
no crime. You may think you are a policeman and | am a nobody. But 
I have legal proof. You might be thinking that if | beat him up, nobody 
can do anything to me. | won't let even pwo minutes go by, you better 
watch it.’ 

Having come out, | saw the Fauzdar, Kochre, or was it Patil, and 
Pandurang and Shinde and Shahji sitting there. Pandurang Shinde 
questioned me. | said, ‘I'll tell you what happened. Its very difficult to 
speak in front of the Fauzdar’. | looked at the Fauzdar and said, “This 
Nannavre who has brought you all the way here, our village Sarpanch, is 
the father-in-law of Dhige Ingle. This Dhige Ingle was the one who got 
the boys drunk. This poor man Nagu was just sitting there. It was in my 
presence that Dhige Ingle told the boys to throw him in the bushes. This 
man’s son-in-law harasses his own relative and I am brought to the police 
station to be beaten up? Is there any sense in this? Am I to blame? Nagu, 
tell them, am I at fault?’ 

The Fauzdar said, ‘Sarpanch, what is this I hear? What is the master 
saying?’ 

‘It’s the truth’. 

‘If it’s true, why have you got this case? Take your people right now 
and get out. And dare you pick up any respectable man from the road to 
soil my reputation’. 

Then the Fauzdar beat up the jamaadar saying, “Who got you drunk? 
What is your position in this police station and you are drinking? You are 
a respectable man and you tried to beat him up. You useless character’. He 
told me, ‘Take your people and go.’ He drove out the Patil and sarpanch 
and asked Thatya ‘Why have you come here?’ 

‘Nothing, it was master’s work only. He called me here... telling me all 
this had happened.’ 

The Fauzdar cursed and said, ‘Who has appointed this foolish man as 
the Sarpanch?’ 

Pandurang Shinde was Devappa Nannavre's close friend. Now Pattu 
has insulted Nannavre and a man from Nannavre's side will not take it 
well. Thatya went away quietly. | brought the boys’ home. 

79 

80 

Ritambhara Hebbar 

Then things started berween him and me. He started ploughing the 
land and I tried to stop it. It’s like this... I had a paternal cousin who 
had only one daughter, Naazan. His eldest brother Babu had one son, 
Prahlad. Since Babu’s younger brother Dagdu had only one daughter, 
the land was joint. It was about fourteen acres. Nannavre sweet-talked 
Naazan. He told her that he would provide her with food, clothes, 
agricultural equipment, whatever she needed. He told her not to listen to 
anybody else but him. This story dates back to 1960-65. 

During that period, she changed completely. She started fighting 
with everybody, particularly with her cousin, Prahlad. She wouldnt 
even talk to me. I pleaded with her and even went to the extent of 
touching her feet. Finally, | managed to marry her off in Kumbhari. 
She had four sons and four daughters through this marriage. Prahlad 
had only one daughter. | told Naazan, ‘You marry your son off to 
Prahlad’s daughter and transfer all the fourteen acres to your son's 
name. But don’t throw the entire thing away into somebody else’s lap. 
I am begging you, Naazan. I’m just a fellow human being. Who the 
land goes to makes no difference to me. I have nothing to gain or lose. 
Don’t sell the land to anyone. You get your son married to her and take 
her portion as well.’ 

She said, ‘I have nothing to say to you, please go back’. 

The reason why Nannavre managed to sweet-talk her was because 
her mother was an old woman. Prahlad would trouble her and wouldn't 
take care of her. This old woman would complain to Naazan saying, ‘He 
doesn't look after me, doesn't give me anything. Make some arrangement 
for me.’ Then she asked Nannavre to provide her with whatever she 
required. Till then there was no such thing. Then what do you think 
Nannavre did? He started ploughing her side of the land in an attempt 
to claim it. On paper, the entire land was still in Babu’s name, though 
in practice it had already been divided. We became alert. About rwenry 
people, including Nannavre’s men, came with their implements. When I 
saw that they had come prepared, | faced them and said, ‘First, you bring 
the plough outside the boundary of the land and then make whatever 
noise you want to.’ 

Who was prepared to listen to this? Only our Bhagwan, the toothless 
one, was on my side. I had no other support whatsoever. In fact people 
questioned Bhagwan. ‘Why is the man dying there for?’ There was 
absolutely no support from my community. What I then did was make 
a cut on Prahlad’s mother’s hand so that we could show injuries. Her 
forehead was made to look bloodied. Then we filed a police complaint 
against them. All of them were put in jail. This was in 1967-68. Pradip 
Kulkarni’s father-in-law turned Babu’s land to Subabai’s name (Naazan’s 
mother) and submitted the document at the police station. They used 
that as justification for ploughing the land. 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 

The men were released. Appa Nannavre had given that man money to 
prepare the copy. Now the case was on us. Naazan bai had also signed on 
it. When the men got released, my spirits fell. What could be done now? 
I was insulted. Our own woman did this co us. What had | planned and 
what did this woman do? She betrayed us. 

However, Dishambar Choudhary was one of the few Maratha people 
who were with me. Prahlad Lokhande, Kisa Lokhande, Dishamber 
Chaudhary and the old sarpanch Balbir were the only ones with me. 
Their support was not because of any faction among the Marathas bur 
because the truth was on my side. Even if | hadn't told them, they would 
say that this boy is in the right. He hasn't done anything wrong. 

My spirits were low and so | went to have tea at Pushpa Vihar hotel, 
the only hotel at that time. About 200 people had gathered there. When | 
approached them, Appa Nannavre and Pandurang Shinde started cursing 
me. I abused them in return. Dishamber Chowdhary was holding back 
Pandurang Shinde who wanted to hit me. “This Pattu master, I want to 
get hold of him and beat him up, Shinde said. Dishamber stopped him, 
but Shinde tugged on. ‘No let me go. Pactu’s calking off the top of his his 
head and abusing me. | cant stand listening to him.’ 

Dishamber then started telling the public what had happened. ‘See, 
this is a Mahar man and this is our Sarpanch. What do you think he 
did?’ 

I interrupted, “This lout, under the influence of prostitutes and 
cheats...’ By then, my cousin Babu came and said, ‘Come, you fool. Why 
are you inviting all this on to yourself? Why are you shooting off your 
mouth? Come on now.’ He took me back to the village. At that time, we 
had to get down at Barulphata to go to the village. There was no state bus 
or any other kind of transport. We went walking from Barulphata. I did 
not sleep that night. My father started abusing me, ‘I told you not to get 
involved in other people's conspiracies. But you obstinately do all these 
things...’ That year my father died. 

After this, all the dirty work would come to me. The land was 
regularised. You know that Kole’s land? Prahlad Lokhande was all set to 
take it. But those people decided that they would not allow Lokhande to 
take it. Shamrao Kole was quite a rich man; he had his own business of 
marketing goats. He is from Barul. He used to deliver water to people's 
houses. For Rs 3,000, he was given three acres of land. It was Prahlad’s 
father’s land and he did not transfer it in his name. He was later told that 
his purchase was null and void when that woman took over the land. So 
he had to buy it from the woman again. He had Rs 1,000 for it. Like this, 
the woman sold all the land she had inherited. Now only Prahlad’s land 
was left. This went on till 1972-73. 

At this time, Jeevanrao Patil died and Padu Patil, his son, had almost 
come of age. Jeevanrao lorded over the village. He was dreaded so much 

81 

82 

Ritambhara Hebbar 

that not only our villagers but even people of the neighbouring villages 
were scared of him. He was a badshah. After he died, Balasaheb Patil, 
Jeevanrao’s brother, took up the Patil’s job. Balasaheb was poor. He was 
like a lamb. He used to ask people like me what should be done about 
this and that. He did the Patil’s job for four to five years after which he 
was asked by Padu Patil to hand over the post to him... Since it was their 
family’s traditional occupation, nobody in the village reacted. The post 
was taken away from Balasaheb. | got angry. This poor man was earning 
his livelihood. Was there anything that said that the post of Patil was his 
jagir? Even if it used to be so, is the jagir system in existence anymore? 

Why didn't he let Balasaheb do the job? I got some fifteen people 
together and four or five applications filed for the post—Tamboli, 
Popat Lokhande, Khambule and I. From the backward castes, I was the 
only one. From OBC, Dattu Khabule (Teli) and Tamboli. I sent all the 
applications to the collector's office. We initiated the struggle. When 
we did this, Padu Patil retaliated by giving money to Vinayakrao, the 
Talathi, on the sly. One control was in Vinayakrao’s hand. He was soon 
to become the Nayab Tehsildar. Vinayakrao quietly took Padu Patil’s 
interview without us getting to know about it. Padu Patil was only fifteen 
or sixteen years old at that time. We followed up on it. We had a spy in 
the collector's office, who kept us informed. We all went to S. K. Kadam 
[a sympathiser of Pattuguruji, influential in the higher ranks of the 
local administration] and told him what had happened. We had quietly 
inquired from the clerk in the collector’s office as to when the Patil’s 
interview was to be held. He said ‘It’s today. Have all your men come?’ 
We had not even been informed. So many of us had applied... 

That's when we spoke to Kadam. 

He was a Dalit. When we met him, he said, ‘I will come with you.’ 

There were ten-twelve people with us, including Prahlad Aba. I got 
a support group ready for this. | wanted to break this hereditary system 
of filling the post. I took this up because I had read the rules and I know 
that this Police Patil’s post was not a hereditary one, that it is changed 
after every three years. The collector had taken a bribe of Rs 5,000 from 
Vinayakrao, Padu Patil’s brother's father-in-law. The collector got furious 
with the clerk. Then S. K. Kadam got up and asked ‘When is the interview 
for Police Patil’s post’? So many people have turned up for it.’ He rang the 
bell and went into the collector's office and asked about the interview. He 
told the collector to interview these candidates immediately and let them 
go. 

He told me, ‘Maske, you sit outside and when the interview starts 
you let me know. I will come.’ We were there till midnight right in front 
of the collector's office. The collector was waiting for Padu Patil who did 
not know what was happening. Vinayakrao said, ‘That fool, I had told 
him to come, why hasn't he turned up?’ The collector was also there. He 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 

wouldn't move. The interview took place at midnight. Popat Lokhande 
was interviewed first, then Tamboli, then Khabule and lastly 1. As soon 
as my interview was over, they asked me “How educated are you’? | said, 
‘7th standard’. They asked, ‘Pass or fail?” | kept quiet. | wanted to ensure 
that Dattu Khabule got the post; | did not want to become the Patil. 
They asked “Why are you keeping quiet? You have been educated up 
to the 7th, why don't you respond? We have seen your backward class 
certificate and whatever you have given us.’ 

S. K. Kadam, intervened and said, ‘Don't ask him anything else; you 
can do what hits into your rules. Don’t ask him beyond your briet. Just see 
whether he qualifies or not.’ 

Vinayakrao came out quietly and the selection was finalised. Dattu 
Khabule was selected as the Patil. They asked me whether | would like to 
be the Pail. I said, ‘My companion is an OBC; you make him the Patil. 
I am in-agreement.’ But we made sure we got the Police Patil’s order 
immediately. 

When we got back to village, abusing, fighting and stone throwing 
erupted. I was on a cycle when something whizzed past. | stopped the 
cycle and looked back. A stone had fallen centimetres trom me. | turned 
the cycle around and started cursing, ‘You cowards, if you have the guts 
come and catch hold of me.’ Then | asked, ‘Why are you throwing stones 
from the top like women? Come in front of me’! 

All the Patil’s people came when I started talking like this. Padu’s 
mother, Radha Bai, came out and said, ‘I threw a stone at you, do what 
you want. 

We had prepared our women. Dattu Khabule’s wife, Sakubai, Ratna 
bai and others. Ratna Bai said to Radha Bai, ‘Come down. We are ready. 
What it is to be done to you is in our hands. Come down’. We pushed 
these two women in front. Radha Bai retreated. And all our people came 
back. 

On the way I told them that when Radha Bai comes out the following 
day, you should catch hold of her. I will take the responsibility. 

As soon as she came in front of Dattu’s (the new patil) house, we 
caught hold of her. The women hammered her. A proper fight started 
after that between them and us. We had some Marathas on our side, but 
they were quiet. They would do their work quietly saying, ‘Let’s see, what 
they'll do. We are there with you.’ 

You remember that incident when the boys were locked up in the 
prison? On returning to the village with the boys, I warned my people. 
‘If I see anybody doing the gaonki from tomorrow, | will nor tolerate it.’ 
After that nobody went for the gaonki, in fact nobody went to the village 
after that. 

I had no idea in my head of giving up the Hindu religion and taking 
up Buddhism. What is important for me is that whatever vision I had, 

83 

84 Ritambhara Hebbar 

I actualised. | have never believed that religion would give me knowledge 
or intelligence. My philosophy is that | am my own support. | won't 
listen to anyone, however scholarly. | am stubborn, absolutely stubborn. 
I did these things not just here in the village, but also in other places. 
Even in Sholapur. | beat up a policeman, a Talathi [a local official to 
maintain land and revenue-related records]... When I asked my people to 
leave gaonki, it was not because they had land to fall back on. The point 
was that I didn’t want them to be spongers. | was deeply convinced of this. 
I wanted them to develop ‘toiling’ bodies. Somewhere they had to learn 
how to earn for themselves. In this earning, birth is justified. 

I used to be involved in the village, doing plays, etc. Village people 
of my age were scared of me. Everybody was scared of me. Once the 
leadership of the present kind emerged, | stopped terrifying these young 
boys or explaining things to them. Even now they are scared of me, but 
how scared are they? They are just scared that I'll say something. But I 
don't. They wouldn't dare to smoke in front of me. Now I am ashamed 
of my own boys. They haven't turned out right. If 1 walk around calling 
my people, they will just follow me. I just have to call them. Not a single 
person—man, woman, child, small, big, nobody—has any kind of 
antagonism against me. The old enmities are forgotten. Now the only 
thing that is spoiling things in the village is my eldest son’s behaviour. | 
have shut my mouth; I dont say anything. | only speak up when some 
grievous wrong has been committed. 

Conclusion 

The narrative reflects the personality of Pattuguruji. He is extremely 
articulate and direct in his speech. He has a powerful voice. The joke 
in the village is that once, when Pattuguruji was speaking with a 
policeman in his typical style, the policeman asked him to not to lose 
his temper. The villagers had to step in and clarify to the policeman 
that Pattuguruji was not angry and that he normally spoke in that 
tone. Bold and dramatic as his speech and character, Pattuguruji’s 
narrative makes for interesting reading. There are two parallel themes 
that run through his narrative: the crisis in his family and work, and 
his social engagement with issues of equality and justice. This is not 
to say that the crisis in his work and family do not concern the larger 
issues of equality or justice. As is evident in the repeated reference to 
not being allowed to drink water from the well through his school 
days to the time when he worked in the fields and the construction 
sites, his struggle for justice was first and foremost personal. His 
decision to take up any and every work as well as his willingness 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 85 

to take up contract-based work almost seems like a realisation that 
financial instabiliry is a part and parcel of his commitment to his 
family and larger goals. And also that one cannot be at the cost of 
the other. Work of any kind, so long as it involves labour and hard 
work, was acceptable to him. There is a sense of pride in doing hard 
work, irrespective of whether it was conventionally a woman or a 
man’s job. 

He reaches a point in his lite when he gives up his work to get 
into social activism on a full-time basis. The shift is dramatic, as 
he actually takes to the stage. He took recourse to a conventional 
medium, though not a forum available to untouchables, to put 
across radical ideas. He mentions ‘Ambedkar's spirit getting into 
his body’ at two points in the narrative. Once, when he begins to 
get aware of the exploitative character of the caste system. Second, 
during the time when he staged the plays in the village. Despite his 
fearless character, these were larger than lite feats for him; something 
that he could not have achieved all by himself. It is almost as if he 
did not do it all by himself. It was Ambedkar’s spirit which made 
him do it. It was the spirit of Ambedkar’s convictions and the faith 
that he had in them, which were also his own, that propelled him 
to take up the challenge. This is the point where the movement gets 
a voice through a socially and historically situated individual and 
in the process loses its sole authorship on what is communicated. 
So was the case here. Pattuguruji was the ideologue for his people; 
Ambedkar found another voice through him. 

All through the narrative, the reference to the village is not 
uniform. Under the balutedari system, it is a place Mahars had limited 
access to and whose borders they guarded. There is a reference in his 
narrative of the times when the Mahars gave up gaonki to the effect 
that they gave up going to the village after that. Here the village 
is not a geographical location, but a configuration of a traditional 
system, which they abandoned. ‘Village’ was also a contested space, 
especially in the context of the new political and social order. There 
is a sense in which there was a struggle not only for equal access 
but also for all the markers that affirmed a sense of belonging to 
the village. The village, therefore, is also a generic term, especially in 
the context of his understanding of rural development and change. 
This observation is further strengthened by Pattugurujis views on 
rural development and change expressed in the presence and, most 

86 Ritambhara Hebbar 

probably, for the benefit of the youth in the village. It is this gradual 
transition from the specific dynamics of caste and village to the 
generic character of the category of caste, village, development and 
change that emerges strongly in his narrative. 

The narrative is not formless, even though eclectic. It gives a peek 
into village life, inter-caste relations, kinship ties, and the law. The 
attention given to details of persons and interpersonal relationships 
within and outside the village demonstrates the complex nature of 
village life. It inadvertently questions the stereotypes about rural life 
as something simple, apolitical and uncomplicated. It demonstrates 
the difficult relationship between reform movements and the 
organisation of everyday life. Although the interpersonal relationships 
and tensions within the village provide the context for social reforms, 
they also slacken the pace of reforms. Interpersonal rivalries are not 
necessarily based along the lines of caste or gender, thus making it 
difficult to draw clear ideological divides across social categories. 
Pattuguruji as a result attempts to communicate the larger message 
of his narration through a detailed account of his struggle—how to 
live a life of dignity. He, in the process, de-centres the characters, 
incidents and categories of caste and village to unfold the story of 
his struggles and his achievements. Therefore, at the end, he makes a 
statement to the effect that no one has any antagonism against him; 
all is forgotten. Whether it is true or not, I do not know. But this 
certainly seemed like a statement of a man who is making peace with 
himself, with his calling. Though he is disillusioned with the present 
leadership in the village as well as the youth across castes, he prefers 
to keep a distance from the goings-on in the village. 

Rural reform and the accompanying processes of socialisation till 
the establishment of a modern, rational political order run through 
his narrative. There is a nominal but constant reference to the 
support he derives from outside the village. The new administrative 
set-up, his network beyond the village and the value of equality 
espoused by the new order, even if only on paper, serve as facilitators 
in his struggle. Pattuguruji comes across as a person not only with 
a good grasp of law, but also as someone with the knowledge of 
how to move the law. He even made false charges against Nannavre 
to restore the land to its rightful owner. Whether it was the Dalit 
leader at the Zila Parishad, or the clerk who gave him information 
regarding the interview of the Patil’s post, he had his networks in 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 87 

place and he used them appropriately. Clearly, his struggle in the 
village was not just a struggle against caste discrimination, but also 
to change the principles along which life was organised. By refusing 
to accept the Patil’s post, he draws attention to the political objective 
of resisting the reasons for Padu's selection as the Patil. ‘The main 
objective was to democratise the post of Patil. His grievance with 
the present efforts at rural development, whether in the creation of 
self-help groups or youth clubs, is that he does not understand the 
larger purpose of such initiatives. The prime concern of selt-help 
groups or savings groups seems to be that of saving money. But he 
cannot comprehend the purpose behind it. According to him, it is 
also important to understand how the savings are utilised —whether 
they are used for personal benefit or for the larger good. Likewise, 
youth clubs have been formed in the village along caste and party 
lines, but there is hardly any justification for the existence of these 
groups. For him, these are sangathanas (organisations) without 
sangharsh (struggle), primarily serving their own ends. Without 
sangharsh, sangathana cannot bring about development; institution 
building without addressing the cause for its creation only encourages 
divisions. Development initiatives promote the value of money and 
create differences within the village. They have no larger purpose of 
improving life in the village. 

Tied to the idea of sangharsh is the issue of development and the 
idea of sponging. A sponger is a sycophant, a parasite, a dependant, 
a person who seeks support from others and depends less on their 
convictions, someone who easily absorbs other's views, is malleable, 
and does not think independently. A sponger then is characterised 
by a ‘weak’ mind and a ‘soft’ body. Pattuguruji’s aversion to spongers, 
whether in the past or present, within tradition or in the modern 
set-up, comes out strongly in the narrative. He subjects his own 
community to his philosophy. Though he had sympathy for his 
people, he was also weary of their submissiveness. According to him, 
their submissiveness is an expression of their laziness to toil fora living. 
Blind faith in religion is also a sign of a hanger-on, of someone who 
cannot think independently. Effectively, he questions the villagers’ 
inability to critically assess the impact of government development 
programmes on their lives and the village before adopting them. 

His narrative encapsulates the nature of his leadership: grounded 
in the immediate experience of caste exploitation, its critique of caste 

88 Ritambhara Hebbar 

and its ideology emerges from the choices he made as well as through 
day-to-day living. The balutedari system for him was characterised 
by spongers (the twice-born), who lived off others’ labour, and the 
rest (toilers). It sustained itself on a divide between the mind and 
the body, and the pure and the impure. His narrative subverts and 
overcomes this divide between thought and practice, mind and body 
and the pure and impure. Organisation of life (sangathana) and the 
struggle for freedom (sangharsh), both personal and communal, 
bring together thought and practice of a life of freedom and dignity. 

Notes 

* This paper is a result of the contributions made by a number of people. My 
sincere gratitude to Pattuguruji for sharing his experiences and philosophy 
on various aspects of life. I would especially like to thank Ram Rathod, 
a social worker with Tata Institute of Social Sciences (TISS), Mumbai, 
without whom I could not have grasped the intricacies of my field, for 
sharing his unpublished notes on caste relations in the village. 1 would also 
like to thank Rekha Mammen, my colleague in TISS, as well as a partner of 
the larger study on rural-ity that we have undertaken, for her support and in 
transcribing Pattuguruji’s life story at short notice. I am thankful to Alpana 
Thadani and Neena Barnes for their help in putting the paper together. I 
am also grateful to Anil Hebbar for a careful and a critical reading of the 
paper, which has greatly contributed to improving the arguments therein. 
Lastly, I dedicate this paper to Vikash Pandey, who is no more, but whose 
ideas continue to provide me with food for thought. 

1. An observation made by Ram Rathod to express how caste permeates every 
aspect of our existence; very often we are not even aware of the same. 

2. The term ‘Dalit’ is now extended to all exploited classes across class, caste, 
religion and gender differences. It includes the Scheduled Tribes, neo- 
Buddhists, landless and poor peasants, women and all those who were 
exploited in the name of religion (Omvedt 1994). 

3. The construction of the category ‘rural’ was never as strongly represented 
as it was in Phule’s work. In articulating a powerful anti-Brahmin stance, 
he contrasted the delicate and prosperous Brahmin to the hardy and toiling 
cultivator/warrior. The daily life and diet of a Brahmin was contrasted with 
those who labour. There was powerful evocation of associations with the 
soil. In this, the Marathas, as warriors and leaders of the Shudras, were 
asked to remember their role as the protectors and supporters of the 
Shudra kingdoms against the Aryan invaders, thereby establishing their 
responsibilities towards their motherland. Caste affiliations were sought 
through symbols of the peasant/cultivator and the warrior. Both these 
symbols were united in their attachment with land (O'Hanlon 1985). Paul 

Dalits in a Maharashtra Village 89 

Brass highlights that ruralism or the idea of rural has ‘roots in romantic 
and conservative notions of an organic society’, wherein detenders of this 
organic framework celebrated the harmonic and idyllic village existence. 
The critics, however, view the large mass of the peasantry as the upholders 
of economic and social hierarchy characteristic of rural life, and, therefore, 
resistant to socialist ideas of transformation and change (2000: 11). 
Thus, whether it is essentialism, populism or a programme for agrarian 
transformation associated with the category rural, village India has been 
the site of crosscutting ideological agendas. 

4. A significant reading of village India emerges from the mother of all 
decentralisation programmes in independent India, the Community 
Development Programme, introduced in the 1950s. Tied to the idea of 
rural reconstruction, it was seen as a movement as well as an administrative 
device towards ‘changing the outlook of all village people...develop rising 
expectations for a higher level of living...with rising expectations of 
village people for more and better food, clothing, education and health 
services, shelter, roads, wells...emphasis ... on improving and modernising 
agricultural practices and methods essential for increased agricultural 
production’ (Extracts trom A Guide to Community Development reproduced 
in India International Centre, 1970: 145-6). There is an assumption about 
‘village people’ in general, about their outlook and their standard of living. 
These assumptions are derived from the larger design for a modern India, 
which demands a change in ‘village people's’ attitude and way of life. The 
present catchword of ‘demand-based development’, that is, to make the 
people demand development benefits, seems to have had its beginnings in 
the CDP of the 1950s. Pattuguruji in effect questions the very basis of this 
model of development.
