The Story of a Dalit Family 
from a Sleepy North Bihar Village 

ASHOK SINGH 

A Dalit boy is insulted for saying pranam 
to the village landlord 

Sometime in the late 1960s: One winter evening a village boy, 
Chamar by caste, returning from school came face to face with his 
landlord. Clutching a bundle of books and notebooks under his 
arm, he folded his palms and mumbled ‘pranam, malik’. The boy, 
who was in his teens, wanted to avoid a head-on meeting with his 
malik (landlord), Harishchandra Singh, but the narrow pagdandi 
(footways) wouldn't let him do that. Before the boy could step aside, 
the landlord, in his late thirties and with a huge paunch for his age, 
had started showering chosen expletives on the boy and his ancestors. 
The landlord also charged at the boy with his baton. Upon his return 
to the house, the landlord narrated the incident to his kin, berating 
the audacity of the Dalit boy for having forgotten the accepted ways 
of paying obeisance to upper-caste landlords under the influence of 
education. 

The incident would rankle in the recesses of the landlord's 
memory. The upper-caste Rajputs of the village would often talk 
about what they thought was unforgivable misdemeanour on the 
part of the boy—Anant Ram. The boy who was the first among the 
Chamars—indeed the first among Dalits—in the village to enroll in 
a school should have bowed low from a distance to say ‘salam, malik’. 
It was the accepted form of greeting by a low caste to an upper-caste 

A Dalit Family in a North Bihar Village 91 

elder, especially to someone who was malik in the village. Anant 
Ram should have known, the Rajputs said, that saying pranam with 
folded hands was a form of exchanging greetings between persons of 
equal caste and stature. Anant Ram was accused of having violated 
an old and established code of behaviour. 

Brothers: Anant Ram and Vinay Ram 

Anant Ram and his younger brother Vinay Ram were fair and 
healthy, with well-cut round faces. In appearance, the two Dalit 
children stood out in the Aarijan tola. Their father Gobin Ram and 
grandfather Dhangar Ram were quietly proud of their appearance, 
character and aloof demeanour. However, Gobin (I’m sure Dhangar 
intended to name his son Govind but it was corrupted to become 
Gobin) and Dhangar seldom spoke about the children in public. 
They shielded the two boys from public gaze and unwanted attention 
as fiercely as they loved them. Any question about the children from 
upper-caste Rajput landowners unnerved the grandfather, Dhangar. 
The old man promptly folded his hands, bowed low and asked for 
forgiveness. ‘Garib ke ladka hai, maaf karu’ (please let them be, they 
are a poor man’s sons). Gobin Ram would grunt and utter something 
incoherent whenever asked about his sons. In the villages, mothers 
apply kajal on the forehead of their young children to protect them 
from the evil eye. The family of Dhangar and Gobin applied surma 
(black eyeliner) to protect Anant and Vinay Ram from the ‘evil eyes’ 
of their Rajput masters. 

The more the brothers were shielded, the more curiosity the 
children aroused in the village, especially among the landlords. 
Unlike the other young boys of Chamar families, Anant and Vinay 
never visited the landlord’s house, they never went to fields to assist 
their parents and grandparents. It was the usual practice for Dalits 
to send their young children as labourers to substitute for elders 
whenever the need arose or elders absented from work. But Dhangar 
and Gobin were exceptions. They had the nerve to keep Anant and 
Vinay away from fields and the drudgery of physical labour, braving 
taunts and, sometimes, abuses from the landlord. 

Dhangar was short and dark. He had defective legs and he walked 
dragging his torso with his feet sweeping the floor. Gobin was tall, 
thin and fair. He sported long hair that he had grown during his 

92 Ashok Singh 

stint in the village nautanki. However, that failed to sustain itself, 
forcing Gobin to return to earning his livelihood as an agricultural 
labourer. As a Chamar, he played another role—of a drum player in 
the small village band of a group of four. He was paid for it in kind 
on an annual basis. The Chamar women performed midwifery (this 
most intimate physical job was traditionally reserved for them) to 
deliver children of upper castes. Occasionally, Chamars also feasted 
on dead buffalo and cow's meat, though the prying eyes of upper 
castes prevented them for indulging in the practice too often. 

First Dalit to attend School 

Anant Ram was the first in the chamar toli to go to school. Another 
boy, Vijay Ram, also a Chamar, and later Vinay, Anants younger 
brother, joined him. They went to the same school as the landlord's 
children but Anant and Vinay kept a distance and maintained the 
same aloofness as Dhangar and Gobin wanted and instructed them 
to do. The other young Chamar boys mingled with the Rajput boys, 
and did their bidding but Anant stood apart, always smiling. His 
equanimity and composure made him look like a haughty and self- 
possessed boy. He had an unspoken dignity about him. 

‘Bahut ghamandi hai. Kya chij ka ghamand hai, us Jagjiiwan Ram 
ki aulad ko (What makes this son of Jagjiwan Ram so haughty), 
asked the Rajput landlords in anger, and Harishchandra Singh 
in particular, who owned the homestead lands on which the 
family of Dhangar and Gobin Ram had settled for generations. 
Harishchandra often vented his anger on Dhangar and Gobin but 
he did little to actually prevent the children from going to school. 
No complaint was ever made to school teachers and nothing 
specific was done to create obstacles in the way of Anant and Vinay 
Ram’s education. 

Instances of overt and covert violence against local Dalits including 
Dhangar and Gobin were many. Cases of occasional violence in the 
nature of physical assaults did take place but abuses were showered on 
Dalit men and women routinely and in a casual manner. Almost the 
entire Chamar caste, about twenty households, and an equal number 
of Dusadh were settled on the homestead lands that were owned by 
the family of Harishchandra Singh and it prevented the Dalits from 
registering a protest against any form of exploitation. 

A Dalit Family in a North Bihar Village 93 
The Village 

None of the Dalit families in village Barahi Jagdish owned any 
land. They were all agricultural labourers, working on the fields of 
Harishchandra Singh and some other Rajput landlords. The Harijan 
tola was settled in the north of the village and Dhangar’s house stood 
at its northern tip. The houses were small thatched huts but clean 
and orderly. Till forty years ago, the Harijan Tola had no hand pumps 
but the Chamar and Dusadh cola each had wells. The Chamars and 
Dusadhs maintained separate caste identities though they worked 
together and sympathised with each other. 

The Rajputs who owned almost seventy-five per cent of the coral 
village lands were settled in the southern tip of the village. Four 
main families of the same lineage whose ancestors were said to have 
settled down fist in the village by clearing forests over 400 years ago 
owned most of the land and also almost the entire Dalit workforce 
as bonded labourers. It was a well-knit village. Except Brahmins 
working as purohits (priests) who were summoned to perform pujas 
and marriage and death ceremonies from a nearby village, Barahi 
Jagdish didn't require workforce from outside the village. 

Wages 

For as long as the village elders could recall, Dalits, and also the 
labourers of other castes, were paid four seers of paddy for a day's 
work, and two seers for half a day. Full-day workers also received 
noon-time meals with water. One seer would measure less than 
half a kilogram. Nobody recalled when the wages were revised last. 
Therefore, one can say with certainty that from 1930 to 1980, the 
wages remained fixed and constant. No demand for wage revision 
was ever heard in that period. It was only during the 1990s that the 
wage was increased by a seer for a full-day’s work. 

It was hard for wage earners to provide two square meals a day 
to the family and meet emergency needs. On account of health, 
marriage, death or some other urgent needs, Dhangar and Gobin 
absented themselves from work and that forced them to borrow. 
They remained perpetually in debt. The debt was mostly in kind— 
paddy. Dalits were not lent money because of their inability to 
mortgage lands. Debt and the homestead land that belonged to the 

94 Ashok Singh 

Rajputs kept the Dalit families in bondage and didn’t allow them the 
opportunity to shift from one landlord to another. 

No overt cases of land-labour conflict or violence took place in 
the village. But the Dalits and the rich Rajputs always remained in 
distrust of each other. Rajputs spoke ill of Dalits. They abused them 
without provocation, and sometimes physically assaulted them. The 
Dalits meekly resented their plight and grudgingly accepted their lot. 
They cursed their fate that had ordained them to serve their masters 
but didn’t entertain any idea or think of any strategy to break out of 
the bondage. 

Elections as catalyst of change 

Significantly, it was not economics but politics that woke up the 
Dalits and brought them into conflict with the Rajputs landlords. 
Elections—from panchayat to Parliament—appeared to work 
as catalyst for change in the relationship between the two. The Dalits 
bore a grudge that they were not allowed to vote. Till the late 1970s, 
they were never allowed to cast votes in the elections; the Rajputs 
sought to create a facade of consensus in the village. Initially, the 
voting preferences of both Dalits and Rajputs happened to be the 
Congress party. The village elders succeeded in persuading the Dalits 
that any conflict on account of elections would unnecessarily create 
a schism in the village, which in the long run would harm the Dalits. 
The underdogs pursed their lips but fell in line. 

The conflict in the voting interests arose when a candidate for 
whom the rich Rajputs had sympathy opposed the Congress party. 
The Dalits supported the Congress from their heart. They had two 
identifiable figures in the Congress party: Indira Gandhi and Jagjiwan 
Ram. By the late 1960s and early 1970s, Mrs Gandhi had succeeded 
in creating a pro-poor image for herself. After the 1971 elections for 
Parliament and during the Emergency, Mrs Gandhi’s pro-poor and 
pro-Dalit image was further consolidated. 

1971 elections and seeds of activism 

The 1971 elections proved to be an important event in the village. 
‘The Congress had put up Nagendra Yadav as its candidate for the Lok 
Sabha while the opposition candidate was a prominent Rajput backed 

A Dalit Family in a North Bihar Village 95 

by the Socialist Party. The village was divided. The majority of the 
Rajputs supported the Socialist candidate. A few Rajput families, the 
Dalits and the Muslims were for the Congress that eventually won 
the election to the Lok Sabha. Though the Chamars and Dusadhs 
would have liked to vote for the Congress, they were prevented from 
doing so. The Rajputs cast their vores. Most of the Muslims too were 
prevented from entering the polling booth, except one schoolteacher, 
Ehrarul Haq, who entered the booth, took a ballot paper and holding 
it up in one hand turned towards the assembled Rajput men and 
said, ‘Look where | stamp’; he stamped on the Congress symbol. It 
was an act of dehance that impressed the Dalits. 

After the announcement of results, some Muslim youths and 
children wanted to take out a victory procession in the village. The 
Dalits wanted to join them but were scared that they might invite 
the wrath of the landlords. They needed to be convinced; their fear 
needed to be put to rest. 

The author, then a student, visited the Harijan tola to speak 
to the Dalits. He sought to convince them that participating in 
a procession was no crime and that it was not aimed against an 
individual or a group or a caste. They were reluctant as they could 
foresee the danger. However, they agreed to join the procession after 
some persuasive talk by the writer who as an adolescent in age and 
political education dropped names of Gandhi, Ambedkar and Marx 
in the same breath. 

It was a disaster. No sooner had the procession of Dalits and 
Muslims started with ‘Congress zindabad slogans than the landlords 
were informed. Tension rose as the procession meandered through 
the village streets. The landlords decided that the enthusiasm and 
the defiant act of the low castes had to be nipped in the bud. Some 
of them proceeded to confront the processionists armed with batons 
and firearms. The members leading the procession were attacked and 
they beat a hasty retreat. Later, the landlords summoned Dhangar, 
Gobin and some other Dalits and abused and threatened them with 
dire consequences if they committed another such folly. 

Elections inflamed passions but in the absence of any organisation, 
party or a leader, the Dalits were prevented from exercising their right 
to vote until in the early 1990s when a separate booth for Dalits was 
set up in the village. The 1977 Parliament election was also a notable 
event. The Dalits’ sympathy and support for the Congress continued. 

96 Ashok Singh 

The Rajputs were swayed by the pro-kulak and peasants’ agenda of 
the Janata Party. Some Muslims and Dalits queued up at the polling 
booth to vote but were physically prevented from doing so. 

Curiously, none of the educated Dalit boys ever showed any 
interest in politics. They certainly influenced the Dalits voting 
preferences but never deviated from their goal to finish education 
and secure employment in the government. Anant Ram became a 
schoolteacher while Vinay Ram joined the Bihar Administrative 
Service as a deputy collector. 

The economic structure of the village had begun to fall apart in the 
late 1970s when the irrigation department of the Bihar government 
began relocating villagers. The village had fallen berween two sides of 
an embankment that was erected asa flood control measure to tame the 
river Baghmati. The turbulent Baghmati that had contributed to the 
enrichment of Rajput landlords now devastated the village economy. 
The relocation and shifting of Dalits by the government made them 
owners of their own homestead land. It freed them from age-old 
bondage. In one stroke, the Dalits got what the Zamindari Abolition 
and Land-Ceiling Acts had failed to do since Independence. 

But even before the relocation, an incident shook the village. 
Gobin Ram was entrusted with the responsibilities of guarding a 
sugarcane-laden bullock cart with rubber tyres. The tyres were stolen 
during the night. Harishchandra Singh blamed Gobin Ram for the 
theft and abused him. In retaliation, Gobin Ram lodged a complaint 
with the police. He also lodged a complaint for non-payment of 
wages in the district labour court. It was a face-off in the court that 
created a sensation in the village. The landlord stood in the witness 
box facing Gobin Ram who, when asked by the court to identify 
him, raised his finger in anger and pointing at Harishchandra Singh 
said, “Haan, yehi hai (Yes, that is the man)’. It was for the first time 
in the village that a Dalit had complained against his landlord to the 
police. 

Mass Media and Government’s Propaganda 

Besides periodic elections and the education of Anant and Vinay 
Ram, what contributed to the rise of Dalit consciousness was Indira 
Gandhi's 20-Point Programme during the Emergency. With the 
presence of educated members amidst them, it was easy for the 

A Dalit Family in a North Bihar Village 97 

Dalits to understand that the practice of bonded labour had been 
abolished; that Dalits could not be displaced from the homestead 
lands; and that they were entitled to the government-approved 
wages. And most important of all, nobody could deprive them of 
their fundamental right to vote and that atrocities against Dalits were 
punishable in law. 

Radio as a tool for mass media education played a significant role 
in the awakening of Dalit consciousness. It was common tor Muslims 
to own radios, listen to BBC Urdu and Hindi programmes, and tune 
in to Pakistan Radio; a few families subscribed to Urdu newspapers. 
By the 1970s, some Dalits too owned radio sets. All India Radio's 
incessant propaganda of the welfare measures of the Indira Gandhi 
government made a huge impact on the Dalits and Muslims in the 
village. 

With the relocation of the entire Dalit population outside the 
original village, ownership of the homestead lands and the general 
worsening of Harishchandra Singh’s economic stature due to division 
in the family and devastating yearly floods, the profile of the village 
changed. Dalits were free to choose their workplace, employer and 
time of employment. Gobin Ram and other Chamars travelled to 
neighbouring villages for earning daily wages. They bargained for 
increased wages, though seldom did they get a better deal. 

Birthday in a Dalit Family 

In March 1995, on the eve of the state Assembly elections, there was 
a buzz in the village. A group of Rajput youths from the village drove 
more than 100 km to a town to attend a birthday function. It was a 
hush-hush visit. On inquiry it was found that the youths had gone to 
attend the first birthday of Vinay Ram’s son. Vinay Ram was posted 
there as deputy collector. It was, perhaps, for the first time that a 
Chamar’s family was celebrating the birthday of its son. And more 
significantly, it was for the first time that upper-caste Rajput youths 
attended a function in the family of a Chamar from the village. The 
Rajput boys said they went because they wanted to enlist Dalits’ 
support in the elections. It was a lame excuse. Times had changed. 
It was the chance education of Anant Ram and Vinay Ram that 
seems to have set the stage for the awakening of Dalits in the village. 
And was it also a chance decision of Dhangar and Gobin to educate 

98 Ashok Singh 

their children? What or who motivated them to send the boys to 
school? What made them so determined or strong in their mission? 
In a village about seven kilometres from Barahi Jagdish, a young man 
of Chamar caste had joined the Congress party as a primary member. 
He pretended to be close to Babu Jagjiwan Ram. He visited Barahi 
Jagdish occasionally but associated himself only with the family of 
Harishchandra Singh. He thought he had moved upwards from the 
level where Dhanagr and Gobin Ram stood. It is doubtful he had 
any role to play in the boys’ education. 

Ten years down the line, the Harijan tola that used to be a cluster 
of huts with thatched roofs has a couple of brick houses. The most 
prominent among them, of course, belongs to Anant and Vinay 
Ram. However, it is doubtful if the economic condition of Dalits 
has changed in any significant way, though the upper caste—Dalit 
relations have witnessed radical and unrecognisable changes. 

The avenues for education of Dalit children in the areas are as 
scarce as it was over fifty years ago; there has been no increase in 
the number of middle and high schools. There is no school for girls. 
Healthcare facilities are as pathetic as they were then. 

Of late, a faction of the Maoist Communist Centre has started 
making its presence felt in the area. The Maoists are reported to be 
operating in tandem with their counterparts in Nepal, a bare fifteen 
kilometres away. The Maoists are trying to introduce the politics of 
violence in the district that has seen a few instances of class-related 
violence. It remains to be seen what impact the Maoists have on the 

Dalits. 

Nature of Change 

Through the story of a Chamar family, especially two brothers who 
broke the shackles of caste and all societal odds to educate themselves, 
moved up the social ladder and became role models for Dalits in 
the village, the paper wishes to prompt a debate on the study of 
change. The village has changed. The land-labour relations too have 
undergone changes. But the changes were not prompted by any 
outside agency—political party, NGO or any leader from within or 
outside the Dalit society. The changes haven't been cataclysmic; they 
have been slow and silent. And predictably, changes that have been 
taking place without active intervention of any agencies have failed 
to achieve much in improving the lot of the Dalits. 

6 

Caste System in India: 
Dr Ambedkar’s Perspective 

P. G. JoGDAND 

Dr B. R. Ambedkar was one of the greatest sons of India. He was not 
merely a leader of the downtrodden; in fact, it is unfair to describe him 
so. He was an original thinker and one of the country’s great visionaries. 
He was a historian, an economist, a philosopher, a revolutionary 
and sociologist, a jurist, an academician and Constitution maker. A 
prolific writer, he had imbibed knowledge that was vast and covered 
diverse fields. Over nearly forty years of academically, socially and 
politically active life, Dr Ambedkar contributed significantly to 
the evolution and development of modern India. In his academic 
pursuit and social and political activities, he was driven by the desire 
to understand the vital national issues that the country faced and to 
find a solution. Dr Ambedkar addressed and contributed practically 
on all issues of national importance that arose between the early 
1920s and the mid-1950s. 

Most memorable of Dr Ambedkar’s contributions include his 
intellectual efforts in the articulation of the political and economic 
framework for India in the form of the Constitution, the problem 
of minorities and the issue of Pakistan, reorganisation of the states, 
reform of the Hindu social and religious order, social policy, labour 
policy, water policy and a number of other issues, but above all the 
social and political efforts for the uplift of deprived classes. 

Of great significance but less well known among Dr Ambedkar’s 
contributions to the nation was his direct participation in the 
formulation of the objective and strategy of the post-war economic 

100 P. G. Jogdand 

plan and development of water and electric power resources. He 
was a cabinet member in charge of labour, irrigation and power 
portfolios between 1942 and 1946. Though he made a stupendous. 
contribution to the overall development of the country, it has hardly 
been studied in our colleges and universities. 

In our country, the caste system is a reality and it is evident in our 
day-to-day life. People from all walks of life are tackling caste (or 
playing with caste) and no one bothers about eradicating it. Why 
has the caste system survived? Does this mean that the caste system 
is a static phenomenon? Is it possible to annihilate it? To grapple 
with these questions we have to take recourse to Dr Ambedkar's 
discourse on the caste system. Dr Ambedkar’s perspective can be 
traced back to his research paper which he presented at the Columbia 
University that was later published as a book titled Castes in India: 
Their Mechanism, Genesis and Development. His subsequent works 
such as Annihilation of Caste (1936), Who were the Shudras? (1946), 
What Congress and Gandhi have done to the Untouchables (1945), The 
Untouchables (1948), and The Rise and Fall of Hindu Women contain 
the rich ideas of his thinking on the caste system. His hitherto 
unpublished writings on caste and the Hindu social order have been 
published by the Government of Maharashtra. This body of literature 
throws new light on his analysis of the caste phenomenon. It is only 
after considering all these sources that one can get a comprehensive 
picture of his perspective on the caste system. 

Prior to formulation of his own theory of the genesis, mechanism 
and evolution of caste in India, Dr Ambedkar studied, criticised and 
discarded a number of existing theories on the subject. He considered 
the earlier works of the origin of caste produced by Western as well as 
Indian scholars. While appreciating the scholarship in these works, 
Dr Ambedkar offered constructive criticism of the way eminent 
persons like H. Risley, Nesfield, Senart and S. V. Ketkar presented 
the analysis of caste. In the first instance, the analysis of caste made 
by each scholar was, according to Dr Ambedkar, incomplete in the 
sense that it highlighted only one of the characteristics of caste as a 
sole cause of its existence though these definitions were not altogether 
wrong. Further, he says that all these scholars, except S. V. Ketkar, 
defined caste as an isolated unit and not as a group within and 
with definite relations to the system of castes as a whole. To him 
‘caste in the singular number is an unreality’. Castes exit only in 

Ambedkar on Caste 10) 

the plural number. Thus, Ambedkar considered ‘caste’ as a system, 
It is only with reference to the system of castes that the mechanism 
of caste-operation becomes meaningful. Dr Ambedkar agreed with 
the definition of caste given by Ketkar, according to whom (a) the 
prohibition of inter-marriage and (b) membership by autogeny, or 
birth, are the two characteristics of caste. However, Dr Ambedkar 
believed that these two aspects of caste are ‘one and the same thing’. 
He, therefore, concluded that endogamy is the only characteristic that 
is peculiar to caste. In addition, Dr Ambedkar highlighted two major 
characteristics of the caste system. One, the lack of interconnection 
between the various castes which form a system; each caste is separate 
and distinct. Second, the caste order is vertical and not horizontal. 
Dr Ambedkar has put forth the interrelated major theses on the caste 
system as follows: 

(i) It is class (varna)-caste system; 
(ii) It is not horizontal; 
(iii) It is not interpenetrating; 
(iv) Superimposition of endogamy over exogamy; 
(v) The Brahmins being the first enclosed group (caste) followed 
by non-Brahmins; 
(vi) Ex-communication of members of caste for their non- 
observance of caste rules; 
(vii) Division of labourers (not division of labour) into water- 
tight compartments; 
(viii) Hierarchy (graded inequality); and 
(ix) Dogma of predestination (status of a person is based on 

birth). 

Dr Ambedkar analysed aspects of the Hindu society and showed 
how religion, philosophy and the social order of the Hindus interacted 
with one another to nurture and maintain the continuity of the 
caste system. He was convinced that unless and until Hinduism was 
obliterated it was not possible to eradicate the caste system. 

In his analysis he maintains that ‘a caste is an enclosed class’; class is 
the father of the institution of caste. Manu (Hindu law giver) did not 
give the law of caste; caste existed long before Manu. Manu was an 
upholder of it and therefore philosophised about it, but he certainly 
did not ordain the present order of Hindu society. Dr Ambedkar 
argued that the Brahmins first became endogamous and enclosed 

102 P. G. Jogdand 

themselves into a caste. The other classes imitated the Brahmins and 
thus caste came into being. 

Endogamy or the closed-door system was a fashion in Hindu 
society. As it had originated from the Brahmin caste it was 
wholeheartedly imitated by all, and every caste became endogamous. 
It is the ‘infection of imitation’ that caught all these sub-divisions 
on their onward march of differentiation and turned them into 
castes. Dr Ambedkar said ‘Some closed the door; others found it 
closed against them’. He maintained that though castes originated 
through this process of varnas being enclosed, ‘caste must be studied 
apart from varna. Why? Because caste as a social group has its own 
peculiarities. Dr Ambedkar stated these peculiarities by defining 
caste as a social group having belief in Hindu religion and bound 
by certain regulations to marriage, food and occupation. To this he 
added one more characteristic and that is the ‘common name’, by 
which the caste is recognised. Dr Ambedkar further maintained that 
this common name gave the caste fixity, continuity and individuality. 
It made the caste an organised and most importantly ‘an involuntary 
grouping. Thus, membership of a caste was no more a matter of 
individual choice; it was a given. 

The caste system is not merely a division of labour. It is also a 
division of labourers. Civilised society undoubtedly needs division of 
labour. But in no civilised society is division of labour accompanied by 
this unnatural division of labourers into water-tight compartments. 
Dr Ambedkar held that division of labour brought about by the caste 
system is not based on choice; individual choice has no role in it. It 
is based on the dogma of predestination. 

To him Hinduism was a ‘bundle of riddles’. He believed that 
like all other religions Hinduism too was a historical happening. 
He had a peculiar understanding of Indian history and culture. He 
categorically maintained that there has never been anything like 
a common Indian culture. Historically, there were three Indias— 
Brahminic India, Buddhist India and Hindu India. Dr Ambedkar 
observed that the history of India before the Muslim invasion is the 
history of a mortal conflict between Brahminism and Buddhism. 
These observations of Dr Ambedkar are crucial to the understanding 
of his perspective on the caste system. 

He made a significant observation when he said that Hindu 
society was not based on inequality but on graded inequality. Thus, 

Ambedkar on Caste 103 

every caste is either higher or lower in its relation to other castes. This 
prevented the process of polarisation between the exploited castes 
against the exploiter castes. 

Dr Ambedkar’s approach to the problem of untouchability and 
untouchables was significantly different from chat of his contemporaries. 
The Savarna reformers tried to understand and solve the problem of 
untouchability in an isolated fashion. Dr Ambedkar invariably linked 
this problem with that of the caste system. Hence, his argument was that 
unless the caste system was abolished, untouchability would not disappear. 
According to him untouchability essentially was a result of the caste 
system. His contemporaries opposed the practice of untouchability on 
humanitarian grounds and they could not understand the nexus berween 
the caste system and the practice of untouchability. Dr Ambedkar was 
very specific in placing the problem of untouchability at the centre of 
his approach and protest. He was convinced that it was only the class of 
untouchables who felt the urgency to abolish the caste system. 

Having formulated his views on the genesis and mechanism of the 
caste system, Dr Ambedkar suggested two remedies to annihilate 
caste, viz., inter-caste marriages and destroying belief in the sanctity 
and authority of the Shastras and Vedas which confer religious 
sanction to the system. He further recommended the abolition of 
hereditary priesthood. 

In a nutshell, Dr Ambedkar’s main views on the caste system were 
as follows: 

1. He developed his perspective outside the frame of Hinduism. 

2. Hetried to point out the necessary link between the philosophy 
of Hinduism and the survival of the caste system. 

3. Through his convincing analysis he pointed out that the 
major contradictions in Hindu society were along caste lines. 
Unless they were sharpened, no effective anti-caste struggle 
could be waged, and without organising an anti-caste 
struggle there could not be any revolutionary social change 
in India. Thus, Dr Ambedkar’s position on the ‘caste system’ 
was essentially ‘confrontationist’ rather than ‘reconciliatory 
(it was protest- and not reform-oriented). This anti-caste 
perspective helped strengthen the politics of ‘confrontation 
vis-a-vis ‘accommodation’. 

4. Finally, Dr Ambedkar’s discourse is related to the emancipation 
of mankind from all kinds of exploitation by resting it on 

104 P. G. Jogdand 

the egalitarian doctrines of Buddhism. Thus, his perspective 
contributed to the politics of emancipation of the Dalits in 
particular and all human beings in general. 

Dr Ambedkar’s analysis amply proved that he had a vision for 
creating a new society, a modern India. But unfortunately sociologists 
and the so-called intellectuals of the country did not accept his 
contribution . He has been made a victim of reduction in which he 
is recognised only as a ‘Dalit leader’ and, at most, a “Constitution 
maker’. There has been a calculated silence and organised neglect 
about his contribution and his agenda for the liberation of all. 

Dr Ambedkar remains a forgotten figure in academia. The 
producers of knowledge systems have little regard for him and it 
is they who have annihilated the author of ‘Annihilation of Caste’. 
Even the series of subaltern studies had to pause after six volumes to 
acknowledge Ambedkar. Why this collective conspiracy of silence 
on the part of Indian scholarship? Why are the communities of 
knowledge and communities of power united in their marginalisation 
of Dr Ambedkar? Neither politics nor knowledge discovers in him 
a vision for India any more. We have to address these questions 
objectively and give justice to the formulation of Babasaheb. 
Dr Ambedkar’s thesis on the Hindu social order and caste system is 
an eye-opener and needs to be seriously pursued in the contemporary 
situation. Why dont Dr Ambedkar’s contribution and writings find 
a place in the syllabus in colleges and universities? I believe that time 
has come to educate the educated in India. 

References

7 

Laws and Homestead Land for 
Rural Landless Labourers and 
Marginalised Communities in Bihar: 
A Micro-perspective 

SANJAY KUMAR 

Housing is one of the basic requirements for human survival. 
Every citizen needs to have a safe, secure and healthy place to live, 
work and lead a life of dignity. A house provides not only physical 
protection against the vagaries of nature but also space and privacy to 
an individual and his family for physical, emotional and intellectual 
growth. More importantly, ownership of a house brings about a 
profound change in a person's social existence, endowing him with 
an identity, dignity and a sense of belonging, thus integrating him 
with his immediate social milieu and enhancing his opportunity for 
participation in the social, economic and cultural life of the society. 
Considering its importance for the overall well-being of individuals 
and families, the right to housing has been enshrined as a basic 
human right in international human rights instruments and treaties 
such as the Universal Declaration of Human Rights (1948), the 
International Covenant on Economic, Social and Cultural Rights 
(1976), the Istanbul Declaration, and the Habitat Agenda, as well as 
in the Constitution of India as interpreted by the Supreme Court in 
many of its judgments. The meaning of adequate housing has also 
been gradually enlarged whereby it does not refer only to a physical 
dwelling unit but also includes provisions for basic services like safe 

106 Sanjay Kumar 

drinking water, sanitation, drainage, clean and healthy surroundings 
and environment, etc., which are essential for decent living. 

Secure rural housing is an integral part of access to and ownership 
rights over homestead land. Recent studies have shown that 
ownership of homestead land can confer multiple other important 
benefits to rural households in terms of income, food and economic 
security, particularly when the plots are large enough to include 
a kitchen garden and space for a few animals. (Hanstad, Brown 
and Roy Prosterman 2002; Hanstad, Haque and Nielson 2008.). 
Ownership of even a small plot of homestead land has been found 
to increase the status and self-image of rural households and their 
ability to participate in the socio-political affairs of the village. It 
can provide landless labour households freedom of choice with 
regard to their employer and increase their bargaining power for 
higher wages and better working terms and conditions. Ownership 
of homestead land has also been found to confer other important 
benefits to rural households in terms of income, food and economic 
security, particularly when the plots are large enough to include 
a kitchen garden and space for rearing a few animals. Homestead 
plots also provide an important safety net through their value as a 
source of food, income and capital for families in times of drought, 
unemployment, or other hardships. Such plots also increase a family’s 
ability to access formal and informal sources of credit for investment 
purposes or in times of distress. 

Against this background of the importance of the right to housing 
to the overall well-being and empowerment of the rural landless, 
this paper examines and analyses the issues and challenges involved 
in effective implementation of the laws and policies in Bihar that 
are meant to guarantee these rights to the rural landless and the 
marginalised communities. In the process it provides key insights 
into the numerous obstacles that undermine the very reach of the law 
to those it is meant to benefit. The paper draws on field data from an 
action research that was conducted in Gaya District of Bihar during 
2006-07’. The paper is divided into three sections. The first section 
describes the existing laws and policies that provide for granting 
access to and ownership rights over homestead land; the second 
section, based on field data, discusses the interface between the laws, 
policies, rules and the processes and practices of its implementation; 

Law, Land and Dalits in Bihar 107 

and the third section identifies issues and obstacles involved in 
effective implementation of the laws and policies. 

Laws and Policies in Bihar on Homestead Land 

The landless rural households in Bihar can be broadly classified 
into two categories on the basis of access to homestead land and 
secure ownership rights over it—first, the households which have 
constructed their house on a small plot of land over which they do 
not have secure ownership rights and, therefore, are always at the 
risk of eviction; and second, those households which do not have 
access to land tor homestead purposes. Access to land for housing 
in rural areas thus essentially involves: (i) granting the landless poor 
ownership rights over homestead land on which they have constructed 
their house, and (ii) allotting homestead plots to those without any 
land. Before discussing the laws and policies providing for these, 
it would be pertinent to first describe briefly the various types of 
homestead land as well as the pattern of settlement/displacement of 
the habitat of rural landless in Gaya District of Bihar over the last 
few decades. 

Types of Homestead Land and Pattern of 
Settlement/Displacement of the Rural Landless 

There are three major types of homestead land in Bihar—vaiyati, 
gairmazarua khas ot malik, and gairmazarua aam—on which 
the landless poor in rural areas have built their houses. Raiyati 
Homestead Land originally belonged to a landowner, on which a 
person was allowed to reside and construct his house. Usually small 
plots of land were provided to them by the employer landowners to 
settle down. This practice was an essential element of the traditional 
kamiauti system (Prakash 1990), widely prevalent in Gaya District, 
which evolved to fulfil the requirement of permanent availability of 
cheap labour for agriculture as well as maintenance of traditional 
tank irrigation in the Magadh region. When a razyat (tenant of a 
zamindar) got a new kamia (bonded labourer), he had to provide him 
with some land including a house plot. He did so with either his own 
land or asked the landlord for a house and some land for the purpose. 
In the former case, the kamia was clearly a service jagirdar (holder of 

108 Sanjay Kumar 

a jagir) of the raiyat. In the latter, the land was clearly not the raiyat’s, 
and the kamia was treated in the records as a non-agricultural tenant 
of the village and not given a separate khatian (record of right). 
Gairmazarua Khas or Malik Lands are those that were under the 
possession of particular landowners under the Zamindari System but 
after its abolition and enactment of land reforms have been vested 
with the government. Ceiling-surplus lands are an example of this 
category of land. Although legally such lands are vested with the 
government, dominant landowners in the villages may still have 
control over them. Gairmazarua Aam lands are those that are reserved 
for common public use, such as village pasture land. At present, this 
category of land is vested with the village Panchayats. 

The majority of the landless rural labour households in Bihar 
were traditionally settled mainly on raiyati land given to them 
by their landowning employers during the Zamindari System. 
However, it seems that during the last twenty to twenty-five years 
the new settlements of landless labourers have come up mainly on 
various types of government or public land. The number of landless 
families residing today on raiyati land is comparatively much less 
than those residing on gairmazarua malik and gairmazarua aam 
land or land owned by other public agencies. There are two major 
reasons that can be identified for displacement/shifting of rural 
landless from raiyati land: (i) growth in the number of members of 
the family and disappearance of the old traditional practice of giving 
house plots to labourers by the landowners for various reasons; 
and (ii) social conflict between the landless and the landowners, 
especially because labourers started asserting against oppression 
and subordination. 

After Independence, the expansion of capitalist agrarian relations 
and continuing population pressure have led to the gradual decline 
of the traditional patron-client bondage that characterised the 
landowner—agricultural labourer link in the past. (Das 1981; Breman 
1974). Various developments such as the introduction of motor- 
driven tube wells, tractors and motor-driven threshers, combined with 
smaller landholdings and increased participation of landowners in 
production have contributed to a decline in the need for agricultural 
labourers for farm operations. On the other hand, the population of 
landless labourers witnessed manifold growth and their small plots of 
raiyati homestead land could not sustain their burgeoning families. 

Law, Land and Dalits in Bihar 109 

In the absence of any avenues available to them tor access to land for 
housing they were pushed to build houses and settle on gairmazarua 
khas/malik and gairmazarua aam land. 

The abolition of Zamindari and the subsequent land and tenancy: 
reforms measures also did not benefit the landless poor belonging to 
marginalised communities such as the SCs and STs in any significant 
manner in terms of accrual of land to them. (Das 2000: Government 
of Bihar 2008). As a result of increasing population pressure together 
with growing landlessness among the rural poor, Bihar was confronted 
in the second half of the 1960s with heightened social tension 
and violent conflicts between the landed class and landless labour 
households (Das 1981). Under these circumstances the landless 
labour households, settled on raiyati lands, started leaving their 
habitats and settling on various government or public land in order 
to escape increased oppression and subordination by the landowners. 
Azad Bigha, Antu Bigha and Shanti Nagar, some fitteen km from 
the district headquarters of Gaya, form three such settlement sites 
that came into being as a result of the collective flight of kamias 
from their parent village Bandhua in the mid-1960s owing to violent 
clashes with upper-caste landlords. Taking a break from his daily job 
of stone-cutting, Phulu Manjhi of Azad Bigha, pointing towards the 
barren hills, poignantly says: 

These hills have given us freedom. Freedom from exploitation and 
suffering at the hands of the upper-caste maliks in our parental village 
of Bandhua. I was some 15 years old when we came here. My father was 
the kamiya of a Rajput malik ...some of us even had lands there. We had 
to not only bear verbal abuse but sometimes even physical torture... it 
was quite suffocating there. There was no space for us... sometimes our 
domestic animals used to enter the maliks’ fields which was a constant 
source of quarrel... Enough is enough... once some kamiyas had an 
altercation in the field with an upper-caste malik, All the kamias present 
in the field regrouped and beat him black and blue. My father who 
was instrumental in this called a meeting of the Bhuinyas of Bandhua. 
Looking at the grave consequences of the incident all of us decided 
to leave Bandhua and seek refuge in these foothills. My father Aklu 
Manjhi named our village Azad Bigha since it gave us freedom from 
the bondage that we had experienced in our parental village of Bandhua 

(Ahmed 2004). 

Like Azad Bigha, the neighbouring settlement sites of Antu Bigha 
and Shanti Nagar share the same history of displacement of Bhuinyas 

110 Sanjay Kumar 

(a tribe that migrated from Jharkhand to south Bihar) from Bandhua. 
Raja Manjhi of Shanti Nagar, ruefully remembering the days, says: 

We had to brave 48 hours of incessant rains standing in the foothills 
when we came here after a dispute with upper-caste maliks of Bandhua... 
employees of the forest and railway department lodged legal complaints 
against us. Many a time our houses ... were razed. The upper-caste maliks 
of Bandhua together with the government officials tried their best. 
Though we did not have money we pooled our resources to fight the 
case in court and at last we won. But we still live in the fear of eviction 

(Ahmed 2004). 

However, it seems today that the landowning farmers generally do 
not want labourers to be displaced from their settlements because 
they need their labour. They even want them to continue residing 
on the land given to them by their forefathers (raiyati land). ‘This is 
because as long as the labourers reside on such land, the landowners 
can hope to exercise control over them. But the landowners are 
opposed to giving legal entitlement of these homestead lands to the 
labourers since it will give the latter independence and freedom to 
work wherever they want and they would no longer listen to their 
landlords’ diktats. For example, ten to twelve landless labourers were 
settled on raiyati land in Pale village in Wazirganj Block of Gaya 
District. When the issue of giving them parcha (the legal ownership 
document) came up there was opposition from the landowner. 
Consequently the Circle Officer of the Block did not proceed to 
take any action to settle the labourers on the land and provide them 
parcha, on the pretext that doing so would create social tension in 
the village. There is a saying among the landowners in Gaya district 
that landless labourers should neither die nor become fat (ma mare na 
mutaye) (Singh 2009); the meaning is that the landowners want the 
labourers to survive so they can utilise their cheap labour, but they 
also do not want to see the labourers becoming prosperous because 
in that case they will no longer work in their fields. 

The landless poor themselves seem to prefer to reside on government 
or public land because it gives them independence and freedom from 
subordination and oppression of landowners. However, instances 
have been found of the dominant landowning groups harassing and 
evicting people settled on even gairmazarua land. In one such case 
in Goreya village in the same Block, when some landless families 
constructed their houses on gairmazarua land, the dominant 

Law, Land and Dalits in Bihar 111 

landowners of the village destroyed them. ‘This happened thrice. It 
stopped only when some social activists of the area intervened and 
threatened to take legal action against the landowners. 

Provisions in Laws and Policies for Granting 
Homestead Rights to the Rural Landless 

Bihar was probably the first state in the country to enact a separate 
law, namely the Bihar Privileged Persons Homestead Tenancy Act 
1947, tor providing security of tenure to landless rural households 
over their homestead land. The act received the assent of the 
Governor-General on 17 January 1948, and the assent was first 
published in the Bihar Gazette of 18 February 1948. This act was 
meant to provide permanent tenurial rights over homestead to those 
landless rural households which have been residing on raiyati lands. 
However, since then various other rules and regulations have also 
been framed by the state government to provide ownership rights 
to landless households over gairmazarua khas and gairmazarua aam 
lands, as well as to allot house sites to those without access to land. 

Bihar Privileged Persons Homestead Tenancy Act 1947 
(BPPHT Act 1947) 

According to the provisions of the Bihar Privileged Persons Homestead 
Tenancy Act 1947, a ‘privileged person’ who has built his house on 
private land given to him by a landowner for residential purposes and 
has been living on that land continuously for one year has permanent 
right over his homestead land. The Act defines homestead as any 
land which is held by the privileged person for residential purposes 
including any building erected on the land together with bari, sahan 
(both refer to pieces of land around a hut) and any vacant land used 
for residential purposes. Land falling under the ambit of the BPPHT 
Act originally belonged to a landowner who had allowed a person to 
reside on it and is known as raiyati land. A person who besides his 
homestead holds no other land or holds any such land not exceeding 
one acre becomes a privileged person under the BPPHT Act after 
completing an unbroken stay up to a period of one year and thus 
becomes eligible to get a permanent entitlement parcha issued by 
the revenue authorities. The Circle Officer has been authorised as 
the competent authority to effect settlement of raiyati land under 

the Act. 

112 Sanjay Kumar 

Provisions for Settlement of Gairmazarua Khas and 
Gairmazarua Aam Land for Homestead 

The provisions of the Bihar Privileged Persons Homestead Tenancy 
Act 1947 do not apply to either gairmazarua khas or gairmazarua 
aam lands. But keeping in view the fact that a large number of poor 
households have constructed houses on such land, the government 
made a policy that where a privileged person had his homestead 
on gairmazarua khas land, his possession should be recognised and 
normal settlement made with him (Government of Bihar 1971a). In 
case of gairmazarua aam land, it was decided that if such land had 
lost its aam character and is no longer used for community purposes, 
such land should also be settled with the privileged persons. But 
before the settlement of gairmazarua aam land, the community 
has to be informed through a public notice, and if any objection 
is raised against the proposal, the settlement cannot be made. The 
policy also states that in settlement of these lands for homestead 
purposes, preference should be given to the following categories of 

households: 

(a) Scheduled Castes 

(b) Scheduled Tribes 

(c) Backward Classes (Annexure-I) 

(d) Serving soldiers and families of martyred soldiers, and 

(e) Refugees from East Pakistan and Burma who came to India 

on or after 2 January 1964. 

While gairmazarua malik or khas land may be settled with the 
eligible category of persons, gairmazarua aam land is reserved for 
public use unless the nature of such land has changed and the Gram 
Sabha has passed a resolution warranting its settlement with eligible 
persons. After the Gram Sabha has resolved to that effect, a case 
record is prepared which goes to the government through the Circle 
Officer, SDO, District Collector and the Divisional Commissioner. 
The settlement finally can be permitted only after its approval by the 
state capital-based Department of Land and Revenue. The routing 
of papers and records from the Gram Sabha to the Department of 
Land and Revenue which is circuitous in nature is obviously time- 
consuming. As regards the gairmazarua malik or khas land, the 
Sub-divisional Officer is competent to settle the same for residential 
purposes with eligible persons. 

Law, Land and Dalits in Bihar 113 

The government has made it clear that the definition of homestead 
under the BPPHT Act 1947 included within it the area of bari and 
sahan also. Accordingly, the government directed that in all cases 
where bari and sahan had been left out while making settlement 
of homestead, these should be re-opened suo moto by the Circle 
Officer, and that steps should be taken to record these, in addition 
to the house, and to distribute revised parchas co the privileged 
persons. It was also decided that no privileged person should have a 
homestead which is less than two decimals in area (Government of 
Bihar 1971b). In this regard the government issued directions that 
it che area of the homestead land for which parcha had been given 
to a privileged person is less than that, the following steps should be 
undertaken by the competent authorities: 

(a) Inclusion of the areas of bari and sahan in the parcha where 
such area had been left out; 

(b) If gairmazarua khas land or gairmazarua aam land is available 
immediately adjacent to the homestead for which parcha has 
already been given, to settle the requisite additional area with 
the privileged tenant; 

(c) Where neither gairmazarua khas nor gairmazarua aam land 
is available immediately adjacent to the homestead of the 
privileged tenant, necessary action to be taken to acquire the 
additional area; 

(d) The minimum area to be settled or acquired under (a), (b), or 
(c) above should be one decimal. Similarly, action under (b), 
(c) or (d) should be taken if even after action under (a) the 
privileged person still continues to have an area of less than 
two decimals for his homestead. 

Policy for Providing Homestead Land to Mahadalits” 

Recently the Government of Bihar has taken up a special initiative 
to provide right to homestead land to Mahadalit households. ‘The 
Mahadalit Commission set up by the Government of Bihar in 
2007 assigned to the Revenue and Land Reforms Department the 
responsibility to identify Mahadalit families without house-sites in 
the entire state and provide them access to homestead land. As part 
of this programme, a survey of 10,380 villages was conducted during 
a span of one and a half years. The survey focused on (a) identifying 

114 Sanjay Kumar 

Mahadalit families without house-sites and with house-sites of their 
own, and (b) identifying suitable land, government or private raiyati 
land, which could be allotted to Mahadalit families without house- 
sites. 

Out of 10,60,029 Mahadalit households surveyed, 1,78,896 
households (about 17 per cent) were found to be without house-sites 
of their own. The survey also found numerous cases among them 
who had not got parcha or parwana (legal entitlement document 
related to government and public land) for their homestead land 
despite entitlement as per law. Hence, a drive was concurrently 
launched by the government to bring to the surface such cases 
and grant settlement parchas, especially to Mahadalit households. 
Till September 2009, 20,022 parchas for raiyati land involving 
an area of 423.36 acres had been issued to Mahadalit households 
and 725.314 acres of gairmazarua malik land were settled with 

14,540 Mahadalit households (Ashokvardhan 2009) 

Securing ownership rights over homestead land: 
an interface with the laws and policies 

The discussion above clearly shows that there are laws and policies 
already existing in Bihar which provide for granting legal ownership 
rights over homestead land of various types to the rural landless as 
well as for acquisition/purchase and distribution of house-sites by the 
government to those without access to land. However, despite these 
provisions it is unfortunate that even by the most conservative estimate 
of the Census of India 2001, Bihar has the dubious distinction of 
having the highest magnitude of rural housing shortage (42.10 lakh) 
among the states. The practices and the processes which unfolded 
during the course of the efforts made by the action research for 
facilitating the process of realising the ownership right to homestead 
land for Dalit households provide meaningful answers as to why this 
huge gap exists between the law and the social reality. 

When the collaborative process of securing ownership of rights 
over homestead land was started initially by Deshkal Society and its 
partners, it was, first of all, confronted with the problem of having 
information about households that do not possess ownership rights 
over the homestead land on which they have been residing. This 
was a basic requirement for proceeding further. However, there was 

Law, Land and Dalits in Bihar 115 

complete lack of data and information on such households. ‘There 
were two options: to find out from the land and revenue records of 
the government at Block and district levels, or to conduct a primary 
household survey in the target villages. When the Circle Officers 
of the Blocks were approached, they denied having any records or 
data of households not having legal entitlement to their homestead 
land. After making rounds of the district offices including the 
Collector's othce we were told that in Gaya District as a whole only 
346 households did not have legal entitlement to their homestead 
land. This was the latest data available with the government for the 
period up to March 2006. 

It was hard to believe that Gaya district, which has a toral 
population of approximately thirty-five lakh people with a Dalit 
population of approximately ten lakh (Census of India, 2001), had 
only 346 households which did not have ownership rights over their 
homestead land. In our discussions with the then District Collector 
it came out that these 346 households were those which had actually 
submitted applications to the government for securing legal rights over 
their homestead land, and were awaiting action by the government 
in this regard. It was found that there was no mechanism to collect 
and compile data and records of the actual number of households 
which needed to be granted legal rights over homestead land as per 
the law. 

Faced with the lack of data, a primary survey of households was 
conducted in 361 villages spread over four Blocks. It was revealed 
that only 14.34 per cent of the marginalised community households 
(2,736 out of 19,081 households) had legal ownership right over the 
homestead land they were residing on (Figure 7.1). 

The survey further revealed that a majority of the marginalised 
community households (17,224 out of 19,081 households) had 
been residing for more than twenty years on the homestead land over 
which they still did not possess legal ownership rights (Figure 7.2). 
Many of these households were unable to specify the numbers of 
years as they had been residing on the land for many generations. 

After identifying the landless households that needed granting 
of homestead rights, Deshkal selected 2,500 households in a few 
villages for initiating the process for providing them legal entitlement 
to their homestead land. The first step was to submit applications 
in the Block office on behalf of those people who deserved legal

@ No response

© Not able to remember 

entitlement as per the legal provisions. The format of the application 
required various types of information regarding the land on which 
the applicant was residing, such as account number, plot number, 
rakba, chauhaddi (area and topography of the land), etc. According 
to the provisions, this information could be cited in the application 
only on the basis of two legal records. One was the khatian or legal 
document of land and the other was the village map. Generally, in 
Bihar, the responsibility of maintenance of both these records lies 
with the karmcharis (local revenue officials). Each karmchari is in 
charge of approximately thirty villages spread over two or three 
Panchayats. When the karmcharis were contacted for the above 
mentioned records most of them denied having these. When they 
were asked as to which ofhice/department could provide us with the 

Law, Land and Dalits in Bihar 117 

records, we were told to contact the land revenue department of the 
government. Accordingly, we put up requisitions for the records of 
villages in the district land revenue department. We were dismayed 
when the official in charge of the land records told us that he did not 
have the village maps for most of the villages. We were instructed 
to contact the government press for the records. Interestingly, the 
Bihar government has only one printing press which prints maps of 
villages and that press is in Patna (the capital of Bihar), Moreover, the 
government maps from this press are not meant for private circulation. 
This was, indeed, a difficult situation as without these records it was 
not possible to fill up the application forms tor homestead land. 

At this stage there were two options: (i) to confront the 
administration as it was their duty to make these records available, 
or (ii) to somehow persuade the administrative ofhcials for arranging 
for the village maps from the government press. If we followed 
the first option, it became clear that our focus would have shifted 
from implementation of legal entitlement to the availability of legal 
records. This could have put us off from our targeted goal. We, 
therefore, decided at this stage that it would be better to adopr the 
approach of persuading the concerned officials in the administration 
for arranging the records. Then followed the process of persuading 
ofhcials from the level of the karmcharis to the officer in charge at 
the district level. After much running around, the records were made 
available to us. But for accessing all these records it was required to 
bear the cost of the service rendered by the respective government 
branches in the Land and Revenue Department at the Collectorate 
office. 

After getting hold of the required records, we started the process 
of filing the application forms. First of all, the applications were 
submitted to the karmchari, the primary government official at the 
ground level. His primary responsibility was to verify the applications 
and recommend them to the Circle Inspector. Now the challenge 
before us was to get the applications verified by the karmcharis. At 
this stage the karmcharis introduced us to one more actor, amin (land 
measurement official), saying that without him the measurement of 
land occupied by a family could not be verified. At the Block level 
the official responsibility of measurement of land lies with the amin. 
In a Block there are normally one or two amins. Thus, one amin has 
the responsibility of 150 villages in a Block. When approached for 

118 Sanjay Kumar 

land measurement, it was very convenient for the amin to say that he 
did not have time as he had the responsibility for all the villages of 
the Block, not just for the villages where we were working. Neither 
the karmchari nor the amin had a fixed office. It was very difficult 
to contact them, more so to arrange a meeting between them. After 
repeated efforts, we were able to do it. In the process of verification 
of the applications, we came to know about the different types of 
land on which the landless labourers, particularly Dalit households, 
resided: raiyati, gair-mazarua aam, gair-mazarua khas, forest and 
bhoodan (a movement for land distribution among landless labourers 
through peaceful means) land being the main types. At this stage itself 
the karmchari rejected approximately 20 per cent of the applications 
related to the forest or bhoodan land saying that these lands were 
not under his jurisdiction. He further added that these people were 
residing illegally on those lands and could be evicted any time by 
the government. The applications, which the karmchari found to be 
correct in his verification, were divided into three categories on the 
basis of the types of land—raiyati, gairmazarua aam, and gairmazarua 
khas Accordingly, he sent applications related to the raiyati land 
for further verification to the Circle Inspector, which were further 
recommended to the Circle Officer. Thus, the various stages of the 
administrative machinery involved in the process of settlement of 
raiyati homestead land are Karmachari and Amin > Circle Inspector 
> Circle Officer. 

The applications relating to the gairmazarua aam or gairmazarua 
khas land required the recommendation of the Gram Sabha and the 
mukhia, the elected Panchayat head, of the respective Panchayats. 
This was possible only with the mukhia’s initiative. Now our task was 
to mobilise him. We found that it was easy to get the applications 
recommended bya Dalit mukhia because the applicants were primarily 
from Dalit communities. But this was not the case with the mukhias 
who were from the upper castes or the Other Backward Classes. The 
non-dalit mukhias created several obstacles in the process of getting 
recommendation from the Gram Sabhas. For instance, they said 
that in order to recommend an application there was the need for 
holding an aam sabha (general assembly of the village), which was 
not possible without a time-frame of two or three months. They also 
objected to the verification done by the amin and the karmchari. 
They said that it was important for them to get the consent of the 

Law, Land and Dalits in Bihar 119 

landowners so that these allotments do not create any social tension 
in the village. 

‘These experiences reflect the deep-rooted realities of a caste-divided 
society and the local power structures and relations. ‘This also shows 
how the local power structures influence the local state agencies and 
the administrative/legal processes, and put obstacles in the process 
of implementation of laws and rules meant for empowerment of 
the marginalised communities. Our immediate challenge was to 
negotiate with the non-dalit mukhias on the issue of verification of 
the applications. At the level of dialogues we did not have much 
success in persuading them to do the needful. Amidst all this, we came 
to know that if the application has been verihed by the karmchari, 
the Circle Ofhcer (CO) could exert pressure on the mukhia for the 
speedy verification and recommendation of the application. When 
we spoke to the CO, he said that he being an outsider could not 
order a local mukhia to recommend the applications. He said that at 
best he could make a request. As a result the applications remained 
pending with the mukhia for verification. 

In the primary phase itself, we had, thus, to negotiate and confront 
the officials at three levels for the verification of the applications. 
Meanwhile, there was a change in the Block administration. The 
Circle Officer was transferred and an Indian Administrative Service 
(IAS) probationer was posted in his place for training. But he 
could exercise all the powers given to a CO. Right at the start, this 
probationary officer created such an atmosphere that, let alone the 
common people, even the well-off could not dare to access him. In 
this regard, the earlier CO was better as we could at least speak to 
him. We were at the juncture where the role of the CO was crucial. 

Incidentally, we met the Commissioner in charge of that area and 
he took an active interest in our programme. He instructed the newly 
appointed IAS officer to dispose of all the pending applications within a 
month. This instruction had the desired effect. The LAS officer instructed 
his subordinates to dispose of the applications within a fixed duration, 
failing which he warned them of suspension. Further, he instructed the 
non-dalit mukhias to dispose of the submitted applications within a 
week, failing which he warned them of setting up an enquiry committee 
on the development projects given to them by the Blocks. We realised 
that the orders and instructions of the [AS officer had immense impact 
on the lower officials as well as the mukhias. The applications which 

120 Sanjay Kumar 

had been lying with the non-dalit mukhias for more than a month were 
disposed of within ten days. 

Now when there was a momentum, the Block did not have the 
required number of papers and proforma which were required to 
take the process further. It was not possible to get them printed from 
the district headquarters within a time limit, because it was outside 
the jurisdiction of the IAS officer. However the officer agreed that if 
Deshkal Society got the required papers and proformas printed, it 
would be possible to get the work completed within the prescribed 
time limit. Deshkal Society took this responsibility and provided the 
Block office with the required papers. Thereafter, the process which 
started at the Block level included preparing the proposal, filling 
up the two check-slips and the format of parcha and parwana and 
enclosing with it the recommendations of the mukhia, karmchari, 
and the Circle Inspector. 

The kind and volume of information required for filling up 
various check-slips and proformas involved a very complex and 
tedious process. First of all, in the proposal format, apart from the 
application, ten such items of information are asked for which are 
difficult to furnish. At the top of one of the check-slips is written 
‘Check-slip to accompany the proposal for settlement or alienation 
of government land’. This format has twelve headings and every 
heading requires 3-11 items of information. This check-slip has 
to be certified by the karmchari, Circle Inspector, Circle Officer, 
LRDC, SDO, Additional Collector and the Collector. The second 
check-slip relating to the land settlement has twenty-five headings. It 
requires the certification of the revenue karmchari, Circle Inspector 
and the Circle Officer. In the case of gairmazarua khas land, the 
processing route for applications is Mukhia > Karmchari > Circle 
Inspector > Circle Officer > Sub-Divisional Officer > LRDC > 
District Collector. In cases of gairmazarua aam land the application 
is further recommended by the District Collector to the Divisional 
Commissioner who recommends it for approval to the Land and 
Revenue Department of the Government of Bihar. Finally, Deshkal 
was able to file 2,519 applications which included 553 applications 
for raiyati land, 861 for gairmazarua khas land and 1,105 for 
gairmazarua aam land. 

In the complex legal process described above, the lower officials 
have to prove at every stage that the information passed on to the 

Law, Land and Dalits in Bihar 121 

upper ofhcials is correct. The senior bureaucrats at the top of the 
administrative machinery don’t have confidence in the officials at the 
ground level, who are directly involved in and are primarily responsible 
for the administration. The process of sending recommendations to 
higher ofhcials for their comments and approval together with the 
distrust they have for the lower ofhcials leads to a pervasive sense 
of fear among the latter of rebuke and punishment from the higher 
officials. This fear curtails the space for the lower officials to share 
the problems they face in their day-to-day work with their seniors. 
As a result the ofhcials at various levels avoid taking decisions and 
expediting the work. For example, although the laws and rules 
regarding settlement of raiyati homestead land is very simple and can 
easily be settled under the provisions of the Bihar Privileged Persons 
Homestead Tenancy Act at the level of the CO, the cumbersome 
procedures and lack of clear guidelines and directions are proving to 
be serious obstacles in their settlement. When a proposal is submitted 
to the CO, due to some reasons he does not want to take a decision 
and sends it to the SDO. The SDO likewise avoids taking a decision 
and sends it to the Additional District Collector and the District 
Collector who sends the file back with some queries. In this way, 
the proposal keeps moving to various officials without any concrete 
decision being taken. 

Identifying obstacles to implementation of law 

The discussion above of the administrative procedures and processes 
as well as the paper work required for acquiring the right to homestead 
land provides insights into the various obstacles that are responsible 
for denial of these rights to the rural landless. Considering the 
cumbersome and complex processes, and the tedious paper work 
involved, it was not surprising to find that only 14 per cent of the 
surveyed households had received either parcha or parwana (legal 
documents of ownership) for their homestead land. Almost 89 per 
cent of the households had not even thought of trying to obtain 
either. Many of the households (38 per cent) were not even aware of 
the existence of the Bihar Privileged Persons Homestead Tenancy Act 
or other rules and regulations for regularisation of their homesteads 
on gairmazarua khas and gairmazarua aam lands. The major reasons 
as perceived by the landless poor themselves for not being able to 

122 Sanjay Kumar 

successfully acquire parcha and parwana were lack of money, lack 
of time (as the processes are time-consuming and landless wage 
labourers are not able to spare much time from their daily grind of 
work) and the administrative hurdles (Figure 7.3). 

B Administrative hurdles 
O Lack of money 
@® Lack of time 

Ficure 7.3 Reasons for not having legal entitlement to homestead land 

While some of the administrative hurdles can be located in the 
structure and the mechanisms of the government administration 
itself, others seem to be a result of sheer neglect of the rights of the 
poor by the officials. There has been no attempt by the government 
in Bihar (except the recent initiative with regard to Mahadalits 
mentioned above) to collect data and information about the number 
of households that do not have legal ownership right (parcha/parwana) 
of their homestead land. Land records have not been updated since 
long. Finding the required land records and cadastral maps of villages 
is a monumental task. The government departments simply do 
not care to keep these records updated. Even old records are very 
difficult to find. Obviously, for a poor landless labour household it is 
a monumental task to arrange for these records if they want to avail 
themselves of the benefits of the law. Copies of government circulars 
and policy guidelines are not available in the Block, Sub-division 
or District offices, including even the District Collectorate. Due to 
this lack, and ignorance about them, different officials are prone 
to interpret the rules and laws differently. The government has not 
prepared any official manual which can provide guidelines to officials 
at various levels for taking appropriate action for implementation of 
the laws, rules and policies regarding homestead land. The officials, 

Law, Land and Dalits in Bihar 123 

therefore, do not know what they ought to do when somebody 
submits an application for grant of ownership right. Besides, since 
land revenue is no longer an important source of revenue for the 
government, there is no training of ofhcers these days to familiarise 
them with the system of land revenue administration, laws and 
rules. As a result when ofhcers are confronted with issues such as 
legal ownership of homestead land, they do not know how to take 
appropriate action to implement the relevant laws and rules. 

The revenue administration, especially at the lower levels, seems 
to have been also the victim of the worst effect of the government 
drive to reduce the size of its administrative structure. The Bihar 
government has recently taken the step to merge the offices of the 
Block Development Officer (BDO) and the Circle Officer (CO). 
Therefore, though technically there is a CO for every revenue 
circle, in substance they do not exist (Government of Bihar 2008). 
The reason is very simple. The functions of the BDO and CO 
are quite dissimilar. The BDO is overburdened with numerous 
activities relating to various Centrally-sponsored rural development 
programmes. Each BDO has to disburse, supervise and keep account 
of crores of rupees, and land revenue now being a minuscule part of 
revenue earning, he has neither the time nor the interest to look after 
the functions of the revenue department. Thus, there is a vacuum at 
the Block level of a senior revenue officer. The supervisory structure 
at the Block/revenue circle level is also quite tenuous. Below the 
BDO/CO there is only one Circle Inspector (CI) for a revenue circle 
consisting of roughly 150 villages. Obviously a single CI cannot 
properly supervise such a large area . Even if he does, its quality can 
be anybody's guess. 

The most unfortunate aspect of the revenue administration in 
Bihar is that the government has stopped fresh recruitment of the 
lowest ground-level revenue functionaries, the karmcharis. As a 
result, a large number of such posts is lying vacant, and the existing 
karmcharis have to cover numerous villages and bear a heavy burden 
of work. Moreover they have no fixed office. One can't approach 
them at any fixed place, at any fixed hour on any working day. If 
someone wants to submit an application for settlement of his 
homestead land, it becomes very difficult to locate and approach 
the karmchari for measuring and preparing the map of the land and 
verify other records. 

124 Sanjay Kumar 

Thus, the complex and cumbersome administrative procedures 
along with tedious paperwork involved in the process for acquiring 
legal entitlement to homestead land together with lack of interest and 
commitment among government officials for implementing the laws, 
rules and regulations that guarantee rights to the poor has resulted 
in a large number of eligible landless households being denied their 
right to homestead land. 

Conclusion 

The paper provides key insights into how the present institutional 
and administrative structures and processes are working as obstacles 
in the process of implementation of the laws and rules, and are 
undermining the reach of law to those it is meant to benefit. The 
obstacles that are revealed and analysed indicate the ways in which 
the laws are contested, negotiated and subverted at every stage of 
the process. The cumbersome and complex processes and procedures 
involve tedious paper work and these make it more difficult for the 
illiterate rural landless population and the marginalised to negotiate 
the processes to make a claim and avail themselves of its benefits. It 
reflects that there exists a big gap between the laws on the one hand 
and social reality on the other. Consequently this gap defeats the 
purpose of a well-meaning law which resolves to ensure the basic 
rights to homestead land of the landless labourers and marginalised 

households.
