SECTION ‘B 

Dalits and Literature 

8 

Dalit Literature: 
A Perspective from Below 

Ray KUMAR 

The objective of this paper is to discuss the historical context of the 
emergence of a new literary genre called ‘Dalit literature’ which 
reflects the growing identity, awareness and consciousness of the 
Dalits during the colonial period and post-Independence days. An 
attempt will be made to analyse the nature of literary representation 
of Dalit problems and the emerging consciousness in the writings 
of select Dalit scholars. In view of the pluralities that exist in India, 
especially due to different languages and culture, it will be interesting 
to compare and contrast the sensibilities articulated in the Dalit 
literature of various Indian states. Finally, an attempt will be made to 
evaluate its literary values and to see whether it can be part of world 
literature. 

While Indian society has, for centuries, been the most hierarchical 
among the known civilisations, the literature of this country, until 
very recently, has never focused on the problems of the ‘untouchables’, 
one of the groups located at the bottom of the hierarchy. The pen 
has, by and large, been in the hands of those who wielded power, 
while those outside the grid of authority and agency have generally 
been rendered invisible, even unmentionable, in the so-called great 
literary texts. At the beginning of the twentieth century, a few 
upper-caste Hindu writers who attempted to portray the lives of the 
untouchables tended to be driven either by a zeal for social reform 
or by sentimental compassion. The works of these writers can be 
termed as ‘emotionalistic’ literature.’ Rarely did a writer take up an 

130 Raj Kumar 

untouchable character and treat him realistically, like an ordinary 
human being full of vitality and hope as well as despair. 

Thus, the untouchables of India have continued to remain 
neglected and ostracised in literature as in Hindu society. The ancient 
Dharma Sastra of the Hindus imposed a series of social, political, 
economic and religious restrictions on the lower castes, making 
the untouchables completely dependent on those above them. As a 
result, the Panchamas (the untouchables, as denoting the fifth varna) 
long lived a life full of physical degradation, insults and personal and 
social humiliation. They were relegated to menial occupations, lived 
outside the village and fed on the leftovers of the high-caste people. 
Physical contact with the untouchables was said to be ‘polluting’, 
and worse still, even their shadows were considered ‘defiling’. Even 
as late as the early part of the twentieth century, the untouchables 
had no access to public facilities such as wells, rivers, roads, schools 
and markets, etc.’ The most heinous rule was that which compelled 
the untouchables to tie an earthen pot around their neck so that 
their sputum did not fall to the earth and pollute others; another 
demeaning practice was the compulsion to tie a broom behind them 
so that their footprints would be erased before others set their eyes on 
them.* None of these issues were taken into account in the literature 
produced by upper-caste Hindu writers. 

For a long time, the low castes did not have any formal education 
which would stimulate and inspire them for a genuine literary 
movement to protest against the monopoly of the established 
literature. It is only in the post-Independence era that some educated 
‘untouchables’, who tasted the fruit of modern education, realised 
the need for an alternative mode of thinking and launched a new 
literary movement. It is no surprise that the movement started in the 
homeland of B. R. Ambedkar, who fought for the rights, liberties 
and equalities of the downtrodden throughout his life. The awakened 
Dalit youths of Maharastra organised themselves in 1972 under the 
banner of Dalit Panthers.* The main objective of this movement was to 
create a counter-culture and to bestow separate identity for the Dalits 
in society. The call given by the Panthers for social reconstruction 
was further articulated by Dalit writers, poets and activists through 
their writings and speeches. Thus emerged ‘Dalit literature’ in the 
early seventies, which subsequently spread to the neighbouring states 

of Gujarat, Karnataka, Andhra Pradesh and then others. 

Dalit Lirerature 131 

The word ‘Dalit’ is found in several Indian languages, and as a 
Marathi word it is found in Molesworth’s Marathi-English dictionary 
of 1975, a reprint of the 1813 edition. It gives the meaning as 
ground, broken or reduced to pieces generally’. It derives from a 
Sanskrit word meaning ‘crushed’ and is understood in all the Indian 
languages that are derived trom Sanskrit. It has been said that Sanskrit 
has borrowed the root word ‘dal’ from Hebrew.’ ‘Dal’ in Hebrew 
may be used in two senses: ‘It may reter either to physical weakness 
or to a lowly, insignificant position in society. When ‘dal’ is used in 
combination with another Hebrew root-word ‘anti’, it describes an 
economic relationship. As clearly indicated by Harvey L. Perkins: 

Dal is derived from a verbal root which recognises that poverty is a 
process of being emptied, becoming unequal, being impoverished, dried 
up, made thin.... so there is social frailty (and those suftering from it) are 
easily crushed and have not the means to recover.” 

Thus, the Dalits are people who are broken, crushed and torn apart 
so much so that they are unable to rise and better themselves. 

‘Dalit’ is now widely used in place of the word ‘untouchable’. 
Over the years, there have been several terms used to describe the 
people of the erstwhile untouchable community, such as ‘Ati-Shudra’, 
‘Scheduled Castes’, ‘exterior castes’, ‘outcastes’, ‘depressed classes’, 
‘Scheduled Castes’, ‘ex-untouchables’ and so on. The Dalits feel that 
these terms coined by the upper-caste Hindus, officials, and social 
reformers were abusive in nature and synonymous with derogation, 
domination and paternalism. That is why the people belonging to 
this category prefer to describe themselves as Dalits, which includes 
all aspects of oppression. Eleanor Zelliot writes: 

In the early 1970s two Maharastrian movements achieved enough 
prominence to be noticed by the English language press, the Dalic 
Panthers and Dalit literature. By substituting the world ‘Black’ for ‘Dalic 
the reader can immediately understand that a phenomenon comparable 
to the American Black Panthers and Black literature has surfaced among 
the lower caste in social and literary affairs in Western India. Like 
the American movemenss, the Dalit Panthers and the Dalit school of 
literature represent a new level of pride, militancy and sophisticated 
creativity. The Marathi word ‘Dalit’, like the word Black, was chosen by 
the group itself... Dalit [refers to] those who have been broken, ground 
down by those above them in a deliberate and active way. There is in the 
word itself an inherent denial of pollution, Karma, and justified caste 

hierarchy.” 

132 Raj Kumar 

Over the ages, the connotation and interpretation of the term ‘Dalit’ 
have been changing. For example, Namdeo Dhasal, the noted Marathi 
poet and one of the leaders of the Dalit Panthers movement, infused 
the term with a leftist vision to include not just the Scheduled Castes 
but also the economically oppressed classes comprising the Scheduled 
Tribes, Neo-Buddhists, landless labourers, and others. Marxists like 
Dhasal would define Dalit in terms of class, generally including 
women, tribals, workers in industries and agricultural workers. For 
Gangadhar Pantawane, another Dalit activist from Maharastra who 
is also the founder editor of Asmitadarsha (Mirror of Identity), the 
chief organ of Dalit literature, the term ‘Dalit’ does not refer to a 
caste, but is a symbol of change and revolution: ‘the Dalit believes 
in humanism. He rejects the existence of God, rebirth, soul, sacred 
books that teach discrimination, fate and heaven because these have 
made him a slave. He represents the exploited men...’.". These are 
only a few instances of how the concept ‘Dalit’ has been interpreted 
by some Dalit scholars. Whatever may be the definition, the term 
refers to the conditions of a group of people who are subjected to 
all forms of oppression—social, political, economic, cultural and 
religious. 

Since the 1970s, an increasing number of poets and writers of 
the Dalit communities in various Indian states have been producing 
poems, short stories, novels, dramas and autobiographies representing 
the themes of caste oppression, untouchability, poverty, repression 
and revolution. Their writings contain powerful denunciations of 
and fierce attacks on the caste system and on Brahmanical Hinduism. 
Dalit literature may be considered to be a unique genre of modern 
Indian literature, for now the erstwhile untouchables themselves, 
using the traditionally denied weapon of literacy, are exposing the 
conditions under which they have lived, as well as directly rebelling 
against the Hindu institution which has caused their perpetual 
subordination to the varna order.'' This is the main thrust of Dalit 
literature. 

Dalit literature has arisen from cultural conflict. Since the 
‘downtrodden’ has no place or hardly any place in the canonical 
literature of India, Dalit writers call it ‘Hindu literature’ and challenge 
its hegemony. In the words of Bahurao Bagul, ‘the established 
literature of India is Hindu literature. But it is Dalit literature which 
has the revolutionary power to accept new science and technology 

Dalit Literature 133 

and bring about a total transformation. “Dalit” is the name of a total 
revolution; it is revolution incarnate’.’” 

Thus, the principal philosophy of Dalit literature is co bring ‘roral 
revolution’ in society. Hindu society believes in the varnashrama 
system with its concepts such as the age-old granthas, pods and 
priesthood, which bring slavery. Dalit literature denies this Hindu 
mentality and afhrms humanitarian freedom. Tarachand Khandekar, 
a Dalit writer, puts it thus: 

Man is the centrifugal force in the philosophy of Dalit literarure. Man 
is supreme. He is above all Gods, sacred books and science. It is man 
who can make and unmake anything. Dalit literature believes chat 
nothing is permanent. Everything is subjected to decay. With every 
decay there is resurgence, new creation. It, therefore, does not accept the 
maxim “Satyam, Shivam, Sundaram.’ On the other hand it proclaims 
that nothing is true which is not applicable for man’s sublime freedom, 
nothing is good if it is not useful for man’s welfare, nothing is beautiful 
which is not useful for beautification of mankind.” 

The argument forwarded by Khandekar in the above passage outlines 
the very idea of making a literature. One of the basic purposes of 
literature is to bring about changes in society. If that is so, then the 
role of literature has to be reviewed in terms of the values it forms. In 
other words, the social responsibility of a writer cannot be dismissed 
even though there is another way of arguing that literature can be 
produced without any purpose. 

The debate as to who can be called a Dalit writer continues to 
exercise literary criticism. Is a Dalit writer one who belongs to the 
Dalit community or any one who writes about the life of Dalits? The 
caste Hindu writers on Dalits have been strongly criticised by Dalit 
writers for superficiality of feeling, experiences and interests vis-a-vis 
Dalits. Even the most progressive and revolutionary minded writers 
like Premchand, Mulk Raj Anand, T. S. Pillai, V. S. Khandekar and 
several others who have tried to describe the sentiments of Dalits 
are not acceptable; only a Dalit by birth can have the sensitivity and 
experience to be a genuine Dalit writer. Their writings. described by 
Tarachand Khandekar as ‘letters of their own blood’, ’ are a natural 
outburst of the feelings and thoughts which have been blocked up for 
centuries. But what are the chief qualities that make Dalit writings 
unique and fresh? To answer this question, D. N. Nagraj has this 

to say: 

134 Raj Kumar 

Usually anger, pity, and melancholy are the dominant feelings in the 
literature on Dalits written by non-dalits. Many a time even the value 
system of the Dalit world is interpreted wrongly. In creative writings such 
baby sitting for other groups and classes is very awkward.” 

Unlike the non-dalits, Dalit writers attempt to be true to their 
lived experiences and feel that their visions and responses must be 
translated into art honestly, in their raw un-distilled form, without 
euphemism." Their vehicle is often brutal, coarse and the crude 
language of the slums, springing from a life of poverty, ignorance 
and violence. The ‘jaggedness of word, the granulated structure, the 
rough hewn expression, the scarcely muted anger’ —anger which may 
spit fire like wrath, burn lambently like satire, scorch like cynicism, 
kindle like anguish and enflame like tragedy’, as one Dalit writer puts 
it.’ All these allotropic forms are found in the works of Dalit writers, 
be it poetry or prose, fiction or autobiography, drama or essay. There 
is even an attempt on their part to evolve a new aesthetics because 
they feel that the genteel expectations of the existing elite literary 
standards cannot do justice to the quality of the life they know and 
render in writing. Polanki Ram Moorthy theorises this phenomenon 
thus: 

Dalit literature is enriched and beautified by the life-force only. Sincere 
revolutions and forceful representation go to make it so. Established 
value has been discarded in Dalit projections as despicably unjustifiable, 
inclined or too narrow in themselves. These creations embrace humanity 
and life fully well. That is why, the so-called style, form, construction that 
is the outward decorum find insignificant attention in Dalit creation. 
Contrarily, it lays an added emphasis on the intensity in experience and 
feeling or vital emotion, the very touchstone in creation. Such a form 
gains quite [sic] naturalness and fragrance of its own kind. So it grows 
and enthuses and injects a new spirit of values to bring about social 
transformation. 

Sharankumar Limbale, the Dalit writer-activist, defines Dalit 
aesthetics as: 

The literature of the exploited is primarily concerned with the search 
for freedom, and giving expression to it. All aspects and dimensions of 
freedom are seen in it. We should remember that the imaginary or idea 
of freedom has an aesthetic aspect, as much as it has political, economic, 
social and moral facets. The sentiment of freedom is present in Dalit 
literature not only as its life essence, but also as beauty. The three values 
of life—equality, freedom and solidarity—can be regarded as constituting 

Dalit Literature 135 

the essence of beauty in Dalit literature. The aesthetics of Dalit literature 
rests on: first, the artist’s social commitment; second, the lite-athrming 
values present in the artistic creation; and third, the ability to raise the 
reader's consciousness of fundamental values like equality, freedom, 
justice and fraternity.” 

Interestingly, many contributors to Dalit literature are poets. ‘The 
process of poetry arising out of a distressed cry can be felt in most 
Dalit poems because in these utterances, protest seems to come from 
the insulted and the injured who have laboured for generations for 
their livelihood. One can take any Dalit poem and feel the rhythm 
of distress arising out of the eternal pain. As one Oriya Dalit poet 
writes: 

They are rage now [sic] 

They are awakened 

Awakened from their mass slumber 
They will burn down the establishment 
For their bread and butter 

They have already given a call 

And are ready to shed their blood 
With red tears.” 

The voice grows more bitter and defiant when the poet confronts the 
enemies: 

Who are those 

Who took away the service-sword from Dalit 
And imprisoned him for eternity? 

The self-respecting Dalit 

Will no more bear it. 

But, break the chains and sickles 

And smash the prison-house 

To be liberated from all red-tapisms 

Around him and the world!” 

The poet makes an effort to use images as well as words which come 
from his own experience. His protest is not against any individual 
or group but the society as a whole. Nay, he even goes further and 
declares war against all the oppressive forces of the world to ‘smash 
the prison-house, to be liberated from all red-tapisms around him 
and the world’. 

In this connection, one remembers the exploitation of the Blacks 

of Africa and America by the Whites. To assuage their guilt and to 

136 Raj Kumar 

veil their brutal exploitative actions the dominant group perpetuated 
a number of negative stereotypes of Blacks during slavery. The long- 
suffering Christ-like Uncle Tom, the child-like Sambo, the loyal 
Mammy, the emasculating matriarch and the wild seductress were 
some such stereotyped images which have endured till today and 
inflicted immense damage on the Black people’s psyche.” Pained 
by the torturous existence of their ancestors and enraged by the 
denigrating stereotypes, many Blacks considered slavery as a dismal 
past best forgotten. Perhaps the most humiliating myth was that of 
the acquiescent slave which has been demolished by the numerous 
slave narratives that recount the valiant resistance of the narrators to 
the institution of slavery and their perilous journey to freedom . In a 
poem titled ‘Africa's Plea’, one Black poet writes: 

Iam not you—But you will not give me a chance, 
Will not let me be me. 

‘If I were you — 

But you know I am not you 

Let me be me. 

You meddle, interfere in my affairs 

As they were yours 

And you were me. 

You are unfair, unwise, foolish to think 
That I can be you, 

Talk, act and think like you. 

God made me, 

He made you, 

For God's sake let me be me.” 

Like the Blacks in Africa, those in America had to undergo a 
humiliating experience under White oppression. But it is no mystery 
that out of the traumatic experience Black Americans came to sing: 

Sometimes I feel like a motherless child: 
Sometimes | feel like a motherless child— 
A |-o-n-g w-a-y-y-y from house.” 

In the same vein, one Dalit poet writes: 

This country which demands a pot of blood 
For a swallow of water 

How can J call it mine 

Though it gives the world 

The (empty) advice of peace?”° 

Dalit Literature 137 

A large portion of Dalit literature is in autobiographical form. 
Whether it is poetry or novels, theatrical dialogues or critical prose 
pieces—all these forms often bear a ‘confessional characteristic 
made by the authors themselves. The ‘contession’ is mostly made 
to invoke the bitter experience the authors have gone through in 
their lives. It is in this sense that they invoke a pain that is mostly 
personal, and yet they narrate their stories with a realisation that 
the other members of their community also sutter in the same way. 
This process is unique in the history of personal narratives which 
is generally absent in non-dalit autobiographies, observes Sudhir 
Chandra, a Dalit writer: 

It is dithcult not to react to this (autobiographical) literature at a personal 
level. There is something elemental about it. It touches you to the very 
core.... The best of Dalit writers have the guts to strip themselves before 
us, but they do not want to interiorise [sic] their suffering by making a 
public spectacle of it. Nor do they wish to earn cheap martyrdom by 
retailing everything they had to suffer for no fault of theirs. If anything, 
they are extraordinarily restrained and ruthlessly introspective. We can 
at least have the modesty to confess that they often drive us to tears and 
oftener make us feel guilty without feeling that the confession entails a 
failure on our part as social analysts and intellectuals.” 

Like Dalit autobiographies, all other forms of Dalit writings are 
different and distinct compared to non-dalit writings. On the one 
hand there is denunciation of the given order dictated by the caste 
society, on the other hand there is strong assertion to claim their 
rights and liberties so that they live with dignity and self-respect.” 
Thus, Dalit literature aims at creating a counter-culture and a 
separate identity for the Dalits in society. Generally, Dalit writers are 
not against any group but against the establishment, the government 
and the social system, which, in their view, keep them depressed and 
deprived. In other words, the search for identity is a basic dynamism 
of Dalit culture. That is why issues related to poverty, powerlessness, 
untouchability, hypocrisy and several other social practices have 
generated a variety of responses from Dalit writers that are forms of 
protest aimed at bringing about social change through a revolution. 
This has been vehemently opposed by the establishment in our 
country. What is more unfortunate is that some critics of Dalit 
literature do not accept it as ‘creative’ art, and term it as ‘reactive 
and ‘propagandistic’. Dalit writers refute these charges by arguing 

138 Raj Kumar 

that all literature is propagandist because all writers follow a certain 
philosophy while writing: 
An author is first and foremost a social being. Few realise that he is 
capable of viewing and examining an event from different aspects. When 
Dalit writers look at the scorching social reality and respond to it in 
an aggressive, bitter manner, their writing is condemned as loud and 
propagandistic. 

In other words, when a writer is oriented towards thinking in terms 
of social change, he is termed propagandist. Those who brand him thus 
themselves follow a certain philosophy by which they could be termed 
propagandists. All literature for that matter is propagandistic, the 
difference being that in some cases one insists on the so-called literary 
values and in others, on values of life.” 

Dalit writers feel that mere literary interpretation or an academic 
point of view cannot enable one to comprehend the true spirit of 
Dalit literature. They argue that their literature has a theoretical as well 
as a sociological base which is hardly understood by its upper-caste 
critics. Therefore, Dalit writers suggest that the critics should have a 
proper orientation to properly understand its various perspectives. In 
this regard, Arjun Dangle writes: 

It is significant that Dalit literature owes its origin to a revolutionary 
struggle for social and economic change. This explains the various aspects 
of serious thought in Dalit literature. The literature is closely associated 
with the hopes for freedom of a group of people who as untouchables 
are victims of social, economic and cultural inequality. Their literature 
is thus characterized by a feeling of rebellion against the establishment, 
of negativism and scientificity. Studying Dalit literature or the role of 
this literature from only a literary or an academic point of view fails to 
present a complete perspective in assessing it. Dalit literature must be 
assessed in the sociological framework. This overall perspective has been 
conspicuously absent in the review of Dalit literature so far.” 

Perhaps the dominant distinction between Dalit literature and 
mainstream literature is whether to reject ‘tradition’ or to accept it 
with or without reforms. While Dalit intellectuals firmly oppose the 
age-old Indian tradition based on the caste system with its religious 
heirarchy, non-dalits maintain that tradition cannot be rejected and 
only what is not required should be thrown out. Dalit writers realise 
that the word ‘tradition’ is deceptive and has been used as a ‘shield’ 
throughout Indian history by a class of people for their vested interest. 
lest the Dalits demand their due. On this point, Dangle argues: 

Dalit Literature 139 

A tradition is born and lives on the strong foundations of thoughts and 
principles and it is these thoughts or principles which enrich or sustain 
a tradition. The base that a tradition gets is subject to the then existing 
social system and the sum coral of the conditions. The established class 
always tries to establish a convenient tradition that does not damage its 
vested interests. The weak groups in society are tied to this tradition. In 
fact, all our traditions so tar, whether religious, social, literary or cultural, 
have been imposed on the majority by a handful." 

Generally, Dalic literature is engaged in carrying out ovo main 
functions: ‘demolition’ and ‘reconstruction..” On the one hand, it 
is keen to destroy what is considered as ‘deadwood’, the decaying 
components of the existing social and cultural order; on the other, 
it is anxious to transform the social reality in the direction of total 
freedom, equality and human dignity. These rwo processes are 
complex and hence, Dalit literature, like every Dalit individual, is 
struggling to achieve its goal, writes R. G. Jadhav, a Dalit writer. He 
explains: 

The actual world is filled with dreadful, terrible, humiliating events. 

Dalit writers cannot escape being tied physically and mentally to this 

world. Dalit writers are doing the difficult task of portraying this life, 

through personal experience and empathy, absorbing it from all sides 

in their sensibility. To live this life is painful enough; it can be equally 

painful to recreate it on the mental level. Dalit writers are deeply involved 

in this process. It is not easy for them to extricate themselves from it and 

write. The creation of literature has its own laws. Higher literary creation 

is possible on the basis of these laws. Those who have the right to say that 

‘our humanity is our burden’ can easily master these rules of literary art. 

The gift of art can never be as heavy as the burden of humanity.” 

Of late, the volume of Dalit writings has been increasing annually. 
Besides Maharastra, Karnataka and Andhra Pradesh, voices of 
protest can be heard from states such as Orissa, Punjab and West 
Bengal. Dalit outpourings have taken all known forms of literature. 
Many Dalit writers are writing about gender-related issues, which 
had been neglected earlier. In fact, a good number of Dalit women 
writers are writing about their personal experiences mostly critiquing 
the patriarchal social order that exists within their communities and 
outside. Thus, a paradigmatic shift is already visible with many Dalit 
writers raising their voices against different forms of oppression 
related to class, gender, ethnicity, language, religion, etc. other than 
caste. It is heartening that more and more Dalit writings are now 

140 Raj Kumar 

being translated into English, published by some of the reputed 
publishers. In the meantime, Dalit studies have been introduced in 
several universities in India and abroad. Thus, the output of Dalit 
liveracure is growing. Hopefully, in the coming days it will grow 
further and establish itself as a significant part of world literature.

Transcending Orbits of 

Dalit Women’s Minor Literature 

SMITA PATIL 

Dalit studies paid scant attention to the question of Dalit women. 
This is understandable as where a whole community is depressed 
its primary focus and concern tends to be on the community as a 
whole rather than on one of its segments even if it is more depressed 
than the rest of the community. However, in the case of the Dalits, 
the women’s issue is imiportant for two reasons. First, many of the 
expressions of marginalisation of the Dalits are manifested through 
their women. Second, Dalit women have over the years evolved their 
own understanding and critique of women’s situation that should 
constitute a part of Dalit studies howsoever it is understood. 

This paper attempts to examine the issue of Dalit women’s 
consciousness by magnifying the body of literature produced by 
Dalit women and men. The main thrust is on four autobiographical 
writings in the Marathi language, Balut by Daya Pawar (1978), 
Kolhatyacha Por by Kishor Kale (1999), Jeena Aamucha by Baby 
Kamble (1990), and Antasphot by Kumud Pawade (1995) to see 
the patriarchal hegemony within and outside the community in the 
specific context of Dalit women in particular and Dalits in general. 
Our discourse is not extended to other literary expressions because 
an autobiographical writing can engage us with the life stories of 
Dalit women in an authentic fashion. Thus, our aim, through this 
exercise, is to foreground how Dalit women are theorising their 
epistemological/ontological locations within their community. 

Dalit Women’s Minor Literature 143 

Political Topography of Dalit Lite ‘ary 
Consciousness in Maharashtra 

While studying gender—caste consciousness in Dalit literary forms, 
we have to go back to the thirteenth century Bhakti tradition of 
saints (which was individualistic) and the nineteenth century Mahar 
movement in Maharashtra.’ During that period, a number of saints 
such as Jnanesvar, Tukaram, Eknath and Chokhamela raised their 
voices in devotion to God. Chokhamela was an untouchable. He 
narrates his social suffering and reflects upon the ethos of that period. 
For example, in one of his Abhangas, he writes: 

O God, my caste is low; how can I serve you? 
Everyone.tells me to go away: how can I see you? 
When I touch anyone, they take offences. 
Chokhamela wants Your mercy.” 

Abhanga 76 

Sharing his experiences of untouchability, he deconstructs the notion 
of purity and pollution in another verse: 

The only impurity is in the five elements. 
There is only one substance in the world. 
Then who is pure and who is impure? 
The cause of pollution is the creation of body. 
In the beginning, at the end, there is nothing but pollution. 
No on knows anyone who was born pure. 
Chokha says, in wonder, who is pure?’ 
Abhanga 11 

Thus, in the end, a number of saints, which includes women such 
as Janabai and Nirmala, brought out a number of Adbhangas to 
denounce their stigmatised location that is produced through the 
ideology of caste, class and gender.’ In addition to that, thanks to the 
nineteenth-century work of the Satyashodhaks (truth-seekers) which 
also had an impact in the Ambedkarian era, activities such as Jalsa 
(traditional musical concerts) and TZamasha (traditional folk plays), 
Lawani (ballads) and Pawada (panegyric poetry) continued as part 
of the Dalit cultural movement. However, in a Deluze-Guattarian 
sense, those genres played the role of minor literature in the Dalit 
community. Deluze and Guattari theorised minor literature as a 
sensible and sound discourse which does not conform to dominant 
models. They contend that ‘man’ is a majoritarian term; thus ‘woman 

144 Smita Patil 

becomes minoritarian. Therefore, one can argue that the writings 
of Dalit women become minor literature in the Deluze-Guattarian 
context; but writings of Dalit males become dominant within the 
discourse of that minor literature. Thus, the question arises whether 
the majoritarian/minoritarian binary can equip us to flee from the 
ideology of caste. 

Eleanor Zelliot argues that Dalit literature was never regarded as 
reputed literature because it is a creation of low castes.” Nevertheless 
it gave detailed descriptions about society, and the exploitation of 
lower castes by landlords, and especially explored untouchabality as 
well as to some extent the oppression of Dalit women by the larger 
society. Moreover, we do not have any discursive sources about the 
major writings of Dalit women of the nineteenth and twentieth 
centuries. 

The Dalit Panther movement in the 1970s was a milestone in 
the history of Dalits in India. It represented the fundamental socio- 
economic anxieties of the Dalits in Maharashtra by challenging the 
patriarchal state agency. It raised its voice on the industrial growth in 
urban spaces and particularly exposed rural caste chauvinism in that 
state. The Panthers castigated Dalit and non-dalit leaders/parties for 
their irresponsible approaches to the atrocities committed on Dalits 
and the alienation of Scheduled Castes and Tribes from education 
provided by the Central and State Governments even twenty-three 
years after Independence.’ Palshikar argues that Dalit Panther was 
formed to challenge and change the political and cultural discourse 
of Dalits in Maharashtra 

While commenting on the Dalit literature of the 1970s onwards, 
Zelliot placed ‘Dalit Sahitya, as a school, a self-conscious movement. 
In the context of the Dalit Panther movement, Gail Omvedt argues 
that it began with the spirit of the Black Panthers in the USA.'" Black 
literature debunked dehumanisation based on racism, and the Dalit 
Panthers absorbed energy from it. Lata Murugkar argued that Dalit 
literature evolved through its engagement with two different schools 
of literature.’ Most of the activists in the Dalit Panther movement 
were influenced by the Marxist ideological leanings of Russian and 
Chinese literature. However, one can argue that the Panther movement 
appeared because most of its activists belonged to the lower caste/ 
class stratum and had been sharing a similar existential angst based 
on antagonism. Dalit literature emerged out of the womb of anger, 

Dalit Women’s Minor Literature 145 

pain and inequality in society. Arjun Dangle, a Dalit writer, who had 
been associated with this movement, writes that ‘it claims for right 
to live an honest and honoured life’. From its foundation many 
other marginal communities developed new branches of a dissident 
literature with identities such as rural, folk, Muslim, tribal or Gondi 
(one of the Scheduled Tribes in Maharashtra).'’ Thus, it significantly 
reflected on the inequality among human beings and its medium was 
used to convey the divide between the larger society and the subjugated 
community. These genres mediated the complexities of multiple-social 
spaces in a Bourdieuian fashion.’ The multiple and unequal social 
spaces attained recognition through such diverse texts. On the other 
hand, in my understanding, it was a path-breaking, rebellious literature 
which remarkably became a thought-provoking source of inspiration 
for Dalit women’s writings that was rarely found in the time of Bhakti 
and Mahar movements. In the writing also it shows us the triple 
exploitation of Dalit women, based on caste, class and gender. 

It certainly draws our attention to the demarcation between Dalit 
and non-dalit women as the former began to introspect critically 
on why and how they are ‘different’. The stress on the difference in 
the representation of stigmatised social bodies such as Dalit women 
became a distinct trope in the narratives by them. 

A Pioneer Dalit feminist of the 
Nineteenth Century: Mukta Salve 

The study of feminist consciousness in Dalit writing requires a critical 
return to the nineteenth century. The Dalit literary movement has 
given shape to a cultural revolution as a part of the Maharashtrian 
culture. However, specifying it as Dalit women’s writing with feminist 
consciousness, perhaps Muktabai is the first person to provide us 
with a legitimate evidence in her brief essay, ‘Mang Maharachya 
Dukhavisatha’ (About the grief of the Mang and Mahar) that 
explores the plight of Dalit women as they delivered their children in 
houses that did not have a roof. She made critical points for Dalits 
in general and Dalit women in particular. For instance, she explains 
how Brahmans confiscated the lands of the poor Mang and Mahar 
people where they built their own mansions. The delineation of the 
diabolic practices of Brahmins operates as a critique of the Hindu 
religion. Simultaneously, pointing at the behaviour of the regime 

146 Smita Patil 

of Bajirao Peshwa, she describes how the Mang and Mahar were 
tortured and treated worse than dogs and cattle. Ifa Mang or Mahar 
crossed the talimkhana (gymnasium), his body would be cut into 
two as punishment. Subsequentially, those bodies were used to play 
cricket with (the head serving as a ball and a sword as a bat). No one 
had the courage to come and stop the killings. If at all the Mang 
and Mahar managed to save their lives Bajirao used to say, ‘How 
do untouchables dare save their own lives’? He further sardonically 
remarked, ‘Are these untouchables expecting the Brahmins to take 
over “their jobs and duties?” Thus, Salve highlighted several crucial 
questions. She also created a space for philosophical queries, e.g., 
what sort of Dharma/religion is ours that perpetuates the closures 
of caste? Simultaneously, she critically evaluates how Brahmans 
manipulated religion and poor people to control the knowledge and 
power relations in the society. 

Contemporary Dalit literary discourse suffers from a lack of such 
penetrating re-readings of the ideology of caste. The absence of critical 
space arises from the paucity of active movements or organisations 
like the Satyashodhaks that undermine the ideology of the Hindu 

religion. 

Feminist shadows in writings of Dalit males 

Dalit autobiographies evolved as dynamic expressions of the social 
worlds of Dalits and their co-relation with the dominant class in 
society. They deploy the shifting connotations of rural spaces and 
urban milieus. They reflect upon the life of Dalit women and also 
position them as free from the shackles of a patriarchal culture. As 
Kancha Ilaiah argues, Dalit women are democratised due to their 
communitarian background and do not have the gendered lack of 
other caste women. But, mere shifting from the private to the public 
domain does not imply that they are liberated from the matrix of 
patriarchy. However, these questions need to be addressed in an 
open-ended manner rather than focus on the essentialism of their 
identities. Is a Dalit woman really more liberated than other women? 
Or is it the legitimate caste-Hindu structural society which forces 
Dalit women to be in an oppressive system and suppresses their 
voices? Or did poverty and the question of livelihood push them to 
remain silent in the constructs of patriarchy? 

Dalit Women’s Minor Literature 147 

In order to unravel these complexities, we must investigate the 

held of autobiographical writing that structures the subjectivity of 
Dalit women. 

‘Kolhatyacha Por’ 

In Kolhatyacha Por, Kishor Kale’s life moves around the social 
experience of an illegitimate *Ko/haci’ child. Kolhati is a Dalit 
community in western Maharashtra. This is a story of a Dalit male 
that narrates his struggle to become a doctor and how he goes 
through trauma and humiliation. The Kolhati women perform the 
folk dance called Zamasha to entertain the upper-caste landlords, 
and the narrator's mother was one among them. The community 
trained their daughters and sisters to adopt the profession of dance 
because they are the breadwinners of the family. The male members 
in the family never undergo any hardship related to labour and enjoy 
the money earned through sisters and daughters. Usually, a wealthy 
patron who often visits a girl provides all the necessities of life in 
exchange for the sexual favours of these dancers. The virginity of 
the girl is highly prized in this act of patriarchy. A virgin would be 
given to the richest man with pre-fixed price in a mock-marriage 
ceremony called ‘Chira Utarana’. Gradually, a man can come and 
visit her house as long as he pays generously all the male members of 
her family. Once she becomes pregnant the patron leaves her alone. 
In those circumstances, she again goes to the stage to perform the 
dance. The life of a dancing girl before and after pregnancy, and even 
after delivering a baby, remains the same. The members of the family 
welcome the birth of a baby girl as she provides the security of their 
bread and butter in the future. The child would remain illegitimate 
and no one takes the responsibility of the child. In the meantime, 
the Kolhati male members of the family always feel threatened 
because their sisters or daughters might run away with a lover. 
Nonetheless such types of cases rarely happen, as the lover usually 
will not accept responsibility of the dancer's illegitimate child. These 
descriptions themselves relate how the manipulation of sexuality of 
Dalit women happens in the Dalit and non-dalit society. ‘The agency 
of Kolhati women becomes part of the cyclical deprivation due to 
their stigmatised labour. The minimal capital they acquire through 
their performances do not get converted to the autonomy of their 

agency. 

148 Smita Patil 

However, it is pertinent to look at the sub-text of this autobiography 
to analyse how the economical transaction of property is controlled 
through male members in the family. The account about the /ii, 
aunt of Kale, helps us to understand the nuances of the patriarchal 
domination within such Dalit families. She (Jiji) was the owner of 
both the house and land of the family. She was always servile to her 
brother. Because she believed, as Kale narrates to us, that since she 
had no children of her own her only option was to serve her brother; 
so that when she is old he would look after her. When she did grow 
old, and was paralysed from the waist down, the brother whom she 
had served faithfully, left her in a hut in the agrarian field. He used 
to send someone there to feed her once a day. When Kale tried to 
take her to Bombay for an operation, the entire family stopped him 
because they were afraid that he would make her transfer the land in 
his name. In this way, Jiji was neglected and left to die. Kale keeps 
insisting on her to move out from that home. Nevertheless, Jiji refused 
to listen to him and wished to remain with her brother. The reason 
behind this was that she could die on her own land and her dear 
brother would perform her last rites to ensure that her spirit finds a 
proper resting place. In this way Jiji's story shows us how tradition 
binds these women and they themselves get trapped in the masculine 
identity of community. The traditional repression binds them in 
slavery so completely as they believe strongly in blood reinforcement, 
which is a construct of patriarchy. However, Kale dedicated this 
writing to three persons, his mother Shantabai, Lonikar and Nagin 
maushi [aunt], who had taken care of him in his conflict-ridden life. 
He considers Shantabai to be an unsuccessful mother but positions 
her as a successful wife. Thus, formulating a patriarchal vision, Kale 
dedicates his book to ‘my mother who was not an ideal mother but 
lived a life as an ideal wife’. One can say that the Dalit male also 
expects, like any other non-dalit, that a woman should be an ideal 
mother and wife, without challenging the society and the position of 
Dalit women in it. Thus, this text re-fashions patriarchal rule through 
its manipulated linguistic as well as epistemological twists. 

*‘Balut’ 

Daya Pawar, in Balut [a reference to the fifty-two rights given to 
Dalits in the traditional systems, involving distribution of caste- 
based occupation, such as carrying corpses, and carcasses, and so on], 

Dalit Women’s Minor Literature 149 

conveys and exposes us to an extremely diverse textualisation of 
Dalit experiences. It also reflects on the sexuality of Dalits and non- 
dalits. Pawar illustrates the narratives of urban and rural life. His 
father, Dada, was alcoholic and never took any responsibility of the 
household. The moment he gets his salary, he will not come to the 
house for around fifteen to twenty days. So the mother has to go out 
and collect waste paper for the survival of their family. Dada used 
to work in the house of a Brahman landlord as a bonded labourer 
for a year. Dalit women as well as women from other subordinated 
castes also used to work with him. Dada used to hide the scythe in 
order to exploit those women. When women would inquire about it, 
Dada asked them to come and sleep with him. Thus, Dada sexually 
exploited many women. Morality in the village was quite distinctive. 
Some of the young Mahars worked as bonded labour for a one-year 
agreement (they had to contact the upper-caste women regarding 
their work) in rich caste Hindus’ houses. During that time some 
Maratha women for their sexual fulfilment utilised these young 
Mahars. However, when it came to provide the food and water, these 
same Maratha women would put out the water and food maintaining 
a distance, Mahars being untouchables. The same logic was applied in 
the context of Dalit women by the caste Hindu male. When it came 
to marriage the upper-caste male never went for it as it was against 
their prestige and status. Daya Pawar lucidly provides us with his own 
classic character of patriarchy—how he left his first wife, Sai. His 
suspicious male nature had a feeling that his wife had a relationship 
with a Muslim boy called Mahboob, and spied on her. Once Sai had 
gone to market, Daya followed her. On her way back, Mahboob met 
her and they started talking to each other. Daya was angry and asked 
Sai to go home. Though Mahboob gives an explanation that for him 
Sai was like a sister, Daya gave an ultimatum to him to leave Mumbai 
immediately. Later on, Daya left Sai at her village. However when 
she came back to stay with him, Daya did not accept her. He sent 
her to the village again, ignored her for a long time and finally left 
her forever. Over a period, Sai goes in for remarriage with an elderly 
person and continued to work as a labourer throughout her life; that 
man left her his small daughter. 

In the other episode, once Daya’ relative took him to the 
prostitutes area and introduced his Maushi (mother’s sister) whose 
name was Jamuna. She was extremely good looking and it was the 

150 Smita Patil 

only reason why her husband, suspicious by nature, used to beat her 
up, ill-treated her and finally one day sold her. In her old age, Jamuna 
Maushi became a beggar. These lines indicate how the Dalit males 
control their wives with a form of suppression and show the power 
relation between husband and wife. 

Daya Pawar shares a number of common experiences on caste 
discrimination at school with other marginal communities. Students 
in the hostel belonging to the Koli caste not only maintained a 
distance but also did not mingle with untouchable students. Muslim 
and Dalit communities reside as neighbours in towns and cities 
such as Mumbai. Though both these communities to some extent 
share a common marginalisation, as untouchables the sexuality of 
Dalit women was always under threat. Thus, Daya writes that such 
situations led to communal conflict between Mahars and Muslims. 
Sometimes Dalits retaliated against Muslims. The Muslim community 
also maintained the concept of untouchability. Commensality was 
absent among these two communities. Muslims maintained a caste- 
based bias towards Dalits even in the midst of their marginalisation 
and ghetto-isation by the Hindu religion. 

Engaging with Dalit feminist energy: 
‘Jeena Aamucha’ 

Baby Kamble’s autobiography, Jeena Aamucha {or ‘Our Ways of 
Living’], is a sociological and psychological exploration in which she 
describes the penury, hunger, and false consciousness of the Dalit 
community. She explored the diversity of life in a Dalit community. 
Dalits are portrayed as honest and simple by nature and they stay 
in the maharwada. Nevertheless, they sustain the deepest pain of 
poverty, without sacrificing humanity. 

Superstitions are described as the common trends within these 
communities, and how they perpetuate amongs them in the name 
of various deities, gods and goddesses. When Baby Kamble was 
about one and a half years old, she fell sick. Her face was looking 
pale. Everyone felt that Baby was dead and started thinking about 
the burial process. Her mother was crying loudly saying that every 
child of hers was buried in the evening. Her grandfather began to 
read verses from ‘Pandav Pratap’. Suddenly Baby moved her eyelids. 
People gathered there realised that she was alive. The first portion 

Dalit Women's Minor Literature 151 

of the writing tells us abour the religious superstitions in the Dalit 
community, where parents accept life as it comes but hand over their 
eldest son under Porraj and Vaghya, a sort of custom in which the 
eldest son should be surrendered to a divine agency. 

The second section of the book reflects how Ambedkar created 
an impact on the minds of Dalits that brought significant changes 
in their standard of living, and they began educating themselves 
and dropping their traditional jobs; they also abandoned their gods 
and goddesses. Thus it shows us that the major shift in the Dalit 
community happened owing to the adoption of the perspectives 
of Ambedkar. Many Mahars admitted their daughter in school 
after listening to an inspiring speech by him. One of the incidents 
narrated by Baby Kamble is that in a girl's school most of the students 
belonged to the upper caste. Earlier they had an image of a Mahar 
girl as untidy, unclean, and so on. The teachers gave a space for Dalit 
girls in the classroom on the last bench and the blackboard was 
invisible to them. In school, the high-caste Hindu girls did not allow 
the lower caste to drink water. Baby and her group of Mahar friends 
rushed for a drinking water area and opened a tap. Then the other 
caste girls were worried about where to drink water. They spoke an 
unusual language and then a debate started, criticising Ambedkar 
and Gandhi, as one belonged to the Mahar caste and the other to 
an upper caste. Hence, one can argue that the mode of resistance 
is entangled in the intricate realms of the discourse of the right to 
education and freedom from the graded inequality/caste. 

Baby Kamble narrates an incident from the 1940s to show how 
a daughter is treated in a Dalit family. The young bride was often 
as young as eight or nine years old and treated in a harsh manner. 
The domestic labour is imposed on her and if she failed to do it 
she was beaten up and abused. Thus she becomes almost a slave. 
Once she reached the stage of puberty, it became more problematic 
and unbearable. In such a situation girls often ran back to their 
parental house alone at night or day. But again the husband and his 
family members brought her back as she belonged to that family. 
The girl’s brothers, father and family members also sent her back to 
her husband’s house. Then, the in-laws and others would not spare 
her. The punishment for her was that the village carpenter would be 
asked to make a stock for her, the log weighing several kilograms, 
with a hole hewn in it. The bride's right foot was inserted through it 

152 Smita Patil 

and then it was blocked with a bolt to prevent her from extracting 
her foot. Every time she moved, the wood chafed her ankle and it 
would bleed. The carpenter did not belong to the Mahar community 
but was ready to touch the untouchable woman and the other society 
members also admitted this punishment for Dalit girls. If at all the 
girl was able to escape again then a similar process was repeated. She 
would be thrown out of the husband’s family but before that all the 
members of her husband's family would point to her as ‘mudi’ or cut- 
nosed one. Then she will never go to her own relatives either. Thus 
the women are converted into a symbol of family purity. 

‘Antasphot’ 

Focusing on Antasphot, an autobiography written by a Dalit woman 
named Kumud Pawade, one can clearly understand the picture of 
Dalit women’s experience in the urban areas. It shows us again the 
positive shift in Dalit communities. The first true story in it, ‘How 
I stopped Savitri (pooja) fasting’, describes the experience of the 
author's relative about that ritual. The husband of that woman used 
to torture her on the ground of having no son and married again. 
He would also demand money. In her natal house her parents died 
and she was compelled to take shelter in the author’s house. Every 
year she would undergo a fast on the occasion of Savitri Pooja. Once 
Pawade questions her, “Why do you fast for such type of husband’? 
Her relative replied, ‘At least God will listen to me.’ So the writer 
asked her to undergo rational and logical introspection. She herself 
had done the introspection and abandoned fasting. She insisted that 
her relative should follow suit. 

Another incident in her autobiography under the caption ‘After all 
we are women’ expresses the Dalit women’s assertion of their rights 
and change in their perception of their own community. This part 
also describes the manner in which the men of their own community 
harass Dalit women. She narrates the incident in which thirty-five 
Dalit women, who are intent on participating in a women's conference 
in Delhi, were harassed by the railway authority through denial 
of tickets, in spite of the fact that the person manning the ticket- 
counter himself was Dalit. Thus, she shows the patriarchy of the 
upper castes as well as Dalits. She convinced herself that Ambedkar 
had converted to Buddhism because it taught rationality, tolerance, 
patience and humanity. Though there is so much of stress within the 

Dalit Women’s Minor Literature 153 

Dalit community on Buddha's teaching, the author is sceptical about 
its actual practice among them. 

Pawade further looks at some recent changes among Dalit women 
while appreciating the increasing participation of women in various 
modern occupations. She suggested that Dalit women needed to 
develop their intellectual faculties to develop a critique of patriarchy. 
She contends that Dalit women are doubly oppressed compared to 
upper-caste women. In her view, Dalit women have to undergo both 
caste-based and patriarchal oppression and therefore both materially 
and non-materially she is more oppressed in comparison to upper- 
caste women. 

Thus, these autobiographies challenge the academic enclosures 
of dominant feminist scholarship. As Pierre Bourdieu argues, 
academic enclosures are the discourses that provide a distinct 
identity to the scholars by detaching them from the questions raised 
by the mainstream society. ° Those become products of the enclosed 
environments that provide space to a minority which possesses 
different forms of cultural and social capital. Therefore one can argue 
that the writings of Dalit women secure space outside the academic 
enclosures of the dominant feminist tradition of India. 

Towards post-Dalit foundationalism 

Dalit discourse projects autobiographies of Dalits as the authentic 
register of their life experiences. These writings are also heralded 
as the alternative texts to the dominant intellectual tradition that 
promotes value neutrality to disguise its ethno-centricism. Therefore 
the question arises whether Dalit discourse can exist without the 
patronage of the nation state and empire (in the Hardt and Negrian 
sense’). In other words, the proliferating sovereignties in the national 
_ as well as global spheres are challenging the ethical core of Dalit 
morality. How far can it challenge the symbolic violence of Indian 
male-centred/feminist academic enclosures? Do we have a Dalit 
praxis that transcends the academic labyrinths? Will intersections of 
the academic stream and non-NGO- ised activism produce any effect 
on the Dalit environs? What will be the endless displacements of 
‘dalit affects’ [in the Thriftian sense, Nigel Thrift having argued that 
‘affect’ is a form of thinking, or thought in action, a different kind of 
intelligence about the world]?

Premchand and Dalit Literature: 
Representation of Dalits in 
Z ] 
the Literature of Premchand 

SHASHI BHUSHAN UPADHYAY 

Introduction 

In recent years, particularly after the development of Dalit literature 
in Hindi, Premchand and his literature have become a field of battle 
between writers not necessarily on politically opposite sides. On the 
one hand, there are writers who consider that Premchand was not 
only sympathetic to the cause of the Dalits but also that he gave to 
the Dalits a realist portrayal and a voice through his literature. They 
aver that Premchand’s writings may be considered as part of Dalit 
literature. On the other hand, the Hindi Dalit writers react strongly 
to any such contention. They say that Premchand, like other high- 
caste writers, was not capable of realistically portraying the Dalits 
and their woes. In fact, they say, Premchand misrepresents them, 
and in certain cases like in “Kafan’, he has vilified the Dalits. The 
attack by some of the Hindi Dalit writers is so severe that they even 
refuse to consider Premchand as sympathetic to the Dalit cause.” It is 
in this context that this essay analyses those writings by Premchand 
that are concerned with the Dalits. These include both his fictional 
and journalistic works. 

Dalits in Hindi Literature: A Brief Survey 

The representation of Dalits in Hindi prose literature in the nineteenth 
century was rare and it mostly took the form of a contestation between 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 157 

the ideologies of the traditional Hindu religion (Sanatan Dharma) 
and of the reform movements, particularly the Arya Samaj. While 
novelists like Kishorilal Goswami and Lajjaram Mehta were ranged 
on the side of tradition, some others like Rudradatt Sharma, Shyam 
Kishore Verma and Ramijidas Vaishya were on the side of reform. In 
either case, however, the Dalits appeared rarely in their writings and 
never in their own right. It was only during the 1920s and 1930s that 
novels and stories were composed with Dalits as central characters. 
Premchand was among the first to portray the Dalits as independent 
and recognisable characters, both as part of the poor and with 
their own particular problems. He also wrote extensively in various 
magazines and journals about social and political issues concerning 
the Dalits. From the 1930s onwards, we find many of Premchand’s 
contemporaries and younger writers taking up the Dalit issues and 
representing their problems in literary and journalistic writings. 
Pandey Bechan Sharma ‘“Ugra (in Budhua ki Beti), Suryakant 
Tripathi ‘Nirala (in Kudli Bhat and Chaturi Chamar), Yashpal (in 
Divya and Amita), Vrindavanlal Verma, and Acharya Chatursen were 
some of the Hindi writers in whose literature we find substantial 
representation of the Dalits.” 

Premchand was one of the rare writers in Hindi who moved 
into the language from Urdu and also kept writing in Urdu till the 
end. He was one of those who almost always took up social and 
political issues as central themes in his novels, stories and plays. He 
was sensitive to the political and social movements of his time and 
considered literature as a potent medium for carrying, critiquing and 
analysing the prevalent ideas. 

Premchand’s Life and Work: An Overview 

Premchand was born in 1880 in Lamahi village near Banaras in 
present-day Uttar Pradesh (UP) in a Kayastha family of modest 
means.’ He was the younger of the two surviving children of his 
parents and was named Dhanpatrai. His father was a clerk in the 
postal department and his mother was a housewife who died early in 
his childhood. The father remarried but the cantankerous stepmother 
was no substitute for the caring mother. The child sought refuge 
from the loneliness of life in romantic literature. 

158 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

Premchand himself was married, at the insistence of his stepmother, 
when he was fifteen and in the 9th class, to a woman whom he and his 
father considered unsuitable. One of his biographers described her as 
an ‘older woman, dark, ugly, fat and pock-marked who was addicted 
to opium and walked with a limp’ and about whom Premchand was 
reported to have said: ‘When I saw her face I had the creeps.”” His 
father complained to his wife through whose contacts the marriage 
was arranged: ‘Your father has pushed my son into a well! Such an 
unspeakable wife for my handsome son! I have decided that he must 
marry again.” Premchand did marry again, but some ten years later 
and this time a widow. But his father was not around to witness his 
second marriage as he had died one-and-a-half years after his first 
marriage. 

The death of his father burdened Premchand with the responsibility 
of providing for his family and he had to take up a job in a small 
missionary school at Chunar in UP. He soon left this job and took 
another one in a government school at Bahraich. It was a transferable 
job and Premchand was shifted from one place to another. He also 
had to sort out the frequent conflicts and quarrels between his wife 
and stepmother. 

It was not easy to wiite under these circumstances. But writing 
was both an internal urge and a mission for Premchand. He was 
concerned about social evils and the enslaved conditions of his 
country. His first novel Asrar-i-Maabid (The Secrets of the Sanctum 
Sanctorum), published under the name of ‘Dhanpatrai a/ias Nawabrai 
Allahabadi’, was serialised in an Urdu weekly of Banaras, Awaz-e- 
Khalq. \t exposed the corruption and moral degradation in Hindu 
religious places. His next novel, which was published in Urdu under 
the title of Hum Khurma-o-Hum Sawab (Together in Body and Soul) 
in 1906 and in Hindi as Prema in 1907, dealt with widow remarriage. 
During 1907-08, he wrote five short stories in Urdu with a strong 
patriotic message which were collected in book form under the title 
of Soz-e-Vatan (The Dirge of the Nation) in 1908. It so rattled the 
colonial bureaucracy that the District Collector threatened him with 
penal action and ordered him to withdraw all the copies of the book 
and get them burnt. He further warned him not to publish anything 
without submitting it to the authorities concerned. To circumvent 
this official regulation, Dhanpatrai now changed his pen-name from 
Nawabrai to Premchand. From now onwards he mostly wrote under 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 159 

this name until his death in 1936 and it was under this name that he 
became immortal in both Hindi and Urdu literature. 

Premchand was a bilingual writer. In his early literary career he 
wrote and published primarily in Urdu; from 1914-15 he started 
publishing primarily in Hindi. But till 1924 he wrote his drafts in 
Urdu and then changed them into Hindi for publication. He almost 
invariably tried to publish his novels and stories in Urdu also. One 
of his last and most famous stories, ‘Kafan’ (The Shroud), was first 
published in Urdu in 1935 before appearing in Hindi in 1936. 
Premchand thus represented one of the truly syncretic personalities 
at a time when language was an issue of bitter contest in UP” 

Premchand emerged as the most important novelist and story- 
writer in Hindi-Urdu streams of literature. His corpus of twelve 
novels and about 300 short stories was created over a period of three 
decades. It was a crucial period in the history of India, both in terms 
of the liberation struggle against British rule and in the making of the 
nation. Premchand with his pan-Indian concerns was deeply involved 
in both these ventures. Although not an activist, he articulated these 
concerns through his writings. He portrayed the lives and thoughts 
of various sections of the Indian people. Since the form of his writing 
is simple, and authorial interventions acute and comprehensive, it 
becomes easy to extract his views even from his literary creations. 
Even more important is the wide sweep of characters and situations 
in his writings, which fairly coincided with the development of 
political and social struggles in the colonial period. His literary 
and journalistic writings neatly capture the political and social 
atmosphere of his time. And, despite the ideological colourings he 
gave to his characters, his novels and stories represent the realities of 
the situation. 

The term Dalit (literally, ‘ground down’, ‘oppressed’) has been 
generally used for those groups in Indian society in general, and 
among the Hindus in particular, whose mere touch is considered 
to be polluting. This term was probably used in the 1930s as a 
Hindi and Marathi translation of ‘depressed classes’. Premchand 
himself uses the term variously as Dalits, Shudras, and Achhoot 
(untouchable), apart from mentioning them by particular caste 
names. In Premchand’s writings, the two main ‘untouchable’ castes— 
the Chamar and the Bhangi—of eastern UP, where his characters are 
located, find substantial representation. Besides these, some smaller 

160 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

caste groups like the Gonds, Khatiks, Dhobis, and Pasis, whose status 
at the bottom of the social ladder remains a little uncertain, are also 
portrayed by him. 

We find Dalit characters in five of his novels and about fifteen of his 
short stories. Premashram (The Abode of Love, 1922), Rangabhoomi 
(The Stage, 1925), Kayakalp (Metamorphosis, 1926), Karmabhoomi 
(The Arena, 1932) and Godan (The Gift of a Cow, 1936) are the 
novels; and ‘Mooth’ (The Spell, 1922), ‘Saubhagya ke Kode’ (Whips 
of Fortune, 1924), ‘Mukti Marg’ (Way to Salvation, 1924), ‘Aga- 
Pichha’ (Dilemma, 1928), ‘Mandir (The House of God, 1927), 
‘Ghaswali’ (The Grass-cutter, 1929), ‘Lanchhan’ (Stigma, 1931), 
‘Sadgati’ (Deliverance, 1931), “Thakur ka Kuan’ (The Thakur’s Well, 
1932), ‘Gulli Danda’ (1929), ‘Doodh ka Daam’ (The Price of Milk, 
1934), ‘Mantra (1926), ‘Jurmana (The Fine, n.d.), ‘Meri Pehli 
Rachna’ (My First Composition, n.d.) and ‘Kafan’ 1936) are the 
stories. There are other instances where the castes of the characters 
are not given but they can be said to belong to the Dalit sections of 
the society. 

Here we are taking those works where the Dalit castes are specifically 
mentioned as well as those with the nearest approximation. These 
novels and stories were published during the 1920s and 1930s, when 
he had started publishing in Hindi.” 

Considering the massive literary output of Premchand, the 
representation of Dalits as a social group with specific problems of 
their own is rather limited. The specific socio-religious problems 
of the Dalits are taken up only in two novels (more prominently 
in Karmabhoomi and partially in Godan) and only in four stories 
(‘Mandir’, “Thakur ka Kuan’, ‘Doodh ka Daam’, and ‘Sadgati’). The 
rest of the above-mentioned stories and novels deal with the Dalits 
as part of the poor, and their particular social problems are touched 
on only to the extent it is necessary for the flow of the narrative. 
Sometimes, as in Rangabhoomi, this social reality is almost completely 
ignored. In his journalistic writings, however, he paid more attention 
to these social issues. The accounts of Dalits with their own social 
problems were written in the late 1920s and the 1930s when the 
Dalit question had acquired national prominence in the wake of the 
Ambedkar-led movement. 

The present essay endeavours to reconstruct the material 
conditions, social existence and beliefs of one important section of 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 161 

Indian society—the Dalits—on the basis of Premchand’s writings. 
It also attempts to discern the perspective through which the writer 
viewed the realities of their existence. And, finally, it tries to view 
Premchand’s achievements in the context of Dalit literature. 

Dalits and Their Families 

All the Dalits, except two, in Premchand’s oeuvre are menial workers 
and belong to the lowest strata of population. Most of them lead 
a rural life. The men are marginal farmers, agricultural workers, 
tanners, shoe-makers, grass-cutters, ploughmen, grooms or syces. The 
women are mostly agricultural workers and grass-cutters and sellers; 
they also engage in making cow-dung cakes, feeding the bullocks 
and doing other odd jobs for the landlords; some also act as mid- 
wives, sweepers and scavengers. It is imperative for them to take up 
a variety of menial jobs to survive. The urban Dalits follow different 
professions according to their castes. The Chamars are shoe-makers, 
grooms, syces and grass-cutters; the Dhobis wash clothes; Kunjras and 
Khatiks are vegetable-vendors; Ghosis are milk-sellers, and Bhangis 
are sweepers and scavengers. But their particular professions are no 
longer a caste privilege in the urban context and, except in case of 
Bhangis, all other professions are open to different castes thereby 
giving rise to competition and insecurity. 

The Dalits in Premchand’s works are generally not literate. In one 
case (‘Aga-Pichha’), however, the Dalit character, fighting against all 
odds, completes his MA and is appointed a lecturer in a college. 
In another instance (‘Saubhagya ke Kode’), the Dalit child survives 
somehow to become a learned musician. But, apart from these, no 
other Dalit in the texts is educated or even literate. In Karmabhoomi, 
however, the non-dalit hero makes attempts to impart education 
to the Dalit villagers and succeeds in generating interest not only 
among the children and young men but also among the older people. 
But none of them is ever seen participating in any activity involving 
literacy. 

Not much information about the size of the Dalit families is 
available in the texts. It is because Premchand is basically interested 
in elucidating the problems of poverty and social discrimination and 
focuses his entire attention on these. A detailed account of other 
aspects of Dalits’ lives is, therefore, not available. This also points 

162 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

to a limitation in his art where a predominant concern with ‘issues 
takes precedence over providing a relatively broader picture. In 
Karmabhoomi, we come to know of two Dalit families closely— 
Saloni’s and Gudar’s. Saloni had a son who went away with a monk, 
while Gudar had three sons, one of them now dead. In ‘Mandir, 
the family consists of Sukhiya and her only surviving son, Jiyawan, 
Sukhiya’s husband having died long ago while two of her other children 
have died within the previous year, and by the end, in a dramatic 
development, both of them perish. In the novel Rangabhoomi, 
Surdas and his nephew, Mithua are the two surviving members of the 
family. In ‘Kafan’, Ghisu recalls that nine children were born to him 
out of whom we find only one surviving. In ‘Mooth’, the ojha (magic 
healer, or sorcerer) and his mother are the only two figures visible 
in the family. In ‘Ghaswali’, Muliya, her husband and his mother 
are the three persons seen in the family. In ‘Doodh ka Daam’, the 
parents of Mangal die in his childhood leaving him behind as a waif 
to survive on others’ leftovers. In Godan, the Dalit family consists of 
Silia, her parents, and her two brothers. In “Thakur ka Kuan’, Gangi 
and her husband Jokhu are the only ones in the family. 

The women, by virtue of their hard work both inside and outside 
the home, are shown to hold a relatively stronger position vis-a-vis the 
menfolk than their counterparts in upper-caste households. The men 
rely upon them not only for household work but also to augment 
the family income and for doing many outside jobs. Thus, we see 
that in ‘Ghaswali’, Muliya cuts grass and sells it in the market to add 
to the income of the family. Sukhiya (in ‘Mandir’) too cuts grass to 
support herself and her son. In Godan, Silia works in the fields and 
earns for herself and is not dependent on either her family or even 
on her Brahman lover, Matadin. Bhoongi (‘Doodh ka Daam’) earns 
the same as, if not more than, her husband, by doing midwifery or 
sweeping and scavenging. 

This equal, if not greater, dependence of the Dalit families on the 
work and earnings of their womenfolk seems to create a significant 
space for the latter’s authority within the family in contrast to upper- 
caste women whose authority, if any, is generally relegated. This 
enables the Dalit women to maintain social parity with their menfolk. 
They can speak to, fight against, shout at and abuse the men on equal 
terms. Bhoongi wins the bet against her husband Gudar that he will 
shave off his moustache if a son is born to the landlord, and tells 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 163 

him jokingly but addressing him in the hard, familiar, ‘you’: ‘You 
can shave it off right now, you old cheat. | said it was going to be a 
son but instead of listening you just went on talking. I'll shave it off 
myself and won't leave even a bristle’. '' 

Bhoongi is referring to the moustache, that essential symbol of 
masculinity in rural India. It is a non-antagonistic relationship, 
yet one in which the gender equality is asserted. She then leaves to 
perform her midwife role leaving her own child behind for Gudar 
to tend. In Godan, Silias mother, along with her father and two 
brothers, takes equal part in fighting against the family of Matadin, 
the Brahman who is seducing her daughter. The equality of speech in 
the cases of Dalit women vis-a-vis their men is universal; they address 
their menfolk with the familiar ‘you’. This is in sharp contrast to the 
upper-caste women who always address their elders and husbands 
respectfully. 

This equality does not appear forcibly achieved as in the case of 
upper-caste women, but as a natural given, as part of the Dalits’ 
existence. This contrast is vividly brought out in an earlier story 
‘Shikar’ (“The Hunt’) in the course of a dialogue between the lady 
of a rich household Vasudha, and her maidservant Munia. (Here the 
caste of the maidservant is not given, and her identity is mainly as 
a worker and earner.) Vasudha’s husband often goes out for games 
and stays out for long periods. She feels lonely and depressed. Munia 
sympathises with her and says that she herself would never allow this 
kind of liberty to her husband. When Vasudha says that Munias 
husband will object to such restrictions, Munia reacts angrily: “The 
day he says such things, I’ll pluck out his moustache. If he’s not my 
slave, I’m also not his servant. If he’s my slave, then I’m his servant’. 
She further asserts her position vis-a-vis her husband by saying: 

If men work outside, we also work inside the house; isn’t that work? From 
the outside job one at least gets freedom in the evening, but the inside 
work remains the same even in the evenings. If the man says that he'll go 
around roaming while I sit at home, then I can‘ tolerate this.” 

This sense of equality is also evident in the common group dance 
in the village portrayed in Karmabhoomi where men and women 
participate freely. 

However, this supposed equality and freedom enjoyed by lower- 
caste women cannot be taken for granted and has been a matter of 

164 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

debate in recent years. Although the women go out to work, there 
is a clear demarcation between the male and female spheres. Karin 
Kapadia has pointed out that among agricultural workers in Tamil 
Nadu there exists a clear sexual division of labour whereby sowing 
and ploughing are male jobs while carrying and weeding are female 
ones. This derives from the patriarchal cultural formation where ‘the 
“sowing of seeds” both in sexual intercourse and agriculture was seen 
as a quintessentially male activity. Digging or the breaking of earth 
was a similar activity identified with the invasive male, while the 
female was identified with the dormant but nurturing earth.””” This 
is a widely recognised phenomenon, and a study of the medieval 
Bengali Mangal Kavyas finds this segregation of gender roles even in 
the pre-colonial period.'’ Even Kancha Ilaiah, who otherwise asserts 
a marked difference between the Dalit and upper-caste women, 
talks about separate male and female domains of work among the 
Dalits.’” 

It is not only a question of divided domains of work but of the 
external work itself. Rosalind O'Hanlon points out that during the 
nineteenth and twentieth centuries there has been an increasing 
tendency among the middle castes like the Marathas, Patidars and Jats 
to enforce stricter patriarchal control over their women by secluding 
them and refusing to marry widows. These patriarchal practices 
slowly gained wider acceptance and were noticed and condemned 
by one of the first feminist writers, Tarabai Shinde, from a Maratha 
family.'° O’Hanlon states, on the basis of the figures from the census 
of 1881, that ‘if the numbers of widowed women relative to men 
are any guide, middle and lower caste practice by the 1880s did 
not actually differ fundamentally from that of the [high castes]’.’ 
Allowing for the possibility of some errors in the census figures and 
a bit of overstatement by the author herself, it can still be said that 
the lower castes were not free from the patriarchal structures which 
plagued the upper-caste households. A recent survey of seven Dalit 
castes of Nagpur concludes that the Dalit families are patriarchal, 
and even earning Dalit women hold a secondary position in the 
family and cannot take independent decisions."* 

It, therefore, appears that although Dalit women enjoyed relatively 
more freedom than their upper-caste counterparts, Premchand 
is idealistic in portraying remarkable equality between the sexes in 
the Dalit families. He seems more concerned about depicting the 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 165 

poverty, discrimination and oppression faced by the Dalits than 
taking a closer look at the contradictions within the Dalit families. 
His relatively little knowledge about them may also have been 
responsible for this. 

The Dalits in Premchand’s literature are extremely poor and it 
is with great difthculty that they can make both ends meet. When 
Amarkant (in Karmabhoom) enters the hut of Saloni, he finds the 
presence of ‘poverty incarnate’. The villagers, despite doing back- 
breaking labour in all forms, find survival difficult, particularly during 
the Depression years of the late 1920s and early 1930s. Hence, the 
occasional availability of a dead cow's meat becomes an occasion for 
celebration. In the story “Kafan’, Ghisu and Madhav ‘owned nothing 
except for some clay pots; a few torn rags was all that covered their 
nakedness... They were loaded with debts’.”” They are so poor that 
they do not have anything either to take care of Budhiya in labour 
pain or the child still to be born. In ‘Mandir’, Sukhiya has to sell her 
bangles to arrange for sweets to be offered to God. In “Ghaswali’, 
Muliya’s husband Mahavir finds his income from driving the tonga 
steadily declining because of the onslaught of the ‘lorries’ which take 
away all the passengers. This compels the newly-married Muliya to 
sell grass, bearing the lustful eyes and obscene comments from all 
and sundry. In ‘Doodh ka Daam’, Mangal survives on the leftovers 
from the landlord’s family. His dependence on this is so acute that 
even his desperate urge to rebel comes to nothing. When, for the 
alleged sin of touching the landlord’s son, he is severely beaten and 
asked not to show his face ever again, and the privilege of getting the 
leftovers is denied to him, it is a time of crisis: 

But as daylight faded Mangal’s despair also began to disappear, while the 
hunger that had gnawed at his childhood and consumed his body had 
now become all the more intense.... He consulted with Tommy [a dog): 
What are we going to eat? I’m just going to go to bed hungry. 

Tommy whimpered as though to say, “You just have to put up with 
this sort of insult throughout life. But if you lose heart, how can you keep 
going? Just consider my case now, sometimes somebody beats me with a 
stick and yells after me, then in a little while I go back to him with my
