tail wagging. That's the way you and I are made, my friend’. 

The child and the dog have become one; the human and animal 
have united in the great fraternity of discrimination and deprivation. 
After waiting stealthily near the landlord’s house for a long time, they 

166 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

ultimately get the leftovers thrown at them by the servants. It was a 
great achievement. They start eating from the same plate. 

Pulling Tommy's head with one hand, Mangal said, ‘Just think we're so 
hungry that if we hadn’t at least got this bread they've thrown away what 
would we have done’? 

Tommy wagged his tail.” 

Social Discrimination and Exploitation 

The Dalits generally dwell in their own exclusive villages or hamlets 
or live outside or on the periphery of villages. The evidence on this 
count, however, is not direct in the texts. Premchand does not seem 
to pay much attention to this fact. He perhaps takes it for granted 
and does not explore the experiences of this social isolation with the 
required intensity. Except in the case of Karmabhoomi where the 
Dalits have a separate village, we do not find much evidence of their 
habitation pattern. There are some indications at places about their 
peripheral living. In “Thakur ka Kuan’, Gangi is said to come ‘into’ 
the village. In Godan, from where Silia’s kinsmen come and to where 
they go is unclear, indicating thereby that they do not live inside 
the village. In Rangabhoomi, on the contrary, Surdas, a Chamar by 
caste, lives right in the centre of the village and participates actively 
in village life. 

While the evidence in the texts about the sites of their abode 
is, in most cases, implied rather than stated, the evidence about 
untouchability is more frequent. Some of the stories are constructed 
around this problem alone. In ‘Saubhagya ke Kode’, Bhagatram, an 
M.A. student, says: 

Unluckily I’m among those whom the society considers low. I’m a 
Chamar by caste. My father was an attendant with the Inspector of the 
Schools. On his recommendation I got admission in a school... Initially 
the teachers in the school avoided touching me. Now the situation has 
improved, but still the boys are not friendly with me.” 

In ‘Doodh ka Daam’, the Bhangi boy Mangal is treated as an 
untouchable even by the young boys. The leftover food is thrown at 
and not given to him. He felt bad ‘when the food was dropped down 
from above into his clay bowls. Everybody ate from fine plates and 
clay bowls for him.’ The consciousness about his lowly social position 
is constantly hammered into him. “There was no one who would 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 167 

ask him to play with him, and even the piece of canvas he slept on 
was untouchable.’ He himself is deeply aware of his ‘untouchable’ 
position. Therefore, when the landlord's son invites him to play, he 
refuses by saying, ‘What if the master should see? I'd get my hide 
skinned off, but as for you, you'd get away with it’.”” 

In ‘Sadgati’, made into a tele-film by Satyajit Ray, Dukhi, while 
doing unpaid labour for the Brahman priest, is not properly given 
even the fire to light his beedi. The fire is thrown at his head thereby 
scarring him. Sukhiya (in ‘Mandir’) is not permitted to enter the 
temple to present her offering to God. Even when they are not 
touching the humans or eatables or the offerings to be made to 
God, their proximity is considered to be polluting. When a priest 
(in Karmabhoomi) finds the Dalits sitting at the door of the temple, 
he is incensed. “Why did you come here’? he says. “The same mat 
is laid from there to here. Everything is spoiled.... Now we have to 
take bath in this cold winter.”” He could not tolerate the presence 
of the ‘untouchables’ even at the place where the shoes of the visitors 
are kept. 

Here, however, it must be added that in Premchand’s literature 
there is enough evidence to suggest that exclusion is not absolute. 
There are various levels of it. So, for example, in “Gulli Danda’, we 
find the Dalit boy not only playing with the upper-caste boy but 
also beating him when the latter tries to cheat. In ‘Mukti Marg’ we 
witness the middle-caste peasant Jhingur smoking from the same 
pipe as the untouchable Harihar. In ‘Meri Pehli Rachna’, the villagers 
join with the Dalits in belabouring the upper-caste seducer of a Dalit 
girl. In Godan, Hori and his wife Dhania provide shelter to Silia, a 
Dalit girl, when she is thrown out of the house by her parents for 
persisting in her unequal relationship with the Brahman, Matadin. 
In Rangabhoomi, the Chamar, Surdas, is in the centre of the village, 
and even though we have a few references about his being a low caste, 
nobody actually practises untouchability with him. This one may be 
an idealised picture, but what is important is the suggestion that not 
every ‘touchable’ caste practises the same amount of untouchability 
with the Dalits. And, secondly, the Dalits are not so excluded from 
the society as many accounts, including some of Premchand’s own, 
imply. 

Whenever this social injunction about untouchability is not 
voluntarily complied with, it is vigorously enforced. Sukhiya (in 

168 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

‘Mandir’) is forcibly stopped from entering the temple. Dukhi is 
scolded and the fire thrown at him when he enters the Brahman’s 
house asking for fire to light his beedi. In ‘Doodh ka Daam’, Mangal 
is beaten up for the alleged sin of touching the landlord's son. In 
Karmabhoomi, the Dalits, when found sitting near the door of the 
temple by the priests and their henchmen, are given a severe beating. 
This discrimination is starkly brought out in “Thakur ka Kuan’. 
Gangi’s husband Jokhu is ill and the water at home is dirty; probably 
some animal has fallen into the well from which she fetches water. 
The only option was to bring water from one of the cleaner wells in 

the village. But Gangi knows that 

No one would Jet her walk up to the Thakur’s well. Even while she was 
far off people would start yelling at her. At the other end of the village 
the shopkeeper had a well but even there they wouldn't let her draw any 
water. For people like herself there wasn’t any well in the village. 

But she decides to go to the Thakur’s well in the dark when nobody 
could see her. As she starts drawing the bucket from inside the well, 
‘suddenly the Thakur’s door opened. The jaws of a tiger couldn't have 
terrihed her more’. The open door of the upper-caste household 
from where she can be spotted acquires the character of a demon's 
visage which is much more ferocious than the ‘jaws of a tiger’. This 
social demon is much more unforgiving, unrelenting and difficult 
to negotiate than a maneater. At that moment, Gangi would have 
preferred to have a tiger facing her to the prospect of encountering 
any member of the Thakur’s family. The social retribution lurking in 
the darkness made her flee leaving her important assets, the bucket 
and the rope, behind. “When she reached home, Jokhu, with the Jota 
at his mouth, was drinking that filthy, stinking water’.”” 

The social discrimination against the Dalits is reinforced by 
economic exploitation. In fact, both of these have become so 
enmeshed that they cannot be separated and are responsible for 
keeping the Dalits in poverty and degradation. The three main 
agencies of exploitation are the officials of the colonial government, 
the landlords, and the Brahman priests. The colonial officials compel 
them to do begaar (forced labour), the landlords charge high rents, 
and impose various levies, apart from forcing them into begaar. The 
Brahman priests demand offerings from them and devise means to 
keep them socially inferior. The most lethal combination is when the 
person of Brahman and the position of landlord become combined 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 169 

in one. Here, the Dalits have no escape route. The weight of tradition 
aligns with the power of the State to extract maximum advantage 
from them. 

Due to their inferior social position, the Dalits are most often 
dragged into begaar. The Shudras (backward castes) who are often 
peasants and cultivators consider it below their dignity to do begaar. 
In Premashram, we encounter an incident when the colonial officials 
ask the villagers for begaar. One of them replies: ‘It is done by the 
Chamars. This is not our work.” In Kayakalp, we find the zamindar'’s 
agents forcing the Chamars into back-breaking begaar. They are not 
even given food while they work. When they object to this, they are 
severely beaten up. 

But the exploitation is most severe when the temporal and spiritual 
authorities combine in one. And this is precisely the position which 
the mahant in Karmabhoomi enjoys. Apart from receiving the rent, 
he charges various customary and new cesses from the tenants and 
everyone pays without a murmur. Besides enjoying enormous powers 
over his tenants, he is backed by the moral and military authority 
of the colonial rulers. This makes his position almost unassailable. 
To protest or rise against him amounts to desperate brinkmanship 
and presupposes the overcoming of many mental blocks, besides 
being organisationally prepared. Only when the Depression years 
drastically reduce the paying capacity of the tenants do they ask 
for some redress. Here the colonial state stands solidly behind the 
landlord and forces the peasants to pay up even while they have 
nothing to eat at home. Their coming under the spell of Brahmanism 
proves even more expensive for the Dalits. Sukhiya (in ‘Mandir’) and 
Dukhi (in ‘Sadgati’) pay with their lives for their devotion. 

The exploitation is effected not only through ideology but by force 
as well. The Dalits in Premashram and Kayakalp are beaten up when 
they object to ruthless begaar extracted by the colonial officials and 
the zamindar’s men, respectively. In “Thakur ka Kuan’, ‘[they] beat 
poor Mahngu so hard that he spat blood for months, and the only 
reason was that he refused to work in the forced labour gang . 

The exploitation takes other forms as well, particularly in the case of 
Dalit women. Here sexual exploitation combines with the economic. 
Gangi (in “Thakur ka Kuar’) feels that “Whenever she came into the 
village they looked at her with eyes full of lust.” In Godan, the Dalit 
girl Silia is sexually, emotionally and economically exploited by the 

170 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

Brahman youth, Matadin. Despite being emotionally attached and 
devoted to the latter and working full time in his field, Silia has 
absolutely no right over anything belonging to him. When she tries 
to pay off her debt by giving just about a kilogram of grain to the 
moneylender, her paramour Matadin objects violently and snatches 
it away from her. When the humiliated Silia asks him whether she 
has no right on things belonging to him, he retorts angrily: 

No, you don’t have any right whatsoever. You work here and get your 
food in return. You can’ eat and throw also. If you don’t feel satisfied here 
you can go and get work elsewhere. There is no shortage of labour. 

The attitude shown by Matadin is common in this kind of unequal 
relationship. The author comments: 

He didn’t want to give anything in return for Silia’s body and affection. 
Silia, for him, was only a machine to extract work, and nothing else. He 
skilfully used her affection to his advantage.” 

The fact that the upper-caste men consider the Dalit women’s bodies 
as their private property in the same way as they consider their 
and their menfolk’s ill-paid or unpaid labour as their right is clear 
from the story ‘Ghaswali’. Chain Singh, a Thakur by caste, pursues 
Muliya, a Dalit woman who spurns his advances. Once he corners 
her and says: ‘Have pity on me’. Muliya feels extremely humiliated 
and angry. She reacts by saying: 

If my man would talk to your wife like this, howd you have felt? Then 
you would've cut his throat, isn’t it? Tell me. Just because Mahavir is a 
Chamar, you think he doesn’t have blood in his body, he has no shame, 
no honour! There are many much more beautiful women around.... Why 
don’t you go and ask for pity from them?... But you wouldn't go there 
because you're mortally scared. You demand pity from me because I’m a 
Chamarin, of a low caste, and a low-caste woman will fall in your lap by 
a little scolding or a small bribe. What an easy bargain!” 

Internalisation and Acceptance of 
Caste Ideology 

Caste has been a central institution of Hindu society for hundreds of 
years. Although there have been numerous changes and adjustments 
in its structure, it has survived. In the course of its long existence it 
has created an ideology of its own that has permeated to the lowest 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 171 

levels. It has even penetrated the non-Hindu religions in its most 
basic forms—hierarchy, endogamy and a sense of purity-pollution. 
It is, therefore, not surprising that the Dalits also have imbibed 
it. Among the several endogamous castes constituting the Dalits, 
a sense of superiority vis-a-vis certain castes combines with an 
equally deep sense of inferiority in relation to certain other castes, 
particularly the Brahmans. The consciousness about untouchability 
has also taken such deep roots in society that even the Dalits believe 
in it. However, it must be pointed out that in Premchand’s literature 
we find a complex and varied manifestation of this phenomenon. 
While, on one end, there is a character like Dukhi (in ‘Sadgati’) who 
accepts the spiritual superiority of the Brahmans, on the other end, 
we encounter Ghisu and Madhav (in ‘Kafan’) who mentally and 
emotionally exist outside the Brahmanical moral order. In between 
we find other Dalit characters occupying the middle ground where 
compliance and defiance are woven together. In this section we are 
concerned only with those aspects of the Dalit mentality which reveal 
an internalisation of the caste ideology. 

When Amarkant (in Karmabhoomi) asks the old Dalit woman, 
Saloni, for a place to stay, she says, ‘Son, this village is inhabited only 
by Raidases’, implying thereby that it is unfit to provide an abode for 
any ‘upper -caste person. The consciousness of the hierarchical social 
organisation is extremely pervasive. Even in Rangabhoomi where the 
Chamar hero Surdas is most idealistically treated, this discriminatory 
caste consciousness is found in many places. When Bhairo, himself 
from a semi-untouchable caste, Pasi, beats up Mithua, Surdas’ 
nephew, he is scolded by villagers for punishing a child. He retaliates 
by saying: ‘He’s not equal to other children. We're abused by the 
Daroga, but should we take the abuses even from the Doms’? In 
another instance, when Bhairo beats his wife Subhagi and throws 
her out of the house, Surdas gives her shelter. The main grievance of 
Bhairo against Subhagi is that she went to a Chamar: ‘If she had gone 
with a man who was caste-wise, looks-wise, wealth-wise superior to 
me, I wouldn't have minded’.™ 

In ‘Aga-Pichha, Bhagatram, an educated Chamar faces 
discrimination all his life. He falls in love with the daughter of a 
former prostitute. They plan to get married. His parents object, 
but the girl by her beauty, service and exemplary behaviour wins 
their hearts and they agree to the marriage. The date of marriage is 

172 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

fixed. Bhagatram, who until now had been delaying the marriage 
on one pretext or another, falls terminally ill. This is because he 
can’t overcome the social prejudice against marrying the offspring 
of a prostitute. Despite the fact that he is in love with the girl, he 
cannot bring himself to marry her. And this interminable and acute 
dilemma results in his death. Here, the fact that even the lowliest 
find someone below them with whom they cannot attach socially is 
brought out in stark nakedness. 

The acceptance of the Brahmanical ideology is witnessed in detail 
in the story ‘Sadgati’.”’ Dukhi, a Chamar, goes to the Brahman 
priest to invite him for fixing an auspicious date for his daughter's 
marriage. The Brahman asks him to perform a variety of jobs before 
he gets ready—to ‘sweep it clean in front of the door’, to plaster the 
‘floor of the sitting room’, to carry hay from the storeroom and last, 
but not least, to chop a knotted log of thick wood ‘on which a great 
many devotees had previously tried their strength and it was ready 
to match iron with iron in any fight’. He finishes the other jobs by 
noon, but splitting the wood proves extremely tough. He works on 
it for hours and beyond the limits of his strength. He finally manages 
to accomplish the work but falls dead in the process. 

Dukhi is so completely grounded under the weight of tradition 
and Brahmanical superiority that he considers himself a non-entity 
before the spiritual might of the Brahman. He is so laden with his 
consciousness of being an untouchable that he follows those norms 
even when there is nobody to observe or enforce it. He strictly tells 
his wife Jhuriya not to put their own cot for seating the Brahman 
priest. Since the Pandit would not sit on their cot, they want to 
borrow a cot from some ‘touchable’. But as that option is also not 
available, Dukhi tells his wife that he would make a mat of leaves for 
the Brahman to sit. Since the Brahman would not accept anything in 
their utensils from their hands, he advises Jhuriya to take along the 
Gond's daughter to buy the ‘complete offering—a full two pounds 
of flour, a half of rice, a quarter of gram, an eighth of ghee, salt, 
turmeric, and four annas at the end of the leaf’. Then he warns her, 
Just don't touch anything because that will be a great wrong. Even 
when he gets burnt in the process of getting fire from the Brahman’s 
wife for lighting his beedi, he does not get angry, upset or irritated. 
Instead he blames himself for violating the sanctity of a Brahman’s 
house and thinks that this ‘punishment’ was well-deserved: 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 173 

She was speaking the truth—how could a tanner ever come into a 
Brahman’s house? These people were clean and holy, that was why 

the whole world worshipped and respected them. A mere tanner was 
absolutely nothing. 

And later: “To himself he said, “This is what comes of dirtying a clean 
Brahmans house. How quickly God pays you back for it”. 

He does the begaar for the Brahman, without eating or drinking. 
The Brahman does not give him anything and Dukhi does not go 
home for fear of annoying the priest. Thirsty, hungry, exhausted, he 
works for him the whole day. While splitting the knotted log, his 
strength and courage finally seem to sap and he falls asleep. Just then 
the Brahman comes and curses and exhorts him. His fear of and 
devotion to the spiritual persona of the Brahman re-energises him to 

the degree that 

some kind of hidden power seemed to have come into his hands. It was 
as though fatigue, hunger, weakness, all had left him. He was astonished 
at his own strength. The axe-strokes descended one after another like 
lightning. He went on driving the axe in this state of intoxication until 

finally the log split down the middle. 

Dukhi worked ruthlessly the whole day and when his kinsmen 
refused to lift his lifeless body, partly as protest and partly for the 
fear of the police, he was dragged unceremoniously by the Brahman 
out of the village where ‘jackals and kites, dogs and crows’ picked at 
his body. He was not economically or legally bound to the Brahman 
in any way and the latter could not force him for any work. And yet 
Dukhi’s was ‘a whole life of devotion, service and faith’. 

Dukhi represents the conscious ultimate in subjection. His 
acceptance of the Brahmanical dominance is total. He does not 
resent even in thoughts. 

Resentment and Rebellion 

In Premchand’s literature, the Dalits generally do not take their 
low social and economic position as eternally giver. and resent the 
exploitation and oppression perpetrated on them. ‘They often feel 
sceptical about this social organisation where there are so many 
differences and so much discrimination. Although they are shown 
to accept and practise many of the values of Hindu society, their 
resentment against their low social position is strong. It is sometimes 

174 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

expressed in individual and collective protests. The chaudhary of 
the village (in Karmabhoomi) refuses to believe that poverty is the 
outcome of one’s karma.” Gangi (in “Thakur ka Kuan’) feels resentful 
‘against the restraints and bars of the custom: “Why was she so low 
and others so high? Because they wore a thread around their necks? 
There wasn't one in the village who wasn't rotten. They stole, they 
cheated, they lied in court’.” 

Sukhiya (in ‘Mandir’) asserts her right to enter the temple. She 
is not satisfied with accepting holy water from the priest's hand; 
she wants to go inside and make her offering in person and put her 
child at the feet of the Lord. She cries in desperate anger not only 
against men but also against God when she is physically stopped 
from entering the temple and is pushed, causing her sick child to fall 
from her arms and die: 

You sinners, why’re you quiet now after killing my child? Why don't 
you kill me also? The Thakurji will be spoiled by my touch!... Wasn't he 
spoiled when he made me? Now I'll never come to touch your Thakurji. 
You can put him under lock and key... You are all so cruel... And yet 
you claim to be the custodians of religion. You all are murderers, pure 
murderers.” 

The orphan Mangal (in ‘Doodh ka Daam’), despite his helpless 
position which forces him to accept the leftovers thrown at him, is 
assertive of his right for equal treatment. When Suresh, the landlord’s 
son, asks him to become a horse on whom he could ride, he enquires: 
“Will I always be a horse or will I get to be a rider, tell me that’. It 
was a difficult question and, initially Suresh rejects outright such an 
audacious demand: ‘Whod let you get on his back? Think of that! 
Are you a sweeper or not’? Mangal doesn’t back out: 

When did I ever say I wasn’t a sweeper? But it was my own mother who 
brought you up and fed you with her milk. So long as I’m not going to 
be a rider 1 won't be the horse. You people are pretry smart! You want to 
enjoy being riders and I’m supposed to stay just as a horse. 

He harbours a strong resentment against this unjust system and the 
wanton hypocrisy of the dominant classes, where his mother nursed 
Suresh, but he, her son, is considered untouchable and lowly. He 
complains to the dog Tommy who is his constant companion: 

‘It was my mother who nursed Suresh.’ 
Tommy wagged his tail again. 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 175 

“They say that nobody can ever pay the price of milk, and this is the 
payment I'm getting’. 
And once more Tommy wagged his tail.“” 

In ‘Sadgati’, a character of Gond caste voices his resentment against 
Dukhi's devotion and the exploitation by the Brahman: 

Even the landlord gives something to eat. The government officials take 
begaar, but they also give a little something as wage. This fellow has 
surpassed them all, and he calls himself a holy man.”' 

The sexual exploitation of women is even more deeply felt. At 
times it becomes a question of honour for the entire community. 
And then the resentment erupts in more personalised violence. 
In Godan, the kinsfolk of Silia are not happy with her completely 
subordinate relationship with Matadin. They view it as illegitimate 
and exploitative to the extreme. They, along with some other persons 
from their community, corner Matadin in the field. When asked 
what the trouble is about, Silia’s father bitterly replies: 

There is no trouble, Thakur. Today we'll either make Matadin a Chamar 
or blood will flow.... You can’t make us Brahmans, but we can make you 
Chamar. If you can convert us to Brahmans, then we're all ready for that. 
But if you're not capable of that, then you should also become Chamar. 
You should eat and drink with us, sit and walk with us. If you're taking 
our honour, then give your religion to us. 

Silia’s mother is equally forthright in castigating Matadin’s father: 
‘Bravo Pandit! This is your justice!... You'll sleep with her but not 
drink water from her hand’.” And, finally, they implement their 
threat by breaking Matadin’s sacred thread and pushing a piece of 
bone into his mouth. 

In ‘Meri Pehli Rachna’,, which is based on a real-life incident, 
the community of Chamars feel resentful against a Kayastha 
landlord who was seducing a Chamar girl working for him. Their 
anger is further exacerbated because he ‘stared shamelessly at the 
daughters and wives of the menial class’. It now becomes a question 
of protecting their honour. So, they hold a meeting of their caste 
panchayat and decide upon a course of action. One day, when this 
fellow entices the girl inside his house and latches the door from 
inside, they descend on his house knocking at and finally breaking 
open the door. They locate him and ‘Whatever weapon anyone could 
lay his hands on—umbrella, sticks, shoes, fist, leg—was pressed into 

176 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

service till at last he swooned and they, thinking him to be dead, left 

" 43 
him alone’. 

Sometimes the protest against discrimination also takes the form 
of a more organised movement. In Karmabhoomi, we witness a 
movement by the untouchables assisted and led by some middle-class 
leaders for entry into the temple. They are attacked by the priests 
and their henchmen, but they resist. The police are called and there 
is firing on the crowd of protesters, leading to the death of many. 
But they persevere, ultimately forcing the authorities and the temple 
guardians to allow them entry and thus making the movement 
successful. In another instance, we find the urban poor launching a 
strike to obtain municipal land for the construction of houses for the 
poor. After a protracted struggle in which many Dalit and non-dalit 
workers participate, they succeed in getting the land. 

The village scene in the novel is turbulent. The Depression 
makes the prices of agricultural commodities fall drastically, thereby 
rendering the peasants incapable of paying the rent and other levies 
which are, in any case, high. The peasants petition the mahant for 
lowering the rent, but to no avail. Having waited for many months, 
the peasants launch a no-rent campaign. The colonial State intervenes 
on behalf of the landlords and a severe repression is launched. The 
struggle continues despite the repression and many individuals, 
including women, show exemplary courage. In one instance, an 
old Dalit woman, Saloni, spits at the police officer and abuses him 
while he beats her severely until she faints. Another woman, an 
Ahir by caste, hacks two policemen when they try to molest her. 
The Dalits and other peasants and workers fight against the might 
of the colonial State with the primitive weapons available at their 
command. Even when the repression is most severe and they have 
to leave their lands and villages, they persist with their struggle until 
the rent is lowered. 

In Kayakalp, the Dalits protest against the forced labour that 
is extracted from them by the landlord. The landlord and his 
henchmen threaten and beat them, but they stand their ground. 
When the landlord’s men surround them and try to force them 
again into begaar, they rebel and launch an attack. They are joined 
by other poor people and together they succeed in throwing back the 
landlord’s men. But, as part of the familiar story, the officials of the 
colonial State intervene and fire at the people killing scores of them. 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 177 

Here we encounter another phenomenon, that of the middle- 
class leadership. It is found in all struggles and movements, but its 
role is a little problematic. While the Dalits and other workers are 
generally militant when aroused, the middle-class leaders are seen as 
having a restraining influence. They contain the violent disposition 
of the crowd, even offering to sacrifice themselves in the process. In 
Kayakalp, \abourers confront Chakradhar, the self-appointed leader 
and a pacifist, and rebuke him for always talking about peace while 
they are being oppressed and beaten up. Chakradhar, however, 
prevails upon them to lay down their arms and not attack the English 
officials. Even then they are arrested, imprisoned, and beaten up by 
the superintendent. When they get an opportunity, they corner him 
and start hitting him. Chakradhar intervenes again and asks them to 
stop in the name of God. One prisoner then reprimands him: ‘Just 
get lost, you who claim to be the follower of God. When he abuses 
and whips us everyday, where is your God sleeping then? Now he’s 
woken up!” 

Even in Karmabhoomi, Amarkant does not favour a movement 
against the high rent and other oppressive levies and prevails upon 
the villagers to petition the mahant instead. Nothing comes out of it 
and, finally the villagers were forced to launch a struggle. Amarkant 
is jailed, but the villagers carry on with the struggle on their own. 

It seems, though, that Premchand’s sympathy lies on the side 
of what he considers non-violent leadership, yet the agency of the 
lower classes is firmly established. The Dalits actively participate 
in the struggle for their social and religious rights and economic 
betterment. - 

Premchand and the Dalit Question 
I 

The tradition of protest against the Brahmanical ideology dates 
back to the ancient period. Its roots can be traced to the high 
philosophical speculations of the Upanishads. But more organised 
protests originated with the rise of Jainism and Buddhism. The 
resurgence of Brahmanism under the Satvahanas and the Sungas led 
to the consolidation of the caste system and its codification by Manu. 
This trend was advanced under the Guptas and many south Indian 
kings such as the Pallavas, the Chalukyas and the Rashtrakutas, and 

178 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

witnessed its justification in the philosophy of Shankaracharya. 
Almost simultaneously, since at least the sixth century AD, we find 
the origin and growth of the Bhakti movement which challenged 
Brahmanical orthodoxy and became popular among the lower 
castes. Starting with the Nayanars and Alvars in the sixth and 
seventh centuries in Tamil Nadu, the Bhakti movement moved 
northwards later. Beginning with Jnaneshwar and Chokhamela 
during the thirteenth and fourteenth centuries, the Bhakti tradition 
in Maharashtra continued till the seventeenth century. In the east, 
Chaitanya (1486-1533) became the fountainhead of the popular 
Bhakti movement. In the north, we find a rebellious Bhakti tradition 
in the figures of Kabir (1440-1518), Nanak (1469-1539) and Dadu 
(1544-1603). The Bhakti saints filled up the space left behind by 
the receding Buddhism and the other heterodox traditions of the 
Siddhas and the Nath Yogis. The Bhakti saints questioned the 
legitimacy of the caste system, denied the authority of the Vedas 
and the Brahmans, and rejected idol worship. By the eighteenth and 
nineteenth centuries, however, many Bhakti movements were either 
incornorated by mainstream Hinduism or remained in the forms of 
sects such as the Kabirpanthis in UP and Bihar and some other parts 
of north India, Nanakpanthis in the Punjab and the Dadupanthis in 
Rajasthan. *° 

In British India, the influence of English education provided a 
new framework for challenging the caste system and the degraded 
position of the Shudras in it. The reform movements in the nineteenth 
century—the Brahmo Samaj, Prarthana Samaj, Social Reform 
League, Veda Samaj, Arya Samaj and Ramakrishna Mission— 
questioned the legitimacy of the caste ideology and criticised its role 
in creating and maintaining a debased hierarchical system. Their 
impact on the nineteenth century Hindu society, particularly on the 
educated middle class, was substantial. The most influential of these 
movements, the Arya Samaj, was more aggressive in its approach and 
spread to the lower castes. 

However, all these movements, except for the Brahmo Samaj, 
remained confined within the Hindu framework. The first radical 
lower-caste protest movement in British India was the Satyashodhak 
Samaj initiated by Jotiba Phule in Maharashtra in the late nineteenth 
century. It considered the Shudras as separate from the Hindu society 
and as having descended from the original inhabitants of India, while 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 179 

the Hindus were said to have been the progeny of the Aryan invaders 
and Hinduism was an imposed and alien religion. Following in his 
footsteps in the twentieth century were Sri Narayan Guru in Kerala, 
Achhootanand in UP and Mangoo Ram in Punjab." 

In UP, which provided the background for most of Premchand’s 
literature, the lower-caste assertion began in the early twentieth 
century. It was mostly an urban phenomenon occurring in cities like 
Kanpur, Allahabad, Lucknow and Banaras where the Dalit castes like 
Chamars, Bhangis and Doms started associating with the teachings of 
Bhakti saints, particularly Kabir and Ravidas. They called themselves 
Bhagats and wore necklaces of beads called kanthis, which were 
distinctive marks of the Bhakti sect, in contrast to the Brahmanical 
sacred thread or janeyu.” 

In the late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, there were 
mainly four trends of engagement with the Dalit question. First, 
orthodox Hinduism which witnessed new consolidation in the wake 
of the reform movements and the reform measures undertaken by 
the colonial state such as the legislation on Sati, widow remarriage 
and age of consent; it justified the prevailing caste system and 
considered the Dalit’s place in this system as appropriate. Second, 
the Hindu reform movements, from moderate to radical ones, 
whose programmes included measures ranging from dilution of 
caste discrimination to abolition of the caste system; all of them 
considered the Dalits as part of Hindu society and wanted to effect 
closer integration, and Gandhi also belonged to this trend. The 
third trend was that of radical lower-caste protest movements and 
was represented by people like Phule, Naicker, Achhootanand and 
Ambedkar; it sought links with the radical trends within the Bhakti 
movement and visualised a future for Shudras/Dalits which was to be 
different from that of the Aryans/Hindus. The last significant trend 
was that of socialism-Marxism which got tremendous boost in the 
wake of the Russian Revolution. It perceived the Indian society in 
class terms and submerged the realities of caste in the categories of 
class. The Dalits were considered simply as part of the proletariat 
and the poor peasantry and their radically different social experiences 
were largely ignored. 

Premchand mostly derived from the Hindu reformist and 
socialist-Marxist traditions; he was aware of the lower-caste protest 
movements, occasionally took them into account, but generally 

180 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

ignored them; and he bitterly criticised orthodox Hinduism from 
the perspective of liberal, reformist Hinduism. 

The Dalit question acquired its prominence in Indian politics in 
the late 1920s, particularly after the Ambedkar-led movement in 
Mahad (in present-day Maharashtra) in 1927 for the Dalits’ right 
to drink water from the common community sources. Ambedkar 
had been actively taking up Dalit issues since 1919. The Congress 
and Gandhi had been aware of the issue and, besides including it 
in the Congress resolutions, Gandhi had been trying at his own 
level to take up many issues relating to untouchability. But after 
the Mahad movement, the path of the Dalits, particularly in the 
Bombay Presidency, began to diverge from the Congress. The 
Mahad movement had invited extreme reactions from the local caste 
Hindus, thereby angering the Dalits and hardening their stance. 
This resulted in the demand for separate electorates by Ambedkar 
and some other Dalit leaders.” Gandhi was opposed to this demand 
as he considered it an imperialist plot to divide the Indian people; 
but he was also opposed to untouchability. He sat on a ‘fast unto 
death’ on 20 September 1932 in the Poona prison, both in order to 
oppose the award of separate electorates to the Dalits as well as ‘to 
sting the Hindu conscience’ for practising untouchability.” Finally, 
the problem was resolved through a meeting between Gandhi and 
Ambedkar and the announcement of what is known as the Poona 
Pact. The demand for separate electorates was dropped but enhanced 
reservations for the Dalits was agreed upon. 

These national issues agitated Premchand the most. It was during 
this period (1927-34) that he wrote some of his most famous stories 
(Mandir’, ‘Sadgati’, “Thakur ka Kuan’, and ‘Doodh ka Daam’) and 
the novel (Karmabhoomi) dealing directly with this issue. Before that 
he had not directly dealt with the problem of untouchability. After 
1934, he did not take up these issues except briefly in Godan, his last 
published novel. 

It does not, however, mean that before 1927 Premchand was not 
interested in the Dalits. In fact, Dalit characters had been present in 
his literature since the beginning. (His first literary creation, a play, 
had Dalit characters but it was lost, and he recounted the incident in 
his story ‘Meri Pehli Rachna’ later on.) Moreover, in one of his most 
famous novels, Rangabhoomi, he created the first Dalit hero of Hindi 
literature, if not Indian literature as a whole. The creation of the 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 18] 

Chamar hero of this epic novel militated against the entire Indian 
tradition of epic heroes. Not only was he from an untouchable caste; 
he was, in addition, a beggar and visually handicapped. Besides these, 
Dalits, in the most fundamental sense of the term, were present in 
some of his other stories and novels. 

In most of these accounts, Premchand considered and dealt with 
the Dalits as part of the deprived and poor sections of the Indian 
popuiation. In many of his writings we do not find the castes of 
the characters mentioned. He considered them as an integral part 
of the exploited classes that stood in contrast and, in many places, 
in opposition to the exploiting classes. In this sense of the term, 
Premchand’s literature is full of such people. Almost all his peasants 
(whom, incidentally, he considered to be the most exploited under 
the colonial dispensation) and labourers, whether in villages or 
towns, belong to these classes. Ahirs, Kurmis, Koeries, Kunjras, 
Julahas, Khatiks, Malis, Pasis and, of course, Chamars and Bhangis 
are all part of this class. 

It is because of his vision of the poor and exploited as a group 
distinct from the rich and middle classes that he often, except in the 
above-mentioned cases where he dealt directly with the problem of 
social discrimination, tended to ignore the social distinction between 
the backward (the Shudra) and the Dalit (the untouchable) castes. In 
these writings, the Dalits do not seem to face social discrimination 
but only economic exploitation and poverty, along with other poor 
groups. Therefore, by ignoring the specific problems of the Dalits, 
Premchand portrays a broader social stratum of the poor who are 
deprived, exploited and oppressed under the colonial regime by 
landlords, colonial officials, the police, usurers, priests and sometimes 
the middle classes as well. 

Although the ideas of Vivekananda, Ranade, Gokhale and Tilak 
had some impact on him, the three most enduring influences on 
Premchand’s thoughts were the Arya Samaj, Gandhi and Marxism. 
Arya Samaj was the early influence and Premchand became a 
passionate admirer of it as early as 1900. “He often attended its 
meetings, and was also probably an enrolled member’.”’ Its social 
reform aspects remained with him throughout his life and as late 
as 1936 he admired its ‘cultural achievements (which) are far better 
known and appreciated than its religious achievements’.” He, 
however, did not subscribe to its divisive aspects and was opposed 

182 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

to the Shuddhi movement and the anti-Muslim slant of its ideology. 
He remained a votary of Hindu-Muslim unity in all its forms till the 
end of his life. 

Marxism, ever since the success of the Russian Revolution, appealed 
to Premchand for its advocacy of the poor and the downtrodden. 
The welfare of the poor and the deprived was always dear to his heart 
and in Marxism he found the most open and vocal support for this. 
He declared in 1918 that ‘The future belongs to the peasants and the 
workers’. He further claimed that this trend would overcome all the 
obstacles in its path.” 

Criticising the Council-entry programme of the Congress in 
1933, he strongly asserted that ‘we do not want the freedom of the 
capitalists. We, instead, want the freedom of the poor, the peasants 
and the workers.’” In his essay ‘Mahajani Sabhyata’ (The Capitalist 
Civilisation), written at the fag end of his life in 1936, he criticised 
the capitalist society and culture for the degradation it had caused: 

In this capitalist society the one motivation for all action is money... 
From this point of view, it is the capitalists who rule the world today. 
Human society has been divided into two sections. The bigger section 
comprises the tillers and toilers, while the far smaller section comprises 
those who through their might and influence hold the larger section 
under their subjugation. They have no sympathy for this larger section 
and take no pity on it whatever. This section exists merely so that it can 
sweat for its masters and bleed for its masters and one day quietly depart 

the world.” 

The third influence, that of Gandhi, was so overwhelming and 
comprehensive that it was reflected in most of his writings after 1918. 

He picked up strands from all the three sources and shaped them 
according to his own feelings and beliefs. He could not forge them 
into any logical whole and this created many inconsistencies in him. 
But these inconsistencies remained within the broad parameters of 
a human, democratic, secular and strongly pro-poor framework. He 
sought solution to the Dalit problems under these broad parameters. 
Socially and politically, he followed an integrationist approach and 
considered the Dalits as part of the Hindu society. He implored the 
Hindu leaders to integrate the Dalits in society as their brethren on an 
equal footing. He strongly condemned the practices of untouchability 
and social and religious discrimination and took to task those who 
supported this iniquitous and inhuman system. 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 183 
Il 

The political engagement in the early 1930s on this social issue led 
Premchand to write copiously. His journalistic writings of these 
years revealed his devotion to Gandhi and his commitment to the 
social, political and economic uplift of the Dalits. His discourse 
largely remained within a Hindu framework, but it was of a liberal, 
reformist and non-communal Hindu. Ir basically centred around 
three themes: 

1. The Dalits were an integral part of Hindu society and should 
remain so. The Hindu society, on its part, should make all 
efforts to integrate the Dalits socially and politically on an 
equal footing and give them all the respect. 

2. The Brahmans, particularly the priestly class, were primarily 
responsible for the exclusion of the Dalits and the social 
and religious discriminations against them. This parasitic 
priestly class was the worst enemy of Hinduism and should 
be chastised and ostracised by the Hindu society. 

3. The problems of the Dalits were not only socio-religious but 
economic as well, and removing poverty is as important as 

anything else for solving the problems of the Dalits.” 

Writing on the eve of Gandhi's fast undertaken to oppose the 
Macdonald Award which proposed separate electorates for the Dalits, 
Premchand said that the Dalits were an integral part of the Hindu 
society. If they were separated from the Hindus, it would not only 
harm the Hindu society but also the Dalits. He stated that “The Dalits 
are so much dependent on the Hindus that even if the Government 
offers them all its riches, it cannot ensure their survival’. He was sure 
that ‘if we enquire from any Chamar, Pasi or Musahar in a village, he 
will never consent to be separated from the Hindu society. He wants 
his liberation within the Hindu society, not outside it’. He referred 
to many Sanskritising tendencies among the Dalits like wearing of 
sacred thread, and taking up many high Hindu rituals and asserted 
that ‘they have the same gods, the same ideals and the same views as 
the Hindus. Every atom of their bodies is filled with Hindutva.” 

Premchand’s anxiety related to Gandhi's fast was relieved once 
an understanding was reached between Gandhi, the Congress and 
the Hindu leaders on the one hand and Ambedkar and other Dalit 
leaders on the other as enshrined in the Poona Pact. But he felt that 

184 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

it was just the beginning and ‘our task will only be over when we can 
uproot the present practice of untouchability from the country’. 

He had no doubt that Gandhi was fighting the battle against 
untouchability and this struggle was bearing fruit. But one of the 
problems was that ‘the untouchable considers himself as low and wants 
to keep away from the higher castes.” He strongly advocated the 
temple entry movement. This, according to him, was the touchstone 
for proving that the Hindu society had accepted the Dalits, as other 
parameters like inter-dining and inter-marriage were difficult to 
achieve soon. He believed and stated it at times that the Dalits had 
many unclean habits, but he felt that ‘their fall has been due to their 
ignorance and immorality’. His solution to it was two-fold. Firstly, the 
Dalits themselves are improving their conduct by wearing the sacred 
thread, giving up unclean food, and performing other rituals of high 
Hinduism.” Secondly, the Hindus had to accommodate them: ‘It is 
true that the Harijans still have many dirty habits. They drink, do 
unclean work, and eat carcasses. But once the Hindu society offers 
them room, these bad habits will automatically disappear’.”' 

But he realised that it was not easy for the Dalits to shed 
those so-called ‘bad habits’ nor was the Hindu society inclined to 
accommodate them. He then thought that the joint electorates are 
the means to integrate them with the mainstream ‘in the face of 
which this discrimination, this false pride cannot survive’.” 

It may appear that Premchand’s views are strongly upper-caste 
and paternalistic. It is true to some extent. But even while holding 
this view about the ‘dirty habits’ of the Dalits, he neither believed in 
birth-related distinctions nor held that the religious books sanctioned 
such distinctions. He went even further and asserted that these ‘dirty 
habits’ were the pretext and not the reason for keeping the Dalits low 
in the social hierarchy. 

In this very Kashi there are thousands of Brahmans who drink liquor... 
but they remain Brahmans. They keep chamarins, but their Brahmanhood 
is not threatened. But an untouchable may bathe daily and be of refined 
character, he cannot even then enter temples.” 

In one of his stories, “Mantra, an old Dalit disputes the myth of 
upper-caste superiority while confronting a Brahman propagandist 
of the Arya Samaj by saying that many Brahmans also drink 
liquor, eat meat and are illiterate and yet they are not considered 
untouchable. 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 185 

Premchand criticised, condemned and denounced in the strongest 
terms those who were opposed to the entry of the untouchables in 
the temples and discriminated against them in many other ways. 
His position derived from a Hindu framework and he feared chat 
if reforms were not soon introduced, Hindu society would lose 
the Dalits to other religions. He held the priests responsible for 
obstructing this venture and considered them to be the worst enemy 
of Hinduism and responsible tor its downfall: 

You have forced eight crore Hindus to become Muslims. Now these 
six crore untouchables are excluded from the society by your scholarly 
injunctions. Will you rest only atter wiping out the Hindu religion from 

the face of the earth?” 

He condemned the hypocrisy practiced by these custodians of Hindu 
religion who exploited the Dalits, did not hesitate to take money 
from them, enjoyed the profits incurred from the temples built from 
the donations given by Dalits, and yet did not allow them to enter 
the temples.” At another place he attacked their gods also: 

The untouchable cannot enter the temple because the gods will become 
impure. If your gods are such weaklings that they get spoiled by the 
touch of humans, then it is wrong to call them gods.” 

He asserted that this greedy, money-minded priestly class ‘is the most 
hateful canker, the most shameful blot on the Hindu society. It is 
sucking its blood like a huge leech and is the greatest obstacle in 
the path of our nationalism’. He further added that ‘If the Hindu 
society does not wish to obliterate itself from the face of the earth, it 
has to eliminate this rule of darkness’.“° He reprimanded the Hindus 
for accepting the leadership of such elements who are ‘cunning and 
deceitful’, anti-Hindu and anti-national.” And he declared that ‘Our 
freedom consists in not only liberating ourselves from the foreign 
yoke, but also from this social yoke... which is more harmful than the 
foreign rule’.”” He strongly advocated the boycott of such persons by 
the Hindus and praised those Hindu candidates who had withdrawn 
in favour of Dalit candidates in municipal elections and criticised 
those who had not. He insisted that if the Dalits were neglected, 
it would be entirely justified on their part to demand separate 
electorates. 

It seems that Premchand failed to see that Brahmanism was not 
limited to the greedy priests but had a much more comprehensive 

186 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

ideological hold and much deeper roots in society. He did, however, 
realise that the campaign for temple entry and against discriminations 
was floundering against the bedrock of tradition and stiff resistance 
not only from the priests and Brahmans but from the Hindus as a 
whole. He regretted that the Harijans had not secured the right to 
enter temples and even the fast undertaken by Gandhi on 8 May 
1933 to enforce it had not resulted in opening the doors for them. 
He then declared that ‘the first condition of nationalism would be 
to root out the varna system, the distinction between high and low 
and the religious hypocrisy.” He maintained that: ‘the nationhood I 
am dreaming about will not have even the smell of the varna system, 
there the workers and peasants shall rule, there will be no Brahman, 
no Harijan, no Kayastha, no Kshatriya. There everyone will be an 
Indian...’.”” 

He also expressed his long-held belief that it was as important 
to improve the economic conditions of the Dalits as to remove 
the social and religious inequalities. In fact, he at times held that 
these discriminations were more due to their poverty. Even though 
he considered the social aspects of the problem important, he 
felt: “Temple entry alone is not going to solve the problems of the 
Harijans. The obstacles in the way of solving these problems are more 
economic than religious’.”” 

Premchand advised those who had the welfare of Harijans at 
heart to create means for their economic uplift such as scholarships 
in schools, concessions in jobs, grant of land for houses, stoppage 
of begaar, and according dignified treatment to them.” In this 
connection he approvingly quoted a letter from someone who 
was working among the Dalits which stated that ‘the Harijans are 
not so keen on temple entry as about improving their economic 
condition. They want to achieve their liberation with the help of 
cottage industries’.”” Later on, Premchand congratulated the Kanpur 
Municipality for providing assistance for the construction of houses 
for the Harijans and said that if adequate measures were undertaken 
to provide the Dalits with the necessities of life, ‘it will go a long way 
in solving the Harijan problem’.”” il. 

‘Kafan’: End of the Road? 

¢ yy 2 P F ‘ v 
Kafan’ is a strange story. It is unlike Premchand’s other stories, 
and Ghisu and Madhay, its central characters, are unlike his other 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 187 

Dalit characters. Had we not known for sure that it was written by 
Premchand, we would have taken it as an interpolation. It is not 
concerned with the usual social and economic problems of the 
Dalits. And yet its precise location in a fractured Dalit household 
and around two aimless Dalit characters puts it in a surreal setting. 

In the beginning of the story, Ghisu and Madhav, respectively 
father and son, are sitting in front of their hut roasting stolen potatoes 
while from inside the hut agonised cries of Budhiya, Madhav’s 
pregnant wife, emerge. They sit there ignoring the heartrending 
groans, eating potatoes, and talking about a memorable feast that 
Ghisu had partaken twenty years back. After that they sleep around 
the burnt-out fire ‘just like nwo enormous coiled pythons’. * It is only 
in the morning that they discover what they had expected and hoped 
all along—that Budhiya is dead, with the dead child in her womb. 
They run around in the village begging for money and material for 
her funeral, and succeed in getting, apart from material, ‘a tidy sum 
of five rupees’. 

Having collected five rupees for the shroud, they roam around the 
market looking at all kinds of cloths, buying nothing and, finally, in the 
evening, stand before a liquor shop, as if by previous understanding. 
They drink heavily, much to the envy of the others in the shop. After 
drinking, they buy two plates of puris and vegetables and enjoy a full 
meal, giving the leftovers to a beggar. They philosophise a little, feel a 
little sad, laugh a little, cry a little and then start singing and dancing, 
and collapse right there in the end. 

Ghisu and Madhav, like Premchand’s many other Dalit characters, 
are agricultural workers, but they, unlike others, are liars, beggars 
and thieves as well. They are notorious in the village for their 
laziness. ‘If Ghisu worked one day he'd take three off. Madhav was 
such a loafer that whenever he worked for a half hour he'd stop 
and smoke his pipe for an hour’. Not many people want to give 
them work because ‘whenever you called these two you had to be 
satisfied with paying them both for doing one man’s work between 
them’. They somehow manage to obtain small loans from people 
even though they never returned them. At harvest time, they steal 
potatoes, peas or sugarcane from others’ fields. “Ghisu had reached 
the age of sixty living this hand-to-mouth existence, and like a good 
son, Madhav was following in father’s footsteps in every way, and 
if anything he was adding lustre to his father’s fame’. They realise 

188 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

that in the society there is no direct relationship berween work and 
wealth: 

In a society where the condition of people who toiled day and night was 
not much better than theirs and where, on the other hand, those who 
know how to profit from the weaknesses of the peasants were infinitely 
richer, it’s no wonder they felt like this. 

They have worked out their own survival strategy against the dominant 
economic rationality whereby every poor person has to work in order 
to survive. But theirs is not a response of economic rationality. The 
extreme dehumanising experience that these two go through and 
the way in which they respond is possible only with someone who 
is socially and economically at the lowest rung of the society. They 
have no land to defend or feel proud about; they have no honour to 
protect. They are abused, beaten up, often go hungry, ‘but they didn’t 
suffer’. They do not feel any physical pain while gobbling up the hot 
potatoes which burn their mouth and throat. They exhibit extreme 
insensitivity towards the woman who has until recently been their 
bread-giver. They do not feel any remorse or shame. And, finally, 
they cross the ultimate social barrier, commit the ultimate social 
crime; they eat off the shroud. There is nowhere below this anybody in 
society could go. This is not possible for a middle-caste peasant like 
Hori who, despite losing all his land and being in extreme poverty, 
upholds the social honour. It certainly is not possible for an upper- 
caste character. It also does not happen with Premchand’s Dalits. If 
anything, his Dalit characters are more honourable and self-respecting 
than the upper classes. But this kind of extreme dehumanising 
response is within the arena of possibility of only those who face 
extreme social and economic discrimination. There are no stakes for 
them; they have nothing to lose. “They felt neither fear of being called 
to account nor concern for a bad reputation. They had overcome 
those sensibilities long before’. It is the socio-cultural response of 
such individuals whom the system has degraded so much that all 
human concerns, except that of hunger, have disappeared. It is not 
a case of conscious decision, but a multi-generational experience of 
enduring economic and social deprivations. This generates in them 
some kind of unconscious defiance of the Brahmanical moral codes. 
By not conforming to any rules of society or economy, they have 
made it impossible for anyone to exploit them. The employers and 
the landlords have no control over them, the Brahmans and priests 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 189 

have no control over them, even the gods have no control over them. 
The fear of hell or heaven does not exist for them, and the societal 
fear is past anyway. Their inadvertent defiance has an element of 
protest; it is an act of will. 

They are beyond the pale of society, not only physically as 
untouchables but also ideologically and emotionally. Many 
other Dalit characters in Premchand’s literature, despite being 
discriminated against and deprived, remain under the ideological 
spell of Brahmanism; they, despite suffering social and economic 
ostracism, try to uphold the so-called moral and ethical values of 
the society; and some, like Dukhi (in ‘Sadgati’), are so much in the 
grip of the Brahmanical ideology that, even at the moment of the 
most extreme exploitation, they lay down their lives rather than raise 
their heads. Ghisu and Madhav, on the other hand, exist completely 
outside society except for the purpose of bare survival. Besides this, 
they do not have any need for the society, as the latter does not have 
any need for them. They are fully outcast. 

But they are not thoughtless parasites. They have their own 
ideas about the society and its values. Ghisu seems to possess a 
comprehensive world view born out of age and experience. He realises 
the mindlessness of social norms which do not provide sustenance for 
a human being but insist that the dead be treated properly. He speaks 
against the thoughtless custom which ensures that the dead body 
should have a proper cloth even though the living one never had any. 
He knows fully well that there is no ‘point in throwing a shroud over 
her. In the end it just burns up. She can't take anything with her’. 
Madhav also seems to have inherited this wisdom. He wonders over 
the mindlessness of the society which throws thousands of rupees on 
Brahmans even though nobody could ‘tell if anybody gets it in the 
next world or not’. 

They are not bothered about social censure for flouting norms and 
neglecting proper conduct. But they are definitely worried about the 
salvation of Budhiya. Madhav entertains some doubts, but Ghisu is 
sure that she will go to heaven as she fulfils the conditions for that: 

She didn’t torment anybody, she didn’t oppress anybody. At the moment 
she died she fulfilled the deepest wish of all our lives. If she doesn't go to 
heaven then those big fat people go who rob the poor with both hands 
and swim in the Ganges and offer holy water in the temples to wash away 

their sins? 

190 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

But one discomfort still remains. And that is the suffering which 
Budhiya had to endure while alive and they could not and did not 
do anything to relieve that. But even here Ghisu knows that living in 
this world is no fun for someone like Budhiya who was a sincere and 
conscientious worker; that she would have no relief, no happiness 
ever; that her fate was to work and live for others. He articulates it in 
his own way: ‘Why weep, son? Be glad she’s slipped out of this maze 
of illusion and left the whole mess behind her...’ 

Thus relieved, they start dancing and singing. But here again they 
choose the song which hints at their defiance of the society. It is a 
song by that great rebel saint-poet of the medieval era, Kabir: 

Deceitful world, why do you dazzle us with your eyes? 
Deceitful world! 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 

What is Dalit Literature? Here we will try to define this term as it 
has discursively emerged through the Dalit movement and literature, 
particularly in Hindi. At first sight, it appears that it can simply be 
defined as the literature concerned with the lives and problems of 
Dalits, in which the Dalits are the subjects and protagonists. Then, 
of course, it will include the writings on Dalits by Premchand, 
Nirala, Rangeya Raghav, Amritlal Nagar, Nagarjun, and many 
others in Hindi. And Dalit Literature would resemble any of the 
literary movements such as Romanticism, Realism, Modernism, or 
Postmodernism, or any artistic movement whose membership is 
voluntary and whose corpus is indifferent to the caste, nationality or 
the race of the contributor. 

But Dalit Literature is not a literary movement in the ordinary 
sense of the term. It is, like Black Literature, a product of an identity 
as well as constitutive of that identity. It is an instrument to create, 
reinforce and strengthen the Dalit identity. It is politics by other 
means. According to the Dalit writers and critics, Dalit Literature, 
therefore, is not the literature written by anybody on the Dalits, but 
only by those who are by birth Dalits. Anybody else, not born a Dalit, 
even though writing on the socially downtrodden with sympathy or 
empathy, cannot be considered a Dalit writer, nor will their literature 
be taken as Dalit Literature. Their argument, particularly of those 
writing in the Hindi language, is that only those who are born as 

Premchand and Dalit Literature 191 

Dalits and have undergone the trials and tribulations borne by a 
Dalit can truthfully write about such matters. Literature by others 
may come under the category of sympathetic literature but not Dalit 
Literature. 

Now it is expected that, even though such literature excludes all 
modern varieties of non-dalit writings, it may include the writings 
of the medieval Siddhas and Nath Yogis, particularly of those who 
belonged to lower castes, and the Bhaktas like Kabir, Dadu, Ravidas, in 
Hindi, and Chokhamela, Namdev in Marathi, apart from many such 
others. But, even though they are considered to form part of the pre- 
history of Dalit Literature, the Hindi Dalit writers do not take them 
to be a part of Dalit Literature proper. It is because the latter is also 
defined as the literature informed by ‘Dalit consciousness’. However, 
this “Dalit consciousness’ is not any oppositional consciousness held 
by the Dalits. In the view of the majority of Hindi Dalit writers, it 
should be informed and shaped by the ideas, principles and vision 
of Ambedkar. 

Thus, Dalit Literature comprises the writings by a Dalit on the life 
and struggles of Dalits, imbued with Dalit consciousness according to 
the teachings of Ambedkar. This puts the question of identity squarely 
in the centre of Dalit Literature, and it is an identity of a certain 
kind—the identity around the figure of Ambedkar. 

This identity, the Dalit writers assert, has to be articulated through 
self-representation. The work of representing the Dalits cannot be left 
to others, however much sympathetic they may be to their cause. The 
non-dalits may support the Dalit’s quest for equality and identity, 
but the job of creating and nurturing such an identity should be 
left to the Dalits themselves. They themselves must be the writers 
and protagonists of this drama. Dalit Literature, therefore, cannot be 
understood as a simple literary movement. It is the creation as well as 
creator of Dalit identity.” 

Taken in this context, it is clear that Premchand’s writings cannot 
form part of the Dalit literature as the term has discursively emerged 
in the course of the Dalit movement, literature and criticism. 
However, the hostility which certain Dalit writers have shown 
towards Premchand’s writings is unwarranted. It is indeed surprising 
to hold Premchand responsible for what was not available to him. It 
is true that Ambedkar was active during some of the period in which 
Premchand was writing. It was basically the Gandhi-Ambedkar 

192 Shashi Bhushan Upadhyay 

stand-off which prompted Premchand to become more concerned 
about Dalit issues. In this battle, he took the side of Gandhi. It is 
another matter, however, that nobody in the Hindi literary world, 
including Dalits, was so influenced by Ambedkar as to take up 
writing according to his precepts. It is, therefore, unfair to criticise 
Premchand for not writing in accordance with Dalit Literature even 
before the term had actually taken shape or there was any practitioner 
of that art. Even in the Marathi language, the term became popular 
only during the 1960s and 1970s. During Ambedkar’s lifetime, 
there was no such literary identity. Premchand was among the 
earliest and most concerned writers to take up the Dalit question 
sympathetically. He was sensitive and opposed to the social and 
economic discrimination which the Dalits were suffering. Therefore, 
the tendency to castigate him as anti-Dalit is not only unfair but also 

short-sighted and misguided.
