SECTION C 

Dalits in History 

1] 

Rajwars in Revolt, 1857-58: 
Saga of Heroism and Sacrifices of 
Little-known People 

A. K. Biswas 

The sepoy mutiny created such an upheaval that the foundation of 
the British colony in India was severely shaken. Though the mutiny 
did not assume an all-India character, its impact reverberated 
throughout the country and abroad. The imperial authorities, 
however, received liberal cooperation and material support from a 
section of the Indian community that sustained them. Southern and 
eastern India remained peaceful. Though Bengal witnessed the first 
spark of rebellion at Barrackpore, twenty-six miles north of Calcutta, 
the Bengalis remained aloof as also opposed to the mutinies in upper 
India. Bihar, Central Provinces, Berar and North-western Provinces 
became theatres of the great upheaval. To cite a specific instance of 
loyalty and subservience of the contemporary elite and intellectual 
classes, we may refer to two cases. Poet Iswar Chandra Gupta, 
also editor of an influential Bengali monthly, Sambad Prabhakar, 
composed editorials in verse. denouncing the mutiny and eulogising 
the brutal suppression of the rebels. He even urged the British 
authorities to use harsh and effective measures against the mutineers, 
whom he declared as anti-national. The second case is that of Harish 
Chandra Mukerjee, a doyen of Indian journalism, who edited the 
Hindu Patriot. Like Gupta, he was focused in his editorials’ and 
demanded that the mutineers be crushed and the flames of anti- 
imperial uprisings in upper India be doused immediately. As the 

200 A. K. Biswas 

fire of mutiny engulfed the British across north India, the powerful 
feudal landlords unabashedly exhibited their steadfast loyalty to 
the beleaguered alien power. The uprising had threatened the very 
existence of the empire in the eastern hemisphere. 

Patna Division under martial law 

Bihar turned out to be an important theatre of the mutiny. Martial 
law was clamped on ‘most parts of the province of Bihar on 31 July 
1857. The Patna division encompassing Gaya, Patna, Shahabad, 
Saran, Champaran and Tirhut districts came under army rule’.” This 
suggests the intensity and seriousness of the situation, since no other 
part of British India, it appears, was placed under martial law. 

Garrisons at Danapore, Ramgarh, Sugauli and Gaya witnessed 
intense military movements and activities. The Commissioner 
of Patna Division, William Tayler, highhanded and dictatorial, 
became the originator of most initiatives aimed at quelling, what the 
authorities and the loyal Indians described as, ‘disturbances’. Tayler 
had at his command the powerful landlords of sprawling estates 
like Darbhanga, Bettiah and Hatwa, who exhibited their loyalty 
publicly. 

However, there are rebels who, regrettably, have been elbowed 
out of the historiography of the mutiny reverentially described as 
the first war of Indian independence. But what made the 1857 
uprising against the British Empire unique and extraordinary was 
the participation of common, non-descript men like Pir Ali Khan 
in Patna, and Jawahir Rajwar, Etwa Rajwar and Futteh Rajwar in 
Rajgir. Pir Ali, a bookseller, hailed from Lucknow and rose against 
the colonial authorities with his friends and compatriots. Jawahir and 
Etwa attacked Englishmen in and around Rajgir, which was then a 
part of the Nawada subdivision under Gaya district. The authorities 
were at their wits’ end as to how to confront the Rajwars led by these 
two heroes, who waged guerrilla warfare against the colonial ruler. 
The extreme sacrifices they made, however, have been overlooked 
by Indian historians. Sadly, those who enjoyed the bounties of 
colonial rule by demonstrating loyalty have occupied centre stage 
in independent India’s political, economic, social, and cultural life. 
History has been the first target of their ravages, shutting out chapters 
unfavourable to them. They also ensured that the sacrifices of the 

Rajwars in Revolt 201 

rebels, falling beyond their ambit, did not find a place in the pages 
of history. Hence, the names of valiant and selfless heroes of the first 
war of Indian independence sound strange to our ears. This apathy 
towards selfless common men and women does not augur well for 
any nation. A time might come when the common man responds to 
the call of sacrifices in emergencies. 

Laws enacted for punishment of sepoys 

We should understand the environment that existed when the mutiny 
broke out. It was marked by hostilities and a revengeful attitude on 
the part of the government towards those who were perceived to be 
sympathetic to the rebels. Such men were regarded as anti-colonial 
forces. A proclamation of the Governor General in Council issued 
by the Home Department (bearing no. 1313) of 10 July 1857 in 
The Calcutta Gazette Extraordinary touches upon the mood of the 
unusual time. The proclamation authorises ‘the payment of the 
under mentioned rewards by British civil or military authorities in 
every part of India’: 

1. For the apprehension of any mutineer or deserter from the Native 
Army, or of any person guilty of seducing or attempting to seduce any 
officer or soldier from his allegiance or duty, a reward of fifty rupees if the 
offender be apprehended with arms belonging to the State, or of thirty 
rupees if he be apprehended without such arms. 

2. For information which shall lead to the apprehension of any such 
offender, a reward of fifty rupees if he be apprehended with arms 
belonging to the State, or of thirty rupees if he be apprehended without 
such arms. 

Prior to this, the Legislative Council of India passed an Act (no. XVII 
of 1857) which received the assent of the Governor General on 
20 June 1857 aimed at ‘apprehension and trial of native officers and 
soldiers for mutiny and desertion’. This act authorised punishment 
such as ‘death, or transportation for life’ and ‘death, transportation 
for life, or imprisonment, with or without labour, for life or for any 
term of years’. Further express provision inbuilt therein says, ‘Such 
sentence shall be final and conclusive; and if a sentence of death 
be passed, the same may be carried into execution immediately or 
at such time as such officer (presiding judge) or other person shall 
direct’ (Section I. Act no. XVII of 1857). 

202 A. K. Biswas 

Collection of intelligence is always of critical importance to plan 
as also guide the course of action, particularly in emergencies like 
outbreaks of mutiny and civil war. This helps preparedness and 
pre-emptive strike against the enemies, and such action, if taken by 
surprise, yields maximum gains. Section VI of the act made elaborate 
provisions for this. Men of trust, loyalry and confidence were called 
upon to perform the task of spying against their countrymen: 

All zamindars, talookdars, and other persons who, by Regulation 
V1.1810 of the Bengal Code, are declared to be accountable for the 
early communication of intelligence respecting the resort to their 
estates of the class offenders therein specified, are hereby declared to be 
accountable for the early communication of intelligence of the resort to 
any place within the limits of their estates of any person against whom 
there shall be reasonable suspicion of his having been guilty of mutiny 
or desertion... 

The import and implication of the provision was fatal for the masses. 
The results were disastrous and far-reaching. 

Another Act (no. XXXII of 1857) that received the assent of the 
Governor General on 28 November 1857 was designed to physically 
brand Indians convicted of mutiny or desertion. It was a savage law. 
According to section I, any person convicted of mutiny or desertion 
‘shall be marked on the left side two inches below the armpit with 
the letter M in the case of conviction of Mutiny and with D in the 
case of conviction of Desertion’ for the purpose of ‘identifying the 
offender in future time, or of denoting the punishment to which 
he has been sentenced’. The law was specific about the size of the 
branding mark of either M or D. ‘The letter shall not be less than an 
inch long; and such letter and other mark shall be made on the skin 
with ink, gunpowder, or other preparation, so as to be visible and 
conspicuous and not liable to be obliterated’. 

The branding, as per the provision of the law in force, was required 
for those refractory elements who were not put to death by trial. 
There are historical documents to show that trials of mutineers and 
deserters were perfunctory during the period. 

Was ita draconian law? Drakon or Dracon, an Athenian statesman 
of the seventh century BC, was notorious for his code, which 
provided severe and cruel punishments. Laws enacted by the East 
India Company were barbarous, and the influential sections were 
least bothered about them. They stood mindlessly by the aggressive 

Rajwars in Revolt 203 

colonial authorities and demonstrated their unflinching loyalty 
without any qualms of conscience. 

Rajahs, Zamindars and Talukdars against rebels 

Example is better than precept to nail any nagging suspicion to the 
contrary regarding generous cooperation and support of the feudal 
as well as intellectual classes. We may call to attention two addresses 
that the powerful landlords of Bengal, Bihar and Orissa submitted 
in December 1857 to the Governor General of India, Lord Charles 
John Viscount Canning, expressing a deep sense of gratitude for the 
measures aimed at suppression of the mutiny and solidarity with 
the colonial rulers. Maharajah Sreesh Chunder Roy of Nadia, Rajah 
Pertab Chunder Singh Bahadur and Prasononath Roy Bahadur and 
Baboo Joykissen Mookerjee, representing 5,000 natives of Bengal, 
Bihar and Orissa, submitted thus: ‘We, the undersigned Rajahs, 
Zamindars, Talookdars, merchants, Tradesmen, agriculturists, and 
other natives of the provinces of Bengal, Behar and Orissa, beg leave 
to approach your Lordship in Council with this address expressive 
of our deep sense of gratitude for the several measures of security 
adopted by your Lordship in Council since disturbances have broken 
out in the Upper and Central Provinces of British India, and of our 
admiration for the wisdom, justice and foresight which characterise 
those measures’.” 

The other address signed by Maharajah Mahatab Chund Bahadur 
of Burdwan, Rajah Radhakanta Bahadur and Rajah Kalikrishna 
Bahadur of Calcutta at the head of more than 2,500 Rajahs, zamindars, 
talookdars and merchants extended ‘warmest congratulations on the 
signal success’ of recapturing Delhi from the sepoys.’ They went on 
to state: 

It has been a prominent object with your Lordship both effectually to 
crush the disaffected and rebellious, and to protect and reassure the loyal 
and obedient. Accordingly, the extensive powers of Icgislation vested 
in your hands have been employed to punish crimes of every form and 
magnitude against the state with promptitude and rigour; to check 
vigorously the progress of sedition and disloyalty; and to give guarantee 
to the people at large that those powers would be wielded with justice 
and discrimination, so as to guard as far as possible against faithful and 
innocent subjects being confounded with disseminators of sedition and 
perpetrators of open mutiny or secret treachery.” 

204 A. K. Biswas 

Maharajah Sreesh Chunder and Maharajah Mahatab Chund in 
Bengal were what the Maharajah of Darbhanga was to the people in 
Bihar. Maharajah Sreeesh Chunder of Krishnagar, Nadia, represented 
an ancient lineage. Countrymen have forgotten their nefarious 
collaborative role with an alien power for subjugating the first Indian 
freedom struggle. 

Rajwars in Mutiny 

The Rajwars of Bihar were in the forefront during the mutiny in 
1857-58. A humble people, the Rajwars added an unforgettable 
chapter to the history of India’s freedom struggle by their anti- 
imperialist activities in that period in the areas adjoining Rajgir 
and Nawada, falling in the district of Gaya, . They, however, forced 
themselves on the official despatches for about a decade from 1857 
for reasons which call for a deeper understanding. The Rajwars were 
daring and fought the British fiercely. They were well aware who 
their enemies were. The British and their local collaborators dubbed 
the Rajwars as dacoits and badmashes. Classified as semi-Hinduised 
aboriginal in the colonial document, the Rajwars numbered 53,106 
in 1872. Gaya district alone accounted for 39,484, i.e. 75 per cent 
of the caste.’ 

The feudal landlords and the intellectuals, by and large, sided with 
the imperial forces during the country’s first freedom struggle. In his 
Discovery of India (1946), Jawaharlal Nehru throws light on their 
nefarious role. According to him, “The villain of the British in India 
is often the hero to Indians, and those whom the British have delight 
to honour and reward are often traitors and quislings in the eye of 
the great majority of the Indian people. That taint clings to their 
descendants’.” This might be true when the alien power ruled the 
country. In free India, the descendants of the quislings and traitors 
have changed colours, assumed the roles of benefactors, and occupied 
the centre stage of social, cultural and political life. The villains in the 
eyes of the British suffered dehumanisation till their last day. The 
new dispensation did not reverse the trend and place history in the 
proper perspective, according dignity to those who staked everything 
for overthrowing the alien domination and rule. If the assessment 
of Nehru was correct, the Rajwars got their recognition for their 
heroic struggles against the British during the mutiny. Educationally 

Rajwars in Revolt 205 

backward and economically weak, they till date exist at the bottom 
of the socio-economic hierarchy. The unique sacrifices made by them 
have not enhanced their status in the eyes of their countrymen. Nor 
have their neighbours taken note of them. 

We may run through the heroic exploits of the Rajwars during the 
tumultuous days of the mutiny. 

The Deputy Magistrate of Nawada, J. T. Worsely, in a letter 
reported to the District Magistrate in October 1857, ‘I have burnt 
the village of Tajpore. Before doing so I searched it but nothing was 
found. I returned through the village of Khrunpore where Etwa 
Rajwar, Sardar dacoit and rest resided. Not a soul in the village, 4 brass 
utensils were taken, I have also burnt this village. These two villages 
were entirely inhabited by Rajwars and this will prove an example, 
which was much wanted in this part of the district’. The Deputy 
Magistrate further records his satisfaction of the job accomplished. 
‘I am happy to say 36 Rajwars were captured with some women and 
children’, says Worsely. “These were released and few succeeded in 
effecting their release. Amongst these Rajwars were two barkendazes 
of the new police, who deserted after the commencement of the late 
disturbances’.’ We have already noted that the government of the day 
passed draconian laws for punishing deserters, either sepoys from 
the army or constables from the police; despite this, two Rajwars 
deserted. 

Wilson, another Deputy Magistrate, who visited Nawada, informed 
the District Magistrate, Gaya, on 24 September that he found the 
place quiet. He reported that from enquiries made it appeared to him 
that ‘the band of men under Jawahir Rajwar near Manjhi Nadeerganj 
have all left and fled into the Rajgir hill jungle. Another leader Etwa 
Rajwar had also fled into the hill near Govindpur’. He referred to 
one Muraee band and another Karampur band under Etwa Rajwar 
who were moving with arms. He was told that there were about 
fifty matchlock men and some of the discharged sepoys. Is it not 
spectacular that socially degraded Rajwars were the leaders of the 
discharged sepoys? The leadership and strategy of the Rajwar heroes 
were decidedly of superior quality; why else did deserters from the 
native army join them against the powerful? Wilson informed that he 
had:sent out spies all around to get information. He further reported 
that the zamindars of e/aka Sautar were disinclined to afford much 
aid. The zamindars of Nawada however gave a list of men. Wilson 

206 A. K. Biswas 

mentions, ‘I have sent out spies to glean all the information they can 
on their return, then | shall take into consideration the advisability 
of attacking them or not with the means at command which in my 
humble opinion are insufficient and not to be depended upon in the 
hour of need. You will perceive on perusing the accompanying list of 
men supplied by the zamindars and present at this moment having 
taken muster [sic]. In this list are mentioned Phool Singh, Mahadeo 
Singh of Sautar, Millind Singh of Ahrunpur, Maharani Rookmund 
Koomari of Maksudpur, etc”."” 

Another letter dated 29 September 1858 by the same Deputy 
Magistrate is shriller in tone and aggressive as also perverse in tenor. 
He wrote, ‘As none but severe measures will keep the Rajwars in 
check and hunting them down in the hills and jungles, to which 
they invariably take after committing any depredation would be next 
to impossible, I would suggest that the whole of the houses of the 
Rajwars in three above mentioned villages be razed to the ground 
and their property (should there be any) confiscated to Government. 
Such an example may probably frighten those of other villages’."' 
These villages were Sukerpur, Barhat and Govindpore. Barbarism 
and brutality of the authority were designed to be demonstrative 
enough in instilling terror and trauma for generations in the area 
among those who did not toe the British line. This is the cardinal 
principle of alien rule. 

The Rajwars were conspicuous by their powerful presence in 
the despatches of high officials in the wake of the sepoy uprisings 
in 1857-58. The Commissioner of Chhota Nagpur, E. T. Dalton, 
reputed for his in-depth ethnological insight and works, in a letter 
dated 31 August 1859, addressed to the Secretary to the Government 
of Bengal, referred to the Rajwars in Nawada. He recommended 
that a police battalion under an officer vested with adequate powers 
should be employed to coordinate with the Deputy Magistrate of 
Nawada in rooting out the Rajwar gangs. It was mentioned that in 
spite of many precautions the Rajwars were still active. They moved 
about the jungles and carefully avoided the places where there were 
troops. Dalton further advised that the zamindars should be made 
responsible for the conduct of those men, as otherwise it was difficult 
to put them down.” The Deputy Magistrate, soon after his posting 
at Nawada, in letter no. 156 of 21 October 1859 reported to the 
District Magistrate that there were two gangs headed by Etwa and 

Rajwars in Revolt 207 

Futteh Rajwar, notorious ‘badmashes’.. He recommended that the 
zamindars be held responsible for these rwo men and they should 
keep the jungles and hill tracts free from them. It was further 
suggested that the zamindars be made to pay the value of the amount 
of property plundered within their respective estates. 

Over 10,000-strong police force deployed 
for Etwa Rajwar 

Organised attempts by the administration to capture Erwa Rajwar 
did not prove successful. He was beyond the reach of the combined 
forces comprising 8,000 to 10,000 men provided by district police 
and zamindari militia of Gaya, Hazaribagh, and Monghyr. The 
administration proclaimed a reward of Rs 200 for Erwa Rajwar. 
But none proved equal to the brave and intelligent Rajwar leader. It 
appears no information in the desired direction, leading to his arrest, 
was supplied to the authorities. 

As the Rajwars had their residence in the range of hills from Bugsotee 
in the west to Kowakhol in the east, which was detached from other 
hills, the District Magistrate drew up a comprehensive plan to scour 
the whole range with a huge force. The administration believed they 
would be captured in a body with arms and ammunitions and their 
conviction under Section 400 of the Indian Penal Code would be 
possible. The District Magistrate of Gaya, J. S. Drummond obtained 
the cooperation of the Tikait of Sattgaon and his Diwan, and 
Phool Singh of Sautar. All the principal zamindars were invited to 
assemble on a pretext of a shikar party. The zamindars and the police 
of Hazaribagh were also called in for assistance. In order to remove 
suspicion of the stratagem from the minds of the gang the District 
Magistrate shifted his camp from the immediate neighbourhood of 
the range of hills which formed the retreat of the robbers to Rajauli, 
some twelve miles off. 

The campaign against Etwa began on 8 April 1863 from three 
directions—east, west and south. The first wing of the army under 
the District Magistrate of Gaya launched an attack from the western 
extreme of Kowakhol. The second army, which was under the 
Superintendent of Police of Monghyr, took charge of the eastern 
direction of the Bugsotee hill. The third wing comprising the men 
furnished by the zamindars attacked from the south, i.e., Hazaribagh. 

208 ) A. K. Biswas 

The forest of the hill was thoroughly scoured with 8,000 to 10,000 
forces. Etwa Rajwar dramatically eluded the combined forces pressed 
for his capture. The campaign failed to catch the Rajwar heroes. 

Ultimately, Drummond acknowledged his failure to the 
Commissioner of Patna Division in a letter (no. 67) of 9 May 1863. 
Therein he detailed the strategy adopted by the administration for 
capture of the gang and its leader. Deployment of a 8,000—10,000- 
strong force to nab one dacoit is without parallel in the history of 
this country. A dacoit does not get so much attention from the 
authorities. The despatches of the officials do not indicate the nature 
and extent of his depredations. Such concerted action involving a 
large force under the District Magistrates and Superintendents of 
Police underlines that Etwa Rajwar and his compatriots Jawahir 
and Futteh Rajwar fought against the British Empire and this made 
Erwa a target for liquidation. This along with the reward on his head 
indicates the importance of the Rajwar leader. It is regrettable that 
Indians have failed to recognise him and his compatriots. 

Explaining the reasons for the failure of the campaign, Drummond 
attributed it to the double-dealing of Phool Singh of Sautar. The 
District Magistrate lamented that “The failure of the measure was 
due to the mutual contract between the principal zamindars and the 
robbers’. In some cases such contract involved the security of life and 
property of the zamindars and the immunity from prosecution of the 
robbers. The contract though primarily based on fear was also made 
for pecuniary consideration for the protecting zamindars, as the gang 
protected figuratively was said to hold such and such villages in thika; 
the relation between the parties was that of receiver of stolen property 
and thief. The same class of beneficiaries of loot was instrumental in 
slapping charges of criminality against the low-caste men to promote 
and safeguard their personal interest. 

The District Magistrate says that most of the robbers belonged 
to the Rajwar tribe, who had been active in the hills mentioned 
since the turbulent days of the mutiny. These Rajwars, he felt, had 
their residence in this part of the hills but habitually belonged to 
the nomadic race, and lived on plunder and rapine. It was this 
gang which usually committed raids in the districts of Gaya and 
Hazaribagh. The pilgrims and travellers of Deoghar frequently fell 
victims to these robbers when they passed through the hill ghats via 
Govindpur. This gang formed the nucleus of the force of robbers 

Rajwars in Revolt 209 

and thieves residing in that portion of the district. When a dacoity 
was to be committed, the information was sent to a certain number 
of Rajwar villages for mustering the requisite forces for attack. The 
booty was divided, and the zamindar under whose jurisdiction the 
dacoity was committed received four annas (i.e., one-fourth) as a 
share and also had the advantage of purchasing the looted property 
at a minimum price. In fact, the zamindars were fountainheads of 
crimes and patrons of criminals for they had deep interest in the vice. 
Their selfish motive kept the crime alive and kicking. This explains 
the long life and powerful presence of criminals in total disregard for 
all efforts for their suppression. 

‘The strength of the gang was not more than sixty, when they were 
put to death by the poisoning of the toddy pots on the trees by someone 
of their own party. Erwa Rajwar, for whom a reward of Rs 200 was 
proclaimed, resided with his family in Phool Singh's estate. 

The District Magistrate proposed further steps to deal with the 
Rajwars and their accomplices. A list of the names of the robbers 
was to be obtained and a special inquiry was to be made to 
ascertain the character of the suspected men. The second plan was 
to corner one of the accomplices of the gang, obtain the necessary 
information through him, and then seize the whole gang in the act 
of commission of dacoity. Preventive measures were to be adopted 
on the unrestrained movements of the gang and its accomplices in 
the plains. A night patrol was ordered around the foothills from the 
Sukri river to Kowakhol. Three stations for the sowars were suggested: 
one at Sautar, another at Nawada, and the third at a village opposite 
Kowakhol. Under Section 15 of the Police Act, an extra body of police 
was quartered at the villages with the worst repute at Kurrumpur, 
Bhutta and Degunwan Koordeh, Lekkurpur and Sautar. A police 
chawki was also established at Bagher at the foot of the hill. These 
measures were calculated to effectively control the activities of the 
robbers." 

The extraordinary time has no parallel in Indian history, at least 
in the colonial period. The British soldiers, as also the authorities, 
were bloodthirsty. They were not content to stop at anything less 
than bloody retributions. Kendal Coghill provides a glimpse of 
unparalleled savagery inflicted on innocent people by the British 
soldiers. ‘We burnt every village and hanged all the villagers who 
had treated our fugitives badly until every tree was covered with 

210 A. K. Biswas 

scoundrels hanging from every branch’.’’ Sanity had taken a back 
seat. Without any pretence, a twentieth-century British historian in 
a frank statement throws light on the nature and quality of trials of 
the accused of the mutiny: ‘The trials—when trials were held—were 
often mere formalities. Officers were heard to swear that they would 
hang the prisoners whether or not they were found to be guilty; and, 
after being condemned to death as a matter of course, prisoners, 
mutineers and rebellious villagers and escaped convicts from the 
gaols were sometimes tortured before their execution by the private 
soldiers whose officers did not intervene’. ® 

No comments are necessary as to the gross violation of the principle 
of fair trial and the severity of the punishment. It was marked by 
vengeance and arrogance. 

Were Etwa and his compatriots dacoits? 

A dacoit does not engage attention of the kind we have noticed above. 
Moreover, specific instances of crimes committed by the Rajwars are 
missing from despatches of the Deputy Magistrate, Nawada. This 
sounds almost incredible and casts doubt on the charges of dacoities 
against the Rajwars. If we look at the contemporary Indian brigand 
Veerappan, the matter becomes clear. The desperado was reported 
in the media as having committed heinous crimes, which included 
killing innocent people, destruction and smuggling of valuable 
sandalwood worth crores of rupees from the forests of Tamil Nadu 
and Karnataka. He kidnapped a Kannada political leader (who was 
killed) and a popular matinee idol. These charges were broadcast over 
and over again across the country. A cash reward of over Rs 5 crores 
was announced for his apprehension. The governments of both Tamil 
Nadu and Karnataka had made several unsuccessful attempts before 
nailing him down. But the deployment of 8,000—10,000 men under 
the leadership of superintendents of police of three districts overseen 
by the district magistrates underlines the point that the Rajwar heroes 
were not mere dacoits. Since they challenged the imperial authority, 
the government of the day could ill afford to overlook the challenges 
thrown to the Empire. 

While the Rajwars were fighting tooth and nail against the British 
and embracing death, the rich and influential collaborators threw 
themselves headlong in currying favour with the authorities. They 

Rajwars in Revolt 211 

went against their countrymen despite their full knowledge about 
the savagery perpetrated on them. Lack of intellectual honesty 
prevented historians from focusing on the perfidious elements and 
bringing the betrayal to the notice of every individual. Else, how 
come holy men of one of the most sacred shrines kowtowed betore 
beef-eating mlechchhas (impure white ‘barbarians’)? And it remained 
under a thick veil of secrecy. Hardly anybody cared to focus on it. 
Alonzo Money, District Magistrate of Gaya, makes this disclosure in 
his letter of 1 August 1857 to the Secretary to the Government of 
Bengal: “This morning a deputation from the Gayawals came here. | 
told them that I could not secure their safety without [sic] they give 
me means to do so. They have promised, in conjunction with the 
zamindars, to supply some 3,000 or 4,000 men; but these priests are 
an indolent race, and | much doubt any useful fulfilment of their 
promises. Meanwhile danger is probable’.' Just one day prior to their 
sacred mission and pledge made solemnly to the District Magistrate, 
the Government had clamped martial law on Gaya together with 
Patna, Shahabad, Saran, Champaran and Tirhut districts. The gayawal 
is a priest, or spiritual guide, who performs the ritual of pinda or 
obsequies at Gaya for a dead Hindu. Total surrender of these holy 
men before the British officer is not only highly intriguing, but also 
raises many searching questions about their pro-colonial proclivity. 
We have quoted what Nehru said about the forces that opposed 
the British as well as those who supported them. The destiny of the 
nation is fraught with grave danger if the countrymen do not know 
their heroes as well as their villains. Men who make sacrifices for the 
nation are like lighthouses in the dark horizon; such men are few. 
Allowing the villains to capture centre stage is to place the country in 
the perennial danger of subjugation by anti-national forces. Why do 
Indians not take note of this glaring reality? 

A profile of the Rajwars 

Who are the Rajwars? What is or was their social. cultural and 
economic standing in the eyes of their neighbours or countrymen? 
Why have they been denied recognition though we, as a nation, 
respect men of eminence or sacrifice? These are questions that merit 
a close look. 

212 A. K. Biswas 

One school of anthropologists is of the view that the Rajwars 
belong to the same stock as the Musahars. Educationally backward 
and socially degraded like the latter, the Rajwars are a Scheduled 
Caste of Bihar as well as West Bengal. Bihar is their home but they 
adopted the neighbouring state when they migrated there in search of 
livelihood in the nineteenth century. There were 2,80,889 Rajwars in 
Bihar in 1991, of whom 1,46,460 were males and 1,34,429 females. 
A mere 17,795, or 6.76 per cent Rajwars were from urban areas, the 
overwhelming balance being rural. The urbanisation of any people, 
community or class determines its strength and status in the socio- 
cultural milieu. Notwithstanding the complexities of urban life, the 
recent trend in the country is to adopt urban life and settlement. 
Opportunities for better education, health care and security being 
ample, urban centres hold out promise to the people. 

If a class of people is too deeply ensconced in a rural area, its 
inevitable characteristic is one of educational and social backwardness. 
The Rajwars exemplify this fittingly. Empowerment of any people 
comes through education (as distinct from literacy). The literacy 
rate of the Rajwars is a lowly 13 per cent (1991); of the 2,63,094 
Rajwars in the rural areas, 2,28,767 were illiterate. In other words, 
34,327, or only 13 per cent, were literate. Of the handful of literates, 
9,883 persons were ‘without educational level’.'” If these persons 
are excluded, the percentage of literacy of the Rajwars plunges to 
10.6 per cent. According to an India-centric study conducted by 
UNESCO in 1952, it was emphasised that any education less than 
four consecutive years makes no impact on the person, and even if 
shown to be literate, he is no better than an illiterate. The position 
has not changed even now and showing persons without educational 
level in the company of literates is hardly justifiable. 

Data pertaining to the literacy of the Rajwars is presented in 
Gable 11.1. 

Table 11.1 indicates that migration to urban areas has not made the 
Rajwars educationally progressive. The literacy rate in urban areas is 
26.9 per cent, which is about 2.5 times more than that in rural Bihar. 
But if we exclude literacy without educational level, the literacy rate 
among the urban Rajwars drops to 19 per cent. Significantly, of 756 
graduates, 238 were in urban and 518 in rural areas. One of every 
372 persons of the caste was a graduate. Female literacy among the 

Rajwars in Revolt 

213 

Rajwars was quite precarious. Of the 1,34,429 females, only thirty- 

nine were graduates.

As many as 71,478 persons—48,343 males and 23,135 females— 
were returned as agricultural labourers.” Incidentally, thirty-two 
male graduates were agricultural labourers. 

It is seen that 135 graduates, six technical diploma and nine non- 
technical diploma holders of the caste were unemployed when the 
census was taken in 1991.”' Table 11.2 shows that the caste was 
primarily rural. Their dependence on the tertiary sector was little. 
They were mainly engaged in the primary sector of the economy, as 
any other backward class people in the country. About 20,733 persons 
were returned as cultivators and 71,478 agricultural labourers. It 
is significant that half of the labour force working as agricultural 
labourers were women. The caste’s presence in manufacturing and 
processing, construction, trade and commerce, transport, and 
storage was insignificant. Until they are employed in a knowledge- 
based industry or occupation, the Rajwars and other similar people 
will continue to be backward, socially as well as economically. They 
could be uplifted, therefore, by formation of human capital. This 
is possible with equitable educational opportunities irrespective of 
caste, creed, place of birth or sex. Until education is imparted to 
every citizen of India, prosperity of the nation will remain elusive. 
The domination of a handful of people is never beneficial for the 
well-being of the country. Many of the ills confronting the nation 
may be attributed to the manipulation and machination of a small 
number of people, whose interests are at variance with those of the 
common man.
