NAW RWN 

15 

Social Exclusion, Resistance and 
Deras: Exploring the Myth of 
Casteless Sikh Society in Punjab 

RoONKI RAM 

Introduction 

The recent violent clashes (May 2007) between followers of the Dera 
Sacha Sauda (syncretic religious centre established in 1948 with its 
headquarters in Sirsa, Haryana) and different groups of Sikhs, and 
also other kinds of social conflicts between jat' Sikhs and Dalits’ in the 
state, mark a crucial turn in the political history of Punjab. The cause 
of these conflicts goes beyond the much talked about ‘short-term 
politics of revenge’ and shows up the deep socio-religious hierarchies 
in the so-called casteless Sikh society in Punjab. On the one hand, 
they lay bare the dormant structures of social discrimination that 
permeate the fabric of Sikh society and, on the other, point towards 
the neoconservative Sikhs’ anxiety about the Sikh-Khalsa identity. 
They pose a serious challenge not only to the political stability in the 
state but also to the institutions of democracy in India. 

The Akali—Dera Sacha Sauda row over the dera chief Gurmeet Ram 
Rahim Singh's imitation of the iconography of Guru Gobind Singh 
seems to have much to do with the prevalence of the doctrinally- 
rejected system of caste hierarchy among the Sikhs. The rising 
salience of the latter within the Sikh panth (society) has disillusioned 
the Dalit Sikhs, who at one point of time had embraced Sikhism in 
the hope of escaping social exclusion imposed on them by the Hindu 
‘varna vyavastha’ (social order). This seems to push them towards the 

274 Ronki Ram 

deras and other non-Sikh socio-religious organisations that promise 
dignity and social equality. Literally meaning a holy abode free from 
the bindings of institutionalised religion, a dera is the headquarters 
of a group of devotees owing allegiance to a particular spiritual or 
holy person, revered as Baba, Sant or Maharaj; a dera thrives on a 
distinct philosophy, rituals and symbol(s) inspired by the teachings 
of that holy person. The majority of the followers of various Sacha 
Sauda-type deras come from the Dalit families. This near-exodus 
of Dalits from Sikhism towards the alternative socio-spiritual space 
provided by the deras invites the hostility of clerics of the established 
mainstream religious order, who see it as a serious challenge to the 
Sikh—Khalsa identity. Moreover, the politicisation of the deras and the 
accompanying pontifications further complicate the issue. Persistent 
attempts by various Sikh organisations to win over disgruntled Dalit 
Sikh followers of the Dera Sacha Sauda during the recent Akalis— 
Dera crisis is a case in point. 

This paper is divided into four sections. The first examines the 
Sikhs—Deras crisis in the larger context of Dalit assertion and its 
implications for religion-based politics in Punjab. The second section 
deals with the phenomenon of caste and caste hierarchy within the 
Sikh panth, and the place it assigned to Dalit Sikhs. The patterns of 
jat Sikh domination in Punjab and how it forced the Dalits to seek 
a separate caste identity is discussed in the third section. The fourth 
section, based on ethnographic research in rural Punjab, documents 
some of the recent jat-Dalit conflicts in the state. 

I 

Sikhs-Deras Crisis in a Larger Context 

Punjab has the distinction of being home to the largest proportion 
(29 per cent) of scheduled caste (SC) population in the country 
with the lowest share in the ownership of land (2.34 per cent of 
the cultivated area). The SCs in Punjab belong to different religions 
and castes. Mazhabis and ramdasias, the two Dalit castes among the 
Sikhs, particularly the former, are the most deprived. They embraced 
Sikhism in the hope of gaining social equality, but even in the new 
religion untouchability continued to be practised against them. 
Evidence of untouchability against Dalit Sikhs is vividly reflected 

Myth of Casteless Sikh Communities 275 

in the Khalsa Dharam Sastra of 1914 (Oberoi 1994: 106) and in 
a number of resolutions adopted by the Shiromani Gurdwara 
Prabandhak Committee (SGPC) from 1926 to 1933 (Singh 1933 
as quoted in Puri 2003: 2697). Although the Sikh reformers of the 
late nineteenth and early twentieth centuries preached in favour of 
removing untouchability, no concerted ettorts were made in that 
direction. Social opprobrium continues to afflict Dalits. Some of 
them feel that jat Sikhs treat them as badly in the gurdwaras as they 
do in their farmlands (Tehna et al. 2007). They have been forced 
to live in separate settlements, contemptuously called ‘thhattis’ or 
‘chamarlees’, located on the western side and away from the main 
body of the villages. They have no say in the local structures of 
power. All the Sikh organisations like gurdwaras, Sikh deras, SGPC, 
and Shiromani Akali Dal are under the control of the jat Sikhs. In 
a recent empirical study of the caste background of the members 
of the SGPC, conducted by Narinderpal Singh, it is found that 80 
per cent of its administrative posts are under the control of the jat 
Sikhs, 15 per cent under other castes and only 5 per cent are with 
the Dalit Sikhs (mazhabis and ramdasias). All the three current 
secretaries of the Shiromani Committee are jat Sikhs. Out of its six 
additional secretaries three are jat Sikhs, one is Labana Sikh and two 
belong to Other Backward Castes (Singh N 2007a). Dalits are often 
heard complaining that the jat Sikhs refused to consider them equal 
even after death by disallowing cremation of their dead in the main 
cremation ground of the village. This has forced them to establish 
separate gurdwaras, ‘janjghars’ (marriage centres) and cremation 
grounds.” It is against this backdrop of social exclusion that a large 
number of Dalits have been veering away from the mainstream 
Sikh religion and enrolling themselves in various forms of non-Sikh 
deras in Punjab. Another probable cause behind the large-scale Dalit 
alignment with the deras could be the absence of a strong Dalit 
movement in the state. 

However, the phenomenon of deras is not new in Punjab. Rather 
it is as old as the Sikh faith. During the period of the historical gurus, 
different deras of udasis, minas, dhirmalias, ramraiyas, handali, and 
massandis sprang up. Those earlier deras were primarily the outcome 
of the disgruntled and unsuccessful attempts of the ‘fake’ claimants to 
the title of guru (for details see: Chaturvedi 1951: 360-69; Marenco 
1976: 28-30; Bingley 1970: 85-93; Archer 1946: 221-226; and Grewal 

276 Ronki Ram 

1996: 39-46). Apart from these, there were many other deras that came 
up at different intervals during the long and tortuous consolidation of 
the Sikh religion. Some of the prominent among them were the Bandei 
Khalsa (Bandapanthis), Nanakpanthis, Sewapanthis, Bhaktpanthi, 
Suthrashahi, Gulabdasi, Nirmalas and the Nihangs (Chaturvedi 1951: 
361-69; Mcleod 1984: 121-33). What distinguished those earlier 
deras from the contemporary ones is that they could not become the 
centres of Dalit mobilisation. 

Proliferation of Deras 

According to a study conducted by the Desh Sewak, a Punjabi daily 
published from Chandigarh, there are more than 9,000 Sikh as well 
as non-Sikh deras in the 12,000 villages of Punjab (Tehna et al. 
2007). In Sikh deras, Sikh rahit (code of conduct) is observed strictly 
whereas in the non-Sikh deras, different ritual practices are followed. 
The Radha Soamis, Sacha Sauda, Nirankaris, Namdharis, Divya Jyoti 
Sansthan, Bhaniarawala and Ravidasis are among the most popular 
non-Sikh deras. Almost all of them have branches in all the districts 
of the state as well as in other parts of the country. Some of them are 
very popular among the Punjabi diaspora and have overseas branches 
in almost all the continents of the world. The composition of these 
deras is along caste lines. Though a majority of the followers in every 
case come from Dalits, backward castes and poor Jat Sikh peasantry, 
their command is still in the hands of the upper castes (Muktsar 
2007). Among them the chief of the Nirankari deras belonged to the 
khatri caste, and those of the Sacha Sauda and Radha Soamis come 
from the jat Sikhs of the Sidhu and Dhillon sub-castes, respectively. 
In the case of Sikh Deras, most of the members belong to the jat 
Sikh community and they are run by jat Sikhs; it is rare that the head 
of a Sikh dera would be a non-jat Sikh. Even if there would be one 
he could never be a Dalit. At the most, Dalit Sikhs’ participation 
in Sikh deras is confined only to various kinds of menial services 
as well as the narration of the sacred text (Guru Granth Sahib) and 
performing of kirtan (musical rendering of sacred hymns) (for 
details, see: Ram 2004a: 5-7). Those who perform kirtan are known 
as raagis, the professional narrators are designated as granthis and 
others who render menial service are called sewadars. The majority 
of the three categories are Dalit Sikhs. Very few jat Sikhs take up 
such occupations. In the Sikh deras, only gurubani of Guru Granth 

Myth of Casteless Sikh Communities 277 

Sahib is recited. But in the non-Sikh deras, along with the recitation 
of gurubani from Guru Granth Sahib, other sacred texts are also 
referred to. Idol worship and devotion towards a human guru are 
also not an anathema in non-Sikh deras. It is due to the presence 
of such non-Sikh practices that the phenomenon of non-Sikh deras 
has been described by Meeta and Rajivlochan as the ‘alternate guru 
movement in Punjab’ (Meeta and Rajivlochan 2007: 1910). 

Challenge to Khalsa Identity 

This alternate movement in Punjab with its ‘loose syncretistic 
practices throws a formidable challenge to Sikh-Khalsa identity. 
Modernity and apostasy are its two other main adversaries (Swami 
and Sethi 2007; see also Singh Madanjeet 2007). Modernity is 
considered to be corrupting the young Sikhs who have become 
lackadaisical in their observance of the Khalsa principles advocated 
by Guru Gobind Singh, the tenth master of the Sikh faith, who 
established the Khalsa as a military order of ‘saint-soldiers’ on 
30 March 1699. Though Bhindranwala tried to assert the Sikh- 
Khalsa identity by opposing a dissident sect of the Nirankaris and 
preaching hatred against the Hindus (K. Singh 2007b), he could 
not prevent the movement of Dalits towards non-Sikh deras. These 
deras, in fact, pose an even more serious challenge to mainstream 
Sikhism. The number of followers of these deras seems to ‘far exceed 
that of the Golden temple-based clerical establishment’ (Swami and 
Sethi 2007). It is in this context that confrontation between the 
deras and mainstream Sikhism assumes critical importance with far- 
reaching implications for the relationship between Dalits and jat 
Sikhs in Punjab. The jat Sikhs of Punjab are primarily an agricultural 
community, the dominant caste in the state. The Sikh empire of 
Ranjit Singh and the subsequent British rule over Punjab helped 
in establishing their hold over the land in the state (Marenco 1976: 
Chps IV-VII; see also Liu 1982: 387-95). Dalits, on the other 
hand, were deprived of the ownership of land under the Punjab 
Alienation of Land Act (1900). This along with the absence of other 
job avenues forced them to work on the land of the jat Sikhs for 
their livelihood. The Dalits’ relationship with the jats, thus, is that 
of landless agricultural workers vis-a-vis landlords, which in turn 
has led to contradictions between them. The two communities are 
engaged in a power struggle. 

278 Ronki Ram 

However, there are many Dalits in Punjab who have improved 
their economic conditions by dissociating themselves from their 
caste occupations as well as distancing themselves from agriculture 
(Jodhka 2004). They have strengthened their position through 
sheer hard work, enterprise, and ventures abroad. Some of them 
have also established their own small-scale servicing units, and work 
as carpenters, barbers, blacksmiths, masons and so on (for details 
see: Ram 2004a: 5—7). In addition, they have also been politicised 
to a large extent by the socio-political activities of the Ad Dharm 
movement (for details see: Juergensmeyer 1988; Ram 2004), and 
the various Ravidass Deras (Qadian 2003). Thus, they have not only 
improved their economic status but have also liberated themselves 
from the subordination of the jat landowners. 

With an improved economic position and a sharpened sense of 
social consciousness, Dalits in Punjab started expecting a higher 
social status that has also probably pushed them closer to the 
alternative religious bodies promising dignity and social equality. In 
the process, they also challenged the dominant caste and its claims 
to represent true Sikhism. The jat Sikhs, however, interpreted it as a 
challenge to the Sikh—Khalsa identity, which further deepened the 
existing contradictions between them and the Dalits. That is what 
has led to a series of violent caste clashes between Dalits and jats in 
Punjab in the past few years, as also the repeated confrontations, viz., 
crises in 1978 (Nirankaris), 2001 (Bhaniarawala) and 2003 (Talhan) 
between the Akalis and followers of one or the other non-Sikh deras. 
While Akali is a word derived from the Punjabi ‘Akal’ meaning 
‘beyond death/the Timeless One’, Khalsa, literally ‘pure’, refers to the 
collective body of all baptized Sikhs; Guru Gobind Singh gave this 
name to all his disciples baptized in the ‘Amrit Sanchar’ ceremony. 
The confrontation between the Akalis and the premis of the Dera 
Sacha Sauda is the most recent case in point. These clashes seem to be 
more about identity politics between jat Sikhs and Dalits than a row 
over religion. They are in no way a manifestation of communalism 
in the state. However, given the religious milieu of the social sphere 
in Punjab they often assume a communal aspect. They, in fact, are 
signs of an emerging Dalit assertion against social exclusion that 
have all the possibilities of snowballing into violent conflicts if left 
unresolved. 

Myth of Casteless Sikh Communities 279 

What we have argued above is that the root cause of various jat- 
Dalit conflicts lies in the structures of social exclusion, and the rising 
process of Dalit assertion and the resistance it encounters. Why the 
Dalits, despite their conversion to Sikhism and better economic 
conditions, failed to overcome their lower social status and how that 
pushes them towards deras and also fomented confrontation between 
them and jat Sikhs is what we will delineate in the next section. 

il 

Sikhs, Caste Hierarchy and Dalits 

Punjab is a Sikh (63 per cent) majority state. Around 60 per cent 
of the Sikh population consists of the jat Sikh caste (Joginder Singh 
1997: 178-9; Puri 2003: 2693). Moreover, jat Sikhs as a single largest 
caste constitute roughly one-third (30 to 33 per cent) of the total 
population of the state. They also wield control over land, religion 
and politics in the state (Pettigrew 1978; Singh Gobinder 1982; 
Muktsar 2007; Subhash 2004). Being agriculturalists, almost all of 
them live in villages (over 90 per cent). Dalits, who constitute as 
much as 29 per cent of the total population of the state, which comes 
close to the number of the jat Sikhs, are another major community 
heavily concentrated (over 80 per cent) in the villages. Moreover, 
in districts like Nawan Shahr, Muktsar, Jalandhar, Faridkot, and 
Hoshiarpur the proportion of Dalits in the total population is as 
high as 40.46, 37.75, 37.69, 36.17, and 34.28 per cent, respectively 
(Census of India 2001). In many villages the population of Dalits 
exceeds 50 per cent, particularly in the Doaba region of Punjab 
consisting of Jalandhar, Kapurthala, Nawan Shahr and Hoshiarpur 
districts. From this, it can be inferred that villages in Punjab are 
predominantly jat and Dalit (cf. Taylor, Singh M and Booth 2007: 
331; Puri 2004: 90). Caste constitutes an integral part of village 
life (Kaur 1986: 229). In an agrarian society like Punjab where 
the two major communities of jat Sikhs and Dalits live in extreme 
contrast of afHuence and deprivation, caste seems to assume critical 
importance. Although the Sikh doctrine does not assign any place 
to the institution of caste, and laid stress on the brotherhood of all 
irrespective of caste, religion, gender and occupation, it must not be 
inferred, as argued by Paramjit S Judge, ‘that Sikhism was able to 

280 Ronki Ram 

transform the caste structure into an egalitarian moral community 
of the Sikhs. Sikhism remained far from a casteless society’ (Judge 
2002: 184; see also: Marenco 1976; Mcleod 1996; Puri 2003). In the 
Punjab censuses between 1881 and 1931, more than 25 castes were 
recorded within the Sikh community, including jats, khatris, aroras, 
ramgarhias, ahluwalias, bhattras, sainis, labanas, lohars, kambohs, 
mahatam, chhimbas, nais, ramdasias, jheers, mazhbis, and rangretas 
(Verma 2002: 33). Out of these, eleven—two agrarian castes (jat and 
kamboh), two mercantile castes (khatri and arora), four artisan castes 
(tarkhan, lohar, nai, and chhimba), two outcaste groups (chamar and 
chuhra), and one distiller group (kalal, also termed as an artisan caste 
in Marrenco 1976: 176)—are considered to form the core of the 
‘caste constituency of the Sikh panth (McLeod 1996: 93-94). Except 
the mercantile caste of khatri, all other castes including the jats fall in 
the shudra (artisans) and the ati-shudra (untouchables) categories of 
the Hindu social order. In other words, Sikhism has primarily been 
the religion of the dispossessed and the plebeians (Singh 1989: 288; 
Grewal 1998: 205). 

Practice of Caste Endogamy 

The issue of caste within the Sikh panth has been affected by the 
attitude/behaviour patterns of the converts (Mcleod 1996 84; see 
also Grewal 1998: 197). The converts continue to follow their 
previous caste practices regarding connubium, or inter-marriage, and 
commensality even after receiving the ‘paul’ (Sikh form of baptism) 
(for details see: Cunningham 1849; Mcleod 1996; Marenco 1976; 
Singh 1977; Singh 1976). They strictly follow the principle of caste 
endogamy. As Sewa Singh Kalsi argues, ‘Inter-caste marriages are 
strongly disapproved by the sikhs’ (Kalsi 1999: 260). In a study of 
caste endogamy among the Sikhs in India and abroad, it is found 
that marriage alliances are sought from the same caste communities. 
In the matrimonial columns in the Indian press, it is found that 
ninety-four advertisements in the column of jat Sikhs and 120 in the 
non-jat Sikh category sought marriage alliances from within their 
own caste communities. In the UK, out of thirty-five matrimonial 
advertisements taken up for content analysis, it was found that 
twenty-four jat Sikhs, three khatri Sikhs, two ramgarhia Sikhs, two 
arora/khatri Sikhs, one saini Sikh, and three others invited marriage 
alliances from their own caste communities (Kalsi 1999: 261). The 

Myth of Casteless Sikh Communities 281 

wide prevalence of intra-caste endogamy among Sikhs in India as 
well as in the diaspora is further stressed in a recently concluded 
ethnographic study covering the jat Sikh community in Newcastle 
(UK) and the Doaba region of Punjab (Taylor, Manjit and Booth 
2007: 341). 

However, there are some scholars who argue that in comparison 
to the orthodox Hindu caste system, the principle of caste endogamy 
is ‘a little weaker among the Sikhs (Puri 2003: 2698; Singh 1989: 
293). This is fine, but given the class and caste background of the Sikh 
families involved in inter-caste marriages, it can be argued that they 
are more common among the marginalised jat Sikh peasants. Even 
in such cases, the normal practice is that ‘the jats willingly accepted 
women from the lower castes, but showed no inclination to give 
their daughters to them’ (Judge 2002: 180; see also: 1. Singh 1977: 
72; Walia 1993: 220). Still what distinguished the phenomenon 
of caste within the Sikh society from that of its counterpart in the 
Hindu religion, argues Jagjit Singh, is that inter-caste marriages are 
neither considered sacrilegious nor are ‘... visited by penalties such 
as those imposed by the caste ideology’ (J. Singh 1989: 293). On 
the contrary, the opposition in that regard, if any, is more to do 
with ‘prejudice/’honour’ than ‘pollution. To quote him further, 
'... intermarriages are prevented by sentiment and not by hard and 
fast rules’ (Jagjit Singh 1989: 293). Prejudice versus pollution apart, 
the phenomenon of caste, irrespective of its form, is very much alive 
within the Sikh society. Even in matters of commensality within the 
panth, the picture is not bright, particularly in the case of mazhabi 
Sikhs. Reflecting on the distinction between the ‘caste’ and ‘outcaste’ 
members of the panth, Joyce Pettigrew observed: “The only custom 
in which any solidarity was expressed among the jats on a caste basis 
was that in the village they did not visit the houses of mazhabis, 
take food from them, eat with them or intermarry with them’ 
(1978: 44; see also: Grewal 1998: 210; Bains and Johnston 1995: 
48). Although the jajmani/sepidari (patron—client relationship) 
mode of production—the prominent socio-economic structure in 
Punjab based on traditional patron—client relationship—has ceased 
to exist of late, the dominant castes still considered the artisan and 
menial Sikh castes as chhotian-jatan/ nikia-minia-jatan (low castes). 
In this regard, it is appropriate to quote Izmirlian: ‘Master (teacher) 

Gurdial Singh was born a Ramgarhia in 1915. The reality of his caste 

282 Ronki Ram 

identification surrounds him like a shroud because Ramgarhias are 
carpenters and viewed as menials by jat Sikh agriculturists’ (as quoted 

in Kalsi 1999: 259). 
Caste Hierarchy in Sikh Panth 

Thus, in addition to the convergence of ‘caste constituency’ and 
‘attitude’, a sort of distinct caste hierarchy has cropped up within 
the panth (Marenco 1976; McLeold 1996; Murray 1970; cf. Gurdev 
Singh ed. 1986; Jagjit Singh 1981; ibid. 1985; ibid. 1989; Grewal 
1998). In the Sikh caste hierarchy, the jats who were otherwise 
assigned a low status in the Hindu social order claim to occupy the 
top position (I. Singh 1977: 70; see also Judge 2002: 178-85). Next 
to jat Sikhs comes the khatri Sikhs who belonged to the mercantile 
caste to which also belonged all the ten gurus of the panth (Marenco 
1976: 296; Singh Bhagat 1982a: 146-47; Alam 1982: 103-07). The 
ramgarhia (former tarkhan/carpenter) and ahluwalia (former kalal/ 
distiller) Sikhs are placed next or even equal to the khatri Sikhs due 
to their military adventures during the ‘mis’ period (from 1767 
to the end of the eighteenth century; almost the entire territory 
between the Yamuna and the Sindh rivers was ruled by twelve Sikh 
Jathas or groups, the region ruled by each Jatha being called a misl). 
(Mcleod 2000: 216-34). In descending order, Marenco argues that 
the other agricultural Sikh castes, like the kamboh, mali and saini, 
the other trading Sikh castes, like the arora, and the other artisan 
Sikh castes, like the lohar or sunmar, came somewhere after the 
aforementioned castes in the hierarchy. Then there were the Sikh 
menial castes (jhinwars, kahars, banjaras, labanas, bahrupias, batwals 
and barwalas), and, last of all, the Sikh untouchables, the ramdasias 
and mazhabis, who ranked lowest despite the many advances they 
had made since conversion to Sikhism (Marenco 1976: 296,; see 
also: Puri 2003: 2698). 

Dalit Sikhs are further divided among themselves along caste 
lines. Ramdasia Sikhs considered themselves superior to the mazhabi 
and rangreta Sikhs. 

Another dimension of the Sikh caste hierarchy is the distinction 
between the sahajdhari (non-baptised) and the keshdhari (baptised) 
orders of the panth. The sahajdhari-keshdhari dichotomy makes the 
Sikh caste hierarchy rather complex. The keshdhari Sikhs, also known 
as Khalsa, are generally considered superior to the sahajdharis. But 

Myth of Casteless Sikh Communities 283 

this is not true in the case of the mazhabi Sikhs who, despite their 
being Khalsa, are still considered inferior to the sahajdhari. The 
practices of endogamy and commensality are considered to be more 
closely observed by the sahajdhari Sikhs in comparison to the Khalsa 
(Marenco 1976: 43, 50, 63, 64-65, 153 and 157: Judge 2002: 180 
and 184). Burt this viewpoint seems to be contested by Rashpal 
Walia who observed that ‘nihangs (saint-soldier/immortal) with 
upper caste background don’t partake of food cooked for those with 
mazhabi Sikh origin... Most important, the “amritpan” ceremony for 
the mazhabi Sikhs among the nihang is also separately performed’ 
(Walia 1993: 219 and 250). 

Scholarship is sharply divided over the patterns of caste hierarchy 
within the panth. Some scholars are of the opinion that the practices 
of brahminic ritual purity do not hold any ground in Punjab 
(Ibbetson 1883, rpt 1970: 1-87; Jagjit Singh 1989: 291-97). In 
Punjab the defining principles of caste hierarchy are different from 
that of the brahminical. They are based on hard manual labour on 
ones own land, caste homogeneity, martial strength, numerical 
preponderance in the mainstream Sikh religion, and hold over the 
politics in the state. Furthermore, it is the complex combination of 
all these different sources of power (social, ethnological, economic, 
political, religious, and numerical) that determines the status of the 
constituents of the Punjabi society. The only caste in which all these 
multiple identities coalesce is that of jats in Punjab. Jats are jat Sikhs 
by caste, khalsa or Singh by religion, martial by virtue of their being 
sturdy, an important part of the armed forces in the past and even 
present, and landowners by virtue of their hold on cultivation.” All 
these different identities reinforce each other and thus help jat Sikhs 
to become the dominant caste in Punjab. Such a rare combination of 
powerful multiple identities and their concentration in a single caste 
- as well as religion is conspicuous by its absence among the Dalits, 
who, interestingly, are almost equal to the jat Sikhs in terms of their 
numerical strength in the state. Unlike jat Sikhs, Dalits are divided 
into thirty-eight castes, observing different religions (Hinduism, 
Sikhism, Christianity and Buddhism), mostly landless, economically 
marginalised, socially oppressed and politically neglected. It is in 
this context of extreme disparity between the otherwise numerically 
comparable communities of jat Sikhs and Dalits that the distinct 
pattern of caste hierarchy assumes critical importance in Punjab. 

284 Ronki Ram 

This unique but often overlooked phenomenon of numerical near 
parity and extreme socio-economic disparity is what brought these 
two communities in open confrontation with each other, on the one 
hand, and also forced the Dalits to seek refuge in various deras in the 
state, on the other. 

On the other side of the debate, there are some scholars (Jagjit 
Singh 1989; Gurdev Singh 1986) for whom caste hierarchy within 
the panth is a misnomer. Jagjit Singh was of the opinion that the 
phenomenon of caste hierarchy stands on three pillars (viz., caste 
ideology; brahmins, at the head of the Varna system, a four-fold 
division of the social set-up, further categorized on the purity/pollution 
criterion; and the caste-society) of the brahminic orthodoxy. Since 
none of them is found within Sikhism, it is absurd to talk about caste 
and caste hierarchy in the panth (Jagjit Singh 1989: 281). Moreover, 
almost all the castes of the ‘Sikh caste constituency’ have been 
able to enhance their social status Jagjit Singh 1981; Judge 2002: 
184). The mazhabi and the ramdasia Sikhs are the only exceptions. 
Despite their lowest rank in the Sikh caste hierarchy, even mazhabis 
and ramdasias consider themselves superior to their counterparts in 
the Hindu caste system. Although ramdasias have originated from 
chamars, they consider themselves superior to the latter (Ibettson 
(1883) 1970: 297, 302; Bingley 1970: 62; Marenco 1978: 130 and 
285-86; Jagjit Singh 1989: 296). Similarly, mazhabis too consider 
themselves socially superior to their erstwhile community fellows 
(balmikis) with whom they would not fix marriages (Walia 1993: 226). 

Though social mobility among the caste constituency of the panth 
is often referred to as one of the major achievements of the new 
religion, which facilitated some of the shudra castes even to acquire 
the status of dominant castes in the state, that was not the sole aim 
of the panth of Nanak and his nine followers, who provided a clear 
vision and also worked meticulously for the creation of an egalitarian 
social order free from the structures of caste and caste hierarchy. But 
soon after the end of the period of the gurus, some sort of caste 
hierarchy emerged within the panth, with the jat Sikhs occupying 
the top position and the Dalit Sikhs at the base. Intermarriages 
and inter-dining are among the prime tests for the dismantling of 
the caste system. But on both these accounts the Sikh society has 
failed miserably, particularly in the context of the Dalit Sikhs. As 
far as inter-dining among the jat, khatri, ramgarhia, ahluwalia, and 

Myth of Casteless Sikh Communities 285 

other artisan Sikh castes are concerned, one can definitely talk about 
the phenomenon of the social mobility among them all. However, 
barring khatris, there was not much difference between the social 
positions of the jats (dominant castes) and those of the other artisan 
castes even betore their conversion to Sikhism. They were all clubbed 
together in the category of the shudras, and commensality was not 
a taboo for them. In fact, what matters the most in this context 
is the change in the status of the Dalit Sikhs and their relations 
with the dominant castes within the panth. They really present a 
tough problem (Mcleod 2000; Grewal 1998: 208). In matters of 
commensality, clear distinctions are made between the ‘caste’ and 
‘outcaste’ members of the panth (Grewal 1998: 210; Walia 1993: 
203 and 233). The dominant castes (jats, khatris and ramgharias) 
continued to identify the ramdasia, rangreta and mazhabi Sikhs (the 
‘outcaste’) by their earlier titles—chamars and chuhars (Ibettson 
(1883) 1970: 268-69). “They are still not tolerated within the main 
halls, and are forced to sit separately in a corner at the entrance of 
the gurdwaras. Among them also sat even the baptised mazhabi and 
ramdasia Sikhs (Bhullar 2007). In her field-based doctoral study of 
the ‘Problem of Untouchability among Sikhs in Punjab’, Rashpal 
Walia found that ‘mazhabi Sikhs feel that their status in Sikh society 
is still lowest... though, they are not removers of night soil’ (Walia 
1993: 264, 266-67). 

What we have argued so far is that caste hierarchy does exist 
within the panth. Mazhabis and ramdasias are continuing to face 
discrimination on grounds of caste. This is probably one of the major 
reasons behind their move towards the deras as well as confrontation 
between them and jat Sikhs. 

iil 

Patterns of Dalit Subordination 

Power in Punjab revolves around the pivot of land. Much of the 
available agricultural land (more than 80 per cent) Is owned by 
the jat Sikhs, while an overwhelming majority of the scheduled caste 
population (over 95 per cent) is landless. They owned only 4.82 per 
cent of the number of operational holdings and 2.34 per cent of the 
total area under cultivation (1991 Census). In other words, despite the 

286 Ronki Ram 

fact of their significant proportion in the population of the Punjab, 
a very small number of them (less than 5 per cent) are cultivators 
(1991 Census). Nowhere in India are Dalits so extensively deprived 
of agricultural land as in the Punjab. This converted a majority of 
them (60 per cent, 1991 Census) into agricultural labourers and 
made them subservient to the landowners, who invariably happen to 
be jat Sikhs. The hold of the jats on the land was so strong that Dalits 
were never considered part of villages. Their residences were located 
on the outskirts of the villages. So much so that the land on which 
the Dalit houses were built was also considered to have belonged to 
the jats (Virdi 2003: 2 and 11). This used to keep the Dalits always 
in fear lest the jat landowners ordered them to vacate the land. The 
abysmally low share of the Dalits in the land seems to be the major 
cause of their hardships and social exclusion. It is also an indication 
of the historical denial of human rights to them (Thorat 2006: 2432). 
The slightest sign of protest by the Dalits for the betterment of their 
living conditions often provoked the jats to impose social boycott 
on them (for an excellent account of social boycott see Bharti 2007: 
5-21; Judge 2006: 12). 

Green Revolution and Jat—Dalit Confrontation 

The existing division between the jats and Dalits got further deepened 
during the course of the green revolution in post-1960 Punjab. It 
transformed the traditional subsistence character of the agriculture 
into commercial farming. The market-oriented agriculture favoured 
the landowners and further marginalised the Dalits (Gill 2004: 225- 
40). Interestingly, it was during this phase that a new middle class 
of educated Dalits emerged in Punjab. This coupled with the rise of 
the Ambedkarite movement in the region led to the formation of 
Dalit consciousness in the state which induced the Dalit agricultural 
labourers to ask for higher wages in rural Punjab, especially in its 
Doaba sub-region. The struggle for higher wages often employed 
pressure tactics of refusal to work unless the landowners increased 
the wages. In fact, it was during this very phase of transition in the 
agrarian economy of Punjab that the process of Dalit migration to 
Europe, North America and the Gulf assumed great importance. 
However, it may be pointed out in passing that the emergence of 
the process of Dalit emigration from Punjab coincided with the 
phenomenon of the influx of migrant labour from Bihar and eastern 

Myth of Casteless Sikh Communities 287 

Uttar Pradesh (Sidhu et al. 1997). That influx further sharpened 
the contradiction between the dominant peasant castes and the 
landless Dalits in that it provided cheaper labour to the former 
compared to the local ones. Moreover, the changed cropping system 
under the green revolution squeezed the demand for farm labour 
to a few peak periods of activity—paddy transplantation, paddy 
harvesting-cum-threshing, and wheat harvesting (Bhalla 1987). The 
traditional agriculture system, capable of providing almost round-the- 
year regular work, was changed into a commercial agriculture set-up 
that did not offer more than seventy-five days’ work annually (based 
on fieldwork calculations). The Dalits now had to seek employment 
in other sectors for the rest of the year. 

Thus, the Dalit labourers, sandwiched between the influx of cheap 
migrant labour on the one hand and mechanised farming on the 
other, began to look for jobs in sectors other than agriculture. The 
alternative job opportunities reduced the dependence of the Dalits 
on landowners. The social mobility of the new middle-class Dalits 
based on their relative emancipation from dependence on agricultural 
labour along with their subsequent diversification into the service 
sector facilitated the emergence of Dalit assertion in Punjab that 
brought them in open confrontation with the dominant caste in the 
state. The phenomenon of Dalit-dominant caste confrontation draws 
heavily on the prevalent structures of social discriminations and the 
politicisation of caste in the state (Judge 2006: 11). The next section 
deals with the phenomenon of Dalit contestation of social exclusion 
and the resistance offered by the dominant caste in the state. 

IV 

Social Exclusion and Resistance in Punjab 

The recent cases of Dalit social exclusion in the form of publicly 
announced social boycotts are not a new phenomenon in Punjab. 
Dalits had been subjected to such humiliation for a long time. Social 
boycotts were imposed on them during their heroic Ad Dharm 
struggle in Punjab in the early decades of the twentieth century 
(for details see Ram 2004: 332-35). Social exclusion continued to 
afflict them even after India became independent. The frequency 
and intensity of atrocities against them increased manifold during 

288 Ronki Ram 

the green revolution as a reaction to the growing Dalit assertion for 
better working conditions and higher wages (Singh 1980; Sidhu 
1991; Gill 2000). It is generally seen that whenever the dispossessed 
raise their voice for their rights, they face severe hardships. 

Rarely a day passes in Punjab when Dalits are spared a social boycott 
by the jat Sikhs in the villages over the last few years. After the much- 
publicised violent conflict in a village called Talhan, Punjab has been 
witness to a series of similar cases. The pattern of conflicts in all such 
cases often remained the same as it was during the green revolution 
phase. In almost all the conflicts, social boycott was imposed on 
the Dalits who were asserting for a share in the local structures of 
power such as partnership in the village common lands, membership 
in the management committees of the religious bodies, entry into 
the panchayati raj institutions, etc. Pandori Khajoor village in 
Hoshiarpur district, village Bhattian Bet in Ludhiana district, Talhan, 
Meham and Athaula villages in Jalandhar district, Patteraiwal village 
in Abhor district, Jethumajra and Chahal village in Nawan Shahr 
district, Aligarh village near Jagraon in Ludhiana district, Domaeli 
and Chak Saboo villages in Kapurthala district, Dhamiana in Ropar 
district, Abuul Khurana village near Malout in Mukitsar district, and 
Dallelsinghwala, Kamalpur and Hasanpur villages in Sangrur, and 
Jhabbar village in the district of Mansa are among the prominent 
cases of jat—Dalit conflicts in the state. The most recent one involved 
clashes between the various groups of Sikhs and the premis of the 
Dera Sacha Sauda in different parts of Punjab. 

However, the Sacha Sauda row is not the only one of its kind. 
An almost similar crisis also rocked the state in 2001, when another 
baba, popularly known as Piara Singh Bhaniara of the Dhamiana 
village in Ropar district, imitated the tenth guru not only by wearing 
a shining coat and headgear but also by riding a horse in a similar 
style. Furthermore, Bhaniara ‘insisted that his sons be addressed as 
“sahibzadas” in the manner of title used to address the sons of the 
gurus’ (Meeta and Rajivlochan 2007: 1191). He also managed to 
produce a separate granth (Bhavsagar Samundar) running into 2,704 
pages parallel to that of the sacred text of the established mainstream 
religion in the state. All this was enough to spark off a series of violent 
clashes between the followers of the mainstream religion and that of 
the sect of the Bhaniarawala.’ Bhaniara, himself mazhabi Sikh, draws 
a large majority of his following from the mazhabis. Almost all the 

Myth of Casteless Sikh Communities 289 

bigwigs in the political corridors of Punjab, particularly union minister 
Buta Singh, his nephew and former legislator Joginder Singh Mann, 
former Akali MP Amrik Singh Aliwal, six-time legislator and former 
Punjab minister, Gurdev Singh Badal, and his son Kewal Badal, 
who supported his sect also belonged to the mazhabi caste. Since 
the majority of the followers of Bhaniara belonged to the mazhabi 
caste, and that of the mainstream Sikh religion to the jat Sikhs, the 
crisis turned out to be an identity conflict berween the Dalits and the 
dominant caste in the state of Punjab. 

Bhaniarawala Phenomenon vs Sacha Sauda Row 

Though ‘the Bhaniarawala phenomenon’ and the Sacha Sauda row 
look similar in many ways, in the volatile territory of realpolitik in 
Punjab they seem to attribute different meanings to the sharpening 
contradictions between the Dalits and the dominant castes. During 
‘the Bhaniarawala phenomenon there was almost a consensus among 
the various factions of the Akali Dal over the course of action to 
be taken against the indicted Bhaniara, while in the case of Sacha 
Sauda there was a difference of opinion among the various groups 
of the Akalis as to the nature of agitation for the arrest of its chief as 
well as the form of punishment. It is, perhaps, for the first time in 
the history of the Akalis that such a difference of opinion emerged 
among them. It also indicates that the moderates and also some 
of the radical groups within the Akalis are concerned more with 
maintaining the hard-earned peace in the state than to bargain it at 
any cost for some sort of long-term political gain. Another unique 
aspect of the present crisis is that the Hindu population of the state 
played a positive role in condemning the sacrilegious act of the head 
of the Sacha Sauda. But still, the Dalit—jat Sikh equation remains the 
focal point of the crisis. In fact, it is this equation with which the real 
_ question of peace in Punjab is tagged. Unless and until an amicable 
and a durable solution to this fast emerging jat—Dalit confrontation 
is found, Punjab is probably bound to plunge into a deep crisis at a 
time when the Sikh religion ‘evinces the need for a reassertion’ and 
Dalit assertion goes global. 

The following sub-sections briefly explore the probable underlying 
causes of the recent caste-based conflicts in Punjab. For paucity of 
space, only the Talhan and Meham conflicts are taken up as two 
ethnographic case studies. In both the cases, the bone of contention 

290 Ronki Ram 

has been the management of the local deras. In the case of Talhan, 
Dalits were denied participation in the managing committee of the 
‘smadh’ (grave)-turned-gurdwara, whereas in Meham, Dalits were 
forced to surrender their control over the Udasi Dera of Baba Khazan 

Singh. 
Talhan 

The Talhan conflict was based on the issue of Dalit representation 
in the jat Sikh-dominated management committee of the smadh- 
turned-gurdwara named after Shaheed (martyr) Nihal Singh, a local 
carpenter (backward caste) who died while laying a gandd (wooden 
wheel) at the base of a well in Talhan village. The primary motive 
behind the conversion of the smadh into a gurdwara was widely 
seen as an effort to grab the large amount of money (approximately 
Rs 50 million or $ 1.1 million offered at its altar by the jats of the 
village and the adjoining areas) (Philip 2003). The jats of Talhan 
(25 per cent), who control most of the agricultural land in the village 
and until recently enjoyed unquestioned domination in the social 
and political life of the village, established their control over this 
gurdwara through the office of the gurdwara management committee. 
Despite being a majority in the village, the Dalits of Talhan (72 per 
cent) were kept out of the membership on the gurdwara management 
committee because of their lowly caste (conversation with Ram 
Talhan, Banga, 16 April 2006). They employed every possible 
method to seek entry into the committee peacefully. But the dispute 
remained unresolved. This ultimately led to a fight between the jats 
and the Dalits in January 2003. Subsequently, the jat Sikhs publicly 
announced a social boycott of the Dalits. They stopped visiting their 
shops and also banned their entry into the fields owned by them 
even for answering the call of nature (conversation with L. R. Balley, 
Banga, 16 April 2006). 

To fight the social oppression, a Dalit action committee (DAC) 
was organised under the leadership of L. R. Balley, a prominent 
Ambedkarite of the region. The DAC organised dharnas and hunger 
strikes in the village and Jalandhar city. Repeated appeals by the DAC 
failed to move the administration (Singh 2003). On 5 June 2003, 
the conflict took a violent turn. And soon it spread to the adjoining 
areas. Boota Mandi, a suburb of Jalandhar city, became the epicentre 
of the violence. It was here that an Ad Dharmi, Vijay Kumar Kala, fell 

a > —\SaIaTICO([TV DLT sii as» 

Myth of Casteless Sikh Communities 291 

victim to police firing, an event that suddenly propelled Talhan and 
Boota Mandi onto the national scene. They were virtually converted 
into a garrison. And the village was sealed off for a couple of days. 
Although the violence was controlled by the district administration, 
it took the contending parties eighteen days to reach a compromise, 
and another two months tor the agreement to come into effect. 

Whatever be the cause of the violence, it is clear that by the time 
it erupted, the Dalits had achieved a state of consciousness that not 
only empowered them to say a firm ‘no’ to their tormentors but also 
encouraged them to ask assertively for an equal share in the structures 
of power at the village level. In contrast, the jat Sikhs, who have 
thrived amidst a meek silence of the Dalits, are finding it hard to 
resist the mounting Dalit assertion against the centuries-old system 
of social exclusion (for more details see Ram 2004b: 906-12). 

Meham 

The Meham conflict is another case of recent jat—Dalit confrontation, 
and a vindication of the presence of caste hierarchy among the Sikhs. 
The village Meham has a total population of 1967 out of which 893 
(45 per cent) belong to the Dalits. Most of the latter belong to the 
balmiki caste. The Ad Dharmi, another Dalit caste, constitutes 20 per 
cent of the total population (Judge 2006: 14). The jat Sikhs are about 
20 per cent of the total population of the village. Jats, balmikis and 
the Ad Dharmis each have their own gurdwara in addition to the 
disputed dera of Khazan Singh Udasi in Meham. Dalits in Meham 
are diversified into various non-cultivation professions that has not 
only helped them abandon their customary caste-based occupations 
but also liberated them from the subordination of the jat Sikhs. 
However, despite the dissociation of the Dalits from their hereditary 
professions and distancing from agriculture, these failed to raise 
their social status in the eyes of the jats, who still consider them 
inferior. This has led to tensions between jat Sikhs and the Dalits in 
the village. 

Though the context of the Meham conflict is different from that 
of the Talhan one, the patterns and the forms of social oppression are 
the same in both. In Talhan, the Dalits were denied membership in 
the jat-dominated management committee of the disputed shrine- 
turned-gurdwara of Shaheed Nihal Singh. In Meham, the jat Sikhs 
forcibly took over the control of the dera of Khazan Singh Udasi 

292 Ronki Ram 

that was being looked after by the Ad Dharmis of the village for six 
decades. They replaced all the udasi symbols with that of the Khalsa, 
and also objected to the offerings of liquor and the distribution of 
the same as prasad (sacramental food) among the devotees at the dera 
as it violates the Sikh code of conduct (conversation with Darshan 
Nahar, Nurmahal, 25 October 2006). 

The Ad Dharmi argued that the tradition of offering liquor at the 
smadh in the dera is in no way an act of sacrilege as the dera was 
never a site of Sikhism. They reiterated that the jat Sikhs brought 
the question of Sikh rahit, or code of conduct, into the picture in 
2003 when they placed Guru Granth Sahib on the premises of the 
dera. Moreover, the very presence of the mazaars (graves) in the 
precincts of the dera ruled out the possibility of its being a gurdwara 
(conversation with C. L. Chumber, Jalandhar, 25 October 2006). 
Probably, the rising cost of land in the state and the tremendous 
increase in the offerings at the dera over the last few years due to 
ample remittances from the Punjabi diaspora, especially from the 
doaba sub-region, could have prompted the jat Sikhs of the village to 
assert their claim on the dera (Kali 2003). However, unlike in Talhan, 
the timely intervention of the police brought the Meham conflict 
under control for the time being and the dispute has been referred to 
the court. Currently, the dera is placed under a government receiver 
who has been assigned the task of the management of the shrine. 

The conflicts in Talhan and Meham, thus, reflect the underlying 
layers of tensions between the hitherto all-powerful and dominant 
jat Sikhs, and the newly emerging assertive class of Dalits. Given 
the rising level of social consciousness among Dalits, the dominant 
caste has been finding it difficult, if not impossible, to ignore their 
demands for a share in the local structures of power. 

Conclusion 

This paper argues that the jat—Dalit confrontation is an offshoot 
of the socio-religious and economic chemistry of Punjab. The 
root cause, perhaps, lies in the perception of Dalits about the 
incompatibility between the egalitarian social ethos of Sikhism and 
the manifestation of social exclusion in the dealings of the dominant 
jat Sikh community in the state. The Dalit Sikhs find that they are 
still considered as the ‘other’. The domination of jat Sikhs, however, 

Myth of Casteless Sikh Communities 293 

does not compare at all with the graded system of brahminical caste 
hierarchy. They became dominant because of their ‘patient vigorous 
labour’ as cultivators par excellence, caste homogeneity, martial 
status, control over the land, numerical preponderance in the Sikh 
community, and their hold over the power structures in the state. 
Dalits, equally sturdy and hard-working as well as numerically 
quite close to the proportion of the jat Sikhs in the state, continue 
to face social exclusion in spite of their conversion to Sikhism and 
relative improvement in their economic conditions. Their social 
exclusion coupled with landlessness and political marginalisation 
appears to be the major factor behind their move towards various 
deras promising dignity and social equality. However, the increasing 
level of Dalit assertion, benefits of afhrmative action, remittances 
and diversification in the realms of the economy have given them 
a strong sense of equality, inspiring them to assert for a share in the 
local and state power structures albeit facing stiff resistance put up by 
the dominant caste. This has created a sort of fault line indicative of 
violent confrontation between the dominant and the downtrodden 
in the state, long boasted of as a casteless society.

Glossary 

Aam sabha: Village assembly 

Abhanga: A form of devotional poetry produced by the saint-poets of 
Maharashtra. 

Ad dharm: Original religion 

Agraharam: Place or street where usually only the Brahmans live. 
Ahluwalia: A Sikh caste, originally Kalals or brewers of country liquor. 
Ajam: Iran 

Ajlaf: Mean, lowly, of humble birth 

Amil: Revenue collector 

Amin: Official for land measurement 

Amritpan: A process of initiation into the Sikh religion. 

Aql: Sense 

Arzal: The very lowest on the social scale 

Ashraf: Noble-born, respectable 

Azadi: Freedom 

Balmiki: A sweeper by caste 

Balutedari: A system of interrelationship in rural Maharashtra where 
twelve major backward and Dalit castes, called balutedars, were responsible 
for the village work and were paid something in return, sometimes including 
a piece of land. 

Banda khalsa (Bandapanthis): Followers of Banda Bahadur (1670- 
1716) who confronted Vazir Khan near Sirhind in 1710 and killed him. 
Bania: Grocer, tradesman or shopkeeper 

Bari: Cultivable part of the homestead land 

Barkendaz: A guard who usually escorted prisoners 

Begaar: Forced, unpaid labour 

Bhadralok: Gentlefolk, used for the upper castes in Bengal 

Bhakri: Bread 

Bhaktpanthi: A follower of the Bhakti order 

Glossary 299 

Bhaniarwala: Name of a controversial, self-styled holy person who 
belonged to a Dalit community in Punjab. 

Bhoodan: Land donation 

Bhuinya: The lowest caste groups among Dalits 

Biradari: Creed 

Chamar tola: A settlement of Chamars, a Dalit caste 

Chamarlee: Dwelling place of Dalits on the outskirts of a village, 
particularly in the Doaba region of Punjab, comprising Hoshiarpur, 
Jalandhar, Kapurthala and Nawanshehr districts. 

Chanderi: A town in Madhya Pradesh, from where famous hand-woven 
clothes are given the same name. 

Chaudhary: The head of a village, may also be used as a surname or title. 
Chauhaddi: Boundary 

Chawki: Police post 

Cheri: Place in town/city where mostly the Dalits/Scheduled Castes live. 
Daru: Liquor > 

Dera: Religious enclosure 

Dhimmi: A believer, one who belongs to any of the religions that Islam 
recognises, whose adherents are the ‘people of the book’. 

Dhirmalia: A follower of Dhir Mal, son of Gurditta and eldest grandson 
of Guru Hargobind, the sixth Sikh Guru. 

Divya jyoti sansthan: A religious sect in Punjab 

Dopatta: Long stole or scarf 

Farman rawa: Ruler 

Farmandeh: Officer 

Figh: Islamic jurisprudence 

Fugaha: Islamic jurists 

Gairmazarua aam: Common public land 

Gairmazarua khas or malik: Government land previously under private 
ownership. 

Gaonki: Of village 
Gayawal: Literally, inhabitants of Gaya in Bihar, but used for a specific 
endogamous community of Brahmans in Gaya. 

Granthas: Religious texts among Hindus, Sikhs, Jains and Buddhists, 
also books in general. 

Gulabdasi: Followers of Sant Gulab Das (1809-73) 

Hadith: Preaching of Prophet Mohammad 

Hadola: The piece of land traditionally given to a Mahar in lieu of the 
village service performed by him. 

Handali: Followers of a sect 

Harijan tola: Settlement of the Dalits 

Ihtiram-I-admiyat: Respect for humanity 

\ 

300 Glossary 

Ijtihad: Divine law 

Iman: Faith 

Intihai: Utmost 

Isnad: Attribution 

Jagir: Estate 

Jagirdar: The owner of an estate 

Jajmani: Patron-client relationship, an arrangement under which low 
castes provided goods and services to their high-caste clients. 

Jalsa: Traditional musical concerts chiefly of Maharashtra which were a 
mode of assertion and impetus for the anti-caste movement. 

Jamaadar: ‘The head constable 

Janeyu: A consecrated thread supposed to be worn by upper-caste Hindus 
to symbolise their twice-born status. 

Janjghar: Large house for marriages 

Jati: Caste 

Kafa’a: Legitimacy of marriage 

Kafir: A non-believer 

Kalal: A caste of brewers of country liquor also known as Ahluwalia. 
Kali Yuga: The fourth and the worst age in the ever-recurring cycle of 
Hindu cosmology consisting of four epochs. 

Kamia: Bonded labourer 

Kamiauti: System of bonded labour 

Kannaku Pillai: One who keeps the village land and other records. 
Kanthi: A necklace of beads worn around the neck for a religious 
purpose. 

Karmchari: Village panchayat level revenue official 

Kattak kaalu: The leg of a pig 

Keshdhari: A baptised Sikh 

Khalsa: The religious order instituted by Guru Gobind Singh on Baisakhi 
Day (first day of the Indian New Year) probably in 1699. 

Khamkhab: Silk cloth woven with gold thread 

Khatian: Legal record of land ownership 

Khatri: An upper /trader caste 

Khellat: Literally, a robe, it was a presentation of honour in pre-colonial 
India. 

Kili: A parrot 

Kili josiyar: A person who using a trained parrot predicts the future of 
another person. 

Kirtan: Devotional singing of sacred hymns 

Kula deivam: Family or clan god 

Lawani: A popular folk dance of Maharashtra 

Lota: A metallic vessel to carry water used in rural households. 

Glossary 301 

Maazlela: A spoilt lad 

Mahant: = The head of a Hindu religious institution known as Math. 
Maharwada: Place or street where usually the Mahars, people of a 
Scheduled Caste, live. 

Majdoor: Worker 

Maldar: Rich 

Malik: Landlord 

Mangal kavya: Literally, poems of benediction, these are a group of 
medieval Bengali Hindu religious texts containing narratives of local deities 
of rural Bengal amalgamated with some gods and goddesses of the canonical 
Hindu pantheon. 

Manuski: Of human beings/humanism 

Marakkal: A cylindrical iron container used for measuring the quantity 
of grains. 

Maravar mahan: Son of a person belonging to the Maravar caste. 
Masavat: Equality 

Massandis: Followers of a Masand (surrogates of the Guru, instituted by 
the fourth Sikh Guru, Ram Das). 

Mast: Really good 

Matn: Text 

Mawali: Muslim non-Arabs 

Mazahib: Religion, Islamic law 

Mazhabi: Sweeper converted to the Sikh religion. 

Mina: Follower of Pirthi Chand, eldest brother of fifth Sikh Guru Arjun 
Dev, who was a disappointed contender for the title of Guru. 

Misl: A territorial order guarded by warrior Sikhs during the middle and 
later decades of the eighteenth century. 

Mlechha: A barbarian, usually denoting people who did not conform to 
the morals and ways of living of the Vedic people. 

Mudi: A woman who does not conform to patriarchal values of the family 
and punished by her husband who chops off her nose. 

Mukhia: Elected head of a village panchayat. 

Mukkulathor: People belonging to three backward castes in Tamil Nadu, 
viz., Kallar, Marvar and Ahambadiyar, known for committing heinous 
“crimes against Dalits. 

Mutassarif: Auditor 

Nalla: A rivulet 

Namdhari: A devotee of Guru Ram Singh of the Kukas order. 
Nanakpanthis: Devotees of Guru Nanak, term commonly used for the 
entire pre-Khalsa Sikh Panth or for those post-Khalsa Sikhs who do not 
follow the Sikh code of conduct. 

Nasb: Lineage 

302 Glossary 

Naukri-pikri: A job 

Nautanki: A type of folk-drama on themes from myth or legend. 
Nihangs: Determined warriors among Sikhs. 

Nirankaris: A Sikh religious sect giving importance to living human 
gurus. 

Nirdos Jiv: Innocent being 

Nirmalas: Devotees of a Sikh sect who wear saffron robes, and observe 
celibacy. 

Nusuz: Obeisance to (male) authority 

Padi: A relatively small cylindrical iron container used to measure the 
quantity of grains. 

Pagdandi: A narrow village track, or a mountain path 

Panchamas: Dalits, untouchables 

Panda: a type of Hindu priest 

Panth: The Sikh community 

Parai: A round drum made of cattle skin 

Parayan: A member of a Parayar caste, one of the Scheduled Castes 
Parcha: Legal document for raiyati land 

Parwana: Legal document for Gairmazarua khas or Gairmazarua aam 
land. 

Pasmanda: Depressed, down-trodden 

Patil: ‘The headman of a village 

Patwari: The village accountant 

Pawada: Panegyric poetry in Marathi 

Periambalagar: A person belonging to an upper caste who delivers 
judgment in a caste panchayat. 

Pesha: Profession 

Pranam: A form of greeting addressed to those higher in age or rank. 
Premi: A follower of Dera Sacha Sauda 

Purohit: A priest 

Qaum: Community 

Raagi: Professional singer of hymns 

Radha soamis: A sect founded by Swami Ji, a holy person, in Dayal Bag 
Agra (UP); its offshoot in Punjab was founded by Baba Jamil Singh at Beas 
in Punjab. 

Rahit: Sikh code of conduct 

Raiyat, Raiyati: Tenant (on agricultural land), tenancy 

Rakba: Area 

Ramdasia: Weaver/cobbler converted to the Sikh religion 

Ramgarhia: An artisan/carpenter caste 

Ramoshi: A category of workers who were expected to perform any and 
all tasks in a village. 

Glossary 303 

Ramraiya: A follower of Ram Rai, son of Guru Har Rai 

Ravidasi: A devotee of Guru Ravidass 

Razil: Inferior 

Sacha sauda: A religious sect founded by Sant Shah Mastana Ji Maharaj, 
with headquarters in Sirsa, Haryana. 

Safaikamdar: Sweeper 

Sahajdhari: A follower of the Sikh taith who is not baptised. 

Sahan: Space around the homestead 

Sahitya: Literature 

Salam, malik: Greeting co the landlord 

Sanatan dharma: __Lierally, eternal religion, used for the traditional Hindu 
religion. 

Savarna: Twice-born (upper) castes 

Savitri pooja: A Hindu ceremony of worship where women fast and 
perform rituals to ensure a long life for their husbands, believing they will 
have the same husband in their seven births. 

Sewadar: A volunteer 

Sewapanthi: A follower of Ghahnaiya Ram who formed an order 
dedicated to the service of the wider community. 

Shastras: Hindu religious books 

Shindi: A local brew 

Smadh: A grave 

Sowars: Horsemen 

Surma: Cosmetic of antimony or lead sulphide for the eyes. 
Suthrashahi: Followers of Sant Suthra 

Tabligh: Propaganda 

Talathi: A worker appointed in the Marathwada region after the post of 
patwari was abolished in 1960; he looks after the land records, maintains 
village accounts, inspects crops and boundary marks, and prepares 
agricultural statistics. 

Talimkhana: Gymnasium 

Talookdar: A powerful landlord 

Tamasha: Traditional folk plays performed by the Dalit women in 
Maharashtra, still found in the western parts. 
“Taqwa: Consciousness of Allah 

Thhatti: Dwelling place of Dalits on the outskirts of a village. 

Thika: Contract 

Thinnai: A slightly elevated portion at the front of a traditional rural 
Tamil house. 

Udasis: A religious sect founded by Sri Siri Chand, son of Guru Nanak 
Dev. 

Ulama: Religious teachers 

304 Glossary 

‘urf: Alias 

Vaali: A metal bucket used for drawing water from a well. 

Varna: Any of the four classes into which the society was divided in the later 
Vedic age, viz., Brahman, Kshatriya, Vaishya and Shudra; the untouchables 
were sometimes considered as the fifth Varna (hence ‘panchama)). 

Varna vyavastha: Social order based on hereditary occupation/caste. 
Varnasamkara: Born of misogynous union or marriage 

Varnashrama: The four stages of life, occurring successively, which a 
devout Hindu is supposed to pass through. 

Vattai: A teacup in a caste Hindu tea-stall used exclusively for the Dalits. 
Wali: Governor 

Watan: Country; also used for the land belonging to a particular political 
order. 

Zamindar: Landlord (of earlier times) 

Zat: Caste 

Zindabad: Long live...! (usually in a political slogan) 

Notes on Contributors 

SuGUNA RAMANATHAN was Dean, Arts Faculty, and Head of the Department 
of English at St Xavier’s College, Ahmedabad. She co-edited a study on 
Dalit women titled The Silken Swing, and co-authored Journeys to Freedom: 
Dalit Narratives. She has published critical studies of novelists Iris Murdoch 
and C. P. Snow, a translation of poetry in collaboration, and a novel titled 

The Evening Gone. 

JyoTsna Macwan received a fellowship from the Ford Foundation. She has 
conducted training programmes for NGOs for strengthening their skills 
and capacity. She has edited publications in collaboration with Fernando 
Franco and Suguna Ramanathan, The Silken Swing, and, in Gujarati, with 
Isudas Vaghela and Shobhana Parmar. 

PapMA VELASKAR is a Professor at the Tata Institute of Social Sciences 
(TISS), Mumbai. She has published in the area of Sociology of Education, 
with focus on caste, class and gender issues in education, and in the area of 
Caste and Patriarchy. She was awarded the Times Fellowship for research on 

Dalit women of Maharashtra. 

A. RaMAIAH is a professor at TISS, and did his PhD on ‘Protest Movement 
and Scheduled Caste Identity: The Impact of Constitutional Provisions on 
the Scheduled Castes in Selected Villages of Tamil Nadu’ from Jawaharlal 
Nehru University (JNU), New Delhi. 

RITAMBHARA Heppar is an Associate Professor at TISS. She co-authored 
the project ‘Rural vs. Rural—An Exploration of the Category in Rural India 
with special reference to Maharashtra’. She published an article on “Tribal 
Self-Rule and Life Support Systems’ in The Encyclopaedia of Life Support 
Systems, Unesco, London. 

AsHok SINGH has held senior positions in mainstream newspapers as well 
as television. He worked as Consulting Editor, The Pioneer, and Executive 
Editor, BiTv. He was also political correspondent, The Indian Express. He is 
a columnist with The Pioneer, Sahara Times and Navhind Times. 

306 Notes on Contributors 

PG. Jocpanp is a Professor of Sociology, University of Mumbai, Mumbai. 
He has published works on Dalit movements, gender issues, human rights 
and empowerment of weaker sections. 

Ray Kumar is an Associate Professor at the English Department, 
University of Delhi. He is working on ‘Dalit Personal Narrative: A Study of 
Autobiography, Nation and Community’ for his PhD. 

Smita M. Patit is Assistant Professor in the School of Gender and 
Development Studies, Indira Gandhi National Open University (IGNOU), 
New Delhi. She has contributed papers in conferences on gender, caste, 
social movements, literature, policies, education and representation. 

SHASHI BHUSHAN Upapbuyay is an Associate Professor at IGNOU. His 
areas of interest include Labour History, Dalit Studies and Literary Studies. 
He authored Existence, Identity and Mobilization: The Cotton Millworkers 
of Bombay, 1890-1919, besides articles on historiography and Premchand. 

A. K. Biswas is a retired IAS officer and a former Vice-Chancellor of the 
Babasaheb Dr. B. R. Ambedkar Bihar University. He has contributed several 
papers in national periodicals and research journals on social-anthropology, 
demography, education, etc. 

PRATHAMA BANERJEE is a fellow of the Centre for the Study of Developing 
Societies, Delhi. She did her doctoral work from SOAS, London University, 
and wrote The Politics of Time: primitives’ and history-writing in a colonial 
society. Her article “The Werk of Imagination: Time and history in colonial 
Bengal’ was published in Subaltern Studies. 

IMT1az AHMAD was a Professor of Political Sociology at JNU. His Caste 
and Social Stratification among Muslims in India is a pioneering work. He 
has written research articles for national and international magazines on the 
politics of communalism, and electoral democracy. 

YOGINDER SIKAND works with the Centre for the Study of Social Exclusion 
at the National Law School, Bangalore. His publications are The Origins and 
Development of the Tablighi Jamaat, 1920-2000: A Cross-Country Comparative 
Study, and Sacred Spaces: Exploring Traditions of Shared Faith in India. 

Dr Ronki Ram is an Associate Professor and Chairman of the Department 
of Political Science, Panjab University, Chandigarh. He has contributed 
articles to The Journal of Asian Studies (Cambridge), Asian Survey (Berkeley), 
Journal of Punjab Studies (UCSB), Economic and Political Weekly, and Indian 
Journal of Politics (AMU). 

Sanjay Kumar is a scholar activist and Secretary, Deshkal Society, Delhi. 
He has jointly edited Asserting Voices: Changing Culture, Livelihood of the 
Musahar in the Gangetic Plain; Dalit Studies in Higher Education: Vision 
and Challenges; and Marginalisation of Dalit Muslims in India. He is 
researching on the theme of social hierarchy and the Notion of Educability 
in Government Primary Schools of Bihar.
